The Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis: Autism as a Long-Term Developmental Consequence of Perinatal Defence Against Hyponatraemia and Hypovolaemia

Kathleen Roberta Bell (Independent Researcher)

Zenodo: https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.18140467  |  OSF: osf.io/p3uvq  |  ResearchGate: ResearchGate

Version: March 2026  |  GitHub: canadiankaty.github.io/sodium-struggle

The Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis: Autism as a Long-Term

Developmental Consequence of Perinatal Defence Against

Hyponatraemia and Hypovolaemia

Author: Kathleen Roberta Bell (Independent Researcher)


Abstract

The Sodium-Struggle / Sodium–Volume Defence hypothesis frames a subset of autism as

a developmental imprint of recurrent movement toward low-effective-sodium pressure

during fetal and early postnatal calibration windows. The initiating signal is not clinical

hyponatraemia per se, but instability in the sodium-weighted support of extracellular

tonicity, effective arterial volume, and tissue perfusion. The central control architecture is

sodium–volume–perfusion defence: vasopressin (AVP), the

renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system (RAAS), autonomic vasomotor recruitment,

glucocorticoid support, and renal sodium–water conservation. Repeated recruitment is

hypothesised to produce distributed imprinting across vascular, renal, autonomic,

hypothalamic, glial, and ion-transport systems despite normal serum sodium.

At the cellular level, recurrent osmotic stress biases toward intracellular Na⁺ and Cl⁻

accumulation, NKCC1-weighted transport, subtle swelling, delayed chloride extrusion,

and prolonged depolarising GABA states, delaying inhibitory maturation and increasing

vulnerability of fast-spiking interneuron circuitry. Restoring these gradients raises

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase work, ATP demand, and purinergic signalling; as oxidative load rises,

ROS-driven BH4 depletion may reduce nitric-oxide signalling, bias vascular tone toward

constriction, and impair perfusion regulation.

At the hypothalamic level, perfusion threat recruits magnocellular AVP neurons in the

supraoptic nucleus (SON) and paraventricular nucleus (PVN); systemic and dendritic

AVP can then engage parvocellular AVP-expressing autonomic and CRH-linked

populations, amplifying sympathetic and endocrine defence while relatively

down-weighting oxytocin buffering. These imprinted operating points accommodate

normal serum sodium through compartmental compensation but anticipate phenotypic

heterogeneity and state-dependent unmasking during periods of increased orthostatic or

metabolic demand.

The hypothesis is not diagnostic, not universal, and not singularly causal; it is offered as a

testable physiological scaffold linking sodium–volume–perfusion defence, ionic

homeostasis, and autism-related neurodevelopmental divergence.

1 Introduction

Autism has resisted every simple story we have tried to tell about it. Decades of work have

mapped hundreds of risk genes, dozens of synaptic pathways, immune signatures, mitochondrial

anomalies, obstetric complications, and environmental associations. Yet these findings sit beside

one another rather than adding up. We can now sequence an exome overnight and image the

newborn brain in exquisite detail, and still we cannot explain why prevalence has risen so steeply

within a few decades, why liability clusters in certain families yet leaves most siblings unaffected,

why symptoms wax and wane with illness or stress, or why county-level autism rates trace

S-curves and arcs that mirror geological and hydrogeologic boundaries.

Genetics alone cannot explain autism. Existing models each resolve one slice of this puzzle.

Genetic and polygenic frameworks highlight strong heritability and implicate synaptic,

chromatin, and cytoskeletal genes, but they do not explain rapid prevalence changes or marked

regional contrasts. Synaptic and excitatory–inhibitory theories capture local circuit behaviour yet

struggle to account for endocrine and vascular signatures, autonomic differences around the

walking window, or geospatial clustering that follows hydrological corridors rather than gene

pools. Neuroinflammatory and microglial models illuminate important mechanisms but do not by

themselves explain why early development so often shows a characteristic sequence of cortical

overgrowth followed by plateau, with patchy laminar architecture, dendritic spine over-density,

and watershed perfusion anomalies. We can describe pieces of the phenotype, but we do not yet

know which physiological constraints were binding while autism was being built.

This manuscript asks a blunt question that autism research has largely overlooked: what variables

must be stabilised in fetal and early postnatal life, or the system fails?

Among all the variables the body manages, sodium-dependent perfusion is among the least

negotiable. Sodium regulation is not optional; breakdown of extracellular tonicity, effective

arterial volume, and pressure control is incompatible with life. The steep sodium gradient across

cell membranes powers action potentials, synaptic transmission, vesicle cycling, regulatory

volume change, and the ionic conditions under which inhibitory circuits mature. At the systems

level, sodium and water together sustain blood pressure, tissue perfusion, and extracellular



stability; if sodium falls too far, neurons fail, the heart fails, and consciousness is lost. Because

the stakes are this high, vertebrate physiology has conserved an unusually powerful defence

scaffold around sodium, water, and perfusion: arginine vasopressin (AVP) to regulate free water

and support circulatory defence, the renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system (RAAS) to conserve

sodium and maintain effective arterial volume, autonomic vasomotor control to redistribute flow,

and glucocorticoid support to preserve circulation when primary loops are strained. In vertebrate

physiology, sodium and volume are inseparable control variables: sodium stabilises extracellular

tonicity, while volume preserves perfusion. Survival physiology therefore defends them as a

coupled system rather than as independent variables.

The central conjecture of the Sodium-Struggle / Sodium–Volume Defence Hypothesis is that

autism, in a substantial subset of cases, may not arise from clinical hyponatraemia itself, and

often not from any measured hyponatraemia at all, but from repeated recruitment of this coupled

defence architecture as physiology moves toward low-effective-sodium pressure during sensitive

developmental windows. Low-effective-sodium pressure refers here not to a single laboratory

sodium value, but to movement toward a state in which sodium-weighted support of extracellular

tonicity, effective arterial volume, and local perfusion becomes unstable, or is transduced as

unstable by osmo-, stretch-, and perfusion-sensing systems. True clinical hyponatraemia is one

initiating route within this broader physiological space, not the defining requirement.

When that state is approached, control shifts toward high-gain short-horizon preservation of

arterial pressure, extracellular stability, and cerebral perfusion. The usual coupling among AVP,

RAAS, autonomic output, and glucocorticoid support can be reweighted or partially decoupled, at

times yielding the AVP–RAAS–cortisol discordant triad described later. In adults, brief episodes

of this defensive state are often reversible. In fetuses and infants, whose receptors, renal

set-points, glial networks, adrenal zones, autonomic circuits, vascular trees, and cortical perfusion

maps are being calibrated in real time, repeated recruitment of the same state may consolidate

into durable operating points. It is the history of how sodium–volume–perfusion instability was

stabilised, not the sodium value on a single laboratory report, that may leave the deepest marks.

This framing is explicitly anti-teleological. It does not posit a fixed anti-hyponatraemia script, a

uniform endocrine pattern, or a single developmental route. The control problem is emergency

triage under constraint: in fetal and early postnatal life, failure of sodium–volume–perfusion

control is immediately lethal, whereas long-horizon regulatory precision is negotiable at the

moment of threat. Under such constraint, stabilisation occurs through gain changes, threshold

reweighting, and routing shifts across endocrine, autonomic, vascular, renal, hypothalamic, and

cellular volume-control loops, without reference to later developmental costs and without

invoking cognition, choosing, or wanting. Which subsystems are recruited, and how strongly,

depends on insult class, severity, duration, developmental window, genetic background, and

pre-existing buffering capacity; some may be heavily engaged while others remain relatively

spared. When such recruitment is repeated during perinatal calibration windows, hysteresis can

convert short-term protective shifts into consolidated thresholds, structures, or set-points with

partial irreversibility. The enduring consequence proposed here is therefore not chronic

electrolyte abnormality but distributed regulatory imprinting across the sodium–volume survival

axis.



A key step in this reasoning is to distinguish measured sodium, effective sodium, and

low-effective-sodium pressure. Routine chemistry panels treat serum [Na⁺] and calculated

osmolality as if they fully capture the relevant control variable, but tissues and regulatory circuits

encounter sodium in relation to the other osmoles, water loads, barrier properties, vascular

signals, and ion-transport constraints that move with it. Gestational hyperglycaemia, non-ionic

osmoles such as polyols and some artificial sweeteners, and selected high-phosphate states can

raise tonicity with non-sodium species, creating what are later referred to as osmotic illusions.

Ion-poor hydration from low-mineral or membrane-treated water can expand plasma volume with

relatively little ionic support, while oncotic shifts and barrier leak can sequester sodium-bearing

plasma water away from effective circulation. On paper, laboratory values may look acceptable;

to tissues and endocrine circuits, sodium can still feel fragile, and physiology can behave as

though hyponatraemia or perfusion failure were always just over the horizon.

Across modern pregnancies and early life, diverse exposures converge on this

low-effective-sodium geometry. Classical routes include true dilutional hyponatraemia from

excess free water or SIADH-like states, salt-wasting profiles in prematurity or hyperemesis, and

translocational shifts in gestational diabetes. More modern routes include low-mineral or

membrane-treated drinking water that expands volume with few ions, phosphate-rich diets and

water-treatment regimes that alter RAAS regulation, sodium-pathway competitors that distort

membrane-level sensing, actin-disrupting toxins that blunt regulatory volume decrease, and

barrier or oncotic leaks that shift sodium-rich plasma water into the interstitium. Still others are

sodium-independent osmotic phenocopies, including excitotoxic metabolites, infection-associated

osmotic stress, and non-nutritive osmoles that increase tonicity without delivering cations. Taken

individually, these exposures may have modest epidemiologic effect sizes; taken together they

describe the same physiological corner: repeated threat to sodium–volume stability and repeated

recruitment of defence systems designed to prevent perfusion collapse.

At the cellular and vascular level, the consequences of this defensive state provide the bridge to

neurodevelopment. Although osmotic stress is one route into this state, chloride accumulation can

arise through several mechanisms, including cytoskeletal disruption that impairs regulatory

volume change, oxidative stress that alters ion-transporter regulation, and signalling environments

that favour NKCC1-weighted ion transport. Different upstream insults may therefore converge on

the same intracellular configuration of elevated Na⁺ and Cl⁻ with mild cellular swelling. Recurrent

sodium–volume threat, or repeated activation of the systems that defend against it, is expected to

favour volume-preserving ionic strategies that are adequate for short-term survival but less

precise for development: increased intracellular Na⁺ and Cl⁻, greater NKCC1 weighting, delayed

chloride extrusion, and subtle cellular swelling. In such conditions the cell effectively operates

within a shifted ionic neighbourhood, in which calcium signalling, excitability, and metabolic

regulation respond to a different electrochemical environment rather than to an isolated molecular

defect. Maintaining these gradients increases Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase work and metabolic demand; ATP

consumption rises, purinergic stress signalling may increase, and oxidative stress can accumulate.

In turn, BH4 availability may fall in some contexts and nitric-oxide-mediated vascular relaxation

may weaken, narrowing perfusion reserve precisely when ion-restorative work is becoming more

expensive. Under these conditions, sodium–volume defence systems may be recruited to preserve

circulation.



At the hypothalamic controller level, circulatory threat engages circuits that normally stabilise

sodium–volume–perfusion balance. These responses may include recruitment of magnocellular

vasopressin neurons in the supraoptic and paraventricular nuclei and, through dendritic signalling

within hypothalamic tissue, modulation of nearby parvocellular autonomic and neuroendocrine

neurons. The precise endocrine geometry is expected to be phase-dependent and

stimulus-dependent rather than uniform, with different defensive configurations variably

weighting AVP, RAAS, autonomic tone, and glucocorticoid support. These responses are not

intrinsically pathological. The hypothesis proposes that when they are repeatedly engaged during

developmental calibration windows, they imprint thresholds and recovery kinetics across renal

sodium handling, vascular reactivity, autonomic gain, hypothalamic controller balance, astrocytic

buffering, and cellular ion-transport systems.

At the tissue level these imprints are expected to matter most in late-maturing,

perfusion-vulnerable circuits. Neurons and glia must execute precise volume and chloride

transitions to exit proliferation, differentiate, stabilise synapses, and support pruning. If cells

remain even modestly shifted toward a chloride-weighted, swollen, energetically expensive state,

inhibitory maturation and circuit stabilisation may be delayed. Association cortex is particularly

exposed because metabolic demand is high, developmental timing is extended, and perfusion

reserve is comparatively narrow. This logic is consistent with autism findings that include early

cortical overgrowth followed by plateau, patchy lamination and minicolumn irregularity, dendritic

spine over-density, prolonged circuit immaturity, and watershed-biased perfusion anomalies. It

also provides a reason why divergence may become more visible during the walking window,

when upright locomotion simultaneously increases orthostatic challenge, cerebellar workload,

cortical motor activity, and ionic buffering demand, thereby unmasking physiological imprints

that were partially compensated during earlier infancy.

The same framework extends from individuals to landscapes. County-level autism prevalence in

the United States traces arcs that closely follow major geological and hydrogeologic boundaries.

Many of these transitions correspond to shifts from divalent-buffered Ca–Mg waters to

monovalent-dominant Na-rich regimes that are often further modified by softening or

reverse-osmosis treatment. These spatial coincidences do not prove causation, but they are

consistent with a world in which some regions more often deliver water chemistries that create

osmotic illusions—sodium that looks adequate on paper but is less effective at the

membrane—and thus more frequently engage sodium-struggle physiology during pregnancy and

early life.

This is a hypothesis, but a strong, convergent, mechanistically coherent hypothesis. It does not

claim that all autism is osmotic in origin, nor that sodium explains every finding. It proposes that

a single class of physiological events—recurrent movement toward sodium–volume instability

and repeated defence recruitment that prevents overt hyponatraemia and perfusion collapse—can

plausibly generate the endocrine geometries, ionic operating points, glial states, vascular biases,

autonomic signatures, hypothalamic controller shifts, cortical architectures, and geospatial

patterns that have until now been explained piecemeal. The remainder of this manuscript

develops that framework step by step, integrating endocrine physiology, cellular ion transport,

neurodevelopmental timing, and hydrochemical landscape patterns into a testable model of



autism as a timing-dependent imprint of repeated defence of sodium, volume, and perfusion

during development.


1.1 The Sodium–Volume Survival Axis

Because sodium–volume homeostasis is a foundational survival axis, early-life

instability in this system would be expected to have disproportionate downstream effects

on high-energy organs such as the developing brain.

Throughout this manuscript, sodium regulation is treated as one component of a

broader sodium–volume survival axis: an integrated control system that stabilises

effective arterial volume, extracellular tonicity, water distribution, and tissue perfusion

sufficiently to preserve cellular function and organ viability. The variables under defence

are not reducible to a single laboratory sodium value. They are maintained through

coordinated control of renal sodium handling, water balance, vascular tone, endocrine

output, and autonomic allocation.

This axis does not operate through one sensor or one hormone. Osmoregulatory

circuits respond primarily to effective osmolality and tonicity. Cardiovascular and renal

control systems respond to effective arterial volume, pressure, and perfusion. The kidney

also samples NaCl delivery through the macula densa and juxtaglomerular apparatus.

These inputs are tightly integrated but not identical, and under stress they need not point

in the same direction.

The hypothesis developed in this manuscript is therefore framed at the level of

control-system recruitment. The physiological issue is not only whether serum sodium

has crossed a diagnostic threshold, but whether the organism repeatedly enters states in

which sodium–volume stability is judged to be under threat and defensive recruitment

becomes necessary.


1.2 Clinical Hyponatraemia and Low-Effective-Sodium Pressure

Clinical hyponatraemia retains its standard clinical meaning throughout this

manuscript: a measured reduction in serum sodium concentration, interpreted alongside

tonicity and clinical context. True hypotonic hyponatraemia, translocational or hypertonic

hyponatraemia, and pseudohyponatraemia are therefore not collapsed into a single

physiological state, and the present hypothesis does not treat all low or apparently low

sodium values as equivalent.

For the broader manuscript construct, the term low-effective-sodium pressure is used

instead. This is a manuscript-level heuristic rather than a new laboratory analyte. For

clarity, effective sodium is used here as shorthand for the sodium-dependent contribution

to extracellular stability, not as a claim that physiology contains a dedicated receptor for a

separate quantity called ‘effective sodium’. The term refers to conditions in which the



relationship among extracellular sodium, free water, tonicity, and effective arterial

volume is moving in a sodium-lean or sodium-fragile direction such that sodium–volume

defence physiology is recruited even though serum sodium may remain within the

reference range.

Low-effective-sodium pressure may arise through more than one route, including true

sodium deficit, free-water excess, and states in which other effective osmoles alter water

distribution and secondarily challenge sodium–volume stability. These routes are not

physiologically identical. Hypotonic dilution, hypertonic translocation, and laboratory

pseudohyponatraemia must still be distinguished. The common feature relevant to this

hypothesis is repeated movement toward sodium–volume instability and repeated

recruitment of defensive physiology, not the assumption that every state shares the same

immediate osmotic signal.

Where later sections use anti-hyponatraemia or anti-hypovolaemia language as

descriptive shorthand, those phrases refer to components of this broader sodium–volume

defence problem and do not broaden the clinical definition of hyponatraemia.


1.3 Sodium–Volume Defence Physiology

When threat signalling is registered, the relevant response is sodium–volume defence

physiology: a coordinated survival programme rather than a single endocrine event. At

minimum this includes AVP-mediated water regulation, renin–angiotensin–aldosterone

recruitment, sympathetic vasomotor support, and glucocorticoid-mediated circulatory

support. Depending on context, the defence programme also extends into renal transport

behaviour, vascular reactivity, astrocytic and epithelial water handling, and cellular

ion-transport responses recruited to preserve volume and tonicity under threat.

AVP behaviour in this framework is phase-dependent, compartment-dependent, and

stimulus-dependent. Hypotonic dilution may suppress AVP. Hypertonicity may stimulate

AVP. Circulatory underfilling, reduced effective arterial volume, pain, stress, or nausea

may recruit AVP non-osmotically. The model therefore does not predict a single AVP

direction. It predicts the possibility of discordant or oscillatory signalling across the

broader defence system, especially when osmotic and haemodynamic inputs diverge

across maternal, placental, fetal, or postnatal compartments.

This point is central to the manuscript’s falsifiability. The relevant unit of analysis is

not serum sodium alone, and not AVP alone, but the pattern of sodium–volume defence

recruitment across integrated controllers, particularly the relative geometry of AVP,

RAAS, glucocorticoid-linked haemodynamic support, autonomic tone, and their

downstream transport and perfusion consequences. Later sections examine whether

repeated recruitment may extend into chloride-weighted cellular adaptations, altered

cotransporter balance, and other volume-regulatory ion-handling responses, but those

downstream effectors are not assumed at the definitional stage.



Where later sections refer to vasopressin escape, the term is reserved for its specific

renal sense of persistent AVP exposure with partial renal escape from antidiuresis.


1.4 Developmental Relevance and Evidentiary Boundaries

In mature physiology, many sodium–volume defence responses are transient and

reversible. During fetal and early postnatal calibration windows, however, repeated

recruitment of this survival axis may alter response thresholds, receptor weighting,

autonomic allocation, renal water-handling expectations, vascular reactivity, and cellular

ionic baselines. The developmental claim of this manuscript is therefore not that autism

requires chronic clinical hyponatraemia. It is that repeated survival-axis recruitment

during sensitive windows may leave durable physiological imprints even when frank

sodium collapse never occurs.

This hypothesis is framed as developmental systems physiology rather than as

electrolyte speculation. Diverse upstream exposures may converge on the same control

problem, namely repeated defence of sodium–volume stability during periods when

endocrine, autonomic, renal, vascular, and ionic systems are still being calibrated.

Environmental or behavioural contributors are treated as testable contributors to this

control problem, not as established universal causes.

For that reason, later sections focus on the structure of the defence response itself:

which sensor systems are recruited, which controllers dominate under different

conditions, how recovery is achieved, and whether repeated recruitment predicts later

endocrine, autonomic, vascular, or ionic drift. Box 1 separates near-term biomarker

expectations from later tissue-level predictions to keep the model falsifiable. With these

definitions and boundaries established, Section 2 formalises the core physiological claim

linking repeated sodium–volume defence recruitment to developmental imprinting.

Box 1A. Immediate biomarkers and near-term readouts

Purpose: measures relevant to testing current or recent sodium–volume defence recruitment.

●​ Serum sodium interpreted with measured or calculated osmolality and clinical context


●​ Copeptin or AVP interpreted phase-specifically and compartment-specifically

●​ Plasma renin and aldosterone patterns, including episodic surges or instability rather than only static

means

●​ Cortisol together with markers of stress-linked haemodynamic support


●​ Urine osmolality, urinary sodium, and renal water-handling markers such as AQP2-related readouts

where feasible


●​ Autonomic or perfusion proxies, including heart-rate variability, vascular tone measures, or blood

pressure lability where available


Box 1B. Later tissue-level predictions



Purpose: putative downstream adaptations to be tested in later sections rather than assumed in §1.

●​ Altered weighting of AVP, RAAS, glucocorticoid, and autonomic thresholds


●​ Renal water-handling recalibration and altered recovery logic after osmotic or perfusion stress

●​ Receptor and transporter drift across endocrine, vascular, and ion-handling systems

●​ Chloride-weighted cellular adaptations, altered cotransporter balance, and persistent volume-regulatory

bias


●​ Central autonomic or hypothalamic reweighting consistent with repeated survival-axis recruitment

2 Core Physiological Claim

This hypothesis proposes that a subset of autism arises from repeated recruitment of

sodium–volume defence physiology during fetal and early postnatal development, when

endocrine, renal, vascular, and neural control systems are still being calibrated. The common

upstream state is not frank sodium depletion in the narrow clinical sense, but

low-effective-sodium pressure: reduced stability in the organism’s ability to preserve effective

arterial volume, extracellular tonicity, and tissue perfusion. Clinical hyponatraemia is therefore

not required, and may often be avoided, because compensatory physiology is recruited before

overt collapse occurs.



The proximate physiological translation of this unstable state is proposed to be mild but recurrent

ionic and vascular stress. Cells may shift toward ion-transport strategies that favour osmotic

stability, including increased intracellular Na⁺ and Cl⁻, greater NKCC1 weighting, and subtle

cellular swelling. As intracellular Na⁺ and Cl⁻ accumulation increases the energetic cost of

restoring ion gradients, ATP demand rises and metabolic stress signalling intensifies. Under these

conditions extracellular ATP release and purinergic signalling may increase, while oxidative

stress rises. Consequently, BH4 availability may fall and nitric oxide signalling may weaken,

ultimately narrowing endothelial relaxation reserve and reducing the capacity for adaptive

vasodilation. In this model, perfusion threat emerges not only from absolute volume loss, but also

from the combination of ionic stress, reduced vasodilatory capacity, and impaired local flow

regulation.

Threatened perfusion then recruits an integrated sodium–volume defence architecture comprising

AVP, RAAS, autonomic vasomotor control, glucocorticoid-mediated circulatory support, and

renal sodium and water conservation. The hypothesis does not require AVP, RAAS, or cortisol to

be uniformly elevated, nor to move in parallel at every stage; their recruitment is expected to be

phase-dependent and stimulus-dependent. At the hypothalamic controller level, circulatory threat

is proposed to engage magnocellular vasopressin neurons in the supraoptic and paraventricular

nuclei. Their systemic AVP output supports water conservation and vascular defence, while

dendritic AVP release within hypothalamic tissue may recruit nearby parvocellular

AVP-expressing neurons, including presympathetic and CRH-linked populations. Dendritic

vasopressin signalling within the paraventricular nucleus is known to influence neighbouring

hypothalamic circuits and provides a plausible mechanism by which circulatory defence states

could recruit parvocellular autonomic and neuroendocrine neurons. This provides a route by

which vasopressin signalling amplifies sympathetic and cortisol-based circulatory support,

making hypothalamic output relatively more vasopressin-weighted with reduced

oxytocin-mediated buffering during survival physiology.

When this defensive configuration is repeatedly engaged during developmental calibration

windows, the enduring consequence is proposed to be distributed imprinting across the

sodium–volume survival axis rather than persistent electrolyte abnormality. Potential imprint sites

include regulatory thresholds for AVP and RAAS recruitment, sympathetic vasomotor gain, renal

sodium handling, endothelial nitric oxide responsiveness, vascular stretch and baroreflex sensing,

astrocytic osmotic buffering, and cellular ion-transport programs. The lasting signature would

therefore reside in altered controller thresholds, signalling ratios, sensor sensitivity, and recovery

kinetics across endocrine, autonomic, vascular, renal, and ionic systems.

Within the developing brain, where precise ion gradients regulate neuronal maturation, these

imprinted operating points may prolong chloride-weighted and mildly swollen cellular states and

delay the normal developmental shift from NKCC1-weighted chloride accumulation toward

KCC2-mediated chloride extrusion. Because intracellular chloride gradients determine whether

GABA signalling is depolarising or inhibitory, prolonged chloride-weighted states may delay

maturation of inhibitory networks, glial buffering systems, and perfusion-regulatory circuits.

These alterations may remain partly compensated in early infancy and become more apparent

during later developmental transitions that increase orthostatic, metabolic, and sensorimotor



demand, including the onset of upright locomotion. In this framework, autism is hypothesised to

represent a downstream developmental imprint of repeated sodium–volume defence recruitment,

rather than a direct consequence of sodium deficiency or overt hyponatraemia itself.


3 Calibration Inputs to the Sodium–Volume Defence Axis

During Fetal and Early Perinatal Development

A central premise of the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis is that developmental calibration is not

organized around a single maternal analyte such as serum sodium. During fetal life, with

refinement continuing through the early perinatal transition, the developing organism calibrates

its long-term sodium–volume defence architecture from the integrated output of the maternal

sodium–volume defence system during physiological challenge. The relevant developmental

input is therefore not maternal sodium concentration per se, but the behavioural pattern by which

maternal physiology detects and corrects osmotic, circulatory, and perfusion disturbances.

In Arthur C. Guyton’s classical systems physiology framework, total body sodium content and

extracellular fluid volume act as a tightly coupled regulatory pair governing long-term circulatory

stability through renal–vascular feedback. Through this feedback architecture, altered sodium

balance changes extracellular fluid volume, arterial pressure, and renal excretory behaviour in

parallel. Extracellular tonicity here refers to plasma tonicity, determined primarily by effective

extracellular osmoles—chiefly sodium salts—relative to water. Plasma tonicity governs

transcellular water distribution and therefore cellular volume regulation. These variables are not

identical, but they are not independently defended. Disturbance at one node propagates across the

others and appears concurrently as altered circulatory filling, tissue perfusion, renal handling, and

cellular hydration.

Because this coupled sodium–volume–tonicity axis is fundamental to survival, its control

architecture must be calibrated early. Calibration is not confined to intrauterine life. It extends

into the early perinatal period, when placental circulation ceases, pulmonary circulation begins,

and sodium–volume regulation shifts rapidly from maternal buffering to autonomous neonatal

control. During fetal life the developing organism cannot directly regulate external sodium

supply, water intake, or effective circulatory state. It therefore receives the maternal response

pattern as a composite physiological signal conveyed through endocrine, vascular, autonomic,

inflammatory, and brainstem-linked channels. During early neonatal life the same architecture is

further refined as those control loops begin to operate independently. These inputs shape receptor

density, ligand sensitivity, transporter expression, neural gain, and response thresholds.

Development therefore establishes not only organs, but the control parameters that will later

defend extracellular volume, plasma tonicity, tissue perfusion, and cellular volume.

During fetal and early perinatal development, calibration inputs arise through multiple convergent

domains:



A. Osmotic and Electrolyte Inputs

• Effective extracellular sodium availability relative to water​

• Extracellular chloride availability and broader anion balance​

• Maternal osmoreceptor recruitment in response to plasma osmolality and tonicity​

• Patterns of cellular osmolyte regulation associated with maternal osmotic signalling


B. Volume, Pressure, and Perfusion Inputs

• Effective arterial blood volume, as indexed by maternal RAAS and AVP recruitment​

• Arterial pressure and vascular tone​

• Regional perfusion distribution, including brain-sparing responses during circulatory stress​

• Placental perfusion state, including subclinical hypoperfusion signals indicating unstable

volume or pressure control


C. Endocrine and Receptor–Ligand Inputs

• Maternal AVP recruitment pattern and pulsatility, conditioning fetal vasopressin receptor

balance​

• Maternal RAAS tone influencing angiotensin receptor signalling, particularly AGTR1-linked

sodium-conserving pathways​

• Cortisol availability together with MR/GR balance, establishing the relative weight of

mineralocorticoid and glucocorticoid signalling​

• Maternal thyroid tone regulating Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase activity, metabolic rate, and osmolyte handling


D. Stress, Cytokine, and Sympathetic Inputs

• Maternal inflammatory cytokine activity altering barrier behaviour and osmotic interpretation​

• Maternal sympathetic tone affecting vascular resistance, renin recruitment, and adrenal output​

• Broader autonomic patterning shaping fetal vascular and adrenal response thresholds


E. Neural and Vascular Threat-Interpretation Inputs

• Arterial baroreceptor signalling​

• Cardiopulmonary stretch receptor input​

• Endothelial mechanosensing of shear stress and vascular pressure, including

glycocalyx-mediated signalling​

• Vagal afferent traffic to the nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS)​



• NTS integration of circulatory threat signals and downstream recruitment of hypothalamic

vasopressin systems through NTS–hypothalamic signalling pathways

This neural domain links pressure and volume sensing to central threat classification and

endocrine response selection.


These domains are analytically separable but physiologically interdependent. Upstream

disturbances may enter the system through different initial pathways, but none remain isolated.

Some perturbations act primarily on plasma tonicity by altering water handling, membrane

transport, barrier permeability, or inflammatory state. Others enter through effective arterial

underfilling or perfusion instability. Venous pooling, reduced vascular tone, or connective-tissue

laxity can reduce effective arterial blood volume and tissue perfusion, thereby recruiting

coordinated RAAS–AVP responses even when sodium intake is not low. A third class acts more

directly on effective sodium availability, for example by expanding free water relative to sodium

or by shifting endocrine control toward water retention. Regardless of the entry point, the

disturbance propagates through the same coupled sodium–volume–tonicity axis.

Within this architecture, circulating hormone concentration alone does not define signalling

strength. For AVP, renin–angiotensin ligands, aldosterone, cortisol, oxytocin, and FGF-23,

biological effect depends on the receptor and cellular context in which the signal is transduced.

Receptor density, ligand–receptor ratios, adrenal zonation, enzymatic gating by 11β-HSD1 and

11β-HSD2, and downstream coupling efficiency determine signal magnitude and direction.

Normal, low, or elevated circulating concentrations may therefore coexist with weak, strong, or

biased physiological output.

Cortisol illustrates this principle. Serum cortisol does not by itself measure effective

glucocorticoid signalling. Shifted adrenal zonation toward zona fasciculata output, increased GR

sensitivity, reduced MR influence, or altered 11β-HSD gating can generate a functionally

cortisol-biased state even at modest circulating cortisol concentrations. Normal or low measured

cortisol therefore does not exclude a cortisol-dominant regulatory architecture; the decisive

variable is the receptor–enzymatic context in which the signal is interpreted, dictating how

strongly the developing organism will rely on glucocorticoids to backstop circulatory volume.

While all developing organisms receive these calibration inputs, the coherence of these signals

varies. In stable physiological states, osmotic, volume, and autonomic signals align, allowing

baseline calibration of sodium–volume defence thresholds. Conversely, when inputs are

discordant—driven by acute maternal events such as dehydration or inflammatory activation,

placental perfusion instability, or intrinsic fetal variation in ion transport and receptor

sensitivity—the developing system must calibrate under constraint. The resulting architecture

may show shifted thresholds, altered receptor balance, biased endocrine recruitment, or persistent

differences in cellular volume and ionic handling.



The fetus does not inherit an absolute maternal sodium concentration. It inherits the maternal

regulatory strategy: the characteristic pattern by which the maternal sodium–volume defence

system interprets and responds to osmotic deviation, volume threat, pressure instability, and

perfusion stress. That response logic, established during fetal life and consolidated through the

early perinatal transition, becomes the template from which the developing organism builds its

own long-term sodium–volume defence architecture.


Section 4 - Maternal-Fetal Signal Transformation and Calibration

of the Sodium-Volume Defence Axis

Maternal osmotic, endocrine, and circulatory perturbations do not reach the fetus directly. At the

maternal-fetal interface, placental transport, peptide degradation, endocrine filtering, and local

vascular regulation convert them into a composite fetal signal. That signal is then interpreted by fetal

endocrine and renal systems and may be re-encountered through recirculating fetal fluid

compartments. Developmental calibration therefore proceeds through maternal perturbation,

placental signal transformation, fetal endocrine and renal decoding, amniotic-fluid recirculation, and

repeated exposure over time.


4.1 Maternal-Fetal Partitioning and Enzymatic Barriers

The placenta functions as a transport epithelium, peptide-metabolising barrier, endocrine filter, and

vascular regulatory surface. Maternal sodium balance, water balance, AVP bioavailability, cortisol

exposure, and perfusion signals therefore reach the fetus only after placental processing.

A central consequence is dissociation between transmission of the fluid state and transmission of the

maternal AVP response. Changes in maternal fluid composition can influence fetal plasma

composition because water and small-solute fluxes are transmitted across the placenta, but the

maternal AVP signal that accompanies those changes is not transmitted equivalently: maternal AVP is

degraded by placental vasopressinase, peptide transfer is limited, and placental 11beta-HSD2

similarly restricts direct cortisol transfer. The fetus can therefore encounter the dilutional

consequences of a maternal water-retentive state without receiving the full maternal AVP signal that

generated or corrected it. This mismatch is physiologically important because it allows fetal tonicity

cues and circulatory threat cues to diverge despite apparently normal maternal sodium

concentration.

Local placental vascular physiology further shapes the signal. Uteroplacental RAAS balance,

endothelial sensing, and placental perfusion state modify the context in which transport and

endocrine partitioning occur. The fetal endocrine milieu is therefore the product of placental

processing plus fetal response, not a direct copy of maternal hormone concentrations.



4.1.1 Parity-Dependent / Vasopressinase-Burden Hypothesis

Reported parity-related epidemiological observations, including first-born effects where they are

observed, are more plausibly interpreted physiologically as variation in vasopressinase burden than

as a primary effect of parity itself. The major determinants are more likely gestational age, placental

mass and syncytiotrophoblast burden, multiple gestation, placental disease, and maternal hepatic

clearance; parity is better treated as a modifier than as the main mechanism. Under this formulation,

altered vasopressinase burden changes the relation between maternal water-retentive signalling and

fetal dilution exposure, and thereby changes maternal-fetal AVP partitioning.


4.2 Placental Transport Asymmetry and Modern Amplification

Placental transport asymmetry is kinetic and regulatory rather than absolute: glucose transfer is

high-capacity and largely GLUT1-mediated, water moves efficiently along osmotic gradients through

aquaporin-linked pathways, and sodium transfer is substantial but actively regulated by

syncytiotrophoblast Na+/H+ exchangers, Na+/K+-ATPase, and ENaC-related machinery. The placenta

therefore shapes the fetal sodium-water signal actively rather than merely delaying it.

The consequence is not sodium exclusion but transient disequilibrium. Glucose and water can shift

fetal tonicity faster than sodium balance is fully re-equilibrated, so the effective contribution of

sodium salts to extracellular tonicity may fall relative to water and other osmoles. From the fetal

perspective, this can resemble anti-hyponatraemic compensation even when maternal sodium

concentration is not frankly abnormal.

Repeated glycaemic excursions and repeated electrolyte-free or low-solute fluid loading are sufficient

examples of maternal perturbations that can amplify this signal transformation by generating

oscillatory inputs without requiring overt maternal dysnatremia.

The placental osmotic signal may also include organic osmolytes. Myo-inositol, taurine, and sorbitol

participate in cell-volume defence, and NFAT5-linked regulation provides a plausible route by which

osmotic stress could alter their partitioning. This layer does not replace the sodium-volume-tonicity

axis; it modifies how fetal tissues buffer changes in plasma tonicity.



Figure 4.1. Maternal-fetal signal transformation pathway. Maternal dilutional, osmotic, or circulatory perturbations are

transformed by placental transport and endocrine filtering, decoded by fetal endocrine and renal systems, reinforced by

amniotic-fluid recirculation, and consolidated as developmental calibration inputs.


4.3 The Fetal Endocrine Readout: The Discordant Triad

When placentally filtered dilutional cues coexist with threatened effective arterial filling or placental

perfusion, the fetal endocrine readout may take the form of a mixed dilution-underfilling state. Its

characteristic components are reduced fetal AVP signalling relative to the degree of dilution,

increased fetal RAAS activity, and cortisol recruitment when circulatory threat persists. The logic is

dissociative: the fluid and tonicity cue is transmitted more readily than the maternal AVP response,

while the fetus simultaneously interprets underfilling or perfusion threat through sodium-conserving

pathways.

This triad is context-dependent and should not be treated as a universal fetal stress signature. Acute

hypoxaemic or severe stress states can increase fetal AVP. The specific claim is that, under conditions

of placental signal transformation, osmotic attenuation of AVP can coexist with circulatory

recruitment of RAAS and cortisol.

4.4 Fetal Renal Decoding and Amniotic-Fluid Signal Amplification

The fetus is not a passive recipient of placental information. The developing kidney decodes the

transformed signal through macula densa sensing of distal sodium chloride delivery, renin-cell

recruitment, developmental maturation of NKCC2, ENaC, and AQP2, and changing mineralocorticoid

responsiveness. The same placental input can therefore generate different renal and endocrine

outputs at different gestational stages. Fetal ANP and BNP provide potential counter-signals when

intravascular expansion predominates.

After the initial fetal readout, the transformed signal can be amplified by recirculating fetal fluid

compartments. Fetal plasma composition shapes fetal urine; fetal urine contributes substantially to

amniotic fluid; and amniotic fluid is re-sampled through fetal swallowing and intramembranous

return. A transient placental cue can therefore recur as fetal plasma, fetal urine, amniotic fluid, fetal

swallowing, and fetal plasma again. The fetus may thus experience multiple cycles of the same

transformed osmotic signal, increasing the effective duration of exposure and converting transient

maternal states into repeated developmental inputs.



Figure 4.2. Amniotic-fluid recirculation loop. A transformed placental cue can be re-encountered through fetal urine

production, amniotic-fluid composition, and swallowing or intramembranous return, increasing developmental salience.


4.5 Temporal Vulnerability: Pre-Keratinization Window and NFAT5 Imprinting

A period of heightened osmotic vulnerability likely exists before and around completion of skin

keratinization, approximately 20-24 weeks' gestation. Placental exchange remains the dominant

route of systemic maternal-fetal transfer, but buffering is likely weaker during this interval because

barrier maturation is incomplete and fetal skin permeability is higher. Transient oscillations in

extracellular tonicity may therefore be less effectively damped.

NFAT5 provides a plausible molecular integrator of this window because it coordinates

osmoprotective gene programmes, including osmolyte transporters and water-handling pathways.

Repeated exposure to transformed sodium-volume-tonicity signals during this period could plausibly

bias later endocrine recruitment thresholds, renal transporter expression, and osmolyte buffering

systems. Astrocytes and barrier-associated cells are relevant because they depend heavily on organic

osmolytes for cell-volume defence. These effects are best framed as plausible programming of

control parameters rather than as fixed lesions.


4.6 Integration with Later Control Systems

These transformed fetal signals provide a physiological route by which later brainstem threat

classification, hypothalamic AVP recruitment, and sodium-conserving endocrine behaviour may

become biased.

Repeated exposure to these placentally transformed signals provides the developmental context in

which regulatory thresholds are established.



The fetus therefore calibrates not to maternal sodium concentration alone, but to the placentally

transformed strategy by which maternal physiology defends the coupled sodium-volume-tonicity

axis.


Section 5 — The Physical Constraint

Figure placement note. Insert the Tri-Axial Constraint schematic here, before the first definitional

paragraph.


Section 4 established that fetal physiology calibrates to patterned signals rather than laboratory

values, and that sodium, water, and perfusion are inseparable variables within a single coupled

control problem.

Section 5 makes the hinge claim explicit: during gestation and early life, the developing organism

cannot reliably stabilise effective sodium, effective circulating volume, and vascular tone and

containment simultaneously under realistic conditions of placental transport asymmetry,

haemodynamic variability, and tonicity fluctuation.

Instability in any one of these axes forces compensatory strain on the other two, and vascular tone

and containment can independently fail or be recruited as compensation even when measured

sodium or total water appear acceptable.


5.1 The Tri-Axial Constraint

Hyponatraemia and hypovolaemia are commonly described as separate clinical categories, but in

a developmental control context they are better treated as two faces of a shared threat whose third

axis is vascular tone and vascular containment. The relevant threat is not a single measured

sodium concentration or a single compartment size. It is instability in effective perfusion when

extracellular tonicity is unreliable and when vascular containment cannot be assumed. Effective

sodium, as used throughout this manuscript, refers to sodium’s contribution to the tonic signal at

cell membranes. Effective circulating volume refers to the perfusion-effective fraction of

extracellular volume, not total body water. Vascular tone and vascular containment determine

whether that volume remains perfusion-effective, and therefore can be a limiting determinant of

microperfusion. When tonicity, volume, and containment are coupled, disturbances that appear

“water-first” or “volume-first” at the bedside converge on the same problem for the fetus:

maintaining microperfusion while preventing injurious cell-volume shifts in a system with limited

buffering capacity and ongoing construction.


5.2 Trade-Offs Within the Constraint

This coupling is not an abstract modelling choice. It follows from physical constraints. Defending

effective circulating volume in a fluid system tends to favour water conservation because water

can be retained rapidly and redistributed quickly, and it can also favour resistance-based support

when volume cannot be made perfusion-effective. Yet water retention without proportional solute

retention dilutes sodium’s share of tonicity and lowers effective sodium. Conversely, defending

tonicity tends to constrain water availability and promote solute-weighted strategies, which can

limit plasma expansion and increase reliance on vascular tone and containment to sustain pressure

and distribution. In either direction, the intended stabilisation along one axis perturbs the other

axes. The coupled system therefore presents trade-offs rather than separable goals.



5.3 Microperfusion as the Immediate Bottleneck

A final coupling is haemodynamic. Any defence that shifts tonicity, volume distribution, or

vasomotor constraint can impair microperfusion. Volume-preserving moves that lower tonicity

increase swelling pressure in endothelial, perivascular, and glial compartments, which can

compress or destabilise the microvascular unit and weaken vascular containment even when total

body water is preserved. Tone-preserving moves that limit water or increase vasomotor constraint

can reduce capillary recruitment and regional flow reserve even when tonicity is maintained and

extracellular volume is not overtly depleted. The defended variable is therefore not “sodium” or

“volume” in isolation, but effective perfusion under tonicity uncertainty, with microperfusion as

the immediate bottleneck.


Section 6 — The Cost of Defence

Defence is therefore unavoidable, mutually destabilising, and often self-reinforcing.

The cost of that repeated defensive work is developmental, not because the responses are

erroneous, but because they are deployed during windows when transporter gain, osmolyte

economies, receptor hierarchies, and structural reserve are still being constructed.

Because sodium–volume instability is coupled, the responses recruited to stabilise effective

sodium, effective circulating volume, and vascular tone and containment are also coupled and can

become self-reinforcing. The central loop begins with the fact that vasopressin-mediated volume

rescue can conserve free water faster than sodium can be conserved or restored. When effective

circulating volume signalling is low or unstable, antidiuretic mechanisms can rapidly retain water

and support pressure, but this same shift can reduce effective sodium by lowering sodium’s

relative contribution to extracellular tonicity. In developmental contexts where sodium restoration

is constrained by placental transfer dynamics, renal immaturity, or concurrent endocrine

geometry, the water component of rescue can dominate the early response.

Lowered extracellular tonicity is not a benign bookkeeping change. Reduced tonicity drives water

entry into cells, including endothelial and astrocytic compartments that sit at the interface

between circulating blood, interstitial space, and tissue microdomains. Swelling in these

compartments can degrade vascular containment and alter the partitioning of water between

plasma and interstitium. Even when total body water is preserved or increased, the distribution

can shift away from the perfusion-effective intravascular fraction. The result is a state in which

apparent volume is present but effective circulating volume remains threatened, not because fluid

is absent, but because it is no longer contained where it supports pressure and microperfusion.

This redistribution feeds directly back into the original signalling problem. When vascular

containment weakens and plasma water is displaced into interstitial compartments,

baroreceptor-linked and perfusion-linked pathways can continue to signal effective circulating

volume threat. Recruitment of further volume rescue can then be sustained, often through

additional antidiuretic tone and vasomotor support, which further loads the tonicity axis and can

narrow the microperfusion margin through constraint. In parallel, tonicity-linked cellular effects

can narrow microvascular function and increase the discrepancy between total water and

perfusion-effective water. The loop is therefore not a sequence of two independent problems. It is

a single coupled oscillation in which attempts to stabilise volume tend to create dilution pressure,



and dilution pressure tends to recreate effective volume threat through containment and

microperfusion effects.

This is the mechanistic basis for persistence. Once defence is repeatedly recruited, the control

system can cycle between states in which tonicity is challenged by water retention and states in

which effective circulating volume is challenged by redistribution and microvascular constraint,

with vascular tone and containment acting as both a compensatory handle and a failure point.

Oscillation does not require extreme inputs. It can emerge from moderate, recurrent perturbations

when recovery kinetics are slow and when the system repeatedly re-enters a defensive range

before fully returning to baseline. In development, where buffering capacity and structural reserve

are still being established, this repeated recruitment becomes a defining feature of the

physiological environment being imprinted.


6.1 Energetic Cost

Repeated activation of sodium–volume defence is uniquely costly during development because it

is imposed on a system that is simultaneously building its hardware and calibrating its control

software. The immediate work of defence is energetically demanding. Restoring ionic gradients

after dilution, maintaining membrane potential under shifting extracellular conditions, and

re-establishing osmolyte balance require sustained ion pumping, transporter cycling, and

coordinated water handling. These processes draw heavily on ATP at precisely the time when

oxygen delivery and regional perfusion can be constrained by the same defensive haemodynamics

and vasomotor constraint that preserve pressure. The energetic cost is therefore not an optional

surcharge; it is embedded in the physical requirements of cell-volume stability, extracellular

homeostasis, and vascular containment.


6.2 Timing Cost

The timing cost is equally central. Developmental programmes are not only sensitive to the

magnitude of perturbations but also to their temporal structure. Many differentiation and

maturation steps depend on stable extracellular conditions and predictable endocrine context.

When the organism repeatedly enters a defensive state, signalling patterns that would otherwise

be transient become a recurrent background. This recurrence can distort threshold calibration,

shift which cues are treated as reliable, and alter how rapidly responses are triggered. The

outcome is not framed here as injury from a single episode, but as cumulative developmental

timing pressure from repeated state transitions during sensitive windows.

Figure placement note. Insert the Osmotic Pendulum / Time-Scale Mismatch diagram here, linked to slow

osmolyte replenishment, delayed transporter and aquaporin relocalisation, persistent endocrine gain, and

re-entry into defence before full restitution.


Recovery is slow in the fetal context for multiple convergent reasons. Compatible osmolyte pools

replenish gradually relative to the speed at which they can be depleted during swelling responses.

Transporter and aquaporin localisation changes can lag behind the initiating event, and endocrine

gain changes may decay more slowly than the perturbation that elicited them. In addition,

ongoing placental exchange means the fetal compartment does not simply “close the loop”

internally; it remains coupled to maternal and placental dynamics, including perfusion variability

and osmotic gradients. In this setting, repeated perturbations can arrive before full restitution,

converting discrete events into an effective chronicity without requiring a chronic initiating insult.



6.3 Imprinting Cost

A final developmental consequence is imprinting. Repeated defence does not merely tax the

system in real time. It provides a patterned training signal that stabilises new operating points.

Tonicity-responsive transcriptional programmes, transporter expression profiles, aquaporin

abundance and localisation, and receptor gain settings can consolidate toward values that reduce

the immediate risk of catastrophic dilution, vascular containment failure, or perfusion collapse

under the experienced conditions. That consolidation can persist even when the initiating stressors

abate, because the developing organism is not restoring a prior adult baseline. It is constructing its

baseline under the statistics of the environment it repeatedly encounters. The long-term cost is

therefore a trade-off embedded in set-point architecture: protective stability under expected

coupled stress, purchased by altered response thresholds and recovery kinetics.


Section 7 — Genetic Modifiers of Recovery Bandwidth

The coupled defence problem described above applies broadly, but the developmental cost of

repeated activation is not uniform across individuals. Genetic variation and structural reserve act

as constraints on resilience, gain, and recovery across the three-axis problem of effective sodium,

effective circulating volume, and vascular tone and containment. Within this framework, genes

are not treated as primary causes of defence activation. They are treated as modifiers of how

expensive defence becomes once engaged, how strongly it must be recruited to stabilise effective

perfusion, and how completely the system can reset between episodes.


7.1 Structural Reserve

Two constraint classes are especially relevant. The first is structural reserve. Nephron

endowment, established during a time-limited construction window, sets the baseline headroom

for sodium and water handling across the life course. Reduced nephron endowment functions as a

gain amplifier: the same upstream sodium–volume perturbation requires higher endocrine drive

and tighter haemodynamic and vasomotor control to maintain effective circulating volume and

effective sodium. This is not a reversible tuning issue. It is a fixed ceiling that forces

software-level compensation to run at higher gain.


7.2 Molecular Resilience and Primary Genetic Constraints

The second class is molecular resilience. Table 5-4 enumerates primary ASD-associated genetic

vulnerabilities that map directly onto core sodium–volume defence components. The shared logic

across these genes is not that they “cause” sodium–volume instability, but that they reduce safety

margins in precisely the subsystems that carry the burden of defence.

Under-tested primary candidates

The genes listed above were selected conservatively based on existing ASD evidence. However,

the Sodium-Struggle framework makes explicit predictions about additional genes that would

qualify as primary vulnerabilities. These candidates include:

REN: Central to renin synthesis capacity and RAAS availability, directly determining

sodium–volume defence thresholds and persistence.

HSD11B2: Governs cortisol–mineralocorticoid receptor specificity; variants could shift sodium

defence toward cortisol-mediated substitution.

WNK / KLHL3 pathway components: Regulate chloride-weighted sodium sensing and

proxy-signal dominance in volume defence.

AQP4: Controls astrocytic water flux and extracellular space dynamics, potentially influencing

the initiation and resolution of osmotic stress signals.

AGT / ACE: Set baseline RAAS substrate availability and could modulate defence gain and

recovery kinetics under identical upstream inputs.

Finally, this framework predicts specific mechanistic roles for genes not currently prioritized in

SFARI curation, based on their function in membrane and volume homeostasis:

Sterol trafficking: NPC1, NPC2 (intracellular cholesterol mobilization), ABCA1 (cholesterol

ADRA2A (sympathetic termination).


7.4 Interpretive Conclusion

The interpretive conclusion is that genetic variation shapes where the dominant costs fall. In one

individual, the limiting factor may be vascular containment and microvascular stability under

dilution pressure. In another, it may be energetic recovery or microdomain reassembly after



osmotic stress. In another, it may be structural renal reserve that forces higher-gain endocrine

recruitment. These differences do not require distinct initiating causes. They are expected

consequences of deploying the same coupled defence logic through systems with different

constraints.


Section 8 — Divergent Defence Geometries

Figure placement note. Insert the Wet vs Dry Defence Geometry schematic here, positioned relative to the

tri-axial constraint defined in Section 5.


A coupled control system with limited reserve cannot minimise all costs simultaneously. When

extracellular tonicity is unstable and effective circulating volume is intermittently threatened, the

organism cannot preserve effective sodium, circulating volume, and vascular tone and

containment simultaneously, and microperfusion inherits the resulting constraint. Under repeated

developmental exposure, this constraint would be expected to bias early weighting toward distinct

defence geometries, stabilised during primary imprinting windows rather than emerging only

after downstream complications. In that sense, divergence is plausibly attractor-like: repeated

excursions through the same constrained state space may consolidate a preferred stabilisation

handle, with hysteresis such that return to a prior weighting is not symmetric once thresholds and

recovery kinetics have adapted.


8.1 Volume-Dominant (Vasopressin-Permissive) Defence

Within this manuscript’s definitions, two archetypal strategies can be stated formally. The first is

a vasopressin-permissive, volume-dominant defence. This geometry is volume-expanded relative

to the imposed constraint and is characterised by permissive antidiuretic recruitment to buffer

effective circulating volume threat when vascular tone or containment cannot provide sufficient

pressure support on their own. It therefore tends to tolerate dilution and a higher swelling burden

as a trade-off, with effective sodium allowed to fall through dilution when rapid water retention is

the dominant stabiliser. Its costs are paid in the tonicity and containment domains: greater reliance

on cellular volume management, osmolyte cycling, and microvascular containment under dilution

pressure, with developmental emphasis plausibly placed on recovery speed and buffering capacity

under repeated hypo-osmotic transients.


8.2 Tone-Dominant (Vasopressin-Sparing) Defence

The second is a vasopressin-sparing, salt-retentive, tone-dominant defence. This geometry is

characterised by reduced reliance on antidiuretic water retention and a stronger dependence on

sodium retention logic and vascular tone to sustain pressure and distribution without incurring

dilution. It therefore tends to tolerate a more constrained plasma volume range and higher

resistance-based support as a trade-off, with microperfusion risk carried through tone and

distribution rather than through dilution. Its costs are paid in the volume and tone domains: tighter

volume budgets, narrower haemodynamic margin, and greater sensitivity to any perturbation that

reduces venous return or redistributes intravascular water. Under this geometry, microperfusion

may become more variable at fine scales as distribution is maintained under tighter volumetric

constraint, such that oxygen- and ATP-sensitive biosynthetic steps may be intermittently

constrained by margin limits during stress and recovery. This is consistent with a plausible

infrastructure vulnerability: not because vasopressin directly regulates heparan sulfate or



cholesterol synthesis, but because a tone-dominant, AVP-sparing weighting could narrow

energetic reserve and increase state-to-state variability in substrate delivery during periods when

extracellular scaffolding and membrane construction are developmentally active. Under that

framing, indirect pressure on heparan sulfate–dependent extracellular scaffolding and on

cholesterol-dependent membrane, synapse, and myelin construction would be contingent and

timing-dependent, emerging through constraint-mediated perfusion and energy limitation rather

than hormone control.


8.3 Shared Constraint, Divergent Weightings

These strategies are sometimes described informally as “wet” and “dry”, as shorthand only for

which costs are tolerated, not as categories. The crucial point is that both arise from the same

coupled sodium–volume instability under a tri-axial constraint that includes vascular tone and

containment. They are not different causes. They are different solutions imposed by constraints.

Systems with limited swelling tolerance, limited membrane buffering, or slow osmolyte recovery

may consolidate toward tonicity-protective, tone-dominant geometries because repeated dilution

carries escalating local costs and can destabilise containment. Systems with limited perfusion

headroom, limited vascular containment, or structurally reduced renal reserve may consolidate

toward volume-dominant geometries because effective circulating volume threat is encountered

more often and must be buffered rapidly when tone or containment cannot reliably stabilise

microperfusion. In either case, the divergence is driven by the interaction between repeated

coupled stress and the system’s available structural and metabolic reserve, and the resulting

distribution of weights is better conceptualised as continuous, with basins of attraction and

path-dependence rather than a clean binary split.


Section 9 — Converging Osmotic and Volume Pathways in

Autism Contributing Factors

The literature on autism contributing factors often reads as physiologically fragmented.

Epidemiological links to pharmacologic exposures, environmental pollutants, placental

complications, connective-tissue laxity, and autonomic instability are typically siloed into distinct

research traditions. Consequently, the accumulating evidence can appear disjointed, presenting a

landscape of seemingly unrelated clinical and environmental coincidences rather than a cohesive

biological narrative.

This fragmentation is largely an artifact of categorization. Viewed through the lens of the tri-axial

constraint defined in Section 5, these disparate observations can be reinterpreted as distinct entry

routes into a single physiological bottleneck: instability in the coupled system that regulates

extracellular tonicity, effective circulating volume, and vascular containment. Whether an

initiating disturbance begins as a fluid and osmotic shift (diluting effective sodium) or a structural

vascular issue (threatening perfusion and containment), it ultimately forces the developing

organism to recruit the same shared sodium–volume defence architecture.

The following subsections therefore reorganize this heterogeneous literature into two broad

physiological families. The first comprises Osmotic Pathways, where environmental, metabolic,



or pharmacologic perturbations disrupt extracellular tonicity and shift water distribution in ways

that drive anti-hyponatraemic defence. The second comprises Volume-Instability Pathways,

where structural vascular compliance, altered autonomic tone, or endothelial inflammatory states

challenge perfusion directly, engaging the defence system even in the absence of primary dilution.

Cataloguing these pathways demonstrates that highly diverse clinical and environmental factors

are not competing theories of autism, but rather converging pressures on the same developmental

vulnerability.


Section 10 — Osmotic Pathway Catalogue

Some pathways categorized as volume-instability mechanisms may secondarily produce dilution

physiology when AVP is recruited to preserve perfusion. In these cases dilution reflects a

downstream defensive response rather than the initiating disturbance.

Pathways in this catalogue are classified according to the physiological disturbance that initiates

sodium–volume defence, not the downstream osmotic state that may ultimately emerge. A given

pathway may therefore culminate in dilution, sodium loss, or cortisol-dominant compensation

even when the initiating signal arose from a different regulatory axis. Classification follows the

dominant initiating mechanism, whether osmotic disturbance, sodium-balance failure, perfusion

constraint, cellular volume-control disruption, or renal transport redistribution.

A mechanistic classification of the endocrine–osmotic pathways shows how developing systems

are driven toward low-effective-sodium states in pregnancy and early life. In contrast to

traditional volume-based descriptions of hyponatraemia, this framework captures the distinct

physiological routes by which cells, tissues, and endocrine networks converge on sodium-threat

signalling under modern conditions.


Dilution-Driven Osmotic Pathways

These represent pathways in which extracellular tonicity is directly reduced or AVP signalling

produces dilution physiology.


True dilution (low-TDS / free-water states) [H]

Ultra-low-mineral and free-water states lower extracellular tonicity and suppress AVP while

perfusion support shifts toward cortisol in both compartments. Their distinguishing feature is a

low-AVP, quiet-RAAS, cortisol-dominant signature with low copeptin and true hypotonic

expansion.


Maternal AVP excess [S/E]

Maternal antidiuresis transfers a hypotonic load to the fetus, producing fetal effective dilution

without a fetal AVP rise. Its distinguishing feature is a discordant maternal–fetal pattern in which

placental vasopressinase prevents AVP transfer, leaving high maternal AVP with low fetal AVP

and low fetal copeptin.


Fetal AVP excess [S]

A fetal AVP-dominant state arises when maternal salt loading elevates fetal effective osmolality

and triggers fetal vasopressin release. It is distinguished by elevated cord copeptin during or after



the peak, transient RAAS suppression, and a brief post-surge dilution during time-lagged

equilibration.


Sodium-Balance Failure Pathways

These represent disturbances that weaken sodium retention or mineralocorticoid-mediated sodium

conservation.


Salt-wasting phenotypes [S]

Salt-wasting phenotypes are true sodium-loss states produced by renal, adrenal, gastrointestinal,

or energy-limited failure of sodium reclamation. Their distinguishing feature is parallel

AVP–RAAS–cortisol activation with copeptin increase as sodium loss outpaces water loss.


RAAS suppression → cortisol dominance [S]

Low-renin states suppress aldosterone-dependent sodium conservation and shift pressure support

toward cortisol. Their distinguishing feature is a low-RAAS, cortisol-dominant geometry, linked

here to FGF-23 increase, Klotho loss, and MR→GR bias, that lowers effective sodium pressure

without requiring overt hypotonicity.


Perfusion-Energetic Sodium Stress

These pathways impair sodium usability through reduced oxygen delivery and energetic

constraints on Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase function.


High-RAAS impact / angiotensin-dominant constriction [S]

High-RAAS impact arises when excessive angiotensin II effect constricts microvascular and

placental beds, limiting oxygen delivery needed for sodium handling. Its distinguishing feature is

the energetic paradox of intensified sodium-defence signalling alongside falling sodium usability

under hypoxic, receptor-fatigued conditions.


Hypoxia-induced sodium stress [S]

Hypoxia-induced sodium stress arises when oxygen limitation reduces ATP availability for

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase-dependent sodium transport. Its distinguishing feature is sodium-defence

activation under energetic failure, including RAAS-driven compensation with functional

hyponatraemic stress despite variable serum sodium.


Cellular Osmolyte-Control Failure

These pathways disrupt regulatory volume decrease and osmolyte recovery following cellular

swelling.


Cytoskeletal volume-control disruption (actin / osmolyte axis) [S]

Cytoskeletal volume-control disruption impairs regulatory volume decrease, VRAC function, and

osmolyte recovery after swelling. Its distinguishing feature is a pseudo-dilution phenotype in

which effective sodium appears low despite near-normal tonicity because cell-volume control and

transporter localization fail.



Renal Sodium-Redistribution Pathway

This pathway alters nephron segment sodium handling through phosphate-induced phosphaturia

and delayed distal compensation.


Dietary inorganic phosphate load [H]

Highly bioavailable inorganic phosphate redistributes sodium handling across nephron segments

by coupling phosphaturia to broader proximal natriuretic pressure and delayed distal

compensation. Its distinguishing feature is oscillatory sodium–volume strain driven by

phosphate-induced segmental sodium reallocation rather than uniform sodium loss.

Table 10.1 presents these pathways in parallel, summarizing—pathway by pathway—the

characteristic maternal and fetal patterns of AVP, RAAS, and cortisol, along with distinguishing

features such as discordant triad configurations, copeptin behaviour, and true-dilution versus

translocational signatures. Together, these pathways define a shared mechanistic vocabulary for

distinguishing endocrine–osmotic states across maternal and fetal compartments.
