11.5.4 Endocrine imprinting — biphasic vulnerability and RAAS rebound

Prolonged suppression of RAAS does not simply weaken the system; it recalibrates it

toward latent fragility.

Hypothesis. Within this framework, it is hypothesized that when renin remains

chronically low, downstream components adapt by increasing their sensitivity:

angiotensinogen conversion becomes more efficient, AT₁ receptors are up-regulated,

and intracellular amplification via PKC and MAPK pathways intensifies. The system

learns to function under renin scarcity by amplifying every remaining signal. While this

adaptive gain preserves perfusion during chronic suppression, it creates a dangerous

biphasic vulnerability. When renin is abruptly stimulated—such as during an infection or

poorly buffered immune activation—the previously quiet axis violently overshoots,

producing a massive Ang II surge that triggers intense vasoconstriction, tissue hypoxia,

and reactive oxygen species generation [cite].

11.5.5 Synthesis — the reassigned-defence pathway

RAAS suppression represents a pathway of reassigned defence rather than absent

defence. The mechanism can be summarized as:

Environmental RAAS suppression → reduced renin/aldosterone precision → alternative

tone support (FGF-23, cortisol) → preserved perfusion with high autonomic cost →

quiet-RAAS, stress-dominant physiology → receptor drift and latent biphasic fragility.

This reflects a redistribution of the regulatory burden away from canonical RAAS control

and toward substitute systems that preserve blood pressure while drastically increasing

developmental cost. This is the signature of the low-RAAS pathway: not failure to

defend, but defence reassigned to the wrong systems.


11.6 Mechanism — High-RAAS impact

11.6.1 Definition and relationship to low-RAAS baselines

In this manuscript, High-RAAS refers to states of excessive angiotensin II (Ang II) effect,

whether arising from sustained RAAS activation or from amplified downstream responses within



a high-gain, low-renin RAAS architecture. Two clarifications are essential. First, High-RAAS

does not require high baseline renin: the relevant variable is the magnitude of Ang II impact at the

effector level, including vascular tone, microvascular flow partitioning, and downstream

oxidative–inflammatory cascades, rather than the resting renin concentration itself. Second,

low-renin physiology can still generate high Ang II impact when downstream responsiveness is

amplified through heightened receptor sensitivity, increased receptor expression in key beds, or

strengthened post-receptor amplification, such that modest Ang II excursions produce outsized

physiological consequences.

Here, “high-gain RAAS architecture” refers to a configuration in which relatively small upstream

changes in renin or angiotensin signalling generate disproportionately large downstream vascular

or adrenal effects because of receptor sensitivity, expression, or post-receptor amplification.

“Low-RAAS baseline” states and “High-RAAS impact” states are not opposites. They are

dynamic modes: baseline tone describes the prevailing resting endocrine geometry, whereas

High-RAAS describes triggered or sustained effector overshoot. In practice, a quiet baseline may

coexist with, or predispose to, episodic Ang II excess when a high-gain, low-renin RAAS

architecture has been established. The clinically relevant pattern is often sequential or alternating:

prolonged suppression of baseline RAAS tone followed by an Ang II-dominant phase under

immune, inflammatory, or stress loading.


11.6.2 Trigger pathways: sustained activation and inflammatory amplification

in high-gain, low-renin RAAS architecture

High-RAAS impact can arise through two non-exclusive pathways.

Sustained RAAS activation describes conditions in which Ang II drive remains chronically

elevated or repeatedly renewed, maintaining arteriolar constriction and restricting capillary

perfusion over time. The mechanistic importance of this sustained mode in the Sodium-Struggle

framework is not simply hypertension or macroscopic vascular tone, but the creation of persistent

microvascular oxygen-delivery limitation that constrains ATP availability for sodium handling.

A second pathway is a transient inflammatory Ang II-dominant phase that becomes

disproportionately large in effect because it occurs on the background of a high-gain, low-renin

RAAS architecture. In this configuration, renin need not rise substantially; instead, inflammatory

or hemodynamic perturbations produce Ang II excursions that are functionally amplified by

sensitized effector pathways. A central example is cytokine-amplified Ang II dominance, in

which immune activation increases angiotensin signalling and vascular responsiveness at the

same time that endothelial buffering is compromised. In such states, the question is not whether

renin is “high,” but whether Ang II effect becomes dominant enough to reorganize perfusion and

oxygen delivery.

Across both modes, infection, inflammation, and stress act as RAAS amplifiers. Infection and

inflammation elevate cytokine tone and disrupt endothelial barrier and signalling balance, shifting

the vascular decision matrix toward constrictor dominance. Stress adds sympathetic and



glucocorticoid context that can potentiate vasoconstrictor coupling and reduce the system’s

capacity to return to sodium-specific regulation after the perturbation has passed. These

amplifiers are particularly consequential when a high-gain, low-renin RAAS architecture is

already present, because the same inflammatory input can produce a larger Ang II output at the

tissue level.


11.6.3 Microvascular consequences: arteriolar constriction, uteroplacental

resistance, and fetal oxygen delivery

The immediate physiological signature of High-RAAS impact is microvascular arteriolar

vasoconstriction. Ang II-dominant resistance elevation reduces conductance into downstream

capillary networks, increases perfusion heterogeneity, and narrows the margin for adequate

oxygen delivery in high-demand tissues. Within the Sodium-Struggle framework, this is not a

generic vasoconstriction story; it is a perfusion–energetics story: reduced microvascular oxygen

delivery becomes the upstream constraint that limits ATP-dependent sodium handling.

In pregnancy, the same logic extends to the uteroplacental circulation. High-RAAS impact can

manifest as placental-tree or uteroplacental constriction, in which Ang II-dominant resistance

rises within the maternal–placental vascular interface and or the placental vascular tree, reducing

the effective oxygen flux to the fetus. Normal pregnancy is often relatively buffered against Ang

II-mediated vasoconstriction, so the present pathway concerns loss of that buffering state under

inflammatory, endothelial, hypertensive, or otherwise pathological conditions.

This linkage can be strengthened by considering the placenta not only as a passive target of

maternal hormones but as a tissue capable of receptor-level adaptation. Recurrent endocrine or

osmotic disturbances may tune the placental bed toward heightened responsiveness, consistent

with the hypothesis of a receptor-imprinted placental vascular tree: repeated Ang II and

vasopressin exposure patterns could bias placental expression or sensitivity of AGTR1 (AT₁R)

and AVPR1A (V1a), locking the placental vascular tree into a high-resistance mode that persists

even when maternal circulating signals later normalize. This is proposed here as a hypothesis

rather than an established general mechanism, but it provides a plausible route by which episodic

High-RAAS impact could be converted into sustained placental resistance in a subset of

pregnancies.

A direct consequence of uteroplacental resistance is reduced fetal oxygen delivery. Even modest

reductions in placental conductance can be amplified at the fetal level because fetal tissues

operate closer to oxygen-delivery limits during rapid growth. Reduced oxygen delivery then

becomes the initiating constraint for the energetic bottleneck described below.

It is also important to distinguish this pathway from other causes of placental underperfusion.

Placental malperfusion is not synonymous with RAAS-mediated constriction. Structural,

thrombotic, implantation-related, or primary maternal vascular mechanisms can produce similar

end-organ patterns of growth restriction or hypoxia without Ang II being the primary driver. The

RAAS-mediated constriction route to placental hypoxia is therefore one specific pathway rather

than an exhaustive taxonomy of placental pathologies.



11.6.4 The energetic bottleneck: oxygen constraint and low effective sodium

High-RAAS impact becomes mechanistically central to Sodium-Struggle physiology when

microvascular constriction produces hypoxia sufficient to limit ATP availability. Sodium handling

is an energetic process: Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase activity is oxygen dependent because it is fuelled by

oxidative ATP production and is among the dominant cellular energy expenditures in excitable

tissue and in renal tubular transport.

Under reduced oxygen delivery, ATP limitation under hypoxia constrains pump activity. When

ATP supply cannot support adequate Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase flux, usable sodium gradients become harder

to maintain and sodium-dependent transport becomes less controllable. In this setting, a crucial

failure mode emerges: collapse of usable sodium gradients and reduced sodium-coupled work

capacity. Sodium may still be present in extracellular compartments, and serum concentration

may remain preserved, yet the cell can no longer reliably convert extracellular sodium availability

into stable transmembrane gradient function because the energy-dependent machinery that

maintains those gradients is constrained.

This distinction motivates the manuscript’s emphasis on “low effective sodium” versus serum

sodium. In this manuscript, “low effective sodium” refers to a state in which extracellular sodium

is less functionally available to support stable osmotic containment, transmembrane gradient

reliability, and sodium-dependent cellular work, even when serum sodium remains within the

conventional range. In this state, the organism recruits sodium–volume defence mechanisms,

including AVP, RAAS, cortisol, and vascular tone, to counter movement toward hypo-osmotic or

dilution-prone stress.

Within the broader Sodium-Struggle framework, the relevant threat is therefore not serum sodium

alone but movement toward a state in which cells face impaired osmotic containment, unstable

chloride-weighted gradients, or dilution-prone stress, thereby recruiting sodium–volume defence

as a survival response. High-RAAS impact can thus generate a sodium-deficit phenotype at the

level that matters for development and neural function, even without classical hyponatraemia.


11.6.5 The energetic paradox: louder sodium defence, weaker sodium

execution

Once the energetic bottleneck is engaged, a characteristic paradox appears. High-RAAS impact

tends to drive sodium-defence signalling upward, with increased Ang II tone and often

heightened vasopressin and sympathetic participation, precisely because the organism interprets

the downstream consequences, including effective volume strain, transport failure, and cellular

swelling risk, as a threat requiring sodium conservation. Yet the same constrictive physiology that

elevates those signals can further restrict oxygen delivery, pushing ATP availability down and

making sodium transport less reliable.

Continued recruitment of RAAS and AVP in this state is proposed to arise from non-osmotic

threat signals, including reduced perfusion pressure, baroreflex and sympathetic activation,



endothelial injury, inflammatory cytokines, and other survival-axis inputs, even when serum

sodium remains near-normal.

The result is that sodium usability decreases as endocrine defence signals intensify. In this

paradoxical state, RAAS becomes loud but ineffective: Ang II-dominant constriction and

signalling amplify the defensive posture, but cannot restore the energetic conditions required for

reliable sodium-gradient function. Instead, escalating vasoconstriction may worsen the upstream

oxygen constraint, deepening the energetic shortfall that precipitated gradient collapse in the first

place.

This is one of the core claims of the present section: High-RAAS impact is not merely a

blood-pressure phenotype, but a perfusion–energetics phenotype in which defence signalling may

intensify precisely as sodium-gradient reliability deteriorates.


11.6.6 Receptor-level adaptations: desensitisation, signalling fatigue, and loss

of sodium-specific control

Sustained or repeated High-RAAS impact also reshapes control systems at the receptor level.

High ligand exposure and persistent activation pressures promote AT₁R desensitisation, and

similar principles apply to vasopressin receptors involved in the sodium-defence program,

including V1a and V2 desensitisation. These changes reduce the fidelity of hormonal control: the

organism continues to produce strong endocrine signals, but tissues respond less predictably.

Beyond receptor-level changes, chronic activation can produce downstream signalling fatigue, in

which intracellular transduction pathways become refractory, depleted, or biased toward

stress-survival outputs rather than precise homeostatic regulation. The functional consequence is

a progressive loss of sodium-specific control. Instead of mineralocorticoid-like precision in

sodium handling, the system shifts toward blunt maintenance of perfusion and pressure, trading

selectivity for survival under constrained energetics.

In the Sodium-Struggle framework, this loss of precision is a key bridge to imprinting: once

sodium-specific control is degraded, subsequent regulation is more likely to be delegated to

generalized stress axes, increasing the probability that endocrine and autonomic set-points drift

toward stress dominance.


11.6.7 Nitric oxide in High-RAAS: secondary amplification via Ang II-linked

oxidative stress

Nitric oxide (NO) reduction can worsen High-RAAS states, but its causal role must be framed

correctly. Here, NO reduction is a secondary amplifier downstream of Ang II-linked oxidative

stress and endothelial dysfunction, not an initiating mechanism. Excess Ang II promotes

oxidative stress, which in turn scavenges NO and reduces NO bioavailability, further shifting the

vascular balance toward constriction and impairing microvascular perfusion.



By contrast, in low-RAAS states of stiffness and tone maintenance linked to the FGF-23/Klotho

axis, NO reduction functions as a primary driver. In the present pathway, NO participates by

amplifying an Ang II-driven constrictive cascade; it does not replace Ang II as the initiating

effector.

A plausible additional amplifier is persistent nitric oxide deficiency, because reduced NO favours

vasoconstrictive tone and may prolong non-osmotic perfusion-threat signalling capable of

sustaining AVP recruitment. In low-NO states, where vasodilatory buffering is reduced, perfusion

maintenance may rely more heavily on endocrine compensation, including Ang II, AVP, and

cortisol. Under these conditions, persistent or repeated AVP recruitment becomes physiologically

plausible as part of a non-osmotic perfusion-defence strategy.


11.6.8 Cortisol takeover: MR→GR bias and stress-dominant endocrine

geometry (hypothesis-level synthesis)

What follows is a systems-level inference. As sodium-specific control becomes less reliable,

endocrine support may shift toward broader glucocorticoid-dominant maintenance of perfusion

and stress tolerance.

As High-RAAS impact persists, and especially as receptor desensitisation and signalling fatigue

accumulate, cortisol may progressively replace RAAS as the dominant support for perfusion. This

takeover does not imply that Ang II disappears; rather, it reflects a shift in which the organism

relies increasingly on generalized glucocorticoid-driven tone and stress programs to maintain

blood pressure and perfusion when sodium-specific control has become unreliable.

A central molecular correlate is MR→GR bias, in which mineralocorticoid-selective regulation is

displaced by glucocorticoid-dominant control. Under this bias, the endocrine system becomes less

capable of fine-grained sodium handling and more oriented toward maintaining perfusion under

threat. The resulting configuration is a stress-dominant endocrine geometry: Ang II signalling

remains prominent yet increasingly ineffective at restoring sodium usability, while cortisol

becomes the stabilizing axis that sustains systemic function through a broader, less specific

control strategy.


11.6.9 Developmental implications: placental hypoxia timing,

neurodevelopmental sodium handling, and persistent imprinting

The relevance of High-RAAS impact to autism-risk mechanisms in this hypothesis is mediated by

timing and energetics rather than by any single endocrine marker. Placental hypoxia timing

matters because fetal brain development proceeds through stages with distinct energetic

requirements and vulnerabilities. When High-RAAS-linked uteroplacental constriction reduces

fetal oxygen delivery during sensitive windows, it increases the likelihood that the fetal nervous

system experiences periods in which ATP supply is inadequate for stable ionic regulation.

Under such conditions, energy-limited sodium handling during neurodevelopment becomes a

plausible proximate mechanism: reduced pump capacity and collapse of usable sodium gradients



occur in precisely the tissues where sodium gradients are essential for excitability, synaptic

development, and volume regulation. The endocrine response to perceived sodium threat may

intensify, with Ang II and vasopressin signalling rising as defensive outputs, while the fetal

cellular substrate remains unable to convert those signals into restored sodium usability.

A second consequence is endocrine-autonomic imprinting. Recurrent episodes of High-RAAS

impact, especially when paired with hypoxia and stress-axis engagement, can bias receptor

expression and set-point tuning across endocrine and autonomic systems. In this manuscript’s

terms, the organism learns a defensive configuration in which generalized stress regulation

displaces sodium-specific precision. Such imprinting is consistent with receptor-level tuning in

vascular beds, including the placental tree in some cases, and with persistent alterations in the

balance among RAAS, vasopressin signalling, and cortisol dominance.

Crucially, these changes can persist even after normalization of conventional measures.

Persistence after sodium normalization is therefore expected: serum Na⁺ may return to normal, yet

the control architecture, including receptor responsiveness, endocrine dominance patterns, and

autonomic tone, may remain biased toward the anti-hyponatraemia program established during

earlier constraint. In this framing, High-RAAS impact contributes to autism-relevant vulnerability

not by requiring chronic electrolyte abnormalities, but by promoting an early-life pattern of

hypoxia-linked sodium unusability and stress-dominant imprinting.


11.6.10 Boundary conditions and deferrals

This discussion is deliberately mechanistic. It does not attempt to adjudicate the full differential

diagnosis of placental malperfusion, nor does it propose that all fetal hypoxia arises from Ang

II-mediated constriction. It also does not provide intervention guidance or clinical management

recommendations.

Population-level comparisons, demographic prevalence patterns, and ancestry-linked distributions

of RAAS baselines or high-gain, low-renin RAAS architecture frequency are not addressed here.


11.6.11 Low-renin, high-gain RAAS phenotypes as a source of developmental

vulnerability [H/S]

Some individuals exhibit a low-baseline-renin phenotype characterised by modest circulating

renin activity but heightened downstream responsiveness to angiotensin II and aldosterone. In

these systems, the dose–response curve linking renin to angiotensin II is steeper and the effective

threshold is lower, such that relatively small changes in renin signalling can produce

disproportionately large vascular and adrenal effects. This configuration reflects a high-efficiency,

high-gain RAAS architecture rather than a deficiency of RAAS function.

This physiology is supported by classic and modern studies. Angiotensin II infusion experiments

demonstrated that individuals with low baseline renin show exaggerated aldosterone secretion and

pressor responses per unit of angiotensin II, confirming a steeper angiotensin-to-aldosterone

response curve. Subsequent work has reinforced that low-renin states can reflect enhanced



adrenal and vascular responsiveness to angiotensin II rather than reduced peptide availability.

Clinical observations that ACE inhibition is less effective in some low-renin states are also

consistent with altered signal amplification at the receptor or adrenal-response level rather than a

simple hormonal shortfall. Such differences in gain and amplification may have developmental

corollaries, because a system optimized for sodium conservation and pressure support in adults

may behave differently under the constraints of placental perfusion and fetal oxygen dependence.

When chronic RAAS suppression occurs, for example through phosphate excess,

FGF-23-mediated renin inhibition, low-mineral intake, or vasodilatory exposures, the endocrine

network adapts to function under conditions of renin scarcity. The result can be a high-gain,

low-renin system that maintains perfusion with minimal renin input but becomes unstable when

renin is abruptly stimulated. During immune activation and cytokine-mediated stress, this

sensitivity can magnify the renin signal. Even a modest stimulus may generate a disproportionate

Ang II-dominant phase, producing intense vasoconstriction and placental vascular tree narrowing.

Angiotensin-driven constriction of the uteroplacental vascular tree has important developmental

implications. Increased tone within placental resistance vessels can reduce oxygen delivery to the

fetus, creating intermittent or regional placental hypoxia. Because the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase is among

the most oxygen-dependent enzymes in human physiology, hypoxia impairs active sodium

transport. This can weaken sodium-dependent transport reliability and promote

low-effective-sodium outcomes despite high angiotensin activity. In this way, High-RAAS-impact

states can paradoxically yield sodium-defence recruitment under conditions where oxygen

availability is constrained. The resulting dual stress, Ang II-mediated hypoxia combined with

impaired sodium handling, establishes an endocrine geometry of low renin input with high RAAS

impact, increasing reliance on cortisol for volume and electrolyte regulation and promoting

osmotic fragility.

Animal lineage-tracing studies demonstrate plasticity between zona glomerulosa and zona

fasciculata cell populations. The present manuscript extends this to a hypothesis of more

persistent cortisol-biased remodeling under repeated developmental stress. Under this hypothesis,

repeated cortisol-dominant compensation may also remodel the adrenal cortex itself. Cells of the

zona glomerulosa, normally specialised for aldosterone synthesis via CYP11B2, may drift toward

a glucocorticoid-producing, cortisol-biased phenotype expressing CYP11B1. This conversion

may be semi-permanent or sticky; once the mineralocorticoid programme is down-regulated,

reversion may be incomplete or delayed. The gland then becomes biased toward cortisol output,

reinforcing the chronic stress geometry that defines the Sodium-Switch state.

Taken together, these observations support a conservative interpretation: variation in RAAS gain

and renin efficiency can shape how identical endocrine or perfusion perturbations are processed

during sensitive developmental windows. Low-renin, high-gain systems do not determine

developmental outcomes, but they may amplify the impact of otherwise modest stressors and

increase the likelihood that a transient episode produces a lasting shift toward cortisol-dominant

compensation with osmotic fragility. This framing emphasizes endocrine architecture, not identity

or exposure category, as one axis of biological vulnerability within a broader, heterogeneous

landscape of developmental risk.



11.6.12 Immune-mediated cytokine activation as a compound

osmotic–endocrine stressor [H/S]

Systemic immune activation represents a potent but non-specific physiological stressor capable of

transiently amplifying renin–angiotensin–aldosterone signalling. During acute inflammatory

responses, pro-inflammatory cytokines such as IL-6, IL-1β, and TNF-α can up-regulate

angiotensinogen expression, enhance angiotensin-converting enzyme activity, and stimulate renin

release through direct, haemodynamic, and endothelial pathways. The resulting Ang II-dominant

phase can produce a parallel rise in aldosterone secretion, increasing vascular tone and

sodium-retentive drive.

The following sequence is proposed here as a model-level extension of known

receptor-adaptation principles rather than an already established unified syndrome. In high-gain

RAAS architectures, cytokine-driven activation may exceed the physiological operating range of

mineralocorticoid signalling. Aldosterone exposure can effectively flood mineralocorticoid

receptors and downstream sodium-handling pathways, saturating the system and reducing

regulatory flexibility during the acute phase. As inflammatory signalling resolves, negative

feedback suppresses further renin release and attenuates angiotensin II generation.

Mineralocorticoid receptor responsiveness and downstream transcriptional programmes may then

become relatively blunted, not because aldosterone is absent, but because receptor-level

sensitivity and downstream programmes have been driven beyond their prior operating range.

This sequence produces a characteristic endocrine mismatch: mineralocorticoid action becomes

insufficient relative to ongoing perfusion and volume demands despite recent RAAS

hyperactivation. Under these conditions, cortisol increasingly substitutes for mineralocorticoid

function via glucocorticoid receptor activation and partial mineralocorticoid receptor occupancy,

particularly when 11β-HSD2 capacity is strained. Sodium conservation thus shifts from

aldosterone-mediated precision to cortisol-mediated compensation, which is less stable and more

context-dependent.

The degree to which AVP is recruited in this state may vary across individuals, reflecting

differing physiological strategies for maintaining perfusion and volume. Some systems appear

more volume-defence-dominant and AVP-responsive, whereas others rely more heavily on

sodium-retentive mechanisms. In some contexts, AVP tone may remain elevated beyond the acute

phase, particularly where non-osmotic perfusion-threat signalling, endothelial constriction, or

reduced nitric oxide buffering persist. When AVP tone remains elevated as aldosterone

responsiveness wanes, water retention persists while effective sodium reabsorption falters. This

promotes dilutional or functional hyponatraemia and reinforces the discordant endocrine triad of

high AVP, low effective mineralocorticoid action, and elevated cortisol that characterises the

Sodium-Switch state.

When immune-mediated endocrine oscillations occur during sensitive developmental windows,

including gestation, early infancy, or periods of rapid neurovascular remodelling, they may

contribute to durable recalibration of receptor expression, adrenal zonation, and vascular

responsiveness. These effects do not require severe illness; rather, they reflect how repeated or



poorly buffered inflammatory episodes interact with pre-existing endocrine architecture. In this

framework, immune activation functions as an endocrine stress test, revealing and amplifying

latent vulnerability within sodium–volume defence networks.


11.6.13 Sex differences, phenotypic modifiers, and developmental context

Sexual dimorphism in endocrine regulation may influence how immune-mediated cytokine

amplification is expressed. Male-typical physiology, often characterised by stronger classical

RAAS tone under some conditions, may produce a more forceful initial Ang II rise during acute

illness or systemic infection, followed by a sharper rebound into RAAS fatigue as inhibitory

feedback intensifies. This pattern may yield more pronounced early sodium retention followed by

a steeper post-inflammatory shift toward cortisol-mediated compensation. This remains a

phenotype-level inference rather than a uniform sex rule.

Phenotypic modifiers are also likely to matter. Individuals with low-renin phenotypes begin from

a baseline in which renin secretion is modest but renin efficiency and Ang II responsiveness are

high. During an acute cytokine surge associated with illness or infection, even a small rise in

renin or ACE activity can provoke a disproportionate angiotensin II and aldosterone response,

amplifying vascular tone and inflammatory signalling. As cytokine levels decline, renin release is

further down-regulated by feedback inhibition, yet residual RAAS signalling may remain highly

efficacious because Ang II receptors and ACE activity remain sensitised.

Only with sustained or repeated post-inflammatory exposure does receptor fatigue and

transcriptional down-regulation emerge, reducing RAAS responsiveness and shifting sodium

regulation toward cortisol dependence. Cortisol then assumes a greater role in maintaining

sodium balance and vascular tone, increasing susceptibility to dilutional hyponatraemia or

interstitial sodium trapping. Thus, phenotypes that conserve sodium efficiently at baseline may

experience exaggerated RAAS activation followed by more pronounced RAAS exhaustion during

immune-mediated stress.

When immune activation associated with illness or infection occurs during pregnancy or early

infancy, cytokine-mediated RAAS perturbations may superimpose on the developing endocrine

landscape. Maternal cytokine surges can transiently alter placental perfusion and fetal sodium

handling, while cortisol-dominant compensation during inflammatory stress may shape

receptor-expression profiles involved in long-term osmotic and vascular regulation. Because fetal

sodium transport and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase activity are highly oxygen-dependent, even modest,

repeated immune-associated hypoperfusion may have disproportionate developmental

consequences.

In early postnatal life, recurrent illness-associated inflammatory episodes may similarly engage

aldosterone overshoot followed by post-inflammatory mineralocorticoid fatigue during periods of

rapid neurovascular and autonomic calibration. These interactions do not require severe illness;

rather, they reflect how common immune challenges interact with pre-existing endocrine

architecture during sensitive developmental windows.



11.6.14 Summary

Taken together, illness-associated immune activation provides a conservative, exposure-agnostic

model of sequential endocrine transition: cytokine-driven RAAS amplification, transient Ang II

dominance, relative mineralocorticoid fatigue, variable AVP recruitment, and cortisol-mediated

sodium compensation. By engaging multiple sodium–volume defence mechanisms in rapid

succession, inflammatory episodes can reproduce the endocrine trajectory that culminates in the

Sodium-Switch state, particularly in high-gain RAAS systems and during sensitive developmental

windows.


11.7 Mechanism — Hypoxia-Induced Sodium Stress

Energetic Constraint, Pump Failure, and Bidirectional Vulnerability

11.7.1 Initiating condition — energetic constraint on sodium handling In this pathway, the

initiating disturbance is reduced oxygen delivery, which imposes an absolute energetic constraint

on sodium regulation. This completes the six-part taxonomy of Sodium-Struggle physiology. In

contrast to the preceding pathways, in which sodium instability arises from dilution,

mis-signalling, mistiming, depletion, or reassigned defence, this pathway reflects a distinct failure

mode: sodium–volume defence is intact in intent, but constrained in execution. Sodium may be

physically present, and endocrine signalling may be appropriately engaged, yet the metabolic

energy required to perform sodium-dependent work is insufficient.

11.7.2 The core mechanism — oxygen dependence of the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase Sodium handling is

fundamentally an energy-dependent process. The Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase, which maintains

transmembrane sodium gradients, is among the most oxygen-demanding processes in human

physiology, accounting for a major share of basal metabolic energy expenditure. Renal sodium

reabsorption is particularly oxygen-intensive; the kidney alone consumes approximately 7% of

total body oxygen at rest, primarily to support sodium reuptake in the proximal tubules and thick

ascending limbs [cite]. When oxygen delivery falls, ATP production declines, and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase

activity becomes mechanically constrained. The immediate consequence is not the absence of

sodium, but the loss of reliable sodium transport and gradient stability. Sodium remains present,

but becomes functionally unreliable. This produces impaired sodium reabsorption, forced

natriuresis, and progressive hypo-osmotic stress, establishing a state in which sodium is available

but cannot be effectively used.

11.7.3 Bidirectional vulnerability — the hypoxia–hyponatraemia loop The defining feature of

this pathway is its bidirectional coupling: hypoxia can both initiate sodium instability and arise as

a devastating consequence of it. In the forward direction, reduced oxygen delivery impairs ATP

production and sodium transport, forcing the system into osmotic instability. In the reverse

direction, sodium instability feeds back to worsen perfusion and oxygen delivery. As plasma

volume falls and osmotic gradients destabilize, tissue perfusion declines. In more severe states,

cellular swelling compresses microvascular beds, physically impairing oxygen diffusion, while

intracellular ionic disruption alters mitochondrial energetics [cite]. This creates a self-reinforcing



loop in which hypoxia and sodium instability amplify one another. Once engaged, the system is

driven toward progressive perfusion–energetic failure.

11.7.4 Endocrine consequence — cortisol compensation and masked failure Under hypoxic

constraint, sodium–volume defence shifts toward a compensatory mode. Because sodium

handling becomes energetically inefficient, canonical RAAS signalling may be suppressed or

functionally ineffective, and cortisol assumes an increasingly dominant role in maintaining

perfusion. In low-renin states (§11.5), this energetic failure may be partially masked.

FGF-23–mediated vascular stiffness and distal sodium retention can maintain perfusion pressure

despite underlying pump failure, obscuring early sodium-handling deficits [cite]. The system

therefore appears stable while the energetic deficit deepens. This masking delays detection and

forces an increasing reliance on AVP, cortisol, and high-tone vascular support to sustain basic

physiological function.

11.7.5 Developmental implication — energetic bottlenecks during critical windows The

developmental significance of this pathway lies in timing and vulnerability. During fetal and early

postnatal development, sodium gradients are essential for neuronal signalling, energy demand is

exceptionally high, and oxygen delivery margins are narrow. Under these conditions, even modest

hypoxic episodes may disproportionately impair sodium-dependent processes. This produces

unstable ionic gradients, altered excitability, and an increased dependence on compensatory

endocrine signalling precisely during periods when the regulatory architecture is being

established.

11.7.6 Endocrine imprinting — the energy-constrained baseline Hypothesis. Within the

Sodium-Struggle framework, it is hypothesized that repeated exposure to hypoxia-induced

sodium stress may imprint a regulatory system adapted to energy-constrained defence. When the

organism is repeatedly forced to operate under conditions in which sodium regulation cannot be

reliably executed, regulatory priorities shift. Cortisol-dominant compensation becomes

entrenched, sodium–volume thresholds shift, and defensive signalling is prioritised over

precision. Even after oxygen delivery and serum sodium normalize, the system may retain a bias

toward survival under energetic constraint, reflecting imprinting at the level of metabolic

feasibility rather than signalling alone.

11.7.7 Synthesis — the energetic constraint pathway Hypoxia-induced sodium stress

represents a pathway of energetic limitation rather than signalling failure. The mechanism can be

summarized as:

Reduced oxygen delivery → ATP limitation → Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase constraint → loss of

sodium-handling reliability → osmotic stress → compensatory endocrine activation →

bidirectional hypoxia–hyponatraemia amplification → persistent regulatory recalibration.

In contrast to earlier pathways, this state reflects neither incorrect signalling, mistimed signalling,

depletion, nor reassigned defence. Instead, it reflects a fundamental constraint: the system is

attempting to defend sodium, but lacks the metabolic currency to do so.



11.8 Mechanism — Cytoskeletal Volume-Control Disruption

Mechanical Failure of Cellular Recovery and Amplification of Osmotic Stress

11.8.1 Initiating condition — mechanical failure of cellular recovery In this pathway, the

initiating disturbance is not primarily osmotic, endocrine, or energetic. It is mechanical. This

distinguishes it from all preceding pathways. Here, the failure lies in the structural machinery

required to restore cellular volume after an osmotic perturbation. The cell loses its mechanical

ability to recover, transforming ordinary physiological fluctuations into disproportionately

prolonged stress events.

11.8.2 The core mechanism — impaired RVD and the osmolyte debt Normal cellular volume

regulation depends on rapid cytoskeletal dynamics. When a cell swells in response to reduced

extracellular tonicity, survival requires a rapid response known as regulatory volume decrease

(RVD). This process depends on actin filaments rapidly reorganizing to permit activation of

volume-regulated anion channels (VRAC), allowing controlled osmolyte efflux and the

restoration of cellular volume [cite]. When environmental toxicants impair actin turnover,

cytoskeletal reorganization is delayed and RVD becomes inefficient. Cells remain swollen longer,

VRAC channels remain open longer, and compatible osmolytes such as taurine, myo-inositol, and

betaine continue to exit the cell beyond the point of adaptive correction. The result is a prolonged

and exaggerated depletion of intracellular osmolytes—an "osmolyte debt"—which represents the

core mechanical lesion of this pathway.

11.8.3 Physiological consequence — amplification of systemic signalling This osmolyte debt

has consequences that extend far beyond local swelling. Compatible osmolytes, particularly

taurine, are essential for astrocytic potassium and glutamate buffering. Their depletion weakens

local buffering capacity and lowers the threshold for excitability disturbances [cite]. At this point,

cells are no longer simply responding to osmotic stress; they have lost the physical capacity to

recover from it. Within the Sodium-Struggle framework, this transforms cytoskeletal disruption

into a massive mechanical amplifier of systemic instability. Because cellular recovery is delayed,

each disturbance persists longer. Because it persists longer, the endocrine system interprets a

larger unresolved threat and scales up its response. The signal is not different in kind, but

amplified in duration and intensity. The disturbance is not necessarily greater—the system is

simply slower to resolve it, and therefore behaves as if it is.

11.8.4 Environmental drivers — actin disruption as a convergent mechanism A wide range

of environmental exposures converge on this exact cytoskeletal vulnerability. Mycotoxins, heavy

metals, and organophosphate metabolites profoundly impair actin dynamics through oxidative

and Rho-GTPase–mediated pathways [cite]. Despite their chemical diversity, these agents share a

common functional outcome: impaired actin turnover, delayed RVD, and prolonged cellular

swelling stress. Within this framework, they are best understood not merely as generic toxicants,

but as targeted mechanical saboteurs of volume recovery.



Chemical toxicants remain the clearest established examples in this subsection, but they are not

the only plausible route into this lesion. Some physical-energy exposures may also intersect with

actin-dependent recovery, and ultrasound is the strongest proof of principle here because it is

itself a mechanical exposure: animal and cellular studies have reported that weak ultrasonic

stimulation can perturb cytoskeletal organisation and actin-linked recovery dynamics, while some

ultrasound studies also report chloride-related physiology, including altered chloride flux and

GABA-related inhibitory signalling, reinforcing the narrower point that mechanical disturbance

can propagate beyond structure into ionic and inhibitory aspects of recovery [cite]. This remains a

bounded mechanistic point, not a claim that routine diagnostic ultrasound is an established

developmental hazard. Ionising imaging belongs to a separate and more medically bounded class,

but it can also alter actin architecture and recovery mechanics, so it should not be omitted entirely

from the broader map of actin stressors [cite]. At the far edge, THz-range and related future

high-frequency platforms remain an emerging watch-list question rather than an established

developmental hazard, though early actin-level findings suggest the need for targeted mechanistic

scrutiny [cite].

11.8.5 Secondary amplification — nanoplastics, barrier disruption, and endocrine

interference Micro- and nanoplastics introduce a second layer of disruption by linking

mechanical injury to barrier dysfunction and endocrine interference. Nanoscale fragments can

penetrate placental and blood–brain barriers, disturbing endothelial integrity and destabilizing

ionic gradients across compartments [cite]. These particles also carry sorbed plasticizers, such as

phthalates and bisphenols, which may directly perturb mineralocorticoid and glucocorticoid

receptor signalling. This creates a compound exposure profile in which barrier integrity is

weakened, ionic gradients are destabilized, endocrine regulation is disrupted, and inflammatory

markers may rise. This positions nanoplastic exposure as a key physical bridge between localized

mechanical vulnerability and systemic endocrine dysregulation.

11.8.6 Endocrine imprinting — structural imprinting of recovery dynamics Hypothesis.

Within the Sodium-Struggle framework, it is hypothesized that repeated exposure to cytoskeletal

disruption during development may imprint a regulatory architecture adapted to delayed recovery.

When cellular recovery is consistently impaired, the overlying endocrine system compensates by

adopting a hyper-defensive posture. Osmotic thresholds shift, baseline regulation becomes more

oscillatory, and cortisol-dominant compensation becomes permanently reinforced. In this model,

the system learns not only to defend, but to defend under conditions of delayed resolution.

11.8.7 Synthesis — the mechanical amplification pathway Cytoskeletal volume-control

disruption represents a pathway of mechanical amplification rather than primary osmotic or

endocrine failure. The mechanism, whether initiated by toxicant-driven or physically induced

actin disruption, can be summarized as:

Actin disruption → delayed RVD → prolonged osmolyte efflux → osmolyte debt → impaired

cellular recovery → amplified endocrine response → persistent instability.

This pathway reveals a distinct failure mode: the disturbance is not inherently greater; the system

is simply slower to recover, and therefore behaves as if it is.



11.9 Mechanism — Dietary inorganic phosphate load

Modern phosphate exposure is not limited to the phosphorus naturally embedded in whole foods.

In contemporary diets, inorganic phosphate salts are widely used in processed foods, including

packaged meats, prepared frozen foods, dry mixes, breads and baked goods, soups, yogurt and

other dairy products, and soft drinks. Dietary surveys consistently identify cola beverages and

processed meats as prominent sources of additive phosphate exposure. In infancy, formula

phosphorus is also relevant because phosphorus contributes directly to the potential renal solute

load of infant feedings, and public water systems commonly use orthophosphate for corrosion

control in distribution pipes. An important confound should be acknowledged at the outset:

phosphate-rich processed diets often raise phosphorus and sodium together because many

phosphate additives are sodium phosphate salts and additive-rich food patterns commonly

co-deliver both minerals. In a controlled menu analysis, additive-enhanced diets provided roughly

60% more phosphorus and sodium than lower-additive menus. This confound does not eliminate

phosphate-specific physiology, however, because sodium-adjusted acute loading studies and

sodium-controlled feeding studies still demonstrate phosphate-related effects on postprandial

phosphate handling, parathyroid hormone (PTH), blood pressure, and sympathoadrenergic

markers beyond simple sodium delivery.

This initiator is relevant because highly bioavailable inorganic phosphate exposure can

redistribute renal sodium handling across nephron segments and alter endocrine signalling in

ways that increase regulatory strain on sodium–volume homeostasis and, under dilution-favouring

conditions, could bias physiology toward lower effective sodium pressure, thereby increasing the

likelihood that anti-hyponatraemia and sodium–volume defence mechanisms are recruited.

The intestinal entry point strengthens this pathway. Added inorganic phosphates are absorbed

rapidly and efficiently compared with much organically bound phosphorus present in intact foods.

The active transcellular component of intestinal phosphate absorption is largely mediated by the

sodium-dependent transporter NaPi-IIb (SLC34A2). At the same time, intestinal phosphate

absorption is not exclusively transcellular, because a substantial paracellular pathway also

contributes, especially when luminal phosphate concentrations are high. Active vitamin D

increases NaPi-IIb expression and enhances intestinal phosphate transport, making vitamin D

status a plausible modifier of absorbed phosphate load. Within this framework vitamin D

supplementation is not being treated as harmful in itself; the narrower point is that, where vitamin

D signalling increases intestinal phosphate uptake, it could theoretically enlarge postprandial

phosphate excursions and the downstream renal regulatory response under some conditions.

Once filtered, phosphate is reabsorbed mainly in the proximal tubule through the

sodium-dependent cotransporters NaPi-IIa (SLC34A1), with NaPi-IIc (SLC34A3) playing a

smaller adaptive role. PTH and fibroblast growth factor 23 (FGF23) reduce the apical abundance

of these transporters and thereby promote phosphate-driven phosphaturia. This makes a sodium

effect physiologically plausible because phosphate transport in the proximal tubule is

sodium-coupled. At the same time, scientific caution is necessary. NaPi-mediated transport

represents only a minority share of proximal sodium reclamation, whereas the Na⁺/H⁺ exchanger

NHE3 carries a much larger proportion of proximal sodium transport. For this reason NaPi



stoichiometry alone should not be interpreted as evidence that phosphate loading produces large

sodium losses in otherwise healthy kidneys.

The stronger argument for proximal natriuretic pressure therefore extends beyond NaPi

stoichiometry alone. In vivo, PTH-induced phosphaturia is accompanied by internalization of

apical NHE3 and NaPi transporters together with inhibition of basolateral Na⁺-K⁺-ATPase

activity, producing natriuresis and diuresis alongside phosphate excretion. On this broader

reading, phosphate-driven phosphaturia can transiently depress several proximal

sodium-reclaiming processes at once. The likely consequence is not fixed salt wasting but

segment-specific sodium reallocation, in which proximal natriuretic pressure shifts sodium

delivery downstream while later endocrine responses recruit distal sodium conservation.

Experimental studies also suggest the existence of a rapid intestinal–renal phosphaturic axis in

which intestinal phosphate exposure can increase renal phosphate excretion even before

measurable changes in PTH or FGF23 occur. This rapid feed-forward phase further supports the

view that the earliest renal response to phosphate load is not reducible to slower endocrine

signalling alone.

In simplified terms, the renal sequence can be conceptualized as:

phosphate load → phosphaturia and proximal natriuretic pressure → increased distal sodium

delivery → delayed endocrine compensation → distal sodium retention.

This early phase is typically mediated by rapid transporter internalisation and PTH release,

whereas slower endocrine integrators such as FGF23 respond over longer time scales. PTH and

AVP are pre-synthesised vesicular hormones that can be released within minutes, whereas FGF23

requires de novo transcription in osteocytes and therefore integrates phosphate exposure over

many hours to days.

The timing of the endocrine response further supports an instability model rather than a single

steady state. Acute oral or additive phosphate loads increase urinary phosphate rapidly and can

raise PTH within hours in some protocols, whereas FGF23 does not appear to mediate the earliest

postprandial adaptation and may rise only later or after repeated loading. In one controlled

five-day loading study in healthy young men, higher phosphate intake increased plasma

phosphate, 24-hour phosphate excretion, and intact FGF23 while suppressing soluble Klotho

without changing PTH in that protocol. Emerging work suggests a possible kidney–bone sensing

loop: proximal phosphate uptake can stimulate renal glycolysis and generation of

glycerol-3-phosphate, which in turn can signal bone to increase FGF23 secretion. If operative

under typical dietary conditions, this mechanism introduces an additional delay between intestinal

phosphate entry and endocrine correction. Because proximal transport responses, rapid

phosphaturic feed-forward signalling, PTH signalling, FGF23 endocrine adaptation, and distal

sodium compensation operate on different physiological time scales, repeated phosphate

exposures could favour overshoot–correction dynamics rather than a single synchronized

response.

A second phase of the response involves distal compensation. Experimental work shows that

FGF23 increases distal tubular sodium-chloride cotransporter (NCC) abundance and activity

through the FGFR1c/αKlotho–ERK1/2–SGK1–WNK4 pathway. In effect, phosphate exposure

can transiently move sodium handling from proximal segments toward distal segments of the

nephron, producing a redistribution of sodium transport rather than a simple increase or decrease



in total sodium balance. Increased distal NaCl delivery may also influence the kidney’s

tubuloglomerular feedback system. The macula densa senses tubular NaCl primarily through

apical NKCC2 transport, while NHE2 appears to modulate macula densa signalling and renin

control. High distal NaCl delivery promotes tubuloglomerular feedback and reduced glomerular

filtration, whereas low distal NaCl promotes renin release. Phosphate-related changes in

segmental sodium delivery could therefore indirectly alter macula densa signalling and renin

thresholds. This interpretation should be framed cautiously. It does not imply a direct phosphate

receptor effect at the macula densa but rather an indirect consequence of altered nephron sodium

distribution.

The RAAS consequences of phosphate loading therefore appear to be context dependent rather

than uniform. In rats, high-phosphate feeding has been associated with increased renin and

angiotensin II in a PTH-dependent manner, whereas sodium-controlled human studies have

reported either no change in renin or aldosterone despite higher urinary metanephrine and

normetanephrine excretion, or patterns more consistent with volume expansion including lower

aldosterone and higher atrial natriuretic peptide. The most defensible synthesis is that phosphate

exposure can reconfigure sodium-handling signals across nephron segments while also engaging

vascular and autonomic modulators, thereby altering the threshold at which sodium–volume

defence systems are recruited.

By inference, these renal phases do not need to produce frank hyponatraemia to be

physiologically relevant. The relevant state becomes most plausible when proximal sodium

reclamation is transiently reduced while water intake is maintained or increased, sodium intake is

relatively low, and distal compensation is delayed or incomplete. Under such conditions

phosphate exposure may increase regulatory strain on sodium–volume homeostasis and, under

dilution-favouring conditions, could bias extracellular physiology toward lower effective sodium

pressure or a more hypo-osmolality-directed state even if serum sodium remains within the

clinical reference range. In this framework the salient signal is not persistent biochemical

hyponatraemia but oscillatory sodium–volume stress, characterized by repeated movement

between phosphate-driven phosphaturia and compensatory sodium retention with increased

control effort required to maintain apparent stability.

Early life may narrow the margin for absorbing such oscillations. Infant renal concentrating

capacity develops progressively during the first year of life, and potential renal solute load

(PRSL) in infancy explicitly includes phosphorus alongside sodium, potassium, chloride, and

nitrogen. PRSL determines the osmotic load that must be excreted in urine; when water intake

falls or water losses rise, the concentrating capacity of the infant kidney can be exceeded and

negative water balance may occur. In this setting a phosphorus-rich formula or processed-food

pattern need not independently produce hyponatraemia to be relevant. It may simply increase

excretory solute burden and narrow the physiological buffer against sodium–volume perturbation

during fever, vomiting, diarrhoea, heat exposure, or reduced intake.

Taken together, dietary inorganic phosphate is best interpreted here as a plausible upstream

destabilizing pressure within sodium-handling physiology, capable of inducing segment-specific

sodium reallocation across the nephron rather than uniform sodium loss. Its potential importance

lies in the combined effects of phosphate-driven phosphaturia, broader proximal natriuretic

pressure, delayed FGF23/Klotho signalling, redistribution of sodium transport toward distal



segments, and increased renal solute load in early life. Under susceptible conditions repeated

exposure could increase the likelihood that anti-hyponatraemia and sodium–volume defence

programmes are recruited, contributing to repeated overshoot–correction cycles within

sodium–volume regulation during sensitive developmental windows.

Additional Mechanistic Possibilities

Established physiology.​

Renal dopamine provides an additional mechanism by which phosphate exposure may broaden

proximal phosphaturic–natriuretic responses. High-phosphate diets increase renal dopamine

content and urinary dopamine excretion in animal studies, and inhibition of dopamine synthesis

impairs acute adaptation to a high-phosphate diet. Because renal dopamine is a well-established

natriuretic and phosphaturic system that modulates both NaPi transporters and proximal sodium

handling, this pathway strengthens the plausibility of a broader proximal transport response

beyond NaPi stoichiometry alone, although it should not be taken as proof of the immediate

postprandial response in every setting.

Emerging evidence.​

Vascular and autonomic findings could strengthen an indirect sodium–volume pathway. Acute

phosphorus loading in healthy adults has been associated in some studies with reduced

flow-mediated dilation, and experimental studies implicate phosphate influx, reactive oxygen

species generation, and reduced endothelial nitric oxide signalling. Separate sodium-controlled

human studies report increased urinary metanephrine and normetanephrine excretion during

high-phosphate intake. Neither mechanism directly lowers serum sodium. Their likely relevance

would instead be indirect: narrowing effective arterial filling or microvascular reserve and thereby

lowering the threshold at which sodium–volume defence mechanisms are recruited.

Mechanisms that weaken a simple narrative.​

Several considerations argue against a one-directional phosphate → low-sodium model.

NaPi-dependent transport accounts for only a minority share of proximal sodium reclamation,

NHE3 remains the dominant proximal sodium transporter, distal FGF23–NCC signalling can

favour sodium retention and volume expansion, and additive-rich diets frequently co-deliver

sodium alongside phosphorus. Human feeding studies therefore show both

phosphaturic/endocrine activation and volume-expanding or hypertensive patterns. The more

defensible interpretation is not uniform sodium lowering but increased variability, segmental

reweighting of transport processes, and context-dependent recruitment of compensatory

regulatory systems.

Speculative extensions.​

Repeated phosphate excursions may also influence transport energetics and recovery kinetics.

Recent work identifies proximal tubular glycolysis as a phosphate sensor upstream of

glycerol-3-phosphate signalling and FGF23 production, and older tubule studies suggest that

phosphate availability can alter proximal tubular oxygen consumption and fluid absorption. It is

therefore plausible, though not established, that repeated phosphate loads could modify the

metabolic time constants governing transport recovery after each challenge. Likewise,

phosphaturia itself may increase urinary solute burden and amplify water stress in infants or

during illness. These possibilities remain hypothesis-level extensions rather than established

causal pathways.



Section 12 — Volume Pathway Catalogue

If Section 11 describes instability of extracellular tonicity, Section 12 describes instability of

sodium deployment within the circulation.

Effective circulating volume (ECV) does not mean total body water. It refers to the fraction of

sodium-bearing fluid that is sensed as available for arterial filling, tissue delivery, and placental

perfusion.

Pathways in this catalogue are classified according to the initiating disturbance that destabilizes

ECV: true loss, internal displacement, barrier leak, failed containment, reservoir sequestration,

flow maldistribution, pump limitation, tone-signal failure, interpretive error, or endocrine control

reassignment. Classical labels such as hypovolaemia, euvolaemia, and hypervolaemia are

therefore treated as downstream descriptors rather than primary causes.

A mechanistic classification of the endocrine-volume pathways shows how pregnancy and early

life can move toward circulatory instability even when total body water is normal or increased.

Volume depends not only on quantity, but on location, retention, containment, forward flow, and

correct signalling.


Volume-Loss Pathways

These pathways reduce ECV by removing sodium-bearing fluid from the body.


True Volume Loss (Absolute depletion)

True volume loss is the canonical depletion state. Bleeding, hyperemesis, diarrhoeal loss, burns,

or renal-adrenal salt wasting collapse both extracellular reserve and arterial filling together. Its

distinguishing feature is simple: volume is truly absent.


Volume-Redistribution Pathways

These pathways lower arterial filling by displacing otherwise available fluid into compartments

that do not effectively support circulation.


Volume Redistribution (Internal displacement)

In volume redistribution, total water may be normal or increased while effective arterial filling

falls. Oedema, ascites, inflammatory third-spacing, adiposity, or metabolic oedema create a state

in which volume is present but mislocated.


Barrier / Oncotic Failure Pathways

These pathways weaken intravascular retention even when water and sodium remain physically

present.



Barrier / Oncotic Failure (Retention / leak)

Barrier and oncotic failure represent a leak-state geometry. Endothelial injury, cytokine-driven

permeability, hypoalbuminaemia, nephrotic loss, or hepatic synthetic failure allow plasma water

to escape or prevent it from being retained. Volume is present, but cannot be held intravascularly.


Vascular Containment Failure

These pathways impair the container rather than the fluid itself.


Vascular Containment Failure (Tone / structural failure)

When venous capacitance expands or vascular recoil weakens, central filling falls despite

near-normal total volume. Hypermobility belongs here not as a generic risk factor, but as the

clinical archetype of volume-containment failure: a visible demonstration that volume can be

present yet poorly contained.


Splanchnic Pooling / Reservoir Shift

These pathways hide volume within capacitance reservoirs rather than removing it from the body.


Splanchnic Pooling / Reservoir Shift

Splanchnic pooling produces a hidden-volume state. Postprandial vasodilation, heat,

histamine-linked reservoir expansion, or autonomic instability sequester blood within mesenteric

beds, lowering central baroreceptor filling while total volume remains present.


Microvascular Flow Failure

These pathways uncouple central flow from tissue delivery.


Microvascular Flow Failure (Distribution failure)

Microvascular flow failure is not primarily a deficit of volume quantity. It is a deficit of

deployment. Endothelial dysfunction, capillary heterogeneity, microthrombotic states, or

placental maldistribution allow volume to circulate centrally while tissue perfusion remains

unreliable.


Cardiac Output Limitation

These pathways convert volume presence into effective undercirculation by limiting forward

flow.



Cardiac Output Limitation (Pump failure)

Cardiac output limitation produces low effective circulating volume even when intravascular

volume is present. Cardiomyopathy, arrhythmia, congenital heart disease, or other low-output

states prevent sodium-bearing fluid from being circulated forward with sufficient pressure and

timing.


Vascular Tonicity Signalling Disruption

These pathways distort vascular-tone cues before gross depletion, structural containment failure,

or central interpretive error is established.


Vascular Tonicity Signalling Disruption

This is a distinct signalling pathway, not a variant of tone failure or mis-sensing. Endothelial

oxidative stress, impaired nitric-oxide buffering, altered arterial compliance, or baroreceptor

mechanotransduction failure produce a state in which volume is present but incorrectly signalled.

Smoking, air pollution, highway proximity, and mould or mycotoxin exposure belong here

because they can degrade vascular signal generation without requiring primary leak or pump

failure.


Volume Mis-Sensing

These pathways arise when the system classifies available volume incorrectly.


Volume Mis-Sensing (Signal interpretation error)

In volume mis-sensing, the circulation is not primarily failing to generate a signal. Rather, the

integrated system reads the state incorrectly. Baroreflex reset, ANP-BNP mismatch,

dysautonomia, or post-stress threshold drift can make adequate volume appear deficient, or true

deficit appear muted.


Endocrine Volume Mis-Regulation

These pathways reassign control away from precise mineralocorticoid-volume regulation toward

unstable substitute control.


Endocrine Volume Mis-Regulation (Control reassignment)

Endocrine volume mis-regulation arises when the wrong controller carries the burden of

circulatory defence. Low-renin states, phosphate-FGF23 signalling, hypothyroid physiology,

glucocorticoid exposure, or endocrine disruptors can suppress or fragment RAAS precision and

shift the system toward cortisol-dominant or rebound-prone control.

Table 12.1 summarizes the characteristic maternal and fetal AVP-RAAS-cortisol patterns across

the volume pathways, preserving the endocrine triad as the organizing grammar of the axis.

Alignment with classical volume categories.

Classical hypovolaemic, euvolaemic, and hypervolaemic labels remain clinically useful, but they

therefore maps each pathway onto the conventional volume language without allowing that

Section 13 — Mechanisms of Volume Pathways

The ten endocrine-volume pathways described here represent distinct mechanistic routes into

effective-circulating-volume instability. These are not interchangeable descriptions of "low

volume." They are different failure modes of sodium deployment within the circulation.

Each pathway is examined in turn to assess environmental, clinical, and developmental evidence

supporting its role as an initiator or amplifier. The question is not whether a contributor uniformly

causes autism. The question is whether it plausibly biases endocrine geometry, placental

perfusion, and early-life calibration toward recurrent volume instability.

Table 13.1 provides a compact ASD-relevant orientation map. The pathway sections that follow

carry the mechanism, uncertainty, heterogeneity, and developmental nuance.
