13.1 Mechanism — True Volume Loss

Absolute Depletion and Canonical Low-ECV Physiology



13.1.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is the actual removal of sodium-bearing fluid from the

body. Effective circulating volume falls because the circulating pool is physically depleted, not

because it has been displaced, leaked, hidden, or misread.

This is the canonical low-volume geometry of the volume axis. Total extracellular reserve and

arterial filling contract together, and the maternal-fetal circulation enters genuine underfilling.


13.1.2 The core mechanism

Because sodium-containing fluid is absent, the baroreflex, kidney, and stress axis converge

immediately on conservation. Uteroplacental flow becomes vulnerable early because no amount

of redistribution can recover a pool that is no longer present.

This distinguishes true volume loss from all later pathways. The defect is not in containment,

signal generation, or interpretation. It is absolute depletion.


13.1.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Major entry points include haemorrhage, hyperemesis gravidarum, diarrhoeal illness, burns,

adrenal insufficiency, renal tubular salt loss, and other states in which sodium and water leave the

body faster than recovery systems can replace them.

Hyperemesis gravidarum is especially instructive because it often combines fluid loss, sodium

loss, potassium depletion, and metabolic stress. It therefore functions as a clinical model of

pan-endocrine depletion rather than a narrow gastrointestinal event.


13.1.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Observational literature has linked severe gestational depletion states, especially hyperemesis

gravidarum, with later neurodevelopmental outcomes in some cohorts, but the signal is not

uniform and may attenuate after stronger family-level adjustment [cite]. Within this framework

HG is treated primarily as a true-volume-loss model, not as a settled or specific ASD determinant.

Haemorrhagic episodes and recurrent salt-wasting crises are better interpreted as

timing-dependent perfusion threats. Their likely developmental significance depends on severity,

repetition, recovery time, and the buffering capacity of the maternal-fetal system rather than on a

single diagnostic label.


13.1.5 Endocrine response

The endocrine geometry is a pan-defence pattern: AVP rises non-osmotically to preserve

perfusion, RAAS rises to conserve sodium, and cortisol rises to support vascular tone under

shock-like constraint. Once placental filling falls sufficiently, the fetal compartment mirrors this

recruitment with parallel AVP, RAAS, and cortisol activation.



The distinctive point is not merely that hormones increase, but that all three systems are recruited

in the same direction because the organism is confronting genuine absence of circulating

sodium-bearing fluid.


13.1.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Recurrent entry into this geometry may bias development toward low-threshold

perfusion defence. The relevant imprint is not chronic hyponatraemia, but repeated exposure to

situations in which survival requires maximal hormonal effort to restore ordinary filling.

True volume loss therefore represents the depletional entry point into the volume axis.

Loss of sodium-bearing fluid -> fall in ECV -> AVP + RAAS recruitment -> cortisol

buffering -> placental/fetal underfilling -> defensive recalibration.


13.2 Mechanism — Volume Redistribution

Mislocated Volume and the Underfilled Circulation


13.2.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is not fluid absence, but fluid displacement. Total body

water may be preserved or increased while effective circulating volume falls because

sodium-bearing fluid accumulates in compartments that do not adequately support arterial filling.

This is the first major divergence between water quantity and circulatory adequacy. The

circulation behaves as though it is depleted even though fluid remains within the body.


13.2.2 The core mechanism

Third-spacing, oedematous interstitial trapping, and serosal sequestration convert available

volume into unusable volume. The mechanism is therefore spatial rather than absolute: volume is

present, but mislocated.

Unlike barrier failure, redistribution does not require the primary lesion to be endothelial leak or

oncotic collapse. The critical event is the movement of fluid away from the effective arterial

space.


13.2.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Representative drivers include inflammatory oedema, ascitic or serosal trapping states,

adiposity-linked interstitial expansion, gestational diabetes, insulin-resistant oedema, and other

conditions that increase total fluid while weakening central filling.



Reduced lymphatic clearance, compliant interstitium, and slow return from third spaces act as

amplifiers because they lengthen the duration of mislocation and convert transient shifts into

persistent underfilling.


13.2.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Preconception obesity, excessive gestational weight gain, and maternal diabetes appear repeatedly

in observational ASD literature, but the strength of evidence varies and umbrella-level summaries

do not support a simple one-exposure-one-outcome interpretation [cite]. Within this pathway

these contributors are mapped not as generic causes, but as redistribution-prone states that

increase the likelihood of oedematous underfilling.

The mechanistic bridge is especially plausible when metabolic inflammation, endothelial stress,

and interstitial trapping coexist. Under those conditions the relevant developmental signal is

unstable effective circulation, not body size or glucose alone.


13.2.5 Endocrine response

The endocrine pattern is a classic underfilling response despite apparent abundance: AVP rises

through non-osmotic recruitment, RAAS rises to defend sodium-bearing volume, and cortisol

rises to stabilize vascular tone when regulation becomes noisy.

The persistence of this triad under conditions of oedema is what makes redistribution clinically

deceptive. The visible excess of fluid masks a genuine signal of arterial scarcity.


13.2.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated oedematous-underfilled states may train the developing system to interpret

fluid abundance as scarcity, increasing the probability of chronic over-recruitment of

volume-defence pathways in later life.

Volume redistribution is therefore the mislocation entry point into the volume axis.

Internal fluid displacement -> effective arterial underfilling -> AVP + RAAS recruitment ->

cortisol support -> oscillatory oedematous underfilling -> developmental recalibration.


13.3 Mechanism — Barrier / Oncotic Failure

Leak States and Failure of Intravascular Retention


13.3.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is failed intravascular retention. Water and sodium are

physically present, but the vascular compartment cannot hold them with adequate fidelity.



The resulting geometry is oedematous low-ECV physiology: total fluid may rise while effective

circulation falls because the barrier leaks or oncotic support is too weak to retain plasma water.


13.3.2 The core mechanism

Endothelial junction opening, glycocalyx injury, inflammatory vasoplegia, or loss of oncotic

pressure allow plasma water to escape the effective arterial space. The circulation therefore faces

a paradoxical state in which visible fluid accumulation coexists with hidden underfilling.

Barrier / oncotic failure differs from simple redistribution because the decisive lesion lies in

retention itself. The container is still present, but it cannot keep volume intravascularly.


13.3.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Maternal infection with fever, cytokine surges, sepsis-like inflammation, hypoalbuminaemia,

nephrotic protein loss, liver dysfunction, autoimmune endothelial injury, and

nanoplastic-associated barrier stress all fit naturally within this pathway.

Low junctional resilience, high VEGF tone, weak lymphatic rescue, and poor albumin reserve

amplify the phenotype because they convert short leak events into sustained oedematous

underfilling.


13.3.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Maternal infection, particularly when accompanied by fever, has one of the stronger observational

signals in autism-related exposure literature [cite]. Within the present framework its relevance is

not merely inflammatory signalling to the fetal brain, but simultaneous creation of a leak-state

circulation with low ECV and unstable placental perfusion.

Maternal autoimmune disease also recurs in ASD literature, but the signal is more heterogeneous

and vulnerable to bias [cite]. It is therefore treated here as a plausible endothelial-leak bridge

rather than a uniform or convincing determinant.


13.3.5 Endocrine response

AVP is typically recruited non-osmotically because the circulation reads underfilling. RAAS

attempts sodium retention but may appear variable when inflammatory vasoplegia and leak

outpace renal rescue. Cortisol rises as a compensatory stabilizer when tone and barrier fidelity are

both unstable.

The endocrine signature is therefore not generic hormone elevation. It is a leak-pattern geometry

in which oedema and endocrine scarcity coexist.



13.3.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated exposure to oedematous low-ECV states may teach the developing system

that tissue swelling does not imply circulatory adequacy. This would favour persistent

non-osmotic AVP recruitment and a lower threshold for stress-dominant perfusion defence.

Barrier / oncotic failure is therefore the retention-failure entry point into the volume axis.

Barrier or oncotic weakness -> intravascular escape -> oedematous low ECV -> AVP

recruitment with variable RAAS -> cortisol stabilization -> developmental recalibration

under leak-state perfusion.


13.4 Mechanism — Vascular Containment Failure

Venous Capacitance, Poor Recoil, and the Hypermobility Archetype


13.4.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

Here the initiating disturbance lies in the vascular container. Total volume may be near normal,

yet venous capacitance expands or vascular recoil weakens enough that central filling falls.

The defining geometry is therefore not loss, leak, or displacement into third spaces. It is poor

containment of otherwise available intravascular volume.


13.4.2 The core mechanism

When venous walls are too compliant or tone is insufficient, a larger share of blood resides in the

capacitance side of the circulation and does not return efficiently to the heart. Central

baroreceptors therefore read underfilling even though fluid remains within the vascular tree.

This pathway is global rather than regional. The problem is not merely that one reservoir expands,

but that the container as a whole does not hold or recoil with adequate firmness.


13.4.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Connective-tissue laxity, pregnancy venodilation, prolonged standing, heat, deconditioning,

orthostatic intolerance, and impaired venoconstriction all amplify this physiology.

Hypermobility belongs here as the clinical archetype of volume-containment failure. It should not

be treated as a generic risk-factor label. Its value is mechanistic: it visibly demonstrates how

reduced recoil and increased capacitance can make present volume behave as if it were absent.


13.4.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Joint hypermobility, hypermobility spectrum disorder, and EDS-like phenotypes have been

reported at increased frequency in autism-related cohorts in recent reviews [cite]. Within this



framework that association is interpreted physiologically rather than symbolically: hypermobility

is evidence that a subset may live within a containment-fragile vascular architecture.

Autonomic dysfunction is also over-represented in ASD populations in several reports [cite]. The

present model treats that signal as a containment-autonomic subset, not a universal rule.


13.4.5 Endocrine response

Because central filling is threatened, AVP rises through non-osmotic recruitment. RAAS may rise

or remain only modestly elevated depending on renal perfusion, posture, and chronic adaptation.

Cortisol frequently becomes a tone substitute when ordinary venous containment is unreliable.

The endocrine pattern is therefore a capacitance-defence geometry: volume is present, but the

system must continually work to keep it centrally available.


13.4.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Recurrent exposure to poor containment may bias development toward an

always-defensive circulatory posture in which posture, heat, and ordinary daily stressors

repeatedly trigger the anti-hypovolaemia program.

Vascular containment failure is therefore the poor-container entry point into the volume axis.

Excess capacitance / weak recoil -> reduced central filling -> AVP recruitment with variable

RAAS -> cortisol tone substitution -> chronic orthostatic cost -> defensive recalibration.


13.5 Mechanism — Splanchnic Pooling / Reservoir

Shift

Hidden Volume in Capacitance Beds


13.5.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is regional sequestration rather than global containment

failure. A substantial share of volume becomes trapped within the splanchnic reservoir and is

rendered centrally unavailable.

The resulting geometry is a hidden-volume state: total volume is present, but central sensors

experience effective scarcity because reservoir beds retain it.


13.5.2 The core mechanism

Mesenteric capacitance vessels can store large amounts of blood. When this reservoir dilates

excessively after meals, during heat exposure, or during histamine-linked vasodilation, central

baroreceptor filling falls even though volume has not left the body.



This pathway is distinct from vascular containment failure. The overall container may remain

competent while one regional reservoir repeatedly captures usable volume.


13.5.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Postprandial pooling, large carbohydrate loads, heat, mast-cell or histamine states, dysautonomia,

and cirrhosis-like reservoir expansion are representative drivers.

Reduced splanchnic emptying, unstable mesenteric sympathetic tone, and hypermobility-linked

visceral dysregulation amplify the pathway by prolonging the time during which hidden volume

remains sequestered.


13.5.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Reports of autonomic dysfunction, orthostatic symptoms, and neurocardiovascular instability in

subsets of autistic individuals are consistent with a reservoir-shift physiology, especially when

paired with hypermobility or connective-tissue laxity [cite].

The ASD linkage here is therefore best framed as a subset model. The relevant bridge is recurrent

hidden-volume signalling, not a claim that all autism reflects splanchnic pooling.


13.5.5 Endocrine response

AVP rises because the central circulation reads underfilling. RAAS is usually posture- and

perfusion-dependent, often variable rather than uniformly high. Cortisol rises when autonomic

buffering is unstable or repeated pooling makes tone support unreliable.

The endocrine geometry is one of concealed scarcity: volume is present in the body, but absent

from the place where the baroreflex expects it.


13.5.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated meal-, heat-, or posture-linked reservoir shifts may bias developmental

calibration toward a circulation that anticipates hidden loss even during ordinary autonomic

transitions.

Splanchnic pooling is therefore the reservoir-sequestration entry point into the volume axis.

Mesenteric reservoir expansion -> central underfilling despite preserved volume -> AVP

recruitment with variable RAAS -> cortisol/autonomic compensation -> recurrent

hidden-volume signalling -> developmental recalibration.


13.6 Mechanism — Microvascular Flow Failure

Flow Without Perfusion



13.6.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance occurs at the level of distribution, not quantity. Central

flow may be adequate, yet microvascular delivery to tissue beds is heterogeneous, shunted, or

intermittently obstructed.

The defining geometry is therefore flow without reliable perfusion. Volume is moving, but it is

not being deployed where oxygen and sodium-dependent work are required.


13.6.2 The core mechanism

Capillary recruitment failure, endothelial dysfunction, microthrombi, and placental

maldistribution convert nominally adequate circulation into tissue-level underdelivery. Effective

circulating volume becomes a problem of deployment rather than possession.

This pathway differs from cardiac output limitation because the pump may be functioning, and

from containment failure because the volume may be centrally present. The lesion lies in the

distribution field itself.


13.6.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Preeclampsia, diabetes, antiphospholipid and microthrombotic states, severe inflammation,

oxidative endothelial stress, and placental villous malperfusion all fit this pathway.

Low microvascular reserve, reduced nitric-oxide buffering, and impaired capillary recruitment

amplify the phenotype by transforming modest shifts in tone into large differences in tissue

delivery.


13.6.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Preeclampsia, maternal diabetes, and placental vascular pathology all recur in observational ASD

literature, though heterogeneity remains substantial and no single vascular diagnosis explains all

cases [cite]. Within this framework they converge because each can produce repeated episodes in

which flow reaches the macro-circulation but not the tissue bed that needs it.

The developmental relevance is strongest when tissue-level oxygen and nutrient delivery become

patchy during sensitive windows. That is a mechanistic bridge, not a claim of direct ASD

causality.


13.6.5 Endocrine response

AVP may be only modestly elevated or clearly elevated depending on how strongly the organism

reads tissue-level underperfusion as a central threat. RAAS is similarly variable because renal

perfusion may be preserved in some cases yet compromised in others. Cortisol tends to rise as a

generalized stabilizer when distribution becomes unreliable.



This variability is itself distinctive. The pathway is not defined by a single endocrine number, but

by a mismatch between central flow and tissue delivery.


13.6.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated microvascular maldistribution may create a developmental pattern in which

the nervous system experiences focal episodes of oxygen and ion-delivery instability without

necessarily showing a sustained macroscopic collapse in circulation.

Microvascular flow failure is therefore the flow-without-perfusion entry point into the volume

axis.

Endothelial / capillary distribution failure -> tissue underdelivery despite circulating volume

-> variable AVP/RAAS recruitment -> cortisol stabilization -> focal developmental

hypoperfusion -> recalibration.


13.7 Mechanism — Cardiac Output Limitation

Pump Failure and Forward-Flow Constraint


13.7.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is insufficient forward flow. Sodium-bearing fluid may

be intravascular, but the pump cannot deliver it with adequate force, volume, or timing.

This produces a low-output geometry in which effective circulating volume falls because

circulation cannot be advanced forward, not because fluid is absent or hidden.


13.7.2 The core mechanism

Reduced stroke volume, arrhythmic inefficiency, myocardial dysfunction, or structural cardiac

limitation decrease renal and placental perfusion despite the physical presence of volume. Venous

pressure may rise even as arterial filling becomes inadequate.

This distinguishes pump failure from microvascular failure. In the latter, flow reaches the wrong

places. Here, the problem begins before sufficient forward flow is achieved at all.


13.7.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Maternal cardiomyopathy, structural heart disease, arrhythmia, congenital heart disease, severe

anaemia, myocarditis, and hypothyroid low-output states are representative drivers.

Low stroke-volume reserve, weak natriuretic buffering, and poor renal-perfusion coupling

amplify the phenotype because small declines in contractile function rapidly become endocrine

underfilling signals.



13.7.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Congenital heart disease has one of the clearer clinical associations with ASD in the

developmental literature [cite]. Within the present framework its relevance is haemodynamic: low

forward flow and abnormal pulsatility repeatedly threaten placental and fetal volume deployment.

Other low-output states are better treated as plausible amplifiers rather than strong ASD-specific

signals. Their developmental effect depends on chronicity, severity, and coexisting placental

vulnerability.


13.7.5 Endocrine response

The endocrine response is classically convergent. AVP rises with low forward-flow signalling,

RAAS rises with renal underperfusion, and cortisol rises to support circulation when contractile

limitation prevents ordinary compensation.

This produces a pump-defence geometry in which endocrine systems attempt to preserve a

circulation that cannot be advanced efficiently.


13.7.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Sustained or repeated low-output states may imprint the developing system through

chronic underfilling, distorted pulsatility, and prolonged dependence on endocrine rescue rather

than through serum electrolyte change alone.

Cardiac output limitation is therefore the pump-failure entry point into the volume axis.

Low forward flow -> renal and placental underperfusion -> AVP + RAAS recruitment ->

cortisol support -> chronic underfilling despite volume presence -> developmental

recalibration.


13.8 Mechanism — Vascular Tonicity Signalling

Disruption

Endothelial Signal Failure Without Structural Container Loss


13.8.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is failure of vascular signal generation. The container

may be structurally intact and the circulation may still contain volume, yet endothelial, arterial,

and baroreceptor systems fail to generate a faithful tone signal.

This must remain distinct from both containment failure and volume mis-sensing. In containment

failure the physical container is too lax. In mis-sensing the interpretation is wrong. Here the signal

itself is degraded before it reaches the interpretive layer.



13.8.2 The core mechanism

Nitric-oxide depletion, oxidative endothelial stress, altered arterial compliance, and impaired

mechanotransduction create signal noise within the vascular tree. The system begins to behave as

though volume were threatened even when gross depletion, leak, or pump failure are absent.

The defining identity statement is therefore precise: volume is present but incorrectly signalled.


13.8.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Maternal smoking, traffic-related air pollution, highway proximity, mould and mycotoxin

exposure, and other NO-disrupting toxicants fit this pathway because they can perturb endothelial

tone, vascular compliance, or baroreceptor signalling without requiring frank volume loss.

Low arterial compliance, weak NO-sGC-cGMP buffering, and impaired baroreceptor

mechanotransduction amplify the phenotype because they increase the probability that minor

vascular stressors will be translated into false deficit signals.


13.8.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Prenatal air pollution and near-roadway exposure have shown recurrent observational association

with ASD-related outcomes in several cohorts [cite]. Within this framework the key bridge is

vascular signal noise and endothelial-tone disruption, not simple direct neurotoxicity.

Maternal smoking fits the pathway strongly at the mechanistic level but has a more mixed ASD

literature signal [cite]. Mould and mycotoxins are treated more cautiously still: they remain

plausible mechanistic contributors because they can affect endothelial oxidative tone and

neurodevelopmental vulnerability, but direct ASD evidence is limited and heterogeneous [cite].


13.8.5 Endocrine response

AVP and RAAS often become noisy rather than uniformly high. AVP may rise or remain near

baseline depending on how strongly false perfusion threat is registered. RAAS may remain quiet

or become secondarily recruited. Cortisol functions as backup tone support when signal fidelity is

poor.

This oscillatory endocrine geometry is a core discriminator. The system is not responding to

actual depletion so much as to corrupted vascular information.


13.8.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated gestational exposure to false-deficit signalling may bias the developing

circulation toward chronic over-readiness, lowering the threshold at which non-osmotic AVP

recruitment and cortisol substitution are triggered after birth.

Vascular tonicity signalling disruption is therefore the signal-generation failure entry point into

the volume axis.



Endothelial / NO / baroreceptor signal failure -> false perfusion-threat signalling -> noisy

AVP/RAAS recruitment -> cortisol backup tone support -> recurrent developmental signal

error.


13.9 Mechanism — Volume Mis-Sensing

Interpretive Error and False Deficit States


13.9.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this pathway the initiating disturbance is interpretive rather than structural. Signals are

generated, but the integrated system classifies the actual state incorrectly.

The resulting geometry is a false-deficit or false-adequacy state. Volume may be present, yet the

regulatory system behaves as though it were absent; or genuine deficit may be under-read.


13.9.2 The core mechanism

Chronic baroreflex reset, ANP-BNP discordance, altered interoceptive integration, and post-stress

threshold drift all permit ordinary circulatory states to be assigned the wrong meaning. The lesion

therefore lies in interpretation rather than in signal production.

This distinguishes volume mis-sensing from vascular tonicity signalling disruption. In the latter

the signal is faulty before it is read. Here the signal reaches the system, but the read-out is wrong.


13.9.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Representative drivers include dysautonomia, chronic illness recovery states, repeated

dehydration or pooling episodes followed by incomplete reset, vascular stiffness, and

natriuretic-peptide mismatch.

Low baroreflex gain, unstable vagal buffering, and weak central error correction amplify the

pathway because they allow interpretive drift to persist after the initiating event has resolved.


13.9.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Autonomic dysfunction, altered interoceptive processing, and central autonomic network

differences are repeatedly discussed in ASD-related literature, but they are neither universal nor

specific [cite]. Within this pathway they are treated as evidence for a subset in which circulatory

state may be persistently over-read or misclassified.

The developmental significance lies in recurrent false-deficit signalling, not in any one autonomic

diagnosis.



13.9.5 Endocrine response

AVP and RAAS become discordant to the true volume state. Either can remain engaged despite

adequacy, or fail to recruit proportionately during real deficit. Cortisol rises because the system

experiences instability even when the physical state is not straightforwardly depleted.

This is therefore an interpretive-noise geometry: endocrine defence reflects the reading error

more than the underlying fluid distribution.


13.9.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated false-deficit signalling may teach the developing organism to defend

against scarcity that is not actually present, producing a lower threshold for orthostatic, thermal,

or illness-related volume alarms later in life.

Volume mis-sensing is therefore the interpretive-error entry point into the volume axis.

Signal reaches regulatory system -> state is misclassified -> discordant AVP/RAAS

recruitment -> cortisol compensation -> chronic false-deficit physiology -> developmental

recalibration.


13.10 Mechanism — Endocrine Volume

Mis-Regulation

Control Reassignment and RAAS Imprecision


13.10.1 Initiating condition and volume geometry

In this final pathway the initiating disturbance is not where volume sits, but who controls it.

Canonical RAAS precision is suppressed, fragmented, or repeatedly replaced by substitute

systems.

The resulting geometry is control reassignment: volume may be present and even blood pressure

may be preserved, yet the regulation of sodium-bearing volume is being carried by the wrong

endocrine controller.


13.10.2 The core mechanism

Low-renin states, phosphate-FGF23 signalling, hypothyroid physiology, mineralocorticoid

interference, endocrine-disruptor exposure, or chronic glucocorticoid load can all reduce the

precision of renin-aldosterone regulation. Cortisol and non-canonical pressor systems then

assume a larger share of the control burden.

This pathway is not identical to volume mis-sensing. The system may correctly perceive the need

for regulation. The problem is that regulation is executed through the wrong hormonal geometry.



13.10.3 Environmental / clinical drivers and amplifiers

Representative drivers include phosphate-rich processed diets and FGF23-Klotho imbalance,

PFAS and related endocrine disruptors, hypothyroidism, exogenous glucocorticoids, chronic

stress loading, and other low-renin or rebound-prone states.

Weak mineralocorticoid selectivity, high downstream RAAS gain, and unstable distal rescue

amplify the phenotype because they make the transition from suppressed control to overshoot

more likely.


13.10.4 Empirical support and ASD-relevant contributors

Hypothyroid and endocrine-disruptor exposures recur in developmental literature, but the

evidence is mixed and highly context dependent [cite]. Within this framework they are treated as

plausible endocrine-volume disruptors rather than uniform ASD determinants.

Phosphate-FGF23 signalling is included because it provides a mechanistic route to quiet-RAAS,

high-tone physiology and delayed sodium-handling compensation. Its importance here lies in

instability of control architecture, not in a claim that phosphate alone produces autism.


13.10.5 Endocrine response

AVP becomes variable because the system is compensating atop an unstable controller. RAAS

may be suppressed, fragmented, or rebound-prone rather than simply low or high. Cortisol rises

as the dominant support axis when mineralocorticoid precision is inadequate.

The distinctive endocrine statement is therefore clear: the wrong controller is maintaining

apparent stability.


13.10.6 Developmental implication and synthesis

Hypothesis. Repeated reliance on cortisol-dominant or non-canonical control may bias the

developing system toward stress-maintained normotension with poor regulatory precision,

leaving later life vulnerable to rebound overshoot, dilutional overlap, or chronic autonomic cost.

Endocrine volume mis-regulation is therefore the control-reassignment entry point into the

volume axis.

RAAS precision weakens -> cortisol / alternative pressor control expands -> variable AVP

and rebound-prone RAAS -> apparent stability with poor fidelity -> developmental

recalibration under the wrong controller.



14 Outliers and Hybrid States: Peripheral or Overlapping

Sodium-Stress Mechanisms

Clarifier — The transient cytokine → phosphate/FGF‑23 sequence is used here as a model of

**state transitions** in the triad—not as a claim about persistent hyponatremia. The

compensatory swing (RAAS spike → suppression, cortisol carry) is the imprinting risk.

Outlier states refer to compound or context-dependent drivers of sodium-handling stress that

reproduce one or more Decapath geometries in whole or in part, often through transient or layered

mechanisms rather than a single dominant pathway. These states may blend ionic mis-sensing,

energetic strain, and oncotic dilution in variable proportions, producing endocrine configurations

that can be fleeting or individual-specific. Immune activation and acute illness are common

physiological events that can, in some individuals, elicit the full Sodium-Switch triad of high

vasopressin, low effective RAAS activity, and elevated cortisol, while in others producing only a

partial or short-lived response. Such variability underscores that Outlier States are defined not by

rarity of exposure but by the complex or transient integration of Decapath mechanisms.


14.1 Lithium Pseudo-Sodium Mimicry [H/S]

Lithium acts as a sodium analogue within excitable tissues and transport systems. Although total

dietary sodium intake far exceeds trace lithium exposure, endocrine regulation responds to sensed

sodium activity, osmotic pressure, and circulating volume—not to absolute sodium mass. In

aqueous solution lithium exists as fully dissociated Li⁺ ions that pass readily through sodium

channels, transporters, and exchangers yet are poorly extruded by the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase. This

asymmetry allows progressive intracellular accumulation: lithium enters more easily than it

leaves. The resulting one-way influx distorts sodium-dependent membrane potentials, interferes

with receptor phosphorylation, and misleads the sodium-sensing feedback loops that coordinate

renin, aldosterone, vasopressin, and oxytocin release.

Reverse-osmosis (RO) and desalination systems can magnify this pseudo-sodium effect. RO

membranes remove nearly all competing cations—Na⁺, Ca²⁺, Mg²⁺—while permitting trace Li⁺ to

slip through because of its small hydrated radius and high mobility. The outcome is water that is

sodium-depleted yet lithium-enriched in relative terms. In individuals who consume large

volumes of low-TDS or RO water—especially when pre-existing dilutional stress suppresses

vasopressin—the remaining lithium ions can substitute for sodium in osmosensitive cells. Even

trace concentrations can exert disproportionate influence within the residual ionic field,

mimicking chronic water retention and perpetuating vasopressin activity despite apparently

normal plasma Na⁺.

Chronic or population-level exposure therefore produces a state of functional or pseudo-sodium

hyponatraemia: total sodium appears normal, yet effective sodium activity at the membrane level

is reduced. Endocrine compensation drifts toward cortisol-dominant tone as renin–aldosterone

signaling quiets. This mis-read sodium signal constitutes a distinct pathway within the broader

hyponatraemia framework.

Environmental and epidemiological studies lend support. Regions with lithium-contaminated or

desalinated drinking water show higher autism-spectrum prevalence, consistent with lithium’s

ability to interfere with sodium-regulated developmental pathways. Within the Sodium-Switch

hypothesis, lithium exposure represents a mechanistic bridge between ultra-low-TDS hydration

and sustained osmotic-endocrine miscalibration—an ionic mimicry that stabilizes antidiuretic

tone and low-effective-sodium physiology even in the absence of true sodium deficiency.



15 The Sodium Struggle Phenocopy Pathways

Not all developmental pathways leading to osmotic stress involve true hyponatraemia or

measurable shifts in serum sodium. A distinct group of exposures can trigger the same

downstream cellular consequences through sodium-independent excitatory or metabolic

mechanisms. These pathways do not reduce the concentration of extracellular sodium yet still

drive intracellular water influx, cellular swelling, impaired regulatory volume decrease (RVD),

and failure of the shrinkage-to-differentiation transition that neurodevelopment depends upon.

Several exposures fall into this category. High-glutamate or glutamate-potentiating substances

such as monosodium glutamate (MSG) induce rapid osmotic and excitatory water entry through

ionotropic glutamate receptors, causing acute astrocytic swelling and transient intracellular

hypotonicity. Ammonia, elevated during maternal or neonatal infection or hepatic stress, produces

intracellular swelling by disrupting potassium and chloride gradients and impairing mitochondrial

handling of glutamate. Perinatal infection, through cytokine-driven excitotoxic signalling, creates

episodes of glial and neuronal swelling independent of serum sodium. Hypoxia, another common

perinatal stressor, increases extracellular glutamate and triggers swelling via energy-dependent

failure of ion pumps.

A second class of sodium-independent osmotic phenocopies arises from non-electrolyte osmoles,

including glucose and artificial sweeteners that are poorly permeable or slowly equilibrating

across cell membranes. Hyperglycaemia, as seen in maternal diabetes or gestational glucose

intolerance, elevates extracellular osmolality without supplying sodium to match it. Cells interpret

the heightened osmotic pressure as if sodium were present, leading to aberrant water movement,

asymmetric volume responses, osmolyte depletion, and chloride-driven GABA depolarisation.

Artificial sweeteners (e.g., sorbitol, mannitol, sucralose) exert similar effects: they raise total

osmolality while bypassing sodium-linked components of tonic signalling, producing a functional

“low effective sodium” state despite normal or high measured osmolality. In pregnancy,

oscillations of maternal glucose or sweetener-related osmoles may transmit discordant cues to the

fetus—high osmolality alongside low effective sodium—forcing immature glia to manage

volume transitions for which they are not developmentally prepared.

Although distinct in their triggers, these sodium-independent stressors share a common outcome:

cells swell when they should shrink, disrupting the developmental timing windows in which

neural precursors must reduce volume to exit proliferation and begin differentiation. The result

mirrors the developmental signature of true hyponatraemia: delayed maturation, excessive

synaptic precursors, unstable dendritic structure, impaired pruning, and prolonged circuit

immaturity.

These osmotic phenocopies therefore occupy a unique position within the broader framework

proposed here. They are not exceptions to a sodium-based model, but parallel routes to the same

physiological state—a state in which neurons and glia cannot execute the regulated volume

transitions required for normal development. Their inclusion underscores the central claim of this

hypothesis: it is the osmotic consequence, not the chemical identity of the exposure, that unifies

diverse autism-associated risk factors.



15.1 Intergenerational Sodium-Struggle Programming

Intergenerational Sodium-Struggle Programming refers to the process by which a mother’s

osmotic interpretation, rather than her literal osmolality or sodium concentration, provides the

endocrine and signalling environment through which the fetus constructs its long-term

osmoregulatory architecture. This mechanism does not require overt hyponatraemia or

recognisable pregnancy pathology; instead, it reflects the mother’s chronic signalling tendencies

across AVP, RAAS, MR/GR, thyroid, sympathetic, cytokine, and metabolic axes. These

tendencies create a regulatory landscape that the fetus evolves within and internalises as

“normal.”

This phenomenon constitutes one of the primary sodium-independent osmotic phenocopies in the

Sodium-Struggle framework. It recreates the intracellular consequences of hyponatraemia —

vulnerability to swelling, altered chloride handling, and reduced osmotic resilience — without

requiring a true dilutional event.

To clarify the mechanisms involved, this section is organised into the following components:

●​ 15.1.1 Maternal Osmotic Interpretation as the Primary Developmental Environment​

●​ 15.1.2 Fetal Osmoregulatory Inputs: What the Developing Brain Actually Learns​

●​ 15.1.3 Maternal Interpretation → Fetal Interpretation: The Two-Layer Calibration

Model​

●​ 15.1.4 Mechanistic Pathways: Receptor-Level Divergence and Axis Decalibration​

●​ 15.1.5 Fetal Osmotic Set-Point Formation Under Mismatched or Threat-Biased

Signals​

●​ 15.1.6 Developmental Consequences: Ionic Handling, Volume Vulnerability, and

Delayed Inhibitory Maturation​

●​ 15.1.7 Why Intergenerational Sodium-Struggle Programming Is a

Sodium-Independent Osmotic Phenocopy​


15.1.1 Maternal Osmotic Interpretation as the Primary Developmental

Environment

A mother’s osmotic regulatory behaviour is shaped by her life-course physiology — by shifts in

AVP thresholds, RAAS sensitivity, adrenal zonation, thyroid-metabolic coupling, inflammatory

tone, sympathetic gain, and cortisol utilisation. These traits may arise from genetics, early-life



imprinting, autoimmune tendencies, metabolic strain, chronic stress, or previous pregnancies.

Critically, they may be present even when maternal laboratory values are normal.

A mother with sodium-struggle physiology does not necessarily exhibit hyponatraemia. Instead,

her body may behave as though sodium is fragile, recruiting AVP, RAAS, cortisol, and thyroid

mechanisms more readily. This interpretive stance creates a signalling ecosystem in which

osmotic and volume-related cues are filtered through her thresholds and biases.

The fetus develops inside this interpretive world. Maternal oscillations in AVP, RAAS, cortisol,

chloride handling, thyroid hormones, blood pressure, and perfusion establish the background

endocrine environment in which fetal osmoregulatory organs mature. The placenta transmits not

only nutrients and oxygen but the mother’s pattern of osmotic meaning-making.

Thus, maternal physiology sets the initial conditions for fetal osmoregulatory development,

independent of whether maternal chemistry appears normal or abnormal.


Segue to Table 15.1

A wide range of maternal physiological phenotypes reflect these long-standing osmotic-endocrine

tendencies, and many of them show partial maternal-line concentration across generations. These

conditions do not act through Mendelian inheritance but through the transmission of osmotic

interpretation patterns and endocrine response biases. Table X summarises several maternal-line

conditions frequently enriched along the maternal lineage and highlights how each aligns with

sodium-struggle–relevant physiology.

15.1.2 Fetal Osmoregulatory Inputs: What the Developing Brain

Actually Learns

The fetus does not imprint on maternal sodium concentrations alone. It constructs its

osmoregulatory architecture from multiple physiological inputs, all filtered through the

mother’s interpretive system.

These inputs include:


Osmotic / Electrolyte Cues

●​ extracellular sodium​

●​ extracellular chloride​



●​ effective osmolality​

●​ intracellular osmolyte strategies​


Volume / Pressure / Perfusion Cues

●​ intravascular volume​

●​ blood pressure and vascular tone​

●​ placental perfusion, including “brain-sparing” patterns​

●​ micro-signals of hypoperfusion or turbulence​


Endocrine / Receptor-Ligand Cues

●​ AVP pulsatility: V1a, V1b, V2 signalling​

●​ RAAS tone, especially AGTR1 gain​

●​ cortisol availability and MR/GR balance​

●​ thyroid tone and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase support​


Stress / Cytokine / Autonomic Cues

●​ inflammatory cytokines​

●​ sympathetic activation​

●​ metabolic rate and mitochondrial support​


Every fetus monitors these inputs, but not every fetus receives a coherent pattern.

In typical pregnancies, these cues reinforce one another and provide a stable framework

from which the fetus can derive adaptive set-points.



In contrast, sodium-struggle physiology emerges when the fetus receives inconsistent,

contradictory, or threat-skewed cues, forcing it to build its regulatory system around

signals that do not logically align.

Segue to Table 15.1.2

Epidemiological studies have identified several maternal physiological states that

increase the likelihood of ASD in offspring. Within the Sodium-Struggle framework,

these conditions are unified not by shared genetics but by shared osmotic, endocrine, and

metabolic signalling patterns that can shape fetal imprinting. Table Y summarises these

maternal phenotypes, the specific conditions they encompass, and the osmoregulatory

mechanisms through which they can bias fetal set-point formation.

15.1.3 Maternal Interpretation → Fetal Interpretation: The Two-Layer

Calibration Model

A central mechanism of intergenerational programming is that the fetus experiences the maternal

interpretation of osmotic conditions, not the maternal osmolality itself.

1.​ Maternal Layer:​

The mother interprets sodium/volume/osmotic conditions through her thresholds and

biases.​

Her body may “decide” that sodium is scarce or volume is threatened even with normal

values.​

2.​ Fetal Layer:​

The fetus observes the patterns of maternal endocrine behaviour — AVP pulses, RAAS

activation, cortisol substitution, perfusion adjustments — and forms its own interpretation

of what these patterns mean about the world it is entering.​


Thus, what is transmitted across generations is not sodium concentration but a pattern of

signalling, a template for how osmotic, volume, and stress cues are handled. The fetus uses this

template to build its long-term osmoregulatory architecture.

This two-layer system explains why sodium-struggle can arise:



●​ in the absence of hyponatraemia​

●​ in the presence of normal laboratory values​

●​ during stable pregnancies​

●​ despite adequate maternal nutrition​

●​ in mothers with healthy chemistries but vulnerable signalling patterns​

●​ AND in fetuses with intrinsic (genetic) sodium-handling vulnerabilities​


It also explains why acute maternal events (infection, fever, cytokine surge, dehydration, stress)

can abruptly distort imprinting during sensitive developmental windows.


15.1.4 Mechanistic Pathways: Receptor-Level Divergence and Axis

Decalibration

When maternal signals are mismatched or chronically threat-skewed, fetal osmoregulatory

systems adapt in divergent and sometimes contradictory ways:

●​ V1a sensitivity increases, biasing toward higher vasoconstrictor tone.​

●​ V1b gain increases, strengthening AVP–HPA coupling and increasing ACTH–cortisol

reactivity.​

●​ V2 signaling may be buffered or partially desensitised, preventing overt hyponatraemia

despite frequent AVP activation.​

●​ AGTR1 gain increases, amplifying the pressor and sodium-retentive effects of

angiotensin II.​

●​ MR–GR balance shifts toward cortisol, especially when aldosterone is inconsistently

available.​

●​ Adrenal zonation (ZG vs ZF) develops under distorted cues, affecting lifelong sodium

and stress responsiveness.​

●​ Chloride-regulating pathways (e.g., WNK–SPAK/OSR1) may tune upward, increasing

intracellular chloride vulnerability.​



●​ Thyroid coupling becomes altered, changing metabolic support for ionic pumping.​


Among the affected axes, thyroid signalling occupies a distinct dual role within this framework.

At the maternal level, thyroid dysfunction reflects underlying sodium–volume instability and

impaired cellular recovery under hypo-osmolar or low-effective-sodium states, consistent with the

known sensitivity of thyrocytes and thyroid hormone synthesis to metabolic and osmotic stress.

At the fetal level, however, maternal thyroid hormone availability constitutes a direct endocrine

exposure: thyroid hormones are essential for metabolic rate, mitochondrial function, and

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase–dependent ionic pumping, and reduced maternal thyroid support during sensitive

developmental windows can therefore amplify volume vulnerability and delay

neurodevelopmental timing.

Together, these divergences produce not uniform hypo-osmolality but a misaligned regulatory

architecture, in which the relationships between sodium, chloride, volume, perfusion, and stress

cues no longer align normally. The system anticipates sodium fragility even when sodium is

normal.


15.1.5 Fetal Osmotic Set-Point Formation Under Threat-Biased or

Conflicting Signals

Once receptor and axis tuning diverge, fetal osmoregulatory set-points shift:

●​ AVP and RAAS activate at lower interpretive thresholds.​

●​ Normal environmental sodium is treated as marginal.​

●​ Volume fluctuations are interpreted as threatening.​

●​ Chloride regulation stabilises around higher intracellular set-points.​

●​ Osmolyte balance adapts to perceived osmotic volatility.​

●​ Neural and glial cells adopt a volume-vulnerable baseline, even without extracellular

hypotonicity.​


These adaptations persist after birth. The child behaves as if hyponatraemia is always a risk —

activating AVP, RAAS, cortisol, and sympathetic pathways with lower provocation, tolerating

narrower ionic margins, and favouring sodium-conserving states.

This is not lifelong hyponatraemia.​

It is a hyponatraemia-like regulatory style shaped by developmental programming.



15.1.6 Developmental Consequences: Ionic Handling, Volume

Vulnerability, and Delayed Inhibitory Maturation

The developing brain depends critically on the timing of chloride reversal, KCC2 expression,

NKCC1 downregulation, and GABA’s shift from depolarising to hyperpolarising.

Sodium-struggle conditions bias these processes by:

●​ maintaining elevated intracellular chloride​

●​ impairing KCC2 trafficking and expression​

●​ prolonging NKCC1 dominance​

●​ increasing reliance on WNK–SPAK signalling​

●​ creating subtle but chronic cell-volume vulnerability​

●​ reducing metabolic support for ionic pumping​


Collectively, these sodium-struggle–induced delays in KCC2-mediated inhibitory maturation are

expected to have disproportionate downstream effects on circuit-level refinement processes,

including those dependent on parvalbumin (PV) interneuron timing (see Discussion).

These effects disproportionately burden rapidly firing interneurons, particularly

parvalbumin-positive cells, contributing to delayed inhibitory maturation, excitatory drift, and

atypical oscillatory development.


15.1.7 Why Intergenerational Sodium-Struggle Programming Is a

Sodium-Independent Osmotic Phenocopy

This mechanism belongs to the Sodium-Independent Excitatory–Osmotic Phenocopy category

because it reproduces the functional consequences of hyponatraemia — ionic imbalance, chloride

vulnerability, reduced osmotic resilience, volume-biased neural behaviour — without requiring

sodium loss or dilution.

The phenotype emerges because:

●​ maternal interpretation → fetal interpretation → miscalibration​



●​ signals are mismatched or threat-biased​

●​ receptor and axis coupling diverge​

●​ fetal thresholds shift​

●​ intracellular ionic behaviour becomes hyponatraemia-like​

●​ yet serum sodium may remain normal throughout​


This is a true phenocopy: a molecular, cellular, and developmental mimicry of hyponatraemia’s

intracellular consequences created endogenously, through imprinting and signalling

misalignment, rather than through external sodium dilution.


15.2 Gestational hyperglycemia as an oscillatory osmotic stressor [E]

During hyperglycemia, plasma osmolality rises even as measured [Na+] can appear low

(translocational hyponatremia). AVP increases to counter dehydration; as glucose falls,

water re‑enters cells, producing a transient true hyponatremia and receptor instability. The

phase‑by‑phase pattern below summarizes expected maternal and fetal readouts.

15.2.1 Chronic RAAS activation and placental hypoperfusion

Clarifier — Injury accrues when repeated RAAS surges with receptor fatigue force cortisol

substitution—the anti‑hyponatremia compensation—which can occur even when serum [Na⁺]

appears normal.

Sustained Ang II/AGTR1 (AT1) activity raises uteroplacental resistance and produces

“brain‑sparing” flow patterns; postnatally this may reset sympathetic and osmotic control

thresholds. When dilutional cues suppress AVP, cortisol can function as a biochemical

“sodium‑grab” via MR occupancy when 11β‑HSD2 is saturated or down‑regulated, temporarily

substituting for mineralocorticoid action.


15.2.2 Glucose–sodium transport imbalance and fetal low‑effective

sodium (translocational)

Because GLUT1 rapidly transfers glucose while Na⁺ transfer is constrained by

Na⁺/K⁺‑ATPase and ENaC, the fetal milieu can become sugar‑rich and salt‑lean. We

describe the resulting fetal state as functional low‑effective sodium (a translocational

condition), not pseudohyponatremia (which is an analytical artifact). The fetus receives a

dilution cue (↓AVP) concurrently with perfusion stress (↑RAAS, ↑cortisol), establishing a

cross‑signal trap that favors receptor recalibration.

Summary: the maternal–fetal cross‑signal trap—low fetal AVP with high

RAAS/cortisol—pushes receptor‑level recalibration that can persist into infancy.


15.2.3 Conditions reproducing the same osmotic–endocrine pattern as

gestational hyperglycemia

Distinct exposures can converge on the same fetal signature (low‑effective sodium with perfusion

stress): low‑TDS over‑hydration [H]; medication‑induced hyponatremia (SSRIs,

carbamazepine/oxcarbazepine, thiazides, oxytocin/desmopressin) [E/S]; pre‑eclampsia/severe

maternal stress (aldosterone/cortisol ↑ with endothelial leak, oncotic pressure ↓) [S]; artificial

sweeteners/polyols (non‑ionic osmoles; cord/amniotic detection documented) [E/S]; early infancy

analogue: formula prepared with very low‑TDS water [S]. States labeled clinically as

“pseudohyponatremia” are treated here as functional low‑effective sodium unless they are the

laboratory artifact.


15.2.4 Fetal Endocrine Hierarchy under Translocational / Functional

Low–Effective–Sodium States (framework)

Low maternal albumin and total protein reduce oncotic pressure, retaining water

disproportionately to sodium and amplifying effective dilution. Epidemiologic studies have

reported higher autism risk in pregnancies marked by extreme vegetarian or protein‑deficient

diets, consistent with this pathway in which low maternal albumin weakens oncotic pressure and

amplifies effective dilution. Dilution inhibits AVP, whereas hypoperfusion activates RAAS and

the hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal (HPA) axis; hypoxia drives cortisol. The fetal kidney tunes

renin release to local oxygenation and perfusion. Note on terminology: we reserve

pseudohyponatremia for laboratory artifact; the physiologic state described here is a

translocational/functional low‑effective‑sodium condition.


15.3 Artificial Sweeteners and Polyols: The Non-Electrolyte Osmole

Hypothesis [S]

Non-nutritive sweeteners and polyols increase total plasma osmolality without contributing ionic

charge. This creates a translocational shift of water into the extracellular compartment while

leaving sodium concentration functionally diluted—a state of low-effective sodium rather than

analytical pseudohyponatraemia. In gestational diabetes, impaired insulin buffering amplifies

these osmotic excursions and raises vascular resistance. The fetal read-out is discordant, with low

AVP and high RAAS/cortisol, despite elevated total osmolality and absent glucose cues.

Predicted biomarker signatures include lower cord copeptin, higher renin and cortisol, an

expanded osmolal gap, and detectable sweeteners in cord or amniotic samples. These measurable

outcomes provide a falsifiable test for the non-electrolyte osmole hypothesis.


15.4 Maternal Salt Loading [S]

Extreme maternal salt exposure represents a supraphysiologic experimental condition that can

reproduce sodium-struggle phenotypes without constituting a primary hyponatraemic pathway.

Excess salt intake during gestation may provoke fetal arginine vasopressin (AVP) surges in



response to acute osmotic, vascular, or placental stress. Because fetal renal sodium handling is

immature, this response may involve AVP overshoot, in which water-retaining signals persist

beyond the resolution of the initial sodium load. As sodium is cleared but AVP signalling remains

elevated, free-water retention can dominate, producing delayed dilution and intracellular water

influx. In parallel, high sodium exposure can damage the endothelial glycocalyx, reducing

vascular sodium buffering and impairing uteroplacental perfusion. Experimental support for this

stress-mediated interpretation comes from murine studies showing that high-salt diets alone

activate sympathetic–adrenal pathways and increase vascular sensitivity without sodium

retention, as well as from rat models in which gestational salt loading induces autism-like

neurodevelopmental outcomes. Together, these findings support classification of maternal salt

loading as a phenocopy or amplifier pathway capable of reproducing downstream cellular

consequences indistinguishable from true hyponatraemia, despite an initial hypertonic exposure.


16 The Amplifier Network


Excessive gestational weight gain as an upstream

Pathway load amplifier

Several epidemiological studies report a modest increase in offspring autism risk when maternal

gestational weight gain falls in the extreme upper tail, commonly operationalised as gains on the

order of roughly 20 to 23 kg (about 44 to 50+ lb) or higher. Importantly, this signal is not

equivalent to pre-pregnancy obesity. Whereas pre-pregnancy BMI reflects a relatively steady

baseline physiological state, very high gestational weight gain captures a pregnancy-specific

trajectory characterised by rapid changes in maternal mass and fluid handling. In multiple

datasets, the association with very high gestational weight gain persists after adjustment for

pre-pregnancy BMI, indicating that the pregnancy load itself can carry mechanistic relevance

beyond baseline adiposity. Within the Sodium-Struggle framework, this literature is interpreted as

evidence that extreme gestational load can antagonise a pre-existing maternal sodium-volume

fragility, amplifying compensatory demands during pregnancy rather than initiating pathology de

novo.

Accordingly, very high gestational weight gain is best framed as a physiological load amplifier

rather than a primary cause. Pregnancy already requires coordinated expansion of maternal

plasma volume and tight coupling among renal sodium retention, autonomic tone, vascular

compliance, and baroreflex control. When maternal mass and extracellular fluid expand rapidly,

the gravitational and haemodynamic cost of everyday upright life increases. Upright posture and

movement shift blood into gravity-dependent venous compartments, and increased venous

capacitance, abdominal mass, and late-gestation mechanical effects on venous return can magnify

venous pooling and increase variability in preload during routine transitions such as standing up,

climbing stairs, carrying loads, or stop–start walking. Under these conditions, stability is not

achieved through a single one-time adjustment. Instead, the system must engage in tighter and

more frequent stabilisation, with greater reliance on intravascular volume expansion, more

consistent renal sodium retention, higher demand for baroreflex precision, stronger venous return



support within capacitance beds, and closer coordination across autonomic and endocrine

effectors, including sympathetic tone, RAAS, and vasopressin signalling.

This distinction is particularly relevant for mothers with pre-existing sodium struggle, in whom

sodium-volume buffering capacity is already limited. When buffering is robust, rising pregnancy

load can be accommodated through relatively smooth, low-noise adaptation. When buffering is

marginal, the same load is more likely to be met by repeated compensatory responses rather than

steady control. These include oscillatory recruitment of RAAS and vasopressin, greater

dependence on cortisol carry when mineralocorticoid support is functionally insufficient, and

more frequent redistribution between central circulation and venous capacitance reservoirs. In the

manuscript’s terms, this represents a shift from relatively steady signalling to fluctuating,

high-gain compensatory signalling. The fetus develops within this signalling environment,

calibrating to maternal patterns of volume defence and perfusion stabilisation rather than to a

single laboratory value or static physiological state.

Within DecaPath logic, excessive gestational weight gain therefore belongs upstream as an

amplifying modifier. It does not initiate sodium struggle, but it can magnify the signalling

consequences of an existing sodium-volume fragility during pregnancy, a developmentally

sensitive period in which maternal endocrine and haemodynamic dynamics constitute part of the

fetal training set. Conceptually, this parallels the later “walking window” principle described

elsewhere in the manuscript. In both cases, gravity-related mechanics convert a largely steady

control problem into one characterised by frequent perturbations of venous return and pressure

regulation, requiring stabilising loops to operate more often and at higher gain. Here, the relevant

window is maternal pregnancy under an unusually large and rapidly changing mass and volume

load.


17 Convergent Pathways - When Multiple Decapaths Interact

Across convergences, the common endpoint is the same: anti‑hyponatremia compensation (triad

discordance) that persists after chemistry normalizes.


17.1 – Maternal Autoimmunity (Rho[D])–Hemolytic Hypoxia

Isoimmune hemolysis (maternal anti‑Rho[D] IgG → fetal red‑cell destruction) produces

anaemia that lowers blood viscosity and vascular tone (placing higher demand on sodium

to sustain tonicity and vascular contractility) while simultaneously depriving tissues of

oxygen. The resulting hypoxia is uniquely stressful for sodium handling because the

Na⁺/K⁺‑ATPase is among the most oxygen‑hungry processes in the body; ATP shortfall

limits renal tubular Na⁺ reabsorption and impairs membrane electrogenesis, yielding

intracellular Na⁺ accumulation, cellular depolarisation, and reduced vascular contractility

even as net external sodium conservation falters. These coupled defects are read as a

baroreflex/perfusion threat and recruit a “sodium‑defence” endocrine

geometry—renin–angiotensin–aldosterone (RAAS) ↑ with concurrent AVP ↑—to restore

pressure and reclaim Na⁺ (aldosterone → ENaC), while V1a‑mediated vasoconstriction

and V2‑mediated antidiuresis stabilise perfusion. Functionally, this maps to the

Salt‑Wasting/Energetic‑Loss lane of the decapath logic (AVP ↑ / RAAS ↑ / cortisol ↑),



with the energetic bottleneck explaining why true sodium loss can coexist with high

RAAS tone under hypoxia (the manuscript’s “energetic paradox”). Under the present

assumptions, antibody cross‑reactivity to Na⁺/K⁺ channels or cognate membrane proteins

adds a second hit at the membrane, deepening ionic instability and further amplifying

RAAS/AVP drive.

Endocrine–vascular programming under chronic RAAS/AVP co‑activation. Sustained

anaemia–hypoxia entrains a high‑RAAS, high‑AVP fetal milieu that conditions both

placental and fetal vascular beds. The framework predicts receptor‑level “locking” within

the placental tree—up‑tuning of AGTR1 (AT1) and AVPR1A (V1a)—which raises

uteroplacental resistance and propagates intermittent hypoperfusion (“brain‑sparing”

patterns) to the fetus. In the fetal brain and pituitary, the same stressor profile favors

AVPR1A/AVPR1B up‑regulation (stronger V1a vasomotor/glial Ca²⁺ signalling; higher

V1b/ACTH gain), with context‑dependent AVPR2/AQP2 behaviour (initial antidiuretic

support followed by “vasopressin escape” if exposure is prolonged). Glucocorticoid

exposure will rise with illness severity but is not the primary compensator here;

nonetheless, repeated cortisol pulses and hypoxic stress are expected to depress OXTR

and to redistribute AQP4 in astrocytes, altering neurovascular coupling and osmotic

resilience—an imprinting pattern the manuscript projects for RAAS‑high or salt‑wasting

states.

Mechanistically, this profile is distinct from the true Sodium‑Switch (dilutional,

cortisol‑dominant) geometry in which AVP is suppressed, RAAS is quiet or suppressed,

and cortisol bears the load (e.g., low‑TDS/free‑water states or RAAS‑suppressed

contexts). Here, by contrast, anaemia→hypoxia forces RAAS‑upregulated sodium

defence with concomitant AVP activation. However, “tip‑over” into a cortisol‑dominant

Sodium‑Switch remains plausible when energetic failure makes aldosterone‑driven

reclamation ineffective, when placental 11β‑HSD2 is weakened (hypoxia/inflammation)

allowing greater cortisol transfer, or when co‑exposures suppress RAAS (e.g.,

phosphate/FGF‑23 axis), thereby shifting compensation toward cortisol despite ongoing

osmotic strain.

Synthesis for the manuscript. We therefore propose an “Autoimmune

Hemolysis–Hypoxia” decapath: anti‑Rho(D) IgG → fetal haemolysis/anaemia →

systemic hypoxia → ATP‑limited Na⁺/K⁺ exchange → intracellular Na⁺ gain and impaired

tubular Na⁺ reclamation → RAAS↑ + AVP↑ sodium‑defence phenotype (± cortisol as

ancillary support). This RAAS‑high track imprints a receptor signature biased toward

AVPR1A/AVPR1B (↑), context‑dependent AVPR2/AQP2 (biphasic/escape), OXTR (↓),

and placental/fetal AT1 (↑), with predicted biomarkers of renin/aldosterone↑ and

copeptin↑. By contrast with the cortisol‑dominant Sodium‑Switch (AVP↓, RAAS↓,

cortisol↑), this autoimmune route preserves a constrictor‑retentive endocrine stance until

energetic limits or RAAS‑suppressive co‑factors force a compensatory pivot. Chronic

hypoxia‑RAAS stress during sensitive windows is expected to leave durable

marks—up‑weighted AVP signalling, down‑weighted OXTR tone, and altered

AQP4‑linked neurovascular coupling—shifting autonomic set‑points (lower vagal tone,

higher sympathetic/AVP gain) and contributing to long‑term neurodevelopmental

imprinting relevant to social‑affiliative circuits in the offspring.



Compound and Convergent Pathways of Low‑Effective‑Sodium Stress

The ten Decapaths outline distinct yet connected routes by which low‑effective‑sodium

physiology can arise during sensitive windows of neurodevelopment. Each path describes a

primary initiator, from maternal or foetal vasopressin excess to true dilutional hyponatraemia,

translocational shifts, RAAS suppression with cortisol dominance, renal salt‑wasting, lithium

mimicry, and actin–osmolyte disruption. In isolation, any one of these can plausibly seed

autism‑spectrum physiology by altering endocrine set‑points and osmotic handling [S/E].

Real‑world exposure histories, however, rarely occur in isolation. Individuals often encounter

overlapping dietary, pharmacological, obstetric, infectious, and environmental inputs that

collectively magnify antidiuretic tone, reduce osmotic resilience, and entrain stress‑biased

homeostatic programmes. The Sodium‑Switch model therefore expects additive or synergistic

effects when Decapaths co‑activate, with the combined signature exceeding the effect of any

single pathway.

Mechanistic convergence across Decapaths

Convergence can occur at multiple levels of control. Consider maternal AVP excess (Path 1)

occurring alongside RAAS suppression with cortisol dominance (Path 5), a pattern seen in

hypertensive pregnancies and preeclampsia [S]. Elevated AVP increases collecting‑duct

aquaporin‑2 insertion and free‑water reabsorption, which lowers effective sodium by dilution.

Simultaneous down‑regulation of renin–angiotensin–aldosterone signalling reduces

aldosterone‑driven sodium reclamation in the distal nephron. The net effect is disproportionate

water retention relative to sodium, a compounded dilutional state. If this milieu is further

accompanied by true dilutional drivers such as low total dissolved solids intake or

hypothyroxinaemia (Path 3) [S], antidiuresis is reinforced from both ends, since thyroid

deficiency can independently raise AVP tone and impair free‑water clearance. The result is a

coherent endocrine–osmotic vector: AVP up, RAAS down, cortisol up, effective sodium down.

Another common convergence links translocational hyponatraemia (Path 4) with

cortisol‑dominant states (Path 5). Maternal hyperglycaemia produces a water shift from

intracellular to extracellular compartments, reducing measured sodium without absolute sodium

loss. The HPA axis responds to metabolic stress with cortisol release, while inflammatory

mediators such as IL‑6 and TNF‑α rise in parallel [S]. Cortisol counters insulin signalling and can

further depress RAAS activity in some contexts, recreating the low‑effective‑sodium pattern even

where total body sodium is unchanged. If phosphate load is high, for example from

processed‑food additives, mineral metabolism shifts through FGF23 and parathyroid hormone,

with downstream effects on vitamin D and potentially on RAAS and HPA set‑points [H]. The

combined signature resembles a translocational‑plus‑neuroendocrine hyponatraemia that is more

persistent than either alone.

Convergence can also be cellular. The actin–osmolyte axis (Path 8) regulates barrier integrity, cell

volume, and transporter localisation. Rho‑GTPase and cofilin pathways couple directly to ion

transporters and volume‑regulated anion channels. When environmental cytoskeletal disruptors

such as PFAS or phthalates are present [H/S], barrier tight junctions loosen and the cell’s capacity

to resist osmotic stress is reduced. If this is superimposed on systemic antidiuretic tone from AVP

excess, the brain and placenta lose buffering capacity against extracellular hypotonicity, and local

ionic microdomains become labile. One‑carbon deficits and reduced osmolyte pools, for example

lower betaine availability, further impair the osmoprotective response governed by

TonEBP/NFAT5 [S]. In this setting, even modest endocrine dilution becomes functionally larger

at the membrane and synapse.

Endocrine amplification and feedback locking



Amplification arises because stress axes share mediators and feedback nodes. IL‑6 and related

cytokines, released during maternal infection or pollutant exposure, raise AVP secretion and

sensitise corticotrophs to CRH, thereby sustaining cortisol output [S]. VEGF and anti‑angiogenic

factors shift placental perfusion and capillary permeability, which alters oncotic and hydrostatic

forces, indirectly favouring water retention. Cortisol interacts with mineralocorticoid receptors

when 11β‑HSD2 is overwhelmed, which can transiently preserve sodium reabsorption but at the

cost of further HPA activation. High cortisol reduces hypothalamic osmoreceptor sensitivity in

some models, raising the set‑point for AVP release and locking the system into a

high‑vasopressin, low‑effective‑sodium state. RAAS suppression removes a counter‑regulatory

brake, since angiotensin II and aldosterone would normally promote sodium reclamation and

stabilise extracellular tonicity. Thus, once two or more paths are engaged, the endocrine network

tends to reinforce itself through positive feedback, prolonging exposure of the foetal brain to a

hypotonic and glucocorticoid‑skewed environment.

Physiological outcomes of convergence

The developmental consequences are consistent with deeper hypo‑osmolar stress and maladaptive

receptor tuning. Prolonged AVP elevation biases signalling at AVPR1A and AVPR1B, which are

richly expressed in limbic and hypothalamic circuits that govern social behaviour, arousal, and

stress gating [S]. Cortisol excess shifts glucocorticoid and mineralocorticoid receptor balance,

altering synaptic pruning and dendritic maturation. Under concurrent actin disorganisation,

oxytocin receptor trafficking and clustering may be impaired, since OXTR localisation depends

on cytoskeletal scaffolds and membrane microdomains. In neurons and glia, hypo‑osmolar stress

promotes swelling, modulates GABAergic chloride gradients, and changes the activity of

volume‑regulated channels. In the placenta and developing blood–brain interfaces, barrier

looseness increases paracellular flux, so that ionic and small solute gradients fluctuate more with

maternal hydration and metabolic cycles. Together these effects accelerate imprinting of

stress‑biased set‑points that persist into infancy and childhood, lowering the threshold for sensory

reactivity, altering social motivation, and increasing the likelihood of heterogenous ASD

phenotypes.

Salt‑wasting phenotypes compound these outcomes by creating true sodium deficits during

critical periods. Preterm infants commonly exhibit renal sodium wasting with negative sodium

balance [S]. If they also experience high AVP due to illness or stress, their ability to correct

hyponatraemia is constrained. Add concurrent cytoskeletal stressors that weaken epithelial

junctions in the gut or lung, and trans‑tissue sodium conservation becomes even less efficient.

Such multi‑pathway activation helps to explain why the same exposure can be benign in one

infant yet consequential in another, depending on background RAAS, HPA, and cytoskeletal

context.

Clinical and environmental realism: clusters that activate multiple paths

Modern exposure bundles often enlist two or more Decapaths at once. Low mineral intake from

reverse‑osmosis or distilled water combined with high phosphate additives in processed foods is a

plausible example [H]. The water lowers total dissolved solids and favours antidiuretic responses,

while phosphate perturbs mineral hormones and may tilt RAAS and HPA set‑points. The

combined signature is low‑tonicity with endocrine reinforcement. Maternal infection paired with

acetaminophen use is another frequently encountered cluster [H]. Infection raises IL‑6 and AVP,

and fever reduction plus repeated dosing can deplete glutathione and shift redox tone, which

interacts with endocrine stress signalling. Although direct ASD associations with acetaminophen

remain debated, the combined biology maps onto an AVP‑high, cortisol‑high, osmotic‑fragile

state. Urban pollution combined with actin‑disrupting agents such as PFAS or phthalates

represents a third pattern [S/H]. Particulate matter induces cytokines, endothelial leak, and



oxidative stress, while PFAS or phthalates remodel actin and loosen tight junctions. The pair

therefore activates the Amplifier network and Path 8, lowering the barrier threshold at which

endocrine dilution translates into neural osmotic stress.

Obstetric contexts also produce convergence. Preeclampsia brings RAAS suppression, high

copeptin, and glucocorticoid skew [S]. If delivery is complicated by foetal hypoxia, a further AVP

surge is added. In preterm infants, renal salt‑wasting and hospital fluid practices intersect with

these endocrine drivers, yielding a prolonged composite hyponatraemic signature. Nutritional

factors can attenuate or accentuate convergence. Periconceptional folate and adequate choline

increase osmolyte pools and support tight‑junction integrity [S], which raises the threshold for

osmotic injury when antidiuretic tone is high. In contrast, low iron increases tissue hypoxia and

HPA activation [S], deepening the Sodium‑Switch.

Integrative conclusion

Compound activation of Decapaths provides a coherent explanation for variable autism severity

and phenotypic heterogeneity. The developmental brain is sensitive not only to the presence of a

single endocrine or osmotic stressor but to the composite signature generated when AVP, RAAS,

cortisol, osmolyte biology, and cytoskeletal integrity move together. The Sodium‑Switch

framework predicts that overlapping exposures produce larger and more persistent deviations in

effective sodium and stress signalling than any one exposure alone. It also predicts threshold

effects, where the same exposure has little impact in a robust osmolyte‑rich, high‑barrier context,

yet triggers maladaptive imprinting in a barrier‑weak, RAAS‑suppressed, AVP‑high background.

Population‑level shifts toward low‑mineral water, higher processed‑food phosphate, chronic

pollutant mixtures, and metabolic inflammation mean that such overlaps are increasingly

common. This convergence, reinforced by shared mediators such as cortisol, IL‑6, and VEGF,

sustains vasopressin elevation, impairs feedback, and constrains osmotic resilience. Taken

together, compound Decapath activation offers a mechanistic rationale for why ASD risk

accumulates across domains, why timing and co‑exposures matter, and why tailored prevention

may need to address endocrine, osmotic, and cytoskeletal defences at the same time.

19 Developmental Canalisation and Hysteresis in

Sodium–Volume Defence

The preceding sections described upstream sodium–volume threats capable of perturbing early

development. This section marks a conceptual shift: from identifying those initiating factors to

explaining how they are translated into persistent autism-associated physiology.

The central problem addressed here is not the persistence of the original insult, but the persistence

of the control architecture constructed to survive it. In what follows, we introduce the

intermediate “middle layer” linking transient neonatal sodium–volume stress to long-lasting

downstream consequences. Some mechanisms and compartments named here (renal, vascular,

endocrine, and neuroglial) are developed in detail in subsequent sections; they are introduced now

only to clarify how stabilisation decisions made early in life can shape the physiological

landscape on which later outcomes unfold.

Developmental canalisation of control architecture provides a parsimonious explanation for the

persistence of sodium–volume struggle in autism. Crucially, the initiating upstream

perturbation—whether dilutional stress or hypovolaemia—need not persist for downstream

physiology to remain durably altered.

During the neonatal period, when regulatory systems are maximally plastic and buffering capacity

is limited, stabilisation is achieved by recruiting the pathways that are most immediately

effective—often high-gain sympathetic and endocrine defences. Repeated reliance on these

pathways produces control hysteresis: sensor thresholds are reset, loop gain is amplified, and

effector allocation becomes biased toward high-cost modes of defence, including sustained

vascular tone, vasopressin–cortisol amplification, renal conservation, and splanchnic volume

redistribution.

Once established, these stabilisation biases act as a canalised attractor state. They do not

automatically revert when the original threat resolves or when resting serum sodium and blood

pressure fall within typical ranges. Instead, the system maintains normal headline values only



through elevated internal control effort, manifesting as increased metabolic demand, reduced

autonomic flexibility, and compartment-specific perfusion and ionic strain.

The resulting control architecture is determined by the interaction between the initiating upstream
