pathway and individual genetic and structural constraints, producing a heterogeneous but

mechanistically unified spectrum of sodium–volume defence phenotypes.


19.1 Effector Allocation Under a Single-Ligand

Constraint

19.1.0 Purpose and scope

The preceding section introduced developmental canalisation and control hysteresis as the

mechanism by which transient neonatal sodium–volume stress can yield persistent physiological

architecture. The next mechanistic step is effector allocation. Once a canalised controller is

operating, it must continuously distribute stabilisation burden across available physiological

systems. In this manuscript, effector allocation refers to how the work of defending effective

arterial filling and tonicity is apportioned among renal water handling, vascular tone, and

endocrine metabolic support.

This allocation problem is central to understanding why normal headline values, such as resting

serum sodium concentration and cuff blood pressure, can coexist with substantial internal control

effort. The architecture described here operates upstream of the organ-specific consequences

developed in later sections. Some downstream domains are named in this subsection to clarify

allocation logic, but their detailed mechanisms are deferred.


19.1.1 The single-ligand constraint

A fundamental architectural constraint in mammalian sodium–volume regulation is that a single

circulating ligand, arginine vasopressin (AVP), serves as a high-gain regulator across multiple

physiological subsystems. The same rise in circulating AVP can simultaneously conserve water in

the kidney, alter vascular tone, and influence pituitary output that supports adrenal cortisol

secretion. Under this constraint, the controller cannot independently tune renal, vascular, and

endocrine axes using separate ligands with fully separable dynamics and feedback loops.

Any increase in AVP therefore necessarily broadcasts to renal, vascular, and pituitary receptors.

Adjustments made to stabilise one target can modify the operating conditions of the others,

sometimes in partially compensatory ways and sometimes in ways that impose additional cost. In

control-systems terms, one signal is coupled to multiple output channels with different time



constants, saturation limits, and downstream consequences. This coupling creates unavoidable

trade-offs in stability and metabolic expenditure across compartments.


19.1.2 Minimal receptor map: V2, V1a, and V1b

The multi-effector reach of AVP is implemented through three receptor pathways that are relevant

here.

V2 receptors are expressed prominently in the kidney collecting duct. V2 activation promotes

aquaporin-2 (AQP2) trafficking to the apical membrane, increasing water reabsorption,

concentrating urine, and reducing free-water clearance. This pathway reshapes body water

balance over minutes to hours and directly influences the relationship between intake, urine

output, and serum sodium concentration.

V1a receptors are expressed in vascular smooth muscle and in select central vasomotor regions.

V1a activation increases vasomotor tone through vasoconstriction, shifting systemic vascular

resistance and influencing microvascular tone. While the pressor effects can be rapid, the

distributional consequences, including changes in regional flow heterogeneity and capillary

filtration, are context dependent and can accumulate over time.

V1b receptors are expressed on pituitary corticotrophs. V1b activation increases release of

adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH), which stimulates adrenal cortisol production. Cortisol

provides permissive vascular responsiveness and metabolic support, modifying how tissues

respond to catecholamines and influencing substrate availability during sustained control effort.

These three pathways share an upstream ligand but differ in their effector targets, temporal

dynamics, and the types of cost they impose when persistently recruited.


19.1.3 Why neonatal context matters

An adult sodium–volume perturbation is imposed on a mature controller with established

buffering capacity, structural reserves, and stabilised feedback gains. A neonatal perturbation is

imposed while thresholds, gains, and coupling strengths are still being calibrated. In early life,

renal concentrating capacity is relatively limited, vascular compliance and regional flow control

are still developing, and endocrine feedback loops are being tuned through experience-dependent

changes in sensitivity.

This developmental context alters the meaning of “what works.” A stabilisation strategy that

maintains perfusion and tonicity under neonatal constraints can do so by pushing the controller

toward parameterisations that remain feasible in early life, even if those parameterisations later

become energetically costly or poorly matched to adult buffering options. This process does not



imply intentional learning. It reflects experience-dependent parameterisation under constraint, in

which repeated recruitment of particular pathways biases receptor expression, downstream

signalling efficiency, and feedback gains. Early reliance on one AVP receptor pathway, because

alternatives are weaker or slower to mature, can become embedded as a default allocation

structure because the developing system repeatedly stabilises using that coupling.


19.1.4 Allocation trade-offs and burden placement

The controlled targets relevant to AVP allocation extend beyond serum sodium concentration and

cuff blood pressure. They include effective arterial filling, reflected in baroreceptor loading;

effective tonicity at key sensor sites, which determines transmembrane water shifts; and stability

of microvascular and cellular water–ion balance, which constrains capillary filtration, interstitial

hydration, and cell volume regulation.

Because AVP couples multiple effectors, stabilisation can be achieved through different patterns

of burden placement.

A V2-weighted allocation places greater burden on renal water handling. Stability is maintained

primarily through water conservation and urine concentration, supporting effective arterial filling

without requiring large increases in vascular resistance. The cost of this strategy is reduced

free-water clearance capacity and increased exposure to dilution pressure when intake exceeds

excretory capacity, along with sustained operational load on renal concentrating machinery. The

downstream renal and compartmental consequences are developed later.

A V1a-weighted allocation places greater burden on vascular tone. Effective arterial filling is

defended by increasing systemic resistance and microvascular tone rather than by retaining water.

The immediate benefit is rapid support of baroreceptor loading under altered volume or

distribution. The cost is paid in the vasculature: increased shear stress, altered flow heterogeneity,

and shifts in capillary filtration dynamics. In other contexts, sustained vasoconstrictive load is

associated with endothelial glycocalyx disruption and capillary rarefaction, suggesting a plausible

route by which chronic V1a dominance could impose microvascular strain even when cuff

pressure remains normal.

A V1b-weighted allocation places greater burden on endocrine support. Stability is maintained

through cortisol-mediated permissive effects on vascular responsiveness and metabolic pathways

that sustain prolonged control effort. The cost is a broader metabolic tax and endocrine spillover

effects, including durable shifts in glucose and lipid flux, which are not captured by coarse

sodium and blood pressure measurements.

Because these compensations can oppose one another, macroscopic readouts can remain within

reference ranges while internal control burden is high. For example, increased vascular resistance

can offset reduced central volume, or enhanced renal water retention can offset lower vascular

tone, with serum sodium maintained through parallel adjustments in intake, urine osmolality, and



solute handling. Normal headline values can therefore mask elevated signalling, higher effector

tone, and tighter feedback gains operating beneath the surface.


19.1.5 Stable allocation regimes and hysteresis

Repeated use of AVP as a multi-receptor allocator introduces nonlinear dynamics into the control

system. Receptor regulation, downstream effector saturation, and negative feedback reshape

effective gains over time. Sensitisation and desensitisation can differ by receptor subtype and

tissue, so repeated exposure can alter the relative effectiveness of V2, V1a, and V1b pathways

even when circulating AVP changes little.

In control-systems terms, an attractor is a self-reinforcing allocation pattern that emerges once a

particular configuration of receptor sensitivity, effector capacity, and feedback gains becomes the

easiest path for the controller to recruit under recurrent challenge. Hysteresis refers to the

persistence of such an allocation after the initiating perturbation has diminished. This framing

does not rely on oscillation alone to explain heterogeneity. While within-person variability can

occur across hydration state, posture, temperature, or illness, the emphasis here is on trait-like

allocation biases that reappear because the controller has been canalised into a particular coupling

structure.


19.1.6 Illustrative allocation regimes

To make the allocation logic concrete without asserting fixed subtypes, two illustrative regimes

can be outlined.

A V2-weighted renal conservation regime relies heavily on water retention under challenge. It

would be expected to show higher urine osmolality or lower free-water clearance for a given

perturbation. Circulating AVP may be higher, or renal sensitivity and downstream AQP2

trafficking efficiency may be increased, such that modest changes in AVP yield larger renal

effects. The principal cost is sustained management of dilution pressure and compartmental water

distribution, which will be examined in the renal and tissue-fluid sections.

A V1a/V1b-weighted vascular and endocrine support regime relies more on vasomotor tone and

cortisol-mediated metabolic buffering. Effective arterial filling is stabilised with less dependence

on water retention. Circulating AVP may be lower if receptor sensitivity is high, or higher if

upstream drive remains elevated. The principal costs are metabolic expenditure and reduced

flexibility of vascular tone, with downstream implications for regional perfusion, filtration

dynamics, and membrane-level adaptations developed later.



These regimes represent viable stabilisation solutions under constraint, but they are not equivalent

in long-term cost. Divergence in developmental outcomes is therefore expected and addressed in

subsequent sections.


19.1.7 Forward hooks

The remainder of Section 13 develops where the costs of these allocation strategies land. Renal

consequences are examined in the context of nephron endowment and pressure–natriuresis.

Central volume handling and venous return are addressed through the splanchnic reservoir. Tissue

sodium buffering is developed as a matrix-level burden. Astrocyte and perivascular water–ion

microdomains, including intermittent swelling phenomena, are treated in the neuroglial section.

Lipid and membrane remodeling are examined as slow, structural adaptations. The present

subsection supplies the allocation logic that links upstream sodium–volume stress to these

downstream domains without presupposing their detailed mechanisms.


19.2 Burden Externalisation: From Acute Allocation to

Secondary Stabilisation Layers

19.2.0 From effector allocation to secondary stabilisation



Section 13.1 described how, under a single-ligand constraint, vasopressin-mediated effector

allocation can maintain effective arterial filling and tonicity despite sodium–volume stress.

However, while these fast regulatory loops can stabilise headline variables such as serum sodium

concentration and cuff blood pressure, they cannot absorb the cumulative metabolic, signalling,

and structural costs of repeated activation indefinitely, particularly in a developing organism.

When upstream contributors impose recurrent osmotic or volume instability during periods of

developmental plasticity, the energetic and regulatory overhead required to maintain stability

through high-gain hormonal and autonomic signalling becomes increasingly constrained.

Under these conditions, stabilisation burden is progressively externalised into secondary

stabilisation layers. These layers are defined here as structural, compartmental, and tissue-level

adaptations that absorb persistent control effort when fast regulatory loops are insufficient,

metabolically costly, or approaching saturation. It is within these secondary domains that

upstream contributors to autism-associated physiology become embodied as durable biological

configurations. The system continues to defend coarse outputs successfully, but the cost of doing

so is displaced into slower, less reversible substrates.


19.2.1 Convergent upstream contributors as control stressors

A diverse set of established contributors to autism converge mechanistically as stressors on

effective circulating volume and epithelial–vascular integrity. AVP-linked dilutional stress,

whether arising from non-osmotic AVP activation or excess free-water signalling, imposes

recurrent demand for volume management. In parallel, epithelial and endothelial injury associated

with mold exposure, air pollution, and tobacco smoke compromises barrier function and increases

vascular permeability. These influences do not operate as isolated risk factors but as convergent

challenges that destabilise effective circulating volume and increase reliance on high-gain

AVP-mediated control.

Critically, these contributors do not need to be continuous to exert lasting effects. Acting during

periods when regulatory architecture is still being established, they impose a physiological load

that necessitates a shift in stabilisation strategy. Once burden has been displaced into secondary

layers, later normalisation of exposure does not guarantee reversal of the structural or

receptor-level adaptations that were engaged to preserve stability.


19.2.2 The insufficiency of fast control during development

Fast regulatory loops, including AVP-mediated antidiuresis, acute vasomotor adjustments, and

short-term endocrine amplification, are effective over short to medium timescales. However,

repeated recruitment of these systems during development carries cumulative energetic cost and

increases the risk of signalling saturation and proportionality loss. Maintaining steep ionic



gradients, sustaining vasomotor tone, and coordinating endocrine support require substantial

metabolic investment and precise coupling between sensors and effectors.

When such loops are recruited repeatedly during critical developmental windows, the system

increasingly offloads stabilisation demands into slower, structural compartments. This represents

a redistribution of control burden from dynamic physiological adjustments to fixed anatomical,

biochemical, and receptor-level configurations. The resulting architecture is less flexible but more

tolerant of ongoing instability, allowing headline variables to remain within viable ranges at the

expense of increased internal load.


19.2.3 Secondary stabilisation layers

Layer A: Tissue sodium buffering and matrix-level capacitance

One secondary stabilisation layer involves partitioning sodium into extracellular matrix

components, particularly through glycosaminoglycan-based non-osmotic binding. This

matrix-level capacitance allows plasma sodium concentration to be buffered without obligate

water retention, stabilising tonicity despite fluctuations in intake or renal handling. The cost of

this strategy is paid through increased demands on matrix synthesis, lymphatic transport required

for matrix turnover, and altered immune–endothelial coupling within the interstitium. While

effective at the plasma level, this adaptation increases structural density and metabolic

maintenance costs within connective tissues.


Layer B: Vascular and epithelial remodelling with endocrine decoupling under

inflammatory load

A second secondary stabilisation layer involves remodelling at epithelial and vascular interfaces.

When permeability and barrier integrity are repeatedly strained, effective circulating volume

becomes harder to stabilise through fast signalling alone, even when resting serum sodium and

blood pressure remain within typical ranges. Endothelial glycocalyx integrity, tight-junction

maintenance, and microvascular tone jointly determine intravascular retention of fluid and solute.

Developmental stress at these interfaces does not necessarily produce a persistent leak detectable

on resting measurements. Rather, it shifts the control burden required to maintain stability,

demanding greater vasomotor work to preserve effective arterial filling, tighter management of

capillary filtration, and increased microvascular flow heterogeneity as different vascular beds

adopt distinct tone and permeability constraints. Stabilisation succeeds at a coarse scale, but the

load is externalised into barrier architecture that is slower to reverse than a hormonal pulse.

Within this vascular–epithelial layer, inflammation can also distort endocrine proportionality in a

manner that is particularly destabilising when baseline renin is low. In established physiology,

renin output reflects renal perfusion pressure, tubular sodium delivery, and sympathetic drive,



while downstream mineralocorticoid effect depends on both circulating renin and tissue-level

responsiveness to angiotensin II and aldosterone. Cytokine-linked inflammatory states can flatten

or suppress renin output through haemodynamic expansion signals and direct signalling effects,

while simultaneously amplifying downstream angiotensin II and aldosterone effects via

renin-independent or stress-coupled pathways.

When baseline renin is already low, this distortion is magnified. Relatively modest inflammatory

inputs can yield disproportionately large downstream mineralocorticoid exposure, effectively

decoupling effector activation from true sodium–volume demand. Under such conditions,

mineralocorticoid receptors may experience sustained stimulation that is poorly informative about

physiological need. A plausible developmental extension is that prolonged exposure drives

durable changes in mineralocorticoid receptor availability and effective gain, including reduced

receptor expression, altered nuclear trafficking, shifts in co-regulator balance, or changes in

11β-hydroxysteroid dehydrogenase type 2 support that blunt transcriptional output. These

changes are protective at the cellular level but destabilising at the system level.

If inflammatory drive later resolves, renin output may remain blunted in low-renin architectures

and mineralocorticoid receptor responsiveness reduced. Aldosterone-mediated sodium

conservation can then become functionally insufficient without producing overt electrolyte

abnormalities at rest, because other stabilisation routes continue to hold serum sodium and blood

pressure within broad bounds. Under these conditions, sodium–volume defence is increasingly

routed through glucocorticoid pathways. Glucocorticoids retain the capacity to support vascular

tone and influence sodium handling, but they do so with lower ionic specificity and higher

systemic metabolic and developmental cost. This sequence provides a mechanistically coherent

route by which transient inflammatory insults during development can produce persistent cortisol

dependence and sodium–volume fragility without ongoing inflammation.


Layer C: Neuroglial water–ion microdomain management

A third secondary stabilisation layer involves local precision management of water and ions

within the central nervous system. Astrocytes and perivascular spaces buffer neuronal

microenvironments against systemic osmotic and volume instability, preserving excitability and

synaptic function. This buffering, however, carries energetic and structural demands. Sustained

reliance on this layer can generate intermittent astrocytic swelling, clearance strain, or altered

perivascular dynamics, producing local microdomain instability even when systemic sodium and

blood pressure appear normal.


Layer D: Membrane and lipid remodelling

A fourth secondary stabilisation layer involves structural modification of cellular membranes,

particularly through altered lipid composition and cholesterol content. Increased membrane order

and lipid packing reduce passive transmembrane water flux, stabilising cell volume under osmotic



stress. This strategy trades metabolic resources for microenvironment stability, diverting energy

toward lipid synthesis and membrane maintenance while reducing reliance on active ion pumping

to counter osmotic gradients. The adaptation is slow to reverse and embeds stabilisation cost at

the structural level.


19.2.4 The emergence of autism-associated physiology

The recruitment of secondary stabilisation layers provides a mechanistic basis for the emergence

of autism-associated physiology. These adaptations are slow to reverse and interact with

concurrent development of the brain, vasculature, connective tissue, and kidney. By altering the

timing, precision, and flexibility of physiological systems, they embed the history of upstream

sodium–volume and inflammatory stress into the organism’s biological fabric. Autism-associated

physiology emerges not because control mechanisms fail, but because they succeed by

redistributing stabilisation burden into developing tissues. The resulting configuration reflects the

structural cost of maintaining stability under constraint, setting the stage for the neural, sensory,

and metabolic features examined in subsequent sections.


19.2.5 Heterogeneity as a function of burden placement

Heterogeneity in autism-associated physiology follows naturally from this framework. Different

combinations of upstream contributors, interacting with individual genetic, structural, and

developmental constraints, bias the system toward particular secondary stabilisation layers. One

trajectory may emphasise matrix-level sodium buffering, another membrane lipid remodelling,

another vascular–epithelial adaptation, and another neuroglial microdomain management. These

differences yield distinct tissue signatures, endocrine profiles, and AVP patterns without invoking

separate disease entities or random noise. They represent alternative solutions adopted by the

same control system in response to shared stability demands.


19.2.6 Forward handoff

Subsequent sections examine the specific mechanisms governing each secondary stabilisation

layer. Renal consequences are developed through nephron endowment and pressure–natriuresis.

Central volume handling and venous return are addressed via the splanchnic reservoir. Neuroglial

microdomains and perivascular dynamics are examined in detail, as are lipid and membrane

adaptations. Together, these sections demonstrate how successful defence of sodium and volume

can drive the structural and functional reorganisation characteristic of the phenotype, even when

resting serum sodium and blood pressure appear unremarkable.



Section 20 — Downstream Expression of Canalised

Sodium–Volume Defence

This section collates downstream, “sticky” developmental trade-offs that can follow DecaPath

engagement of the Sodium-Struggle program during sensitive windows. The features below are

framed as adaptive survival trade-offs induced by DecaPath engagement (brain-sparing

hemodynamics, renal and placental sacrifice, oxygen-limited sodium handling), not as baseline

vulnerabilities.


20.1 Roadmap: downstream consequences ordered by developmental

permanence

Sections 13 onward describe downstream developmental reconfiguration that can follow the

engagement of anti-hyponatraemia and anti-hypoperfusion compensation during sensitive

windows. For readability, these downstream sections are ordered by degree of developmental

permanence (“stickiness”): from the most structurally fixed changes (renal architecture), to

intermediate architectural plasticity (adrenal zonation and steroid routing), to more plastic

signalling biases (receptor and signalling imprinting), and finally to functional regulatory

set-points that remain the most context-dependent and reversible (autonomic regulation).

●​ Section 21 Nephron endowment, renal reserve, brain-sparing haemodynamics, and

the renin paradox (most permanent)

●​ Section 22 Adrenal zonation (ZG, ZF, ZR), developmental plasticity, and steroid

routing (intermediate plasticity)

●​ Section 23 Membrane defence and sterol supply, reconciling low and high

cholesterol in ASD (plastic)

●​ Section 24 Receptor and signalling imprinting, gain, thresholds, overshoot–recoil,

discordance, and oscillation (plastic)


●​ Section 25 Vascular tree imprinting: calibre bias, microvascular

density, and perfusion constraint

●​ Section 26 Astrocytic osmotic imprinting: volume regulation, osmolyte debt, and loss

of neurovascular precision (conditionally plastic)

●​ Section 27 Autonomic (vagal) regulation and time-window vulnerability (most

plastic, functional)



21 Nephron endowment, renal reserve, brain-sparing

haemodynamics, and the renin paradox

21.1 Physiological software versus anatomical hardware

The receptor-level and endocrine adaptations emphasized throughout this hypothesis—AT1 gain

changes, AVPR1A/V1a partitioning, adrenal zonation drift (ZG→ZF steroid routing), and

autonomic recalibration—are best conceptualized as software. These processes are

state-dependent, developmentally plastic, and at least theoretically modifiable across the lifespan.

Their very malleability, however, implies a deeper constraint: software adaptations cannot exceed

the capacity of the hardware on which they run.

Software can reallocate workload and keep outputs “looking normal.” It cannot restore missing

renal hardware; it can only mask reduced capacity by driving the remaining units harder and

spending reserve.

What matters mechanistically—and what must be stated upfront—is that the fetus does not “lose”

nephron endowment randomly, nor does it primarily “fail” to build nephrons as a passive defect.

In the Sodium-Struggle framework, nephron endowment is spent. The anti-hyponatraemia

programme (the integrated endocrine–autonomic response that prevents a fall in effective sodium

and preserves arterial pressure) is a survival mode whose core requirements are fundamentally

incompatible with the requirements of nephrogenesis. This is an active physiological trade-off:

future renal reserve is exchanged for immediate perfusion security. You cannot simultaneously

run an emergency perfusion-triage state and keep the renal “construction site” open.

Two predictable misunderstandings can be pre-empted here. First, this section is not arguing that

affected individuals are “in renal failure” during childhood, or that autism is a disguised chronic

kidney disease diagnosis. The claim is about reserve: a kidney can deliver apparently adequate

baseline homeostasis with fewer nephrons, but it does so with a narrowed safety margin. Second,

“normal labs” do not neutralize the claim: a normal serum creatinine and an apparently normal

whole-kidney GFR do not imply normal nephron endowment. They often reflect single-nephron

hyperfiltration—compensation that preserves whole-organ outputs while quietly reducing reserve.

This “normal labs” trap is unpacked in detail in Section 14.3, but it belongs in the reader’s mental

frame from the start.

This section also introduces, early and explicitly, the renin paradox in its developmental form,

because it is otherwise easy to misread the later discussion as a contradiction. Renin–angiotensin

signalling is required for the kidney to branch and build (growth-range RAAS), yet the

anti-hyponatraemia state drives angiotensin II into pressor amplitude—an emergency range that

halts branching and closes the construction programme. In other words, the same system that is

obligate for nephrogenesis in a growth-permissive context becomes anti-nephrogenic when driven

into survival gain.



Nephrogenesis—ureteric bud branching, nephron induction, and the expansion of nephron

progenitors—depends on a narrow “Goldilocks” physiology: generous renal perfusion, low

vascular resistance into the metanephros, stable oxygen delivery, and morphogenic signalling that

remains developmental rather than pressor. Anti-hyponatraemia physiology deliberately breaks

each of those conditions, because its priority is immediate cerebral perfusion.


21.1.1 Brain-sparing haemodynamics close the renal construction site

The fetal anti-hyponatraemia response is, in practice, a response to threatened effective

circulating volume and threatened pressure. The organism treats low-effective-sodium states as an

existential perfusion risk and executes a classic brain-and-heart prioritization: intense sympathetic

activation and peripheral vasoconstriction redistribute flow toward the brain and myocardium.

Anatomically, this “brain-sparing” pattern is not a vague stress metaphor. It is a flow-diversion

architecture in which cerebral inflow is protected (carotid prioritization), while resistance rises in

downstream territories—including the descending aorta distribution—so that organs supplied

downstream (including the kidneys) receive less inflow when perfusion must be rationed.

In this triage hierarchy, the developing kidney is relatively expendable for immediate survival

because the placenta carries the dominant clearance and buffering workload in utero, and

short-term fetal viability is not limited by renal excretion capacity.

The casualty is straightforward and physical: sympathetic vasoconstriction constricts the renal

arteries and intrarenal resistance vessels, reducing perfusion of the nephrogenic cortex and the

ureteric bud–metanephric mesenchyme interface. In other words, the fetus preserves cerebral

perfusion by restricting inflow to the kidney’s growth zone—and that inflow is physiologically

downstream of the protected cerebral circulation. That restriction is not a vague “stress” signal; it

is a haemodynamic shutdown of the supply line. Branching morphogenesis is an

energy-intensive, oxygen-dependent construction process. When renal blood flow is clamped

down, the nephrogenic zone becomes ischemic, and nephron induction slows or arrests because

the tissue is being asked to build while simultaneously being deprived of oxygen and substrates.


21.1.2 The renin–angiotensin paradox rewrites the blueprint

Angiotensin II is not merely a vasoconstrictor in development; under physiological concentrations

and normal redox conditions, it is also a trophic morphogen that supports renal patterning and

ureteric bud branching. In a calm developmental state, angiotensin signalling helps instruct the

kidney to branch, differentiate, and build. Put plainly, the blueprint requires RAAS tone in a

growth-compatible range.

The paradox is that the anti-hyponatraemia state demands angiotensin II at pressor amplitude.

When effective sodium or pressure is threatened, the fetal system escalates RAAS drive to defend

vascular tone. In that high-gain context—especially when paired with the oxidative stress created



by renal ischemia—angiotensin II changes functional identity. It shifts from a developmental

guidance cue to a vasoconstrictive, pro-inflammatory effector that amplifies resistance, increases

oxidative signalling, and suppresses the very branching programme it would normally support.

In short, the same ligand that can act as a growth factor in the right context becomes an

anti-growth signal when its concentration is driven into an emergency range and the tissue

environment is oxidant and hypoperfused. The blueprint is not absent; it is overwritten by a

survival instruction set: constrict, defend pressure, and tolerate collateral damage.


21.1.3 Metabolic trade-offs make nephrogenesis unaffordable

Anti-hyponatraemia is not just hormonal; it is energetically expensive. Fighting dilution and

defending effective sodium requires sustained ATP investment in ion gradients, membrane

pumps, and active transport programmes. That ATP demand rises precisely when oxygen delivery

is being restricted by the same brain-sparing vasoconstriction described above.

Nephrogenesis, meanwhile, is a biosynthetic enterprise: cell-cycle progression, replication,

protein synthesis, and coordinated morphogenesis. These processes compete directly with the

energy budget required for high-gain ion retention and gradient maintenance. Under

Sodium-Struggle conditions, the fetus is forced into a metabolic allocation decision: spend ATP to

keep membranes and perfusion viable now, or spend ATP to proliferate nephron progenitors for a

future organ reserve.

The anti-hyponatraemia programme resolves that competition in the only way compatible with

immediate survival—by prioritizing ion-homeostasis work and pressure defence, thereby

imposing a functional stop-code on growth.

Taken together, these three mechanisms make the central claim of this section precise:

anti-hyponatraemia physiology arrests nephrogenesis because the very measures that preserve

cerebral perfusion—sympathetic shunting, high-gain RAAS pressor tone, and ATP diversion into

ion retention—simultaneously remove the oxygen, haemodynamic openness, and

growth-permissive signalling environment required for ureteric bud branching and nephron

induction. The kidney’s developmental programme is not merely sensitive to adversity; it is

specifically incompatible with the emergency state required to prevent sodium-linked perfusion

failure.

This incompatibility is what makes nephron endowment true hardware. In humans, nephrogenesis

is finite and time-locked: ureteric bud branching and nephron induction proceed during

mid-to-late gestation and terminate by approximately gestational weeks 34–36. After this window

closes, new nephrons are not generated under normal human developmental biology. Any episode

that repeatedly or persistently engages brain-sparing vasoconstriction, converts angiotensin

signalling into pressor or inflammatory mode, and diverts energy away from proliferation



therefore does not merely perturb renal function; it fixes a lower structural ceiling on renal

capacity by reducing the number of filtration units constructed.

Prematurity as construction interruption (mechanistic support, not proof).​

Because nephrogenesis normally continues into late gestation, preterm birth can be understood

mechanistically as delivery occurring while the kidney is still under construction. The transition

from the intrauterine environment to extrauterine physiology alters perfusion patterns, oxygen

exposure, and endocrine context during a window when branching and induction would otherwise

still be ongoing. Prematurity is also associated with increased ASD prevalence. This temporal

alignment strengthens the plausibility of a pathway in which interrupted or constrained

nephrogenesis contributes to later vulnerability, but it is not presented here as definitive proof of

causation.

Accordingly, congenital oligonephronia (low nephron count) is not a regulatory downshift that

can later be corrected by receptor tuning or hormonal adjustment. It is a developmentally

constrained reduction in filtration surface area, tubular transport capacity, and renal reserve. A

kidney built with fewer nephrons can still maintain homeostasis—often with apparently normal

baseline indices—but only by running its control systems at higher gain: leaning harder on

RAAS, sympathetic tone, vasopressin-linked strategies, and pressure-dependent natriuresis across

the life course. In engineering terms, nephron number defines engine displacement. A smaller

engine can be kept functional through aggressive tuning, but the cost is chronic operating stress

and reduced headroom—a narrowed safety margin rather than baseline failure. The key

interpretive conclusion for this manuscript is therefore not that autism has small kidneys as a

disconnected defect, but that—within this hypothesis—a low nephron count is the most durable

footprint of fetal survival triage: the fetus preserved immediate cerebral perfusion by sacrificing

future renal reserve. Section 13.2 then extends this logic by mapping how deviations in sodium or

perfusion state across gestation constrain nephron endowment and thereby set the lifelong gain

requirements of the Sodium-Switch.


21.2 A U-shaped vulnerability curve: why both scarcity and abundance

reduce nephron number

A common misconception is that nephron loss should arise only from fetal

deprivation—hypovolemia, hypoxia, placental insufficiency—while sodium abundance ought to

be protective. Renal developmental physiology demonstrates the opposite. Nephron endowment

follows a classic U-shaped vulnerability curve with respect to sodium availability and perfusion:

both extremes reduce nephron number, but by disrupting different requirements of kidney

construction.

Nephrogenesis depends on two non-substitutable inputs.

The blueprint (instructional signal).​

Angiotensin II signalling via AT1 receptors is an obligate trophic driver of ureteric bud branching



morphogenesis and nephron induction. In fetal life, angiotensin II functions not merely as a

vasoconstrictor but as a developmental growth signal guiding renal patterning.

The bricks (metabolic supply).​

Adequate renal perfusion and oxygen delivery are required to meet the energetic cost of

branching morphogenesis and epithelial differentiation. Growth cannot proceed in the absence of

oxygen, regardless of signalling strength.

Violation of either requirement arrests nephrogenesis.


21.2.1 Scarcity and hypoxia: nephron loss via haemodynamic triage

Under conditions of uteroplacental insufficiency, chronic fetal hypoxia, or effective circulating

volume stress, the fetus engages haemodynamic triage. Blood flow is preferentially redistributed

to the brain and heart, while renal perfusion is reduced. This brain-sparing response preserves

immediate survival at the cost of renal development. Importantly, this is implemented through

concrete flow diversion: the protected cerebral circulation is prioritized, while resistance rises in

downstream beds distributed through the descending aorta supply, so renal inflow to the

nephrogenic cortex falls.

In these states, RAAS activation is often robust. However, at high concentrations and in

conjunction with sympathetic co-activation, angiotensin II’s dominant effect becomes distributive

and vasoconstrictive rather than trophic. The renal arterial bed is constricted, oxygen delivery

falls, and branching morphogenesis arrests because the construction site is effectively closed.

Experimental models of chronic fetal hypoxia and growth restriction demonstrate marked

reductions in glomerular number through precisely this mechanism, with downregulation of

angiogenic and growth pathways secondary to ischemia.

Thus, in scarcity states, the blueprint may be present or excessive, but the bricks are unavailable.


21.2.2 Abundance paradox: nephron loss via suppression of the growth

signal

At the opposite extreme, maternal hypernatremia or high salt intake can also reduce nephron

endowment—but by eliminating the blueprint rather than the supply. Expanded volume and

sodium abundance suppress fetal renin and angiotensin II signalling. While appropriate for

short-term sodium balance, this suppression is developmentally maladaptive: without sufficient

AT1-mediated trophic signalling, ureteric bud branching fails to proceed. Put differently, renin

suppression can look reassuring from a balance perspective while being anti-growth from a

construction perspective, because the kidney requires a growth-range RAAS signal to receive the

command to build.

Animal models demonstrate that both high-salt and low-salt maternal diets result in offspring with

reduced glomerular number, confirming the U-shaped relationship between sodium availability



and nephrogenesis. In high-salt conditions, perfusion is adequate, but the instructional signal is

silenced, and the kidney simply does not receive the command to build.


21.2.3 Convergent outcome, distinct mechanisms

The unifying point is that scarcity and abundance converge on the same endpoint—congenital

oligonephronia—by breaking different components of the nephrogenic equation:

●​ Scarcity or hypoxia: blueprint intact, bricks missing → ischemic arrest of branching​

●​ Abundance or high salt: bricks present, blueprint missing → signalling arrest of

branching​


Nephron endowment therefore requires a narrow Goldilocks window in which trophic signalling

and perfusion are simultaneously sufficient. Deviations in either direction impose a structurally

fixed hardware deficit that constrains lifelong sodium handling.


21.3 Compensatory hypertrophy and the “large nephron” trap

Congenital oligonephronia is frequently clinically silent in childhood because the kidney

compensates through single-nephron hyperfiltration. Whole-kidney glomerular filtration rate is

preserved by increasing filtration pressure and flow through each remaining nephron. This is the

first place the normal-labs trap becomes operational: serum creatinine and an apparently normal

eGFR can be the signature of compensation, not evidence of intact nephron endowment, because

the measured whole-organ output can be maintained by pushing each nephron closer to its

mechanical and metabolic limits. Morphologically, this manifests as glomerulomegaly and

tubular hypertrophy—large nephrons attempting to carry the workload of a reduced nephron

population.

This adaptation is achieved primarily through efferent arteriolar constriction, mediated by

intrarenal angiotensin II/AT1 signalling. The resulting rise in intraglomerular pressure maintains

filtration but imposes chronic mechanical and metabolic stress on the filtration barrier. In

engineering terms, the system is red-lining the engine: performance is preserved, but reserve is

consumed.

Two long-term consequences follow.

Accelerated renal ageing.​

Sustained hyperfiltration promotes podocyte injury, mesangial expansion, and progressive

glomerulosclerosis. What appears as resilience in youth becomes vulnerability later, with early

decline in renal reserve.



Systemic pressure bias.​

The renal survival strategy is not organ-confined. Persistent RAAS and sympathetic activation

remodel resistance vessels system-wide, shifting the pressure–natriuresis relationship toward

higher operating pressures.

This configuration produces the renin paradox in its clinically confusing form: circulating renin

may be suppressed by volume expansion, even as intrarenal angiotensin II tone remains high to

defend single-nephron filtration. Low plasma renin therefore does not imply a low-RAAS kidney

in oligonephronic physiology; it may indicate a high-gain intrarenal RAAS masked by systemic

feedback. The reader should now see how the two levels of paradox align rather than conflict.


21.4 Copy-number syndromes as hard-wired evidence for a shared

kidney–brain–metabolic root

21.4.1 The renal blind spot in neurodevelopmental disorders

Direct nephron counts have not yet been performed in post-mortem ASD cohorts. Congenital

oligonephronia in autism therefore remains a hypothesis rather than a confirmed histologic

finding. However, this absence reflects a broader renal blind spot in neuropsychiatric research

rather than a lack of biological plausibility. Kidneys are rarely examined in psychiatric autopsy

studies, and nephron quantification is technically demanding.

Importantly, ASD shares its strongest environmental risk architecture—prematurity, intrauterine

growth restriction, maternal inflammation, placental insufficiency—with conditions in which

reduced nephron endowment is well documented. The hypothesis therefore does not require a

novel renal insult unique to autism; it requires only repeated engagement of fetal triage

physiology during nephrogenesis.


21.4.2 Copy-number syndromes that collapse kidney, brain, and

metabolism into a single lesion (continued)

Copy-number variants provide a powerful natural experiment because a single genetic lesion

produces a reproducible multi-organ phenotype. They are invoked here to demonstrate biological

coupling—kidney–brain–metabolic co-patterning—rather than to claim that idiopathic autism is

genetically determined by renal genes.

22q11.2 deletion syndrome establishes the kidney–psychosis link. In addition to cardiac and

craniofacial features, this deletion frequently produces congenital renal and urinary tract

anomalies, including renal hypoplasia. Simultaneously, it confers one of the strongest known

genetic risks for schizophrenia and a markedly elevated risk for autism. A single lesion thus

destabilizes renal development and higher-order neuropsychiatric trajectories, validating

kidney–brain coupling as a biological reality.



17q12 deletion syndrome (HNF1B) provides the platinum link that closes the metabolic loop.

This copy-number variant causes Renal Cysts and Diabetes (RCAD) syndrome, explicitly uniting

structural renal underdevelopment with early-onset diabetes. On the hardware side, 17q12

deletion produces renal dysplasia and reduced nephron endowment. On the metabolic side, it

causes MODY-like diabetes, embedding insulin dysregulation directly into the same lesion that

compromises renal architecture. Critically, carriers of the 17q12 deletion also exhibit a markedly

elevated risk of autism and schizophrenia. This single genomic event therefore generates the full

Autism + Kidney + Metabolic Disease phenotype predicted by the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis,

demonstrating that these domains can arise from a common upstream developmental program

rather than secondary comorbidity.

Complementary evidence comes from TSHZ3, a developmental regulator whose disruption links

autism-relevant neurodevelopmental phenotypes with congenital urinary tract and renal

abnormalities. While narrower in scope than large copy-number variants, TSHZ3 illustrates the

same principle: perturbation of shared patterning pathways can simultaneously affect cortical

circuits and renal endowment.

Together, 22q11.2, 17q12/HNF1B, and TSHZ3 form a hierarchy of genetic evidence supporting a

unified kidney–brain–metabolic cluster anchored in developmental renal constraint.


21.5 Long-term consequence: a permanently salt-sensitive,

pressure-dependent system

A reduced nephron endowment shifts the pressure–natriuresis relationship. Sodium balance

becomes increasingly dependent on higher arterial pressure and stronger neurohormonal drive.

The organism is rendered salt-sensitive not by preference but by physics: fewer nephrons require

more pressure per nephron to excrete the same sodium load.

Within this framework, the high-RAAS, high-sympathetic phenotype observed in autism is

reinterpreted as a structural necessity. Receptor imprinting, adrenal steroid routing, and

autonomic amplification represent compensatory software layers erected atop constrained renal

hardware. The cost is a globally elevated vasomotor set-point that compromises microvascular

flexibility, including in cerebral circulation, particularly under postural or interoceptive stress.


21.6 The heritable deficit: intergenerational amplification of renal

hardware debt

Crucially, nephron endowment is heritable via the intrauterine environment, even in the absence

of direct genetic transmission. Mothers with reduced renal reserve or salt-sensitive physiology are

predisposed to gestational haemodynamic maladaptation, including exaggerated RAAS

activation, impaired volume buffering, and placental hypoperfusion. These conditions increase



the likelihood of fetal hypoxia and haemodynamic triage during nephrogenesis, further arresting

nephron formation in the offspring.

This establishes a feed-forward cycle: each generation inherits not only genes, but a progressively

constrained renal substrate shaped by maternal physiology. The result is a cumulative hardware

debt—a non-genetic, intergenerational decline in renal capacity that amplifies sodium struggle,

pressure dependence, and neurodevelopmental vulnerability over time.

This mechanism underpins the Intergenerational Sodium Struggle (Section [X]) and provides a

biophysical explanation for the rising prevalence and familial clustering of autism in the modern

era. What appears as increasing genetic penetrance may, in part, reflect the silent accumulation of

developmentally constrained renal limits across generations.

A reduced nephron endowment is a developmentally fixed outcome of maternal–fetal sodium and

perfusion struggle during nephrogenesis, not a postnatal insult and not a progressive renal disease.

Once nephrogenesis closes, the kidney enters life with a structurally constrained filtration surface

and transport capacity. Homeostasis can still be maintained, often effectively—and often with

“normal” creatinine or eGFR—but it must be maintained with higher regulatory gain and reduced

reserve. The consequence is therefore a narrowed safety margin rather than chronic

hypoperfusion or tissue injury: baseline function may appear adequate, yet the system operates

closer to constraint across the life course. In this framework, downstream vulnerability reflects

the physics of limited reserve established during a specific prenatal window, not damage accruing

after birth.


22 Adrenal Zonation (ZG, ZF, ZR), Developmental Plasticity,

and Steroid Routing in the Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis of

Autism

22.1 Adrenal zonation as a coordinated, competitive system

The adrenal cortex is organised into three steroidogenic zones that partition endocrine tasks

essential for sodium conservation and perfusion stability. The zona glomerulosa (ZG) is

specialised for mineralocorticoid output (principally aldosterone) and therefore for

sodium-retentive, volume-supportive signalling. The zona fasciculata (ZF) is the primary source

of glucocorticoids (principally cortisol), which provide rapid, multi-system support for vascular

tone, fuel mobilisation, and pressure maintenance under threat. The zona reticularis (ZR)

produces adrenal androgens, notably dehydroepiandrosterone (DHEA) and DHEA sulphate

(DHEA-S), which contribute to longer-horizon anabolic, neurosteroid, and buffering functions.

Within the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis, the key point is not simply that these zones differ, but

that they are metabolically coupled and developmentally programmable. They draw from shared

precursor pools and operate under shared constraints: when sodium pressure and perfusion are

repeatedly threatened during development, the endocrine system must repeatedly decide which

output is most immediately protective. In this framework, repeated challenges to effective sodium

pressure—even with normal serum sodium at many timepoints—evoke anti-hyponatraemia and



anti-hypoperfusion compensation that is biased toward cortisol-mediated pressure support. The

consequence is not merely altered hormone levels in the moment, but the possibility of altered

zonal weighting, precursor routing, and reserve capacity across the lifespan.


22.2 Developmental plasticity of adrenal zonation: ZG→ZF

conversion and limited reversibility

A critical feature of adrenal architecture—often underemphasised in cross-sectional

endocrine discussions—is that zonation is not fixed at birth. Histologically, CYP11B2

marks aldosterone-producing glomerulosa identity, whereas CYP11B1 marks

cortisol-producing fasciculata identity. Experimental and lineage-based work supports

the concept that ZG cells can convert into ZF-like cells as part of adrenal remodelling,

particularly when physiological demand persistently favours glucocorticoid production.

Importantly, this conversion is directionally biased: once cells adopt a ZF phenotype,

reversion to a ZG phenotype appears limited, especially outside early developmental

windows when the gland’s organisational program is still being established.

This asymmetry has direct relevance to the Sodium-Struggle framework because it makes

developmental timing—not simply “high cortisol” as a magnitude—central to long-term

outcome. If early life is characterised by recurrent endocrine states that repeatedly recruit

cortisol to defend effective sodium pressure and perfusion, then the adrenal is repeatedly

“taught” that ZF expansion and glucocorticoid readiness are high priority. Under such

conditions, ZG→ZF conversion may act as a structural mechanism by which a repeatedly

invoked cortisol-dominant solution becomes embedded in tissue geometry.

In this section’s terms, ZG identity should be treated as actively maintained rather than

passively present. Aldosterone-producing cell clusters (APCCs) support this view,

suggesting that the aldosterone-producing compartment is dynamically remodelled

rather than permanently fixed across life. Mineralocorticoid competence is sustained not

only by RAAS/angiotensin-II signalling but also by potassium tone, which provides a

continuous depolarising input to ZG cells. Potassium therefore functions here as more

than an acute aldosterone stimulus: it is a tonic electrophysiological and

transcription-supportive signal that helps stabilise ZG-specific programs (including

calcium-linked signalling and CYP11B2 expression). A sustained developmental bias

toward low potassium tone is therefore expected to weaken ZG-specific maintenance

signals and favour drift toward ZF-like phenotypes, particularly when the gland is

simultaneously incentivised (by repeated sodium-perfusion threat) to expand

cortisol-ready capacity.

Two implications follow:

● Early cortisol dominance can be structurally self-reinforcing. A gland biased toward ZF

capacity is better able to mount cortisol responses, making cortisol substitution easier to recruit in

subsequent sodium–perfusion challenges. This can stabilise a “cortisol-first” posture even if the

initiating triggers later diminish.



● ZG capacity may be constrained in a way that persists. Because ZG is both the

mineralocorticoid output zone and a source pool for centripetal remodelling, a sustained early-life

tilt toward ZF may reduce the system’s ability to re-expand mineralocorticoid-oriented

architecture later, particularly if the period of maximal plasticity has passed.

Importantly, the reversibility of ZG→ZF conversion appears constrained not only by

developmental timing but by the persistence of the signals that maintain ZG identity.

Because potassium provides a continuous depolarising and transcription-supportive input to

ZG cells, sustained potassium deficiency during development may actively bias cells away

from a mineralocorticoid phenotype. Once ZG-specific programs are silenced under

low-potassium conditions, subsequent restoration of potassium later in life may be

insufficient to fully re-establish ZG identity, particularly after chromatin and lineage

commitments have stabilised. Within the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis, low potassium tone

thus functions as a plausible ratchet variable: it can accelerate directional ZG→ZF drift during

sensitive windows and limit later recovery of mineralocorticoid capacity even if sodium

status partially normalises.

Within this framework, these changes are not framed as adrenal “damage,” but as

developmentally rational prioritisation under perceived threat: when effective sodium pressure is

fragile, cortisol is repeatedly recruited as a pressure-support hormone, and the gland’s structure

may drift toward the output that is repeatedly required to keep perfusion stable.


22.3 Cortisol dominance and precursor competition at the

17-hydroxypregnenolone branch point

Even without structural remodelling, the ZF and ZR are functionally coupled through precursor

competition, particularly at the 17-hydroxypregnenolone branch point, where upstream steroid

flux can be routed toward cortisol synthesis or toward adrenal androgen production. In a state of

recurrent pressure-support demand, increased drive through the glucocorticoid pathway can bias

precursor allocation toward cortisol, leaving proportionally less substrate available for DHEA

synthesis.

Within the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis, this competition is not interpreted as a generic “stress

response” but as a specific anti-hyponatraemia / anti-hypoperfusion solution: when effective

sodium pressure is challenged, cortisol provides fast, integrative support for maintaining

perfusion (via permissive effects on vasoconstrictor responsiveness, metabolic mobilisation, and

coordinated cardiovascular readiness). Under repeated recruitment, cortisol can therefore become

the dominant “routing sink” for shared precursor traffic.

Two forms of ZR suppression then become mechanistically plausible and need to be

distinguished:

● Functional ZR suppression (routing-level): DHEA/DHEA-S output is reduced because shared

precursors are preferentially diverted into cortisol synthesis, particularly when upstream drive is

high and cortisol demand is repeatedly prioritised.



● Structural ZR suppression (architecture-level): If early-life

ZG→ZF conversion (and broader ZF expansion) becomes locked in, the

gland’s capacity for sustained androgen production may be

constrained by zonal proportion and maturation state. In this case,

reduced DHEA-S in later life reflects not only current routing

competition but also reduced reserve architecture.

These mechanisms can coexist and compound. A ZF-heavy gland increases the likelihood that

cortisol will dominate precursor routing during subsequent challenges, while a constrained ZR

reduces the capacity to maintain androgen reserve under repeated diversion. In this model, the

relevant developmental hazard is not any single cortisol elevation, but the repeated pattern of

cortisol prioritisation during periods when zonation is still plastic.


22.4 ASD-relevant steroid patterns: developmental divergence rather

than a single endocrine signature

Endocrine findings in autism spectrum disorder (ASD) do not resolve into a single uniform

pattern. Within the Sodium-Struggle framework, this heterogeneity is expected, because different

developmental stages can express different “solutions” to the same underlying problem: repeated

threats to effective sodium pressure and perfusion.

Observed adult ASD pattern (reported across adult cohorts):

Adult ASD samples have been reported to show lower DHEA-S and a higher cortisol:DHEA-S

ratio, even when absolute cortisol values vary. In the present framework, this is interpreted as

consistent with a state of relative ZR suppression (functional and/or structural) in the context of

enduring reliance on glucocorticoid-mediated pressure support. The ratio is emphasised because it

captures relative routing bias—how strongly cortisol dominates compared with the sulphated

androgen reserve—rather than treating cortisol in isolation.

Observed prepubertal pattern in ASD girls (reported in targeted steroid profiling):

A distinct pattern has been reported in prepubertal girls with ASD, characterised by:

● elevated 17-hydroxypregnenolone, ● elevated DHEA-S, ● normal free DHEA, and ● no

elevation of downstream 17-hydroxyprogesterone.

This constellation is difficult to interpret as a simple global androgen excess or a uniform adrenal

hyperfunction phenotype. Instead, it points toward upstream activation with selective buffering,

where steroidogenic traffic is increased but the bioactive androgen fraction remains constrained.

Within the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis, these ASD-linked patterns are not treated as

contradictory. Rather, they are interpreted as different developmental-phase readouts of the same

core mechanistic axis: precursor competition and zonation reweighting under repeated

sodium–perfusion challenge. Early in development, the system may still be capable of buffering

and reserve-building (e.g., sulphation into DHEA-S). Later, after prolonged competition and/or

architectural drift, reserve depletion may dominate the endocrine phenotype (low DHEA-S; high

cortisol:DHEA-S).



22.5 DHEA-S as buffered reserve: an endocrine containment strategy

during early adrenal activation

DHEA-S differs from DHEA in ways that are especially relevant to developmental physiology. It

is less acutely bioactive, more stable in circulation, and can serve as a storage and transport form

that tissues may locally convert when needed. In developmental contexts where upstream adrenal

activation occurs but free androgen exposure must be constrained, sulphation provides a

mechanism for preserving capacity while limiting immediate signalling.

Accordingly, the reported prepubertal profile in ASD girls—elevated precursor and elevated

DHEA-S with normal free DHEA—can be interpreted (hypothesis) as buffered adrenal readiness:

the gland is receiving signals consistent with increased steroidogenic demand, yet bioactive

androgen output is held within a tighter range by channelling product into a sulphated pool.

In Sodium-Struggle terms, this buffering may be adaptive under repeated cortisol recruitment. If

cortisol surges repeatedly divert 17-hydroxypregnenolone toward ZF output, maintaining a

DHEA-S reservoir may protect longer-horizon neurosteroid and anabolic needs against transient

precursor diversion. This interpretation does not require that DHEA-S buffering is “protective” in

a generalised sense; it only proposes that sulphation is a physiologically plausible way to preserve

steroid reserve when precursor competition is intermittently unfavourable.

Critically, this buffering concept also aligns with the developmental plasticity argument: if

early-life endocrine traffic is repeatedly pulled toward cortisol, buffering into DHEA-S may

represent a temporary countermeasure during the window when zonal architecture and reserve

capacity are still being negotiated. If sodium–perfusion challenges persist beyond that window,

buffering may be insufficient to prevent later reserve depletion.


22.6 Sex-specific buffering hypothesis: different solutions to the same

precursor-competition problem

The above pattern raises a mechanistic question that must be handled carefully: why might

buffered DHEA-S elevation be especially visible in prepubertal girls, while later-life ASD

patterns often show low DHEA-S and cortisol dominance?

Hypothesis (sex-differentiated resolution):

The Sodium-Struggle framework proposes that males and females may resolve the same upstream

constraint—precursor competition under recurrent cortisol recruitment—through different

endocrine “packaging” strategies, shaped by sex-differentiated developmental constraints on free

androgen exposure.

In females, tighter constraints on early-life free androgen variability plausibly favour sulphation

as containment: DHEA can be preserved as DHEA-S, maintaining a reserve while limiting

systemic androgen signalling during sensitive neurodevelopmental periods. This does not imply

that females are uniformly protected; rather, it posits that sulphation provides a relatively safer

storage route when upstream drive is high but free androgen exposure must remain constrained.

In males, the model does not claim an absence of buffering. Instead, it proposes that buffering

may be less dominated by sulphated storage and more resolved through different allocation



dynamics under cortisol dominance—potentially involving more direct utilisation, greater

tolerated variability in free androgen availability, or different timing of reserve deployment. The

net consequence (hypothesis) is that males may show greater apparent stress-reactivity and less

“shock-absorption” at the level of circulating DHEA-S during early activation, even if the

underlying precursor competition problem is shared.

This sex-differentiated proposal is intentionally non-deterministic. It does not assert that all girls

buffer or that all boys fail to buffer. It argues only that sulphation-based buffering is a

physiologically plausible, developmentally constrained strategy that may be more apparent in

females under early-life adrenal activation, whereas males may display a different balance

between buffering, utilisation, and depletion across development.


22.7 Integrative Sodium-Struggle synthesis: zonation as a

developmental imprint of sodium-perfusion defence

The Sodium-Struggle hypothesis centres on repeated early-life challenges to effective sodium

pressure and tissue perfusion, and the organism’s endocrine effort to avoid hyponatraemia and

hypoperfusion through a compensatory triad involving AVP, RAAS, and cortisol. In this section’s

adrenal-specific terms, the proposed sequence is:

● Recurrent low-effective-sodium / perfusion-threat episodes during sensitive windows

repeatedly recruit endocrine pressure support. Serum sodium may frequently appear normal

between episodes; the imprinting signal is the pattern of compensation, not a single laboratory

nadir.

● Cortisol is repeatedly recruited as a pressure-support hormone, particularly when

sodium-retentive reserve is insufficient, oscillatory, suppressed, or energetically constrained. This

yields recurrent cortisol dominance at the level of function (routing) and potentially at the level of

tissue program (zonal weighting).

● Precursor routing shifts at 17-hydroxypregnenolone, favouring glucocorticoid synthesis during

repeated cortisol demand. This produces functional ZR suppression via competitive diversion,

expressed as elevated cortisol:DHEA-S ratios and reduced DHEA-S reserve in later stages.

● During early developmental windows, zonation is plastic. ZG→ZF

conversion provides a plausible structural pathway by which

repeated cortisol prioritisation becomes a durable architectural

bias. Because reversibility appears limited outside early life, early

cortisol dominance may “lock in” a ZF-heavy adrenal configuration,

thereby increasing the probability of sustained precursor diversion

and constraining later ZR capacity.

● ZG maintenance signals modulate how easily this drift proceeds.

Potassium provides a continuous depolarising and

transcription-supportive input that helps stabilise ZG identity;

sustained low potassium tone during development may therefore

weaken ZG-specific programs and accelerate directional ZG→ZF



drift. Once ZG programs are silenced and tissue proportions

stabilise, later improvement in sodium status (or transient

normalisation of potassium) may be insufficient to reconstitute

mineralocorticoid-oriented architecture—framing low potassium

tone as a ratchet mechanism that limits recovery after

developmental plasticity closes.

● ZR suppression can therefore be both functional and structural. Functional suppression reflects

real-time routing competition; structural suppression reflects a constrained or underdeveloped

androgen-producing reserve as zonation stabilises.

● ASD-linked endocrine patterns can be developmentally staged. Early-life or prepubertal

phenotypes may show evidence of upstream activation with buffering (e.g., elevated

17-hydroxypregnenolone and elevated DHEA-S with normal DHEA in girls), whereas adult

phenotypes may converge toward reserve depletion and cortisol dominance (low DHEA-S; high

cortisol:DHEA-S), particularly if sodium–perfusion challenges are recurrent across development.

In sum, adrenal zonation is positioned here as a developmental readout of how the organism

repeatedly solved sodium–perfusion threats. The Sodium-Struggle hypothesis does not require

chronic hyponatraemia, nor does it interpret these patterns as generic “stress endocrinology.” It

proposes instead that repeated anti-hyponatraemia / anti-hypoperfusion compensation can

reweight adrenal architecture and steroid routing during sensitive windows, with downstream

consequences for glucocorticoid dominance, androgen reserve dynamics, and sex-differentiated

buffering strategies. Timing is therefore the primary axis: early-life cortisol dominance may

matter most not because it is large, but because it occurs when adrenal zonation—and the balance

between immediate pressure support and longer-horizon reserve—can still be programmed.

Safety and scope clarification (potassium): In this manuscript section, potassium is discussed as a

mechanistic and developmental variable that contributes to ZG identity maintenance and thus to

steroid-routing bias under the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis; it is not presented as a therapeutic

target. Potassium cannot be safely or effectively supplemented in isolation, particularly in

children, because potassium handling is inseparable from renal physiology, acid–base status, and

broader mineral–endocrine context. In addition, potassium status is commonly secondary to

systemic regulation, including magnesium-dependent potassium retention and selenium-linked

redox/endocrine support that influences magnesium handling. Accordingly, low potassium

tone—when present—should be interpreted here as a potential marker of wider physiological

dysregulation and constrained mineralocorticoid capacity, not as an isolated deficiency to be

corrected outside comprehensive clinical supervision.



23 Membrane Defence and Sterol Supply: Reconciling Low

and High Circulating Cholesterol in ASD

23.0 Membrane Cholesterol as a First-Line Buffer Against

Hypo-Osmotic Swelling

A central, unifying physical claim in the Sodium-Struggle / Hyponatraemia / Sodium-Switch

framework is that cholesterol in the plasma membrane lipid bilayer can function as a first-line

anti-swelling buffer when extracellular conditions drift toward hypo-osmolality. This can occur as

overt hypotonic hyponatraemia, or as a subtler, recurrent low-effective-sodium state even when

serum [Na⁺] is often near-normal. [effective sodium definition; AVP–RAAS–cortisol triad]

Measurement limitation and interpretive caveat: Nearly all human cholesterol findings in ASD

cohorts are derived from serum or plasma lipid measures (total cholesterol, LDL-C, HDL-C,

triglycerides). These panels quantify circulating lipoprotein-associated cholesterol, not cholesterol

residing in intracellular pools or the membrane-associated cholesterol that directly sets bilayer

order and microdomain stability. Therefore, low circulating cholesterol does not, by itself,

demonstrate cellular or membrane cholesterol deficiency, and high circulating cholesterol does

not, by itself, demonstrate cellular or membrane cholesterol excess. Both directions can plausibly

reflect differences in cellular retention, efflux versus clearance, tissue uptake, hepatic handling, or

cholesterol utilization (for example bile acids, steroidogenesis, membrane construction), rather

than a simple statement about “more” or “less” cholesterol within cells. In what follows, serum

lipid direction is treated as a readout of cholesterol traffic and supply-line state under sodium and

osmotic pressure, not a direct measurement of membrane cholesterol content.

This is a membrane physics claim. The plasma membrane lipid bilayer is not a passive wrapper.

Its permeability and mechanical compliance depend heavily on lipid packing. When membrane

cholesterol content is higher, cholesterol intercalates between phospholipids and increases lipid

packing order, especially within cholesterol-rich microdomains such as lipid rafts and caveolae.

Expected physical consequences include the following:

●​ Higher membrane cholesterol is associated with tighter lipid packing and higher

acyl-chain order.

●​ Tighter packing reduces the frequency and size of transient free-volume defects in the

bilayer core, which reduces diffusive permeability to small neutral molecules, including

water.

●​ Increased packing order raises membrane stiffness and can reduce area expansion under

osmotic load.

●​ Lower diffusive permeability combined with higher stiffness can slow early swelling

kinetics and reduce strain during rapid hypotonic challenges.

Clarification on water versus sodium permeability: Water crosses membranes primarily by

diffusion through the lipid bilayer and via aquaporins, whereas Na⁺ movement across the bilayer

itself is negligible under physiological conditions and requires channels and transporters.



Increasing cholesterol does not block sodium in the same direct manner that it can reduce bilayer

water permeability. Cholesterol can, however, modulate sodium handling indirectly by altering

lipid raft organization, influencing the localization and coupling of channels and transporters, and

changing membrane mechanical properties that can affect mechanosensitive proteins. These

effects are context-dependent and should be treated as modulatory rather than determinative.

A diffusion-accurate intuition is as follows. Water molecules cross the membrane either by

partitioning into the lipid phase and diffusing across it, or by passing through aquaporins when

present and active. The diffusive component depends on how often transient molecular gaps

appear between lipid acyl chains. Cholesterol increases packing order and reduces the number of

transient free-volume defects, so opportunities for water molecules to traverse the bilayer core

occur less frequently, and net water flux slows. An intuitive analogy is a doorway with a loosely

packed crowd versus a tightly packed crowd. In the loosely packed case, brief gaps open often

and passage is frequent. In the tightly packed case, gaps are rarer and passage slows. In hypotonic

exposure, the hazard is often the rate of water influx and the resulting swelling kinetics, not only

the final equilibrium volume.

This matters most during neurodevelopmental timing windows, when neural circuits and glial

systems are calibrating baseline “gains” (ionic microdomains, receptor clustering, excitability

thresholds, neurovascular coupling, and the osmotic buffering role of astroglia). In the

Sodium-Struggle framing, repeated or prolonged drift toward dilution, whether frank

hyponatraemia or low-effective-sodium pressure, imposes compensations that preserve

cell-volume stability. Membrane cholesterol is one of the few levers that can directly shift bilayer

permeability and mechanics at the cellular boundary.


23.1 Core Unifying Principle

Low and high circulating cholesterol findings in ASD cohorts can represent different stable

outcomes, and sometimes different timepoints, along the same upstream sodium and osmotic axis.

In the Sodium-Struggle model, early-life low-effective-sodium states increase the requirement to

buffer cellular water entry and to stabilize sodium-dependent signaling. Whether serum lipid

panels trend persistently low or persistently high depends on interacting constraints, including

sterol “supply lines,” renal tubular precision, endocrine geometry (AVP–RAAS–cortisol

discordance), and whether membrane reinforcement is feasible or constrained during sensitive

windows. [effective sodium definition; AVP–RAAS–cortisol triad; Benachenhou 2019; Tierney

2021]

Key clarification: because the literature is predominantly serum-based, the framework does not

require that low serum cholesterol implies low membrane cholesterol, or that high serum

cholesterol implies high membrane cholesterol. Serum direction is interpreted primarily as a

trafficking phenotype that reflects distribution, utilization, efflux, and clearance, and current

datasets usually cannot distinguish cholesterol scarcity from cholesterol conservation or retention

at the cellular level.



23.2 Developmental Bifurcation Model (timing + severity + capacity;

“forks”)

In this framework, early sodium and osmotic stress does not dictate a single lipid outcome.

Instead, it creates a bifurcation problem because two simultaneous demands for cholesterol are

imposed during critical windows:

Construction demand (brain building): membrane expansion, synaptogenesis, myelination

trajectories, and cholesterol-rich microdomain organization needed for reliable receptor clustering

and signaling.

Defense demand (anti-swelling): relative cholesterol enrichment of the plasma membrane lipid

bilayer (rafts and caveolae) to reduce water permeability and increase stiffness, slowing swelling

kinetics during dilutional threat.

Whether a given individual presents with low versus high circulating cholesterol on serum lipid

panels depends on interacting fork variables:

●​ Severity, frequency, timing of sodium and osmotic threat

●​ Recurrent maternal–fetal or infant dilution pressure, osmotic-illusion states (non-ionic

osmoles), or intermittent true hyponatraemia can repeatedly activate anti-hyponatraemia

countermeasures and can imprint set-points. [Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis; effective

sodium definition]

●​ Sterol supply-line capacity

Can cholesterol be delivered where and when needed via (i) intestinal absorption, (ii)

enterohepatic bile-acid recycling, and (iii) de novo synthesis bandwidth? A key choke point is the

sodium-dependent ileal bile acid transporter ASBT/SLC10A2 and the stability of the

enterohepatic loop. [ASBT/SLC10A2 physiology]

Renal reserve and tubular precision

How often is physiology pushed into AVP-dominant or cortisol-dominant sodium-defense states

because the kidney cannot finely match water excretion to solute handling? Repeated tubular

stress can become self-reinforcing. Renal tubular epithelial swelling and dysfunction can narrow

lumens, disrupt transporter polarity, and reduce free-water precision, which reduces renal reserve

and precision even if nephron number is unchanged.

Compensatory capacity and regulatory operating point

In some individuals, regulatory control can stabilize at a higher circulating availability of

cholesterol, supporting distribution to membranes and cholesterol-rich microdomains. In others,

supply-line ceilings, malabsorption, bile-acid loss, inflammation-driven transport instability, or

intrinsic synthesis limitation constrain circulating pools and limit the ability to sustain membrane

reinforcement. Serum data alone cannot separate a primary supply limitation from a state in



which circulating levels are low because tissue uptake, intracellular utilization, or retention

dominates, but both mechanisms map onto the same compartmental ambiguity emphasized above.

This fork logic yields at least three coherent trajectory classes without contradiction:

1.​ Persistent low circulating cholesterol: serum measures remain low because supply lines

cannot meet construction plus defense demand, and or because cholesterol is

preferentially diverted into utilization or tissue retention with reduced circulating

availability.

2.​ Persistent high circulating cholesterol and dyslipidaemia: serum measures remain

elevated because circulating availability is maintained and or clearance is shifted,

consistent with a bias toward avoiding low-availability episodes under chronic dilutional

threat.

3.​ Low early, then high later: early scarcity or high utilization can shift subsequent handling
