toward conservation, later improvements in supply can permit overcorrection, and

elevated circulating pools can become stable.


23.3 Low-Cholesterol ASD Trajectory (mechanisms + why it can persist)

In the Sodium-Struggle frame, persistent low circulating cholesterol in a subset of ASD is not

necessarily a benign lipid profile. It can indicate constrained sterol logistics under developmental

demand, with limited ability to keep cholesterol broadly available for membrane construction and

membrane defense. At the same time, the measurement limitation remains central: low serum

cholesterol does not prove cellular deficiency. It can also reflect reduced circulation driven by

increased intracellular use, increased tissue retention, suppressed efflux, or increased clearance.

The mechanisms below emphasize supply-line constraints and obligatory utilization because they

are physiologically coherent within the framework and generate testable signatures.


23.3.1 Sodium-dependent bile-acid reclamation as a choke point

(functional ASBT inhibition)

The most model-native bridge between sodium-struggle and low cholesterol is the enterohepatic

loop. Ileal reclamation of bile acids via ASBT/SLC10A2 is explicitly sodium-dependent. If the

intestinal transport ecology is sodium-strained (impaired Na⁺ gradients, energetically stressed

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase support, inflammation-altered transporter localization), the phenotype can

resemble functional ASBT inhibition even without a primary transporter mutation.

[ASBT/SLC10A2 physiology; Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis]

Mechanistic consequences follow standard physiology:

●​ Reduced bile acid reclamation increases fecal bile acid loss.

●​ Bile acid loss increases hepatic replacement synthesis, drawing on cholesterol as

substrate.

●​ A less stable bile acid pool worsens micellar absorption of dietary fats and cholesterol.



Net effect at the level of serum lipid measures: lower measured LDL and total cholesterol can

occur despite apparently adequate dietary cholesterol exposure because absorption logistics fail

and cholesterol is diverted into bile acid replacement. Within Sodium-Struggle logic, this is a

double-hit because sodium-dependent transport instability can both reduce absorption and

increase cholesterol utilization for bile acid replenishment.


23.3.2 Inflammation and barrier-leak physiology (IL-6/VEGF lane) as a

cholesterol-lowering amplifier

The Sodium-Struggle model includes an IL-6/VEGF permeability lane that shifts oncotic and

capillary forces, promotes third spacing, and drives low-effective-sodium pressure even when

total body sodium is not obviously low. [IL-6/VEGF lane; Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis]

This lane can contribute to low circulating cholesterol through two converging mechanisms:

Acute-phase lipid lowering and redistribution

IL-6 dominant physiology can lower measured cholesterol via altered hepatic handling and

clearance phenotypes, consistent with the inflammatory signature described in the framework.

[IL-6 LDLR; Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis]

Worsened malabsorption and transport instability

Barrier leak and inflammation can impair enterocyte function and transporter localization,

compounding bile-acid loss and reducing fat-soluble nutrient absorption, which penalizes

individuals already near sterol constraint.


23.3.3 True hypocholesterolaemia endophenotypes become more

hazardous under sodium-struggle exposure

A distinct subgroup likely exists in which cholesterol is intrinsically limited (for example

synthesis constraints such as DHCR7/SLOS spectrum or other sterol bandwidth limitations). In

Sodium-Struggle terms, these individuals begin life with lower sterol reserve. [SLOS/DHCR7;

Benachenhou 2019; Tierney 2021]

That starting point is uniquely hazardous in a modern osmotic ecology because it constrains both

membrane construction capacity and the ability to shift membrane mechanics toward lower

permeability and higher stiffness during dilutional challenge. Under repeated

low-effective-sodium pressure, cholesterol-limited tissues can experience greater swelling stress

per unit challenge because bilayer reinforcement is less accessible. The predicted developmental

outcome is not necessarily overt necrotic injury, but mis-specification, including altered

maturation timing, compromised microdomain signaling reliability, and reduced network reserve

during critical windows.



23.3.4 Why low circulating cholesterol can persist rather than “flip”

The low-cholesterol trajectory persists when the constraint persists:


●​

●​

●​

●​


ongoing bile acid loss and unstable recycling (ASBT-like functional limitation),

chronic inflammatory or barrier-leak physiology,

persistent malabsorption and restricted intake, or

intrinsic synthesis limitation that does not resolve.


In such cases, there is limited capacity to sustain an elevated circulating pool, and the physiology

remains locked to a low-availability regime on serum lipid measures.


23.4 High-Cholesterol/Dyslipidaemic ASD Trajectory (mechanisms +

why it can persist)

In this trajectory, elevated cholesterol on serum lipid panels is interpreted as a circulating

availability and handling phenotype that is compatible with membrane defense demands under

sodium and osmotic threat. The interpretation is deliberately conservative. It does not claim that

ASD membranes are measurably cholesterol-enriched. It proposes that regulatory set-points can

favor maintaining higher circulating pools and or altered clearance, preserving the option for

membrane reinforcement when dilutional challenges recur.


23.4.1 Cholesterol elevation as a membrane-level anti-swelling

adaptation (membrane wall concept)

When dilution threat is frequent, feedback control can bias regulation toward maintaining higher

circulating cholesterol availability so that membrane cholesterol supply is less likely to become

limiting, particularly for cholesterol-rich microdomains that organize mechanosensing and

receptor signaling. This framing treats elevated circulating cholesterol as consistent with a

defensive posture that prioritizes membrane stability under recurrent low-effective-sodium

pressure. [Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis; Sodium-Switch]


23.4.2 Developmental hysteresis: why it becomes sticky even if overt

hyponatraemia becomes less obvious

A key concept in the Sodium-Switch framework is set-point drift and partial irreversibility. Once

an architecture is built to avoid hyponatraemia, it does not necessarily unwind when laboratory

sodium appears normal. The operating point can remain tuned to anticipated vulnerability rather

than to a single cross-sectional lab value.

The same logic can be applied to membrane composition and sterol handling. A later reduction in

cholesterol availability can increase bilayer water permeability, reduce stiffness, and destabilize

previously tuned microdomains. Those shifts can increase vulnerability to rapid swelling during



future dilutional episodes. Consequently, set-point hysteresis can favor persistence of higher

circulating pools even when they carry later metabolic trade-offs.


23.4.3 Scarcity, followed by conservation: low early can program high

later

A crucial reconciliation pathway is that early cholesterol scarcity, or early periods of high

obligatory utilization, can shift subsequent cholesterol handling toward conservation.

Developmental calibration under repeated sodium and osmotic stress can bias later lipoprotein

production, clearance, and efflux in a direction that reduces tolerance for low-availability

episodes and prioritizes availability for membranes. If supply-line function improves later

(dietary environment changes, gut ecology stabilizes, bile-acid losses diminish), this conserved

operating point can manifest as persistently elevated circulating cholesterol.

This pathway remains compatible with the compartmental ambiguity of serum measures. A low

early serum panel may reflect true scarcity, reduced circulation due to high utilization, or reduced

circulation due to tissue retention with suppressed efflux. Each can contribute to a later bias

toward conservation if early availability is repeatedly constrained.


23.4.4 Endocrine geometry amplifies dyslipidaemia in adulthood

(trade-off, not failure)

The Sodium-Struggle model’s discordant AVP–RAAS–cortisol pattern and Sodium-Switch drift

(cortisol-dominant pressure support; altered mineralocorticoid program confidence) provides an

endocrine geometry that can reinforce adult dyslipidaemia, especially via insulin-resistance-like

lipid routing (ApoB burden, triglyceride shifts, HDL suppression). Because HDL participates in

reverse cholesterol transport, a coupled process involving cellular efflux and hepatic clearance, a

pattern of low HDL with normal or mildly elevated LDL can be interpreted as consistent with

reduced flux through cholesterol removal pathways. This is compatible with a conservation

posture under chronic low-effective-sodium pressure, while remaining non-committal about the

magnitude of tissue cholesterol retention in any specific ASD subgroup.

The framework emphasis is not behavioral blame, but physiological routing trade-offs. Defensive

pressure and tonicity architectures can bias metabolic routing in directions that reduce dilutional

vulnerability and impose later costs. [AVP–RAAS–cortisol triad; Sodium-Switch]


23.4.5 Laboratory interpretive note (to prevent category errors)

While there is no evidence that hyperlipidaemic states in the general population produce red

blood cell swelling, such states are well known to produce pseudohyponatraemia due to plasma

volume displacement artifacts in certain laboratory measurement techniques.

[pseudohyponatraemia definition]

This matters because sodium-struggle arguments can be derailed if pseudohyponatraemia is

mistaken for true hypotonic swelling physiology.



23.5 Why These Are Not Contradictions (explicit reconciliation; cohort

mixing; timepoints)

23.5.1 Same upstream axis; different feasible outcomes

Low circulating cholesterol and high circulating cholesterol are not competing explanations. They

are different feasible outcomes along the same upstream constraint axis, repeated drift toward

hypo-osmolality and low-effective-sodium pressure during development.

Low circulating cholesterol subgroup: physiology is supply-limited and or cholesterol is diverted

into utilization or tissue retention such that circulating panels run low, and membrane

reinforcement capacity can be constrained during challenges.

High circulating cholesterol subgroup: physiology is defense-capable in the sense that circulating

availability is maintained and or clearance is shifted, supporting sterol availability for membrane

reinforcement while accepting later metabolic costs.

These statements refer to circulation and trafficking phenotypes measured by serum panels. They

do not assert direct measurement of membrane cholesterol content.


23.5.2 Cross-sectional studies sample mixed trajectories

ASD cohorts are mixtures of ages, developmental stages, medication exposures, diet selectivity

patterns, gastrointestinal phenotypes, and renal or endocrine states. Cross-sectional lipid

measurements therefore capture mixed timepoints, including early low-availability states, later

conservation and overcorrection states, and adult endocrine-amplified dyslipidaemia patterns (for

example higher triglycerides with lower HDL). Apparent contradictions emerge when different

studies sample different mixtures of trajectories rather than a single biology.


23.5.3 Low early, then high later is predicted under specific conditions

The Sodium-Struggle model predicts flipping when supply lines improve sufficiently to permit a

conservation-biased operating point to express as elevated circulating cholesterol. A

low-cholesterol infant or child subgroup can become a high-cholesterol adult subgroup if early

scarcity or high utilization calibrates conservation and later gut and bile-acid function improves.


23.5.4 Optional modern osmotic ecology note: why low cholesterol might

not have “meant ASD” in 1925

Within the Sodium-Struggle framing, the century-shift is not “more stress” in general. It is more

frequent, directionally consistent dilutional and sodium-lean signaling during sensitive windows

(ultra-low-TDS fluids, non-ionic osmoles that create sodium-lean illusions, pharmacologic AVP

influences, and barrier-weakening exposures). In such an environment, cholesterol-limited

membranes are tested more often. A trait that could remain phenotypically quiet when dilution



challenges were rarer can become neurodevelopmentally destabilizing when the osmotic ecology

repeatedly demands membrane-level anti-swelling defenses.


24 Receptor and Signalling Imprinting After

Anti-Hyponatraemia and Anti-Hypoperfusion Compensation

Section 23 argued that recurrent anti-hyponatraemia and anti-hypoperfusion

compensation can reorganize endocrine “routing” at the organ level, including

sustained reliance on cortisol-mediated pressure support. The present section

extends that logic downward in scale. Repeated compensatory states are not

merely experienced acutely; they are embedded during development. Across

sensitive windows, developing tissues consolidate how incoming physiological

signals are weighted, amplified, and acted upon.

Over time, this consolidation establishes what kinds of signals reliably precede

threat, what patterns tend to accompany safety, and what response magnitudes are

typically required to maintain perfusion and volume stability. Within the

Sodium-Struggle framework, this process is described as receptor and cell-state

imprinting: a durable shift in receptor balance, signalling gain, transcriptional

set-points, and response thresholds that can persist even after circulating chemistry

and hormone levels return to ranges typically considered normal.

A further implication, developed below at hypothesis level, is that imprinting does

not necessarily progress in a single direction. Receptor systems calibrated under

constrained or intermittent ligand exposure may initially up-tune toward high

sensitivity, then undergo overshoot followed by recoil after abrupt changes in

ligand pattern or amplitude. Such trajectories can produce sign flips, oscillatory

behaviour, or mixed sensitivity profiles without requiring new primary insults.


24.1 What receptor and signalling imprinting is: interpretive drift

Receptor imprinting refers to developmentally consolidated changes in how a

tissue responds to a ligand. This includes receptor density, subtype balance,

surface trafficking, downstream coupling efficiency, and effective gain. These

changes are not short-term desensitization phenomena of hours to days. Rather,

they reflect a statistical consolidation of response properties shaped by the



frequency, amplitude, and contextual meaning of ligand exposure during

developmental construction.

Cell-state imprinting refers to a broader stabilization of response identity. A

tissue that consolidates an interpretation of recurrent sodium or perfusion threat

may not exhibit persistently elevated hormone levels. Instead, it may show a

durable bias toward particular defensive outputs—such as vasomotor tone,

sympathetic gain, water conservation, or cortisol reliance—once a triggering

pattern is detected. The critical distinction is that imprinting alters thresholds and

gains—how readily a response is triggered and how strongly it unfolds—rather

than enforcing a constant “high-hormone” state.

Together, receptor and cell-state imprinting can produce interpretive drift: a

lasting shift in how physiological signals are weighted and translated into action at

the tissue level. In this framework, autism-relevant phenotypes need not reflect

ongoing dysnatremia or overt endocrine pathology at the time of presentation.

Instead, they may reflect a consolidated response architecture in which tissues

continue to react to ordinary internal or sensory inputs as if they carried elevated

threat significance.

Overshoot–recoil (defined).​

Overshoot–recoil refers to a non-linear imprinting trajectory in which receptor

systems initially up-tune under conditions of constrained or unreliable ligand

exposure (overshoot), then subsequently down-regulate, uncouple, or internalize

after abrupt high-amplitude stimulation (recoil). Following recoil, tissues may

stabilize at a lower-gain or blunted set-point, producing an apparent shift from

hypersensitivity to hyposensitivity without the introduction of a new causal

exposure.


24.2 Multivariate fetal calibration: imprinting is not memory of one

variable

A central principle of the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis is that the fetus does not

imprint on a single maternal variable such as serum sodium concentration. Instead,

it consolidates response properties based on multivariate calibration: convergent

signals filtered through maternal thresholds, maternal receptor sensitivities, and

placental gating. In this sense, the fetus does not respond to isolated



measurements; it is shaped by the patterned physiological state conveyed across

domains.

Within this framework, several informational streams converge on a single

computational pressure: the effective severity and frequency of sodium- and

perfusion-related threat.

A. Osmotic and electrolyte inputs

Fetal calibration incorporates extracellular sodium concentration as it is

transmitted through maternal AVP and RAAS engagement, rather than as an

absolute value. Extracellular chloride provides an additional informational channel

by shaping WNK–SPAK signalling and neuronal volume regulation risk.

“Effective osmolality”—the tonic signal experienced by the brain—may diverge

from calculated osmolality, particularly in translocational states. In parallel, fetal

intracellular osmolyte composition adapts to maternal osmotic signalling patterns,

imposing longer-lived constraints on cellular tolerance to dilution and swelling

pressure.​

Interpretive consequence: repeated signalling patterns consistent with osmotic

threat bias response thresholds toward rapid conservation and defence.

B. Volume, pressure, and perfusion inputs

Intravascular volume status is conveyed less as a direct volume signal and more

through the temporal structure of maternal RAAS and AVP activation. Blood

pressure and vascular tone, including V1a-mediated vasoconstriction,

communicate whether pressure defence is being routinely recruited. Regional

perfusion redistribution—particularly “brain-sparing” patterns—provides evidence

that sodium or volume instability is sufficient to warrant protective reallocation.​

Interpretive consequence: recurrent pressure-defence signalling consolidates

higher-gain vasomotor responsiveness.

C. Endocrine and receptor–ligand inputs

Maternal AVP pulsatility, more than baseline level, shapes fetal V1a/V1b/V2

sensitivity. Maternal RAAS tone entrains AGTR1-linked sodium conservation

pathways, even when cortisol substitution later predominates. Maternal cortisol

exposure, filtered by placental gating, biases how often stress hormones are

engaged in sodium defence. Maternal thyroid tone influences Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase



expression, metabolic rate, and osmolyte handling, thereby constraining the

energetic feasibility of sustained volume stability.​

Interpretive consequence: endocrine patterning biases which hormonal axes are

preferentially recruited under challenge.

D. Stress, cytokine, and sympathetic inputs

Maternal inflammatory cytokines reshape fetal barrier properties and osmotic

interpretation, altering how safe dilution or hypoperfusion is treated. Maternal

sympathetic tone calibrates fetal vascular reactivity by establishing the frequency

with which pressure defence accompanies routine physiological states.​

Interpretive consequence: inflammatory and sympathetic context lowers

response thresholds for defensive activation.

In combination, these domains can converge on a developmental conclusion that

low-effective sodium pressure is a recurrent threat, even in the absence of

documented hyponatraemia. Receptor and cell-state programs then consolidate

around an anti-hyponatraemia and anti-hypoperfusion posture. Under such

conditions, constrained ligand patterns can preferentially tune receptor gain,

increasing vulnerability to overshoot when signalling patterns later change

abruptly.


24.3 Why imprinting is long-lasting

Imprinting persists because it is embedded during developmental construction

rather than layered on as a reversible adjustment.

Critical windows and irreversible allocation.​

During fetal and early postnatal periods—including mid-gestation and the

postnatal walking window—tissues are allocating capacity while differentiating

and refining networks. Receptor subtype balance, baseline signalling gain, and

ion-transport commitments are set as part of this construction. Once a defensive

architecture consolidates, later reversal may be incomplete because cellular

composition and transcriptional identity have shifted.

Threshold learning rather than ligand tracking.​

Developmental consolidation targets response rules rather than absolute hormone

concentrations. Recurrent recruitment of AVP–RAAS–cortisol triads teaches



tissues when to trigger defence and how strongly. When initiating conditions

resolve, the learned thresholds remain, allowing normal circulating hormone levels

to coexist with non-typical response profiles.

Coupled feedback loops that reset “normal.”​

Shifts in receptor gain alter feedback dynamics. Higher-gain receptors may

require less ligand to achieve a given physiological outcome, masking imprinting

at the level of baseline assays. The imprint is therefore expressed primarily as

state-dependent over- or under-reactivity.

Overshoot–recoil dynamics (hypothesis-level).​

Developing receptor systems may behave analogously to sensitive fuses. Under

chronically low, absent, or intermittent ligand exposure, receptors and downstream

coupling can become over-expressed or high-gain. If ligand availability later

increases abruptly or arrives in atypically high-amplitude patterns,

disproportionate activation may trigger protective recoil—internalization,

uncoupling, down-regulation, or altered trafficking. Following recoil, the system

may stabilize at a lower-gain set-point, producing an apparent sign flip from

hypersensitivity to hyposensitivity. Importantly, this trajectory does not require a

new insult.

This framework anticipates intermittent, context-dependent physiology rather than

continuous biochemical abnormality.


24.4 Why imprinting is tissue-specific: different tissues “hear” the same

hormone differently

Endocrine signals are broadcast system-wide, but imprinting is local.

Tissue-specific receptor mixtures, downstream effectors, and developmental

constraints shape how identical ligands are interpreted.

Neurodevelopmental tissues.​

V1a/V1b-biased signalling can alter excitability regulation, threat salience, and

stress-responsive gain within developing circuits. Concurrent down-tuning of

affiliative buffering pathways may reduce tolerance to sensory or social load.

Overshoot–recoil histories can yield mixed channel profiles within the same

individual.



Vasculature.​

Imprinted V1a gain favours rapid pressure defence. While adaptive under

recurrent hypoperfusion micro-signals, such gain can impose costs during

developmental transitions involving orthostatic or metabolic load.

Kidney.​

Renal tissue must reconcile water and sodium imperatives. V2-linked water

handling may undergo escape-like adaptations, while sodium conservation remains

prioritized through RAAS or cortisol substitution. Imprinting may therefore

manifest as altered relationships between AVP signalling and water output rather

than chronic retention.

These examples illustrate a central principle: signalling is global; interpretation is

tissue-specific.

24.6 Vasopressin as the archetypal discordant ligand: one ligand, three

imperatives

Vasopressin (AVP) exemplifies discordant signalling architecture because a single

ligand coordinates three distinct imperatives via separate receptors:

●​ V1a: vascular tone and CNS signalling​

●​ V1b: pituitary and stress-axis regulation​

●​ V2: renal water handling​

In acute emergencies these functions align. Under recurrent low-effective sodium

pressure, they can conflict. Water may need to be excreted to correct dilution while

pressure must be defended aggressively.

Independent imprinting of V1a, V1b, and V2 pathways can therefore occur across

tissues. Pulsatility, amplitude, and contextual pairing of AVP exposure further

shape which imperatives dominate. Overshoot–recoil trajectories can unfold

independently across receptor channels, yielding complex repartitioning of AVP’s

meaning even when circulating levels normalize.



Illustrative developmental scenario (bounded; not causal).​

If fetal receptor systems calibrate under constrained or oscillatory AVP exposure,

high-gain V1a/V1b configurations may consolidate. At the peripartum transition,

labour introduces abrupt, high-amplitude signalling. Exogenous oxytocin

(Pitocin), while intended to engage oxytocin receptors, can cross-activate

vasopressin receptors under some conditions, presenting a sudden abundance

signal to a receptor landscape calibrated for scarcity. This constitutes a

signal-pattern mismatch. In hypothesis-level framing, such mismatch can

plausibly trigger overshoot–recoil dynamics without implying causation or

singular exposure effects.


24.7 How discordant signalling persists with “normal” hormone levels

A common objection to developmental endocrine models is the absence of

consistent hormonal abnormalities later in life. Receptor and cell-state imprinting

resolves this paradox.

Shifted stimulus–response curves.​

Normal ligand levels can elicit exaggerated or blunted responses depending on

receptor gain and prior recoil history. Challenge states reveal discordance not

visible at rest.

Reweighted downstream priorities.​

Even when receptor activation is unchanged, downstream programs may favour

defensive outputs over buffering or affiliative responses. Autism-relevant features

thus arise from persistent mismatches between circulating signals and tissue-level

interpretation rather than from constant chemical derangement.


24.8 Intergenerational oscillation and reversal: imprinting as a moving

target

Because fetal calibration is filtered through maternal physiology, imprinting can

oscillate across generations. Adaptive shifts in one generation modify the

signalling environment of the next, allowing attenuation, overshoot, or reversal.

Overshoot–recoil trajectories provide a mechanistically coherent route to such



oscillation, particularly when receptor channels and tissues diverge in their

response histories.

(Box 24.1 — BPA illustration retained with strengthened disclaimer in subsequent

pass.)


24.9 Autism relevance: a plausible contribution via persistent

interpretive drift

This section advances a bounded proposal: receptor and cell-state imprinting may

contribute to autism-relevant phenotypes by stabilizing differences in how

developing tissues weight osmotic, vascular, and social-affiliative signals. Such

contributions do not require persistent hyponatraemia, persistent AVP elevation, or

a uniform receptor signature. Instead, they predict context-dependent reactivity,

tissue-specific trade-offs, and nonlinear histories shaped by overshoot–recoil

dynamics within a developmentally consolidated interpretive architecture.


25 Brainstem Volume-Sensing and Autonomic Control

Imprinting

Section 24 argued that repeated anti-hyponatraemia and anti-hypovolaemia states can alter

receptor balance, signalling gain, and tissue-level interpretation. The next question is how those

altered inputs are translated into whole-body defensive action. In this context, brainstem

“volume-sensing” does not denote a single central sensor for bulk volume. It denotes the central

interpretation of arterial pressure, cardiopulmonary filling, chemoreceptor threat, and osmotic

information as a composite estimate of effective circulating volume and perfusion stability. This

section therefore addresses the controller layer that sits between receptor-level interpretation and

the later vascular imprinting argument in the present draft.

The central claim is a bounded one. The autonomic controller is not a fixed relay, but a

calibratable regulatory system. Experimental studies demonstrate that baroreflex performance,

hypothalamic vasopressin signalling, and medullary sympathoexcitatory recruitment can all shift

under osmotic, sodium, dehydration, and cardiovascular challenge. Developmental programming

studies then support the possibility that repeated challenge during sensitive windows could make

some of those shifts durable. This does not prove one universal autonomic phenotype, nor does it

by itself prove autism causation. It does support the possibility that repeated sodium-volume

instability could recalibrate controller thresholds, gain, and recovery logic in ways that later shape

vascular and cellular environments.



25.1 Afferent convergence into the

controller

The first point is anatomical and physiological convergence. Arterial baroreceptors,

cardiopulmonary stretch receptors, and peripheral chemoreceptors do not arrive at isolated central

relays. Hines (1994) recorded nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS) neurones responsive to both

mechanically and chemically sensitive heart-lung afferent stimuli, while Mifflin (1992) showed

that carotid body activation depolarises NTS neurones and that some of these same cells also

receive excitatory baroreceptor input. These findings indicate that the NTS receives a composite

signal about pressure, filling, and blood-gas threat rather than a single modality. In practical

terms, effective circulating volume is inferred centrally from patterned afferent convergence, not

read directly from one sensor.

The vasopressin system is embedded within this same architecture. Jhamandas (1987)

demonstrated a central GABAergic baroreceptor pathway that inhibits vasopressin cells in the

supraoptic nucleus, and Grindstaff (2000) showed that stretch of the caval-atrial junction

suppresses firing of putative vasopressin neurones through a vagal mechanism. In parallel,

Miselis (1982) described strong subfornical organ projections to vasopressinergic neurones in the

supraoptic and paraventricular nuclei, and Shi and colleagues (2007, 2008) showed that OVLT

neurones activated by hypertonic NaCl project to the paraventricular nucleus and contribute to

sympathetic activation. Taken together, these studies indicate that the controller integrates two

partially distinct but convergent streams: an NTS-centred cardiovascular stream and a

lamina-terminalis-centred osmotic and hydromineral stream. The relevant defended variable is

therefore effective perfusion under unstable tonicity and filling, not serum sodium in isolation.


25.2 Circulatory-defence recruitment of

vasopressin systems and parvocellular

autonomic output

A central premise of the sodium-struggle framework is that the developing organism may

encounter physiological states in which circulatory-defence signals and osmotic signals do not

align neatly. In such cases the dominant homeostatic problem may not be classical

hyperosmolality but rather threatened effective arterial filling. Central hypovolemia,

cardiopulmonary unloading, venous pooling, or sodium-diluted states can create a mismatch

between circulatory and osmotic signals. Human studies show that arginine vasopressin and

plasma renin activity can rise during central hypovolemia as pulse pressure narrows, even before

mean arterial pressure falls, and AVP can remain unsuppressed in hyponatremic states when



nausea, hypotension, or blood-volume contraction provide strong non-osmotic drive. In situations

where circulatory-defence signals dominate, vasopressin recruitment may therefore persist despite

osmotic conditions that would ordinarily inhibit magnocellular neurons, reflecting the

physiological priority placed on preservation of perfusion.

Within the hypothalamic controller, these signals converge on the paraventricular nucleus and

supraoptic nucleus, where magnocellular vasopressin neurones act as a key endocrine–autonomic

gain stage. When strongly recruited, these neurones generate a dual output. One arm is endocrine,

via neurohypophyseal vasopressin release into the circulation. The other is local: magnocellular

neurones release vasopressin somatodendritically within hypothalamic tissue. This local pathway

is important because magnocellular and parvocellular populations occupy a shared extracellular

microenvironment. Son and colleagues demonstrated that dendrites from magnocellular

vasopressin neurones extend into presympathetic parvocellular zones and that dendritically

released vasopressin can directly excite presympathetic neurones roughly 100 μm away,

generating a diffusible peptide pool that facilitates sympathoexcitatory recruitment during

homeostatic challenge.

The paraventricular nucleus contains several distinct parvocellular populations, including

presympathetic neurones projecting to medullary and spinal autonomic centres, neuroendocrine

CRH/vasopressin neurones projecting to the median eminence, and parvocellular oxytocin

neurones projecting to brainstem parasympathetic circuits. Among these groups, the

presympathetic population currently represents the most directly supported target of dendritically

released vasopressin. Through projections to the rostral ventrolateral medulla, nucleus tractus

solitarius, nucleus ambiguus, and spinal sympathetic neurones, these cells translate hypothalamic

vasopressin signalling into cardiovascular stabilization.

This configuration corresponds to a circulatory-defence controller state in which endocrine

vasopressin release, local peptide diffusion, and descending autonomic recruitment act together to

stabilize pressure and perfusion. Vasopressin neurones also influence brainstem arousal circuits,

projecting to regions involved in vigilance and wakefulness, suggesting that strong AVP-linked

defence states may simultaneously bias the organism toward heightened alertness while

cardiovascular stabilization is maintained.

Within this controller state, oxytocin signalling is best interpreted as relatively down-weighted

rather than suppressed. Hypothalamic oxytocin pathways project to parasympathetic brainstem

circuits including the dorsal motor nucleus of the vagus, where oxytocin facilitates cardiovagal

signalling and recovery physiology. When paraventricular output becomes dominated by

AVP-linked presympathetic recruitment and circulatory stabilization, oxytocin-mediated

parasympathetic buffering therefore contributes less to the net autonomic output. This shift

reflects a state-dependent reweighting of the hypothalamic–brainstem controller, rather than

a global shutdown of oxytocin signalling.



25.3 Vasopressin neurones as a plastic

endocrine-autonomic gain stage

The hypothalamic vasopressin system shows equally strong evidence of plasticity. Uhl (1985)

reported that dehydration approximately doubled vasopressin mRNA signal in both the supraoptic

nucleus and paraventricular nucleus, indicating transcriptional reprogramming under

hydromineral stress. Miyata and colleagues (1994) then showed that chronic osmotic stimulation

produces structural plasticity in magnocellular neurones, including enlargement of soma size and

altered patterns of membrane juxtaposition and synaptic organisation. These findings indicate that

PVN and SON are not passive endocrine outputs. They are adaptive control elements whose

transcriptional and anatomical operating state changes with osmotic history.

More recent work sharpens the mechanism. Choe et al. (2015) showed that chronic high salt

intake impairs normal baroreceptor inhibition of vasopressin neurones through

BDNF-TrkB-dependent downregulation of KCC2, thereby increasing spontaneous firing and

making circulating vasopressin part of the blood-pressure phenotype. Balapattabi et al. (2018)

showed that salt loading increases BDNF within supraoptic vasopressin neurones and that local

BDNF knockdown blocks the associated increase in AVP transcription and plasma AVP. These

findings indicate that hydromineral challenge can remodel how readily vasopressin neurones are

inhibited, how strongly they fire, and how tightly they couple endocrine output to autonomic

state. Within this manuscript, PVN and SON are therefore best treated as a plastic

endocrine-autonomic gain stage rather than as a simple downstream water-conservation module.


25.4 Recruitment of sympathetic pressor

pathways and vagal buffering

Hydromineral challenge also recruits identifiable central pressor circuits. Stocker et al. (2005)

showed that inhibiting the paraventricular nucleus lowers renal and lumbar sympathetic nerve

activity and arterial pressure more strongly in water-deprived rats, and that these effects are

attenuated by acute rehydration. Stocker et al. (2006) then showed that the PVN to rostral

ventrolateral medulla projection activated by water deprivation is predominantly glutamatergic.

Colombari et al. (2011) extended this logic by showing that chronic dehydration increases

sympathetic activity while shifting dominant control of thoracic sympathetic outflow toward the

commissural NTS, with AP1 transcriptional activity required for that switch. At the medullary

effector level, Adams et al. (2008) showed that elevated dietary salt enhances the

sympathoexcitatory actions of angiotensin II in the RVLM, and Stocker et al. (2015) showed that

acute cerebrospinal-fluid hypernatremia increases lumbar and adrenal sympathetic activity

through lamina-terminalis-dependent glutamatergic drive onto selective RVLM neurones. Taken



together, these studies demonstrate that repeated hydromineral challenge can change not only

whether sympathetic outflow is engaged, but how readily specific central nodes recruit it.

The parasympathetic side belongs in the same controller, but the evidence is less direct. Output

allocation necessarily includes cardiovagal and broader visceral vagal pathways, including

nucleus ambiguus and dorsal motor nucleus channels, yet the hydromineral plasticity literature is

much richer for PVN, NTS, and RVLM sympathoexcitatory recruitment than for direct long-term

remodelling of those vagal output nuclei themselves. The more cautious inference is therefore

functional rather than structural. Babcock et al. (2018) showed that dietary sodium can alter

cardiovagal baroreflex behaviour, and Koshimizu et al. (2006) showed that V1a receptor

deficiency impairs baroreflex sensitivity while reducing circulating blood volume. The safest

conclusion is that repeated sodium-volume threat could bias controller allocation toward earlier

sympathetic recruitment and tighter AVP-autonomic coupling, with vagal buffering becoming

more conditional and less forgiving under challenge, rather than proving fixed vagal failure.


25.5 Developmental programming and

limited ASD compatibility

Adult plasticity alone does not prove developmental imprinting, but the programming literature

makes that extension plausible. Gardner et al. (2004) showed that peri-implantation

undernutrition in sheep produces a leftward shift of the baroreflex function curve and blunted

angiotensin II-evoked baroreflex responses in young adulthood. Soukhova-O’Hare and colleagues

(2006) showed that postnatal intermittent hypoxia produces persistent adult alterations in

baroreflex function and impaired control of renal sympathetic nerve activity. These models are

not sodium-volume models, but they demonstrate that early physiological stress can durably

recalibrate autonomic and cardiovascular control systems. Sodium-specific evidence is newer and

particularly relevant here. Ziegler et al. (2023) showed that early-life sodium deprivation in mice

programmes sustained changes in ingestive behaviour, autonomic function, and energy

expenditure, while Ziegler et al. (2025) showed that sodium restriction during a critical

developmental period programmes later changes in autonomic cardiovascular control,

salt-sensitive blood-pressure responses, and losartan sensitivity, even without a dramatic resting

phenotype. This supports the possibility that repeated developmental sodium-volume instability

could leave a durable bias in controller thresholds and response logic.

Autism cohort findings should be read only as compatibility evidence. Ming et al. (2005) reported

lower resting cardiac vagal tone and lower cardiac sensitivity to baroreflex in children with

autism. Bricout et al. (2018), however, found a different pattern, with higher supine baroreflex

sensitivity and stronger parasympathetic responses during head-up tilt in a subgroup without overt

clinical dysautonomia, underscoring heterogeneity rather than a single fixed autonomic lesion.

Thapa et al. (2019) reported reduced resting heart-rate variability in adults with autism. On the



vasopressin side, Oztan et al. (2018) found lower cerebrospinal-fluid vasopressin in autistic

children, and Parker et al. (2019) reported that intranasal vasopressin improved social deficits in a

pilot trial. These observations are compatible with altered controller calibration and altered

vasopressin biology, but they do not establish developmental sodium-volume instability as the

cause of autism.

Taken together, the evidence supports a bounded but substantive claim. Central autonomic control

circuits are plastic; hydromineral stress can alter baroreflex performance, remodel

vasopressin-neurone operating state, and recruit PVN–NTS–RVLM circuitry in ways that favour

pressure-preserving rescue. Developmental programming studies then support the possibility that

early repeated challenge could make such changes durable. Within the sodium-struggle

framework, recurrent developmental sodium-volume instability could therefore bias the controller

toward faster sympathetic recruitment, tighter AVP-autonomic coupling, and less flexible vagal

buffering under challenge. Once that bias becomes recurrent, downstream vascular beds are no

longer exposed to neutral haemodynamic conditions. They are exposed to a repeated instruction

set of vasomotor tone, flow redistribution, and altered recovery kinetics. That recurring

haemodynamic environment provides the bridge to the next section, where vascular structure and

perfusion reserve are considered as downstream imprints rather than as primary initiating events.


26 Vascular tree imprinting: calibre bias, microvascular

density, and perfusion constraint [H]

Beyond transient Ang II-mediated constriction, recurrent osmotic or endocrine oscillations may

imprint maladaptive RAAS- and AVP-receptor profiles within the placental vascular tree itself.

Depending on exposure pattern and the prevailing balance among vasopressin, RAAS, and

cortisol during disturbance episodes, some placentas may develop a hyper-responsive,

high-resistance state, whereas others remain relatively unaffected. Over-expression or

sensitization of AGTR1 (AT1) or AVPR1A (V1a) could, in certain cases, “lock” the placental bed

into a constricted mode that amplifies fetal hypoperfusion even after maternal hormones

normalize. In other cases, alternate receptor tuning or milder osmotic shifts may preserve

adequate flow, producing subtler or predominantly endocrine (rather than vascular) consequences.

This variability aligns with the clinical heterogeneity of autism and underscores that

receptor-imprinted placental constriction is a potential mechanism - not a universal one - within

the broader Sodium-Switch framework. Clinically, a sustained high-resistance state can manifest

as intra-uterine growth restriction (IUGR), a recognized endpoint of placental perfusion stress.

Because fetal cortisol, AVP, and RAAS patterns can modulate choroid-plexus function, placental

hypoperfusion trajectories plausibly couple to cerebrospinal-fluid production dynamics - a

testable extension of the same hemodynamic-endocrine pathway.


26.1 Energetic Paradox - Oxygen Dependence of the Sodium Pump.

Excessive maternal RAAS activation can paradoxically induce fetal hyponatremia. Elevated

angiotensin II constricts uteroplacental arteries, reducing oxygen delivery to the placenta and

fetus while maintaining maternal pressure. The resulting fetal-placental hypoxia limits



Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase activity within the fetal kidney and trophoblast, weakening sodium reabsorption

despite high circulating renin-angiotensin tone. The outcome is a mixed state - maternal

hyper-RAAS with fetal sodium loss and cortisol compensation - that reinforces the

Sodium-Switch geometry described in this model. Because the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase is highly

oxygen-dependent, this mechanism illustrates how oxygen deprivation can compromise the

fundamental ion-exchange system on which many later mechanisms rely.


26.2 Cross-reference

Cross-reference. The same pattern of placental tree constriction and oxygen-limited

sodium-potassium exchange described here underlies several later sections of this manuscript specifically §10.2 (Fetal AVP Excess), § 10.5 (RAAS Suppression → Cortisol Dominance), and §

10.6 (Salt-Wasting Phenotypes). Each of these sections illustrates a distinct physiological

outcome of placental or fetal hypoperfusion, building on the hemodynamic and energetic

principles established in § 11.6.


27 Systemic Vascular Imprinting: Resistance, Capacitance, and

Interface Calibration Under Early Sodium–Volume Instability

27.1 Framing and Hierarchy

In the Sodium–Volume–Tonicity Defence framework, “vascular imprinting” denotes durable

shifts in how resistance vessels, capacitance vessels, and neurovascular interfaces express

controller signals after patterned early-life instability in effective circulating volume (ECV) and

extracellular tonicity. The primary claim is functional: repeated recruitment of

baroreceptor-linked and osmoreceptor-linked controllers during sensitive windows can recalibrate

vascular gain, reserve, and recovery kinetics. Structural claims about microvascular geometry are

secondary, conditional, and require geometry-level evidence.

This module does not assume vascular primacy and does not require abnormal resting cerebral

perfusion. Resting perfusion can be typical while discriminating differences appear during state

transitions, including orthostasis, cold exposure, controlled CO₂ manipulation, hydration shifts,

fever, and inflammatory states. Because most vascular readouts are state-sensitive, interpretation

requires explicit control of CO₂, temperature, hydration, posture, arousal and motion, and

vasoactive medications. Autism-associated vascular reports are heterogeneous and are used only

to motivate discriminating tests.

Mechanistic hierarchy: patterned ECV or tonicity instability (inputs) engages baroreceptors,

osmoreceptors, and renal NaCl sensing (sensors), recruits RAAS, vasopressin (AVP) signalling,

sympathetic vasoconstrictor and venoconstrictor output, and cortisol-linked signalling (effectors),

which act through endothelium, vascular smooth muscle, pericytes, and venous reservoirs

(interfaces) to bias baseline resistance, venous allocation, cerebrovascular reserve, and drainage



dynamics (network outcomes). Imaging and physiological tests are treated as readouts that require

paired validation.


27.2 Early-Life Sodium–Volume Instability Index (SVII)

Branch inference requires an exposure measure. The Early-Life Sodium–Volume Instability Index

(SVII) is a structured composite intended to quantify patterned instability in ECV and

extracellular tonicity across prenatal life, early infancy, and the “walking window”. SVII is not a

single serum sodium value. It is defined by episode frequency (F), episode amplitude (A_ECV

and A_Ton), recovery kinetics (R), controller engagement gain (C, emphasising response curves

and hysteresis rather than baseline levels), and developmental window weighting (W).

Conceptually:

SVII_total = Σ (W_window × [F × A × R × C]_window)

The manuscript does not require a final numeric scale at this stage, but it requires an SVII

exposure–response relationship for vascular claims. Crucially, vascular phenotypes are predicted

to scale with SVII severity rather than diagnosis alone.

Within high-SVII individuals, a pre-specified polarity stratifier constrains structural

interpretation.


27.2.1 Volume-Dominant Phenotype

A volume-dominant phenotype is defined by relatively stronger AVP/V2-linked

water-conservation signatures for a given osmotic context, for example higher urine osmolality

and lower free-water clearance.


27.2.2 Tone-Dominant Phenotype

A tone-dominant phenotype is defined by steeper vasoconstrictor and venoconstrictor response

slopes under provocation, for example cold or orthostatic challenge, with relatively less emphasis

on water-conservation markers.

Polarity assignment must precede geometry analysis. Failure of polarity mapping weakens the

conditional structural module and does not permit post hoc reassignment of phenotype labels to

preserve structural coherence.


27.3 Functional Gain and Reserve Calibration (Primary Branch)

Repeated early recruitment of RAAS, AVP-linked signalling, sympathetic output, and

cortisol-linked signalling is hypothesised to recalibrate how readily constrictor and capacitance



responses are recruited, how strongly they scale with perturbation magnitude, and how rapidly

they reset.


27.3.1 Resistance Vessel Gain Calibration

Three calibrations are anticipated as non-exclusive possibilities in high-SVII histories:

●​ Lower activation thresholds for constrictor recruitment​

●​ Higher gain in one or more effector pathways with reserve compression through earlier

saturation​

●​ Slower recovery kinetics with increased hysteresis after perturbation​


These are not claims of persistent vasoconstriction. They are claims about altered

stimulus–response relationships and recovery time constants across states, including potential

compression of neurovascular reserve.


27.3.2 Venous Capacitance and Splanchnic Redistribution

Venous capacitance and splanchnic reservoir allocation are co-primary with arteriolar resistance.

Early-life oscillations would be expected to bias how strongly venous return is defended in

upright posture and how readily the splanchnic bed is recruited as a reservoir. These links are

elaborated in the manuscript’s splanchnic and walking-window sections; the requirement here is

that any venous imprint be tested directly rather than inferred from cerebral perfusion.


27.4 Contractile Gain Modifiers (Secondary Within

Functional Branch)

Endocrine and sympathetic inputs are expressed through local transduction. Several modifiers can

alter vascular gain without becoming primary drivers of the manuscript.


27.4.1 Receptor and Signal Transduction Modifiers

Receptor density and coupling, including AT₁R and V1a-linked constrictor signalling, can shift

effective gain.


27.4.2 Calcium Entry and Sensitivity



Ca²⁺ entry and Ca²⁺ sensitivity remain the proximal effectors of contraction, with altered channel

availability or downstream sensitivity modifying how strongly a given depolarising input

produces force.


27.4.3 Chloride Handling as an Excitability Lever

Chloride handling functions as a gain-setting lever for contractile excitability in smooth muscle

and pericytes by shifting resting membrane potential and influencing the probability of Ca²⁺

channel activation. Notably, chloride participates in both osmotic stability under hyponatraemic

or hypo-osmolar threat and in contractile responsiveness under hypovolaemic pressure defence,

illustrating how conserved ionic mechanisms are reused across neural and vascular domains

during sodium–volume stress. This dual role does not imply a chloride-centric model but

highlights a shared stabilisation axis.


27.4.4 Purinergic and Shear-Mediated Modulation

Extracellular ATP is treated as a plausible shear and strain mediator. Shear-dependent ATP release

and purinergic signalling can amplify or dampen endothelial and pericyte responses under stress

conditions, without assuming chronically elevated ATP.


27.4.5 Membrane Microdomain Effects

Membrane cholesterol and microdomain organisation can modify receptor clustering and channel

kinetics, acting as slow amplifiers of gain calibration rather than independent drivers.


27.5 Measurement Bundles and Two-Modality Null Rule

Functional calibration claims require paired readouts plus state control.


27.5.1 Resistance Gain and Reserve

Require CO₂ CVR mapping plus an independent endothelial or resistance-vessel test such as

flow-mediated dilation or plethysmography.


27.5.2 Capacitance and Redistribution

Require standardised tilt or active-stand haemodynamics with continuous blood pressure plus a

redistribution measure.

State control minimum includes end-tidal CO₂, temperature, hydration, posture, arousal and

motion, and vasoactive medications.



Two-modality null rule: in high-SVII strata, if both CO₂ CVR and reactive hyperaemia are

normal, and tilt or stand haemodynamics and redistribution measures are normal, with no SVII

exposure–response relationship, this branch is substantially weakened.


27.6 Neurovascular Interface Domain

This section separates coupling dynamics, BBB set-point calibration, and drainage dynamics

under a unified interface framework while preserving distinct falsifiers.


27.6.1 Coupling Dynamics

Neurovascular coupling maps neural activity to local haemodynamic responses. Under repeated

early-life instability, coupling can be recalibrated without chronic hypoperfusion. The

hypothesised imprint is altered latency, gain, or saturation behaviour during challenge states.

Required paired readouts include electrophysiology-anchored neural timing paired with perfusion

timing measures, plus independent reserve metrics such as CO₂ CVR. Two-modality null applies.


27.6.2 BBB Set-Point Calibration

BBB claims are framed as developmental calibration of transport and tight-junction regulation

rather than assumed chronic permeability. Operational definition of “leak” must be used if

invoked. Two-modality null applies.


27.6.3 Drainage and Perivascular Dynamics

Perivascular space metrics are treated as downstream readouts reflecting altered pulsatility,

venous outflow, and sleep-state dynamics. They are not treated as proxies for BBB permeability.

Two-modality null applies accordingly.


27.7 Conditional Structural Module: Angiogenic Stabilisation and

Polarity Constraint

Structural vascular claims remain conditional. Repeated early-life instability may shift angiogenic

stabilisation timing and shear-guided maturation, yielding measurable differences in

microvascular density or branching geometry in some high-SVII histories.

Within high-SVII strata:

●​ Volume-dominant physiology is predicted to bias toward conditional overpatterning or

altered branching complexity, including increased tortuosity and delayed pruning.​



●​ Tone-dominant physiology is predicted to bias toward conditional rarefaction associated

with sustained shear reduction.​


Failure of pre-specified polarity mapping weakens this module and does not permit

reinterpretation after the fact.

Structural inference from perfusion alone is disallowed.


27.8 Guardrails and Disconfirmation

State-control minimum, exposure–response requirement, and two-modality null rules apply across

all branches. Diagnosis-only contrasts are insufficient.


27.9 Energetic Integration

If baseline resistance is calibrated upward or reserve is compressed, achieving a given increment

in functional hyperaemia requires increased contractile and transport work at the vessel wall.

Mechanistically, this reflects increased Ca²⁺ duty-cycle within contractile cells and amplified

shear-dependent endothelial work, including ATP-mediated purinergic signalling. Under these

conditions, achieving equivalent functional hyperaemia requires greater ATP expenditure at the

neurovascular interface, effectively imposing a metabolic tax on coupling bandwidth during

high-demand states. These vascular calibrations are positioned alongside other downstream

sodium–volume defence imprints and interact with neuronal chloride-weighted maturation and

receptor imprinting logic rather than replacing them.

27.10 Cross-Threat Chloride Reuse: A Shared Stabilisation Axis in the Sodium-Struggle

Framework

Chloride participates in both osmotic defence under hypo-osmolality and pressure/volume

defence through renal NaCl sensing and vascular excitability, although these roles are typically

developed in separate literatures. In osmotic contexts, chloride is treated as a rapid ionic

participant in cell-volume control and extracellular space preservation under hypotonic stress. In

volume/pressure contexts, chloride appears in NaCl sensing at the macula densa with downstream

RAAS coupling, and as a gain-setting excitability modifier in vascular smooth muscle and

pericytes. Section 19 has already positioned these mechanisms hierarchically: endocrine and

autonomic controllers remain primary drivers, while local ionic handling shapes how those

signals are expressed.

The synthesis advanced here is that these established roles represent reuse of a shared

chloride-linked stabilisation axis across two recurrent survival threats: extracellular tonicity

instability and effective circulating volume instability. In both contexts, chloride functions as a



coupled constraint at interfaces where rapid stabilisation is required. It contributes to volume

recovery because water redistribution is solute-constrained, and in osmotic contexts it can also

function as a rapidly adjustable osmolyte through cation–chloride cotransport systems, allowing

fast volume stabilisation when compatible osmolytes recover more slowly. It preserves

electroneutrality because large ionic shifts require counter-ion balance. It also influences

membrane-potential regulation because chloride gradients shape the probability that depolarising

inputs will recruit calcium entry and contraction. This cross-threat reuse does not confer primacy

to chloride. Rather, chloride is treated as a stabilisation interface through which higher-level

controllers such as RAAS, AVP signalling, and sympathetic output are transduced under

instability.

Within the Sodium-Struggle framework, this reuse becomes consequential because SVII

formalises patterned early-life exposure to both tonicity and volume perturbations. Repeated

recruitment of chloride-dependent stabilisation modules during sensitive windows would be

expected to alter recovery kinetics and response gains. Under conditions of frequent oscillation

between swelling risk and perfusion risk, incomplete resetting may occur. In that setting, a

gradual drift in baseline intracellular chloride, altered cotransporter gain, and slower chloride

recovery kinetics becomes a plausible calibration outcome rather than an initiating lesion. The

distinction between transient chloride flux and a shifted chloride operating point therefore

becomes central.

This framing requires explicit guardrails. The cross-threat reuse hypothesis is not a

chloride-centric reformulation of sodium–volume defence. It does not displace RAAS, AVP,

catecholamines, nitric oxide signalling, potassium conductances, or organic osmolyte systems.

Chloride is positioned as a coupled constraint and gain modifier that influences thresholds, slopes,

and recovery kinetics at renal and vascular interfaces. The novelty lies in proposing that the same

chloride-linked stabilisation logic is repeatedly engaged across distinct threats in a manner that

may permit calibration costs to accumulate under high SVII exposure.

A disconfirmation condition follows from this architecture. The reuse hypothesis would be

weakened if high-SVII exposure predicts endocrine and haemodynamic recalibration without any

convergent chloride-linked signatures across threat classes. Specifically, if individuals or models

with repeated early-life tonicity and volume instability exhibit typical renal NaCl sensing, typical

vascular gain modulation, and no durable shift in intracellular chloride baselines or

chloride-dependent recovery kinetics beyond non-specific stress effects, then chloride reuse

would not function as a unifying stabilisation axis within this framework. In that case, chloride

would remain a set of context-specific mechanisms without cross-threat consolidation.

With these constraints established, the next section narrows from system-level reuse to the

cellular-level imprint that would be expected if resetting is incomplete: elevated intracellular

chloride as a durable operating-point shift. The aim is to specify how repeated recruitment of this

stabilisation interface may leave a persistent intracellular chloride trace that interacts with the

endocrine, autonomic, and vascular calibration logic already developed.

28.1 Scope and framing



Persistently elevated intracellular chloride is framed here as a proximal cellular imprint

that can emerge downstream of repeated early-life hyponatraemia, hypo-osmolality,

hypotonic exposure, and/or effective circulating-volume instability, including states in

which baroreceptor-linked signalling makes effective volume unreliable despite

apparently adequate total body water. The chloride-weighted state is not treated as the

initiating insult, not as a synapse-only or neurotransmitter-only lesion, and not as

autism-specific pathology. Rather, it is interpreted as a durable volume-defence baseline

that can sit upstream of later inhibitory, metabolic, redox, and network-level constraints.

This ordering remains volume-first and ionic-first, with metabolism, extracellular ATP,

ROS, perfusion effects, and inflammation entering later as downstream or convergent

amplifiers rather than as default primaries.

A glia-first expectation is important from the outset. Astrocytes are the principal CNS

volume buffers and are equipped with NKCC1, AQP4, VRAC, Kir4.1, and

connexin-coupled buffering networks. Neurons, by contrast, must preserve low

intracellular chloride if GABAergic inhibition is to become strongly hyperpolarising. That

asymmetry makes astrocytes more permissive than neurons to assume a chloride-based

defensive burden under repeated osmotic challenge, with neuronal chloride

consequences expected to emerge secondarily through extracellular-space

compression, potassium accumulation, glutamatergic spillover, and microdomain

coupling. This compartmental framing is maintained throughout: serum and CSF chloride

are not equated with astrocytic or neuronal intracellular chloride.

In essence, repeated osmotic and volume-regulatory stress may gradually bias brain

tissue toward a chloride-weighted intracellular operating point in which volume safety is

prioritised over inhibitory precision.

28.2 Mandatory serial causal arc

The mandatory causal arc remains ionic-first. Repeated hypo-osmolality or related

volume instability drives water entry and swelling. Swelling triggers regulatory volume

decrease (RVD), with efflux of chloride, potassium, and compatible organic osmolytes.

Repetition produces osmolyte debt and constrained recovery. Under those conditions,

the least costly recovered state may no longer be the original state. Astrocytes are the

most likely first compartment to defend volume through a chloride-weighted solution, via

sustained NKCC1-biased ion handling and water re-entry, while neuronal chloride reset

may become delayed, blunted, or unstable where KCC2-dependent extrusion matures

incompletely or is secondarily destabilised.

Raising intracellular chloride shifts E_Cl in the positive direction and brings E_GABA

closer to resting membrane potential, so GABA_A openings exert less hyperpolarising

and less stabilising leverage and, in some states, become weakly depolarising rather

than protective. The expected consequence is not uniform excitation. It is a higher

probability that routine activity produces small subthreshold depolarisations, more

frequent openings of voltage-gated Ca²⁺ channels, and, during coincident glutamatergic



activity, more frequent relief of the Mg²⁺ block of NMDA receptors. Repeated

frequency-encoded Ca²⁺ entry then provides a plausible route to biased developmental

signalling and transcriptional persistence.

Sustained NKCC1-mediated chloride loading also entails coupled Na⁺ entry. Because

intracellular sodium must still be kept low, Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase turnover rises, ATP margin

narrows, and the system becomes more vulnerable to extracellular ATP signalling, ROS

amplification, perfusion constraint, and later chloride reset failure. Recurrent

hypo-osmolality is the clearest entry route emphasised here, but it need not be the only

one. A similar chloride-weighted intracellular state could also be entered through

ROS-mediated impairment of KCC2 or other transport components, or through primary

ATP limitation that weakens pump support for Na⁺ and K⁺ gradients and thereby biases

cation-chloride cotransport toward chloride retention. These routes are best understood

as convergent and mutually reinforcing, not mutually exclusive.

28.3 Initiating physiology: hypotonic threat to cell volume under a low-sodium

constraint

Cells match osmolality, not sodium concentration. Hypotonic extracellular fluid threatens

cell volume through water influx, whereas intracellular sodium must remain low for

electrochemical, enzymatic, and energetic reasons. The hierarchy should therefore be

explicit: ATP supports Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase activity; the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase establishes Na⁺ and K⁺

gradients; those gradients support NKCC1 and KCC2 behaviour; intracellular chloride is

the downstream state variable. This means that ATP limitation can be either a

downstream consequence or an alternative upstream trigger. If pump support narrows

early, Na⁺ and K⁺ gradients degrade, cotransporter balance shifts, and chloride reset may

fail even without a large preceding osmotic insult.

The initiating physiology can also be widened without changing the causal order.

Solute-poor water replacement can add to dilutional stress when water intake outpaces

electrolyte replacement. In susceptible settings, mineral-poor or very

low-total-dissolved-solids water, such as reverse-osmosis or distilled water without

mineral replacement, can contribute to a hypo-osmolar load if intake is substantial and

recovery solute is limited. One concrete physiological example that fits this broader logic

is severe vomiting, especially maternal hyperemesis gravidarum and, more

speculatively, prolonged severe infant vomiting states. Such states can combine chloride

depletion, metabolic alkalosis, effective volume instability, and non-osmotic arginine

vasopressin (AVP) release. In that setting, WNK-SPAK-OSR1 and cation-chloride

cotransporter signalling could plausibly be biased toward an NKCC1-dominant recovery

mode during rehydration, fluctuating solute replacement, or repeated volume correction.

28.4 Acute response: swelling, regulatory volume decrease, and the RVD-chloride

accumulation paradox



The acute RVD-chloride accumulation paradox is only apparent. Classical swelling

physiology remains intact at the level of the individual episode. Swollen cells open

pathways that permit net loss of KCl and compatible organic osmolytes, often through

VRAC and KCC-linked mechanisms, and water follows osmotically. The relevant

extension concerns the recovery phase and the reset between episodes.

Osmotic-stress-dependent regulation of cation-chloride cotransporters is already

established in renal physiology, epithelial transport, and brain oedema or cell-volume

research. This framework therefore integrates those literatures into developmental

neurophysiology rather than introducing a new transport principle.

In astrocytes and other volume-loaded CNS settings, NKCC1 can materially contribute to

recovery and to pathological ion-water loading. The claim, therefore, is not that RVD

fails. It is that repeated RVD, repeated rebuilding, and repeated transporter

reconfiguration can progressively alter what counts as baseline. Each episode can still

export chloride acutely while the long-term system drifts toward higher resting astrocytic

chloride, slower neuronal chloride reset, or both.

28.5 From repeated RVD to baseline drift: osmolyte debt, constrained recovery,

and stabilisation of a new operating point

The conceptual centre of the section is osmolyte debt. Repeated RVD episodes are not

merely disruptive. They are progressively costly because they repeatedly dump

potassium and compatible osmolytes whose restoration is slower than inorganic-ion

redistribution and increasingly constrained by ATP availability, transporter abundance,

transcriptional capacity, and inter-episode time. Compatible osmolytes protect enzyme

function, macromolecular folding, and cytosolic biochemical stability, but they are not

rebuilt rapidly. Repeated loss with incomplete restitution therefore creates a cumulative

debt rather than a series of isolated perturbations.

Under those conditions, perfect return to the original baseline after every episode may

no longer be the lowest-cost solution. A slightly altered baseline can reduce the need for

repeated rapid osmolyte dumping, repeated potassium loss, and repeated full

reconstruction of the prior organic osmolyte pool. In that sense, osmolyte debt gives the

system a physiological reason to defend a new operating point rather than repeatedly

reconstruct the old one. It is the organising principle linking episodic volume stress to

persistence. The most plausible stabilising mechanisms are slowed recovery kinetics,

altered VRAC response thresholds, repeated recruitment of chloride-sensitive recovery

signalling, and a durable shift in transporter and kinase gain toward a chloride-weighted

resting state. This remains a plausible extension rather than an already demonstrated

continuous in vivo chain, but it follows directly from well-established principles of

cell-volume regulation, compatible-osmolyte biology, and transporter plasticity.

28.6 Why chloride becomes the defended intracellular osmolyte



Under constrained recovery, chloride becomes the fastest defended intracellular

osmolyte. It is abundant, rapidly adjustable through existing transport systems,

secondary-active rather than directly ATP-hydrolysing at the transporter itself, and tightly

coupled to water movement. NKCC1 exploits the Na⁺ gradient generated by the

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase, allowing rapid osmolyte adjustment without direct ATP hydrolysis at the

cotransporter itself. In that limited energetic and kinetic sense, chloride is the cheap and

quick osmolyte, although not a free one, because its defence still increases downstream

pump work.

Compatible osmolytes such as taurine, betaine, myo-inositol, sorbitol, and related

amino-acid-derived or methylamine osmolytes are, in biochemical terms, better

osmolytes. They can accumulate to high concentrations with far less disturbance of

cytosolic ionic strength, macromolecular stability, enzyme function, and protein folding.

The difficulty is speed and recovery cost. Their replenishment depends on uptake

systems or synthesis over longer time scales, often under NFAT5-linked transcriptional

control and with meaningful energetic demand. Under repeated challenge, especially

when recovery windows are short, chloride may therefore be used more heavily as the

fast substitute even though it is functionally less compatible. The tradeoff is central:

chloride is energetically economical for volume defence, but in neural tissue it is

functionally costly. A chloride-weighted solution carries costs for inhibitory polarity, Ca²⁺

signalling duty cycle, Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase workload, and later redox, perfusion, and

transcriptional stability. Because neurons must preserve low intracellular chloride for

inhibitory polarity whereas astrocytes do not, this trade is more available to astrocytes

than to neurons.

28.7 Mechanisms that stabilise a chloride-weighted operating point

28.7.1 NKCC1/KCC2 balance and structural persistence

A sustained contribution of astrocytic NKCC1-mediated chloride accumulation, together

with delayed, blunted, or unstable neuronal KCC2 upregulation, provides a direct route

to a chloride-weighted intracellular state. The cross-field bridge should be explicit:

osmotic-stress-dependent regulation of cation-chloride cotransporters is already

recognised in renal physiology, epithelial transport, and brain oedema or cell-volume

research. The physiological elements are therefore not novel at the level of core

transport biology. What is more specific here is the ordering claim, namely that repeated

osmotic challenge in a developing brain could stabilise a recovered baseline that

remains chloride-weighted beyond individual episodes.

In neurons, incomplete maturation or renewed destabilisation of KCC2 preserves higher

chloride and weakens the normal shift toward strongly hyperpolarising GABA. In

astrocytes, persistent NKCC1-biased handling can sustain chloride and water loading

without the same inhibitory-polarity constraint. Recovery may be further slowed if KCC2

surface stability is reduced by internalisation and degradation, producing a lag that

outlasts the initiating stress. This interpretation links established transporter physiology



with developmental persistence rather than treating developmental persistence as a

separate transport mechanism.

28.7.2 WNK-SPAK-OSR1 gain-setting and degradation control

WNK-SPAK-OSR1 provides the most direct molecular bridge between intracellular

chloride, osmotic history, and transporter state. WNK kinases are bona fide

chloride-sensitive kinases, and reductions in intracellular chloride disinhibit WNK activity,

which in turn favours phosphorylation states that activate NKCC pathways and suppress

KCC pathways. The CUL3-KLHL3 module adds a persistence layer by governing WNK

degradation. Within this framework, repeated osmotic cycling, repeated recovery from

chloride loss, gradient instability, or incomplete restitution could increase the fraction of

time this system spends in an NKCC1-favouring configuration.

Importantly, the model does not require WNK signalling to remain tonically maximal once

intracellular chloride has drifted upward. It requires recurrent episodes of chloride

depletion or unstable recovery during RVD to repeatedly bias kinase state, transporter

trafficking, and reset kinetics in the same direction. If relaxation between episodes is

incomplete, the recovered baseline can progressively move even though the signalling

pulses are episodic. That would not require a new transport principle, only a durable shift

in signalling gain and reset fidelity.

28.7.3 NFAT5-dependent transcription and transporter localisation

NFAT5 should be foregrounded as a molecular anchor. NFAT5 is the canonical

tonicity-responsive transcription factor in mammalian osmoadaptation and one of the

clearest examples that osmotic stress can produce durable transcriptional adaptation. Its

relevance here is not that it alone proves a specifically hypo-osmotic mechanism.

Rather, it demonstrates that cells already possess recognised transcriptional machinery

for converting repeated tonicity disturbance into altered osmolyte-handling gene

expression. That logic strengthens, rather than competes with, the chloride-weighted

model.

In a developing brain repeatedly exposed to osmotic instability, NFAT5-dependent and

related osmoadaptive programmes could plausibly contribute to lasting changes in

compatible-osmolyte handling, transporter abundance, transporter localisation, and

recovery thresholds, especially in glial microdomains where channel polarisation and

cotransporter positioning materially shape local volume behaviour. Its inclusion therefore

helps anchor durability at the molecular level rather than leaving persistence to inference

alone.

28.7.4 Mechanotransduction and cytoskeletal stabilisation

Repeated swelling also creates a mechanotransductive route to persistence.

Mechanosensitive Ca²⁺-entry pathways, including TRPV4 and likely other

stretch-sensitive channels, respond to cell-volume increase and membrane deformation.



Recurrent activation can couple mechanical stress to calcium-dependent kinase states,

cytoskeletal remodelling, and altered transporter anchoring. This offers a plausible route

whereby repeated swelling episodes do not merely transiently perturb ion flux, but

gradually stabilise a new transport topology in specific subcellular regions.

28.8 Acid-base coupling and bicarbonate dependence of GABA_A currents

Any chloride-centred account must also specify acid-base coupling. GABA_A currents

depend on both chloride and bicarbonate, not chloride alone. Carbonic anhydrases

replenish intracellular bicarbonate, and SLC4-family bicarbonate transporters together

with chloride-bicarbonate exchangers help set the effective anion gradients seen by

GABA_A receptors. Accordingly, a chloride-weighted intracellular state may be

functionally amplified or partially masked by local pH and bicarbonate handling. The

narrow claim here is not that acid-base disturbance is the primary driver. It is that

bicarbonate dependence makes the consequence of a given chloride set-point

context-sensitive, and therefore strengthens the case for keeping chloride, bicarbonate,

pH, and transporter state within the same physiological frame.

28.9 Functional output: altered GABA polarity and calcium entry frequency

In a chloride-weighted intracellular state, elevated intracellular chloride shifts E_Cl in the

positive direction and therefore shifts E_GABA toward resting membrane potential. As

E_GABA approaches resting membrane potential, a given GABA_A conductance

provides less hyperpolarising reserve and less shunting leverage. GABA_A openings

therefore become less hyperpolarising and, depending on state and bicarbonate

contribution, may become weakly depolarising or less stabilising. The key claim is not

uniformly excitatory GABA. It is weaker inhibitory leverage.

When routine synaptic activity more often leaves membranes nearer the activation range

of voltage-gated Ca²⁺ channels, small Ca²⁺ entry events become more frequent. During

coincident glutamatergic activity, the same depolarising bias can more readily relieve the

Mg²⁺ block of NMDA receptors. The relevant variable is therefore the probability and

frequency of small Ca²⁺ entries during ordinary network use, not necessarily the

amplitude of any single event.

28.10 Frequency-encoded calcium signalling and developmental bias

This frequency-over-amplitude distinction matters developmentally. Repeated small Ca²⁺

entries provide a plausible route to frequency-encoded signalling that can bias gene

expression, maturation timing, and chromatin occupancy during sensitive windows. A
