chloride-weighted state would be expected to increase the chance that routine activity

produces subthreshold depolarisations, and thus to increase Ca²⁺ duty cycle even if

gross excitability appears near normal at rest. In a system with intact transcriptional

reset, that may remain graded and reversible. In a system with reduced reset fidelity, the

same increase in signalling throughput could more readily stabilise defensive



configurations. The model therefore links chloride weighting to developmental

persistence through signalling frequency rather than through a claim of one universal

calcium abnormality.

28.11 Energetic cost, ROS amplification, and extracellular ATP signalling

A sustained contribution of NKCC1 to chloride loading entails coupled Na⁺ entry and

therefore greater Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase work to keep intracellular sodium low. The

consequence is not necessarily frank energy failure at baseline, but a narrower energetic

reserve under load. This ordering matters. In this framework, metabolic strain may arise

downstream of chronic ionic defence even if it can also serve as an alternative upstream

trigger in other cases. As membrane stretch and swelling recur, extracellular ATP

release is best viewed as a downstream broadcast of mechanical and energetic strain

rather than a primary cause of chloride weighting.

ROS then creates a secondary reinforcing loop that should be made explicit. Oxidant

pressure can deplete or oxidise BH4; low BH4 promotes NOS uncoupling; uncoupled

NOS produces less NO and more superoxide; reduced NO biases toward

vasoconstriction and poorer effective microvascular adequacy; and that circulatory signal

may favour baroreceptor-linked non-osmotic AVP release. AVP can then reinforce renal

water retention, hypo-osmolar pressure, and renewed swelling stress. The same oxidant

pressure can also impair transporter integrity or surface stability, providing a more direct

convergent route into chloride-retention bias. This ROS-low-NO-AVP pathway is best

understood as a secondary amplifier, not the sole primary trigger.

28.12 Glia-first localisation, extracellular-space constraints, and use-dependent

unmasking

Astrocytes are more likely than neurons to adopt chloride-based volume defence for two

linked reasons. First, they carry the principal burden of water handling and spatial

buffering. Second, they are not constrained to preserve low intracellular chloride for

inhibitory signalling polarity. They already express the relevant machinery for a defensive

volume-buffering role, including NKCC1, AQP4, VRAC, Kir4.1, and connexin-coupled

networks that couple ion influx, water movement, potassium siphoning, and lateral

redistribution of local burden.

Astrocytic chloride retention via NKCC1-linked ion loading increases astrocytic volume

loading, reduces extracellular-space volume fraction, and increases diffusion resistance.

Because the extracellular space constitutes only a modest fraction of brain volume, even

modest astrocytic volume changes can disproportionately alter diffusion conditions and

the concentration impact of activity-dependent ion and transmitter release. A smaller

extracellular space allows activity-dependent K⁺ and glutamate to accumulate faster. At

the same time, astrocytic swelling and ionic stress can erode glutamate-clearance

margin and potassium-buffering efficiency. The predicted result is a load-sensitive

microenvironment that may appear near normal at rest but destabilise under repeated



demand, with fast-spiking parvalbumin interneurons especially vulnerable because their

high-frequency firing and high metabolic burden leave limited reserve against

extracellular K⁺ rise, glutamatergic spillover, or chloride-dependent weakening of

inhibition.

28.13 Developmental timing windows favouring imprint formation

The probability of imprint formation depends strongly on timing. Fetal and early postnatal

periods combine high osmotic plasticity with incomplete chloride maturation. Before

robust neuronal chloride extrusion is established, and before astrocytic end-foot

organisation and extracellular-space regulation are fully stabilised, repeated tonicity or

volume instability is more likely to be embedded as altered gain-setting rather than

treated as a transient perturbation. The developmental GABA switch is especially

relevant here: if chloride extrusion matures late, intermittently, or at higher energetic

cost, the system may remain longer in a state where volume safety and inhibitory

precision are in tension.

28.14 Genetic susceptibility as modifier logic

Genetic susceptibility is best framed as modifier logic rather than deterministic cause.

Variants or expression differences that alter cation-chloride cotransport, WNK

degradation, compatible-osmolyte handling, astrocytic buffering, acid-base coupling,

mitochondrial reserve, or transcriptional reset would be expected to change how readily

a chloride-weighted intracellular state forms, how strongly it is compartmentalised, and

how slowly it resolves. In that framing, genes do not replace physiology. They shape the

gain, persistence, and reversibility of the same volume-defence architecture.

28.14.1 Chromatin regulation as a gain-and-reset modifier under a

chloride-weighted, high-duty-cycle Ca²⁺ operating point

Chromatin regulation is best treated as a gain-and-reset modifier, not as an alternative

initiating lesion. The mechanistic bridge is that chloride weighting increases the duty

cycle of small Ca²⁺ entries during ordinary network use. Chromatin and

transcriptional-state regulators determine how proportionally, how persistently, and how

reversibly those repeated signals are translated into gene-expression programmes. In a

more reset-vulnerable subgroup, the same chloride-to-calcium shift could therefore

produce more persistent transporter, cytoskeletal, or developmental states without

invoking DNA injury or a primary chromatin lesion. This is a plausible extension that may

help explain heterogeneity of persistence, not the origin of chloride weighting itself.

28.15 Downstream consequences and state-dependent unmasking

If a chloride-weighted intracellular state persists through critical windows, several

downstream bridges become more plausible without displacing the initiating chloride-first

logic. One is parvalbumin-interneuron vulnerability and less precise gamma timing.

Fast-spiking PV interneurons are specialised for high-frequency firing and rhythmic



synchrony, which makes them metabolically demanding and unusually sensitive to

disturbances in inhibition, extracellular potassium handling, and conduction precision.

Because these cells operate with narrow firing margins, astrocytic volume loading,

reduced extracellular-space volume fraction, and faster activity-dependent K⁺

accumulation can reduce buffering margin, while chloride-dependent weakening of

inhibition reduces inhibitory reserve; together, those conditions may degrade

gamma-oscillation precision and sensory gain control.

A second is altered oligodendrocyte timing. Oligodendrocyte-lineage cells retain strong

NKCC1 expression, oligodendrocyte maturation and myelination are Ca²⁺-sensitive, and

myelination of PV interneuron axons materially affects conduction velocity and temporal

precision. A chloride-weighted developmental environment could therefore plausibly bias

myelin timing or oligodendrocyte maturation as a downstream consequence rather than

as the initiating lesion. This may be especially relevant on PV interneuron axons, where

small shifts in myelin timing can disproportionately affect conduction precision.

A third is lower reserve against epilepsy-like decompensation, because impaired chloride

extrusion, weakened inhibitory leverage, and reduced extracellular-space buffering

margin all favour hyperexcitable transitions. The same architecture may also increase

sensitivity to sensory load or sleep disruption, producing a system that appears near

normal at baseline but fails earlier under demand. Illness, dehydration, or other states

that increase osmotic, metabolic, or network demand may unmask the same instability

even when baseline function appears near normal.

28.16 The self-perpetuating chloride loop: from osmotic challenge to redox-energy

lock-in

The self-perpetuating loop can now be stated compactly. Repeated swelling or related

volume stress produces osmolyte debt. Under constrained recovery, chloride becomes

the fastest defended osmolyte. Astrocytes, being more permissive to chloride-based

volume defence, take on more of the initial burden. Astrocytic chloride and water loading

compress the extracellular space, increase diffusion resistance, accelerate

activity-dependent K⁺ and glutamate accumulation, and raise the probability of

secondary neuronal chloride consequences. The resulting GABA and Ca²⁺ shifts

increase energetic load through Na⁺-coupled chloride cycling. As ATP margin narrows,

ROS-BH4-NOS-low-NO signalling can tighten perfusion and favour baroreceptor-linked

non-osmotic AVP release, which reinforces renal water retention, renewed hypo-osmolar

pressure, and further swelling stress.

This loop is not offered as the only route into chloride weighting. Energy-first and

redox-first paths remain possible. The narrower claim is that once chloride weighting

forms, it can materially help stabilise its own persistence.

28.17 Operational definition, falsifiability, and interpretive boundaries



This section maintains strict compartment specificity, distinguishing serum and CSF

chloride from astrocytic and neuronal intracellular chloride. It distinguishes acute

compensation from durable imprinting, and it treats inflammation, oxidative signalling,

extracellular ATP, and metabolic strain as integrable amplifiers rather than default

primary causes. The proposed chloride imprint is not autism-specific pathology. Similar

chloride-immature or chloride-destabilised configurations occur in seizures, trauma,

hypoxia, and inflammatory states.

The physiological elements described here are individually well established. The specific

claim concerns ordering and persistence, namely whether repeated osmotic challenge,

through osmolyte debt and incomplete recovery, can stabilise a chloride-weighted

intracellular operating point that persists beyond individual episodes under comparable

extracellular conditions. That claim is falsifiable. It predicts persistence beyond initiating

episodes, a shifted resting baseline or slowed return, likely astrocyte-dominant early

burden with secondary neuronal readouts, and dependence on durable mechanisms

such as NKCC1/KCC2 imbalance, WNK-SPAK-OSR1 gain-setting, NFAT5-linked

transcription, altered transporter localisation, and narrowed energetic reserve. It is

weakened if repeated osmotic challenge leaves no persistence effect once extracellular

conditions normalise, or if osmolyte debt fails to predict delayed chloride reset. No

therapeutic inference is implied by this mechanistic framing alone.


29 Calcium Signalling in a Chloride-Weighted Ionic

Neighbourhood

29.1 Calcium findings in autism research

Calcium abnormalities are reported across a wide range of autism spectrum disorder (ASD)

models, but the recurring picture is not a single uniform “calcium defect”. Rather, the literature

shows a distributed disturbance across entry, clearance, intracellular store handling, mitochondrial

uptake, and network-level calcium activity. Patient-derived NRXN1α+/− cortical neurons have

shown increased frequency, duration, and amplitude of spontaneous Ca²⁺ transients with

enrichment of voltage-gated calcium-channel pathways, whereas other idiopathic ASD

iPSC-derived neuronal models have shown fewer spontaneous calcium transients, weaker calcium

waves, and impaired connectivity. In 22q11.2-deletion cortical models, calcium deficits have been

linked to altered resting membrane potential and abnormal inactivation of voltage-gated calcium

channels. A recent idiopathic ASD differentiation study reported an especially instructive split:

ATP-evoked responses were increased at the iPSC stage, but differentiated ASD neurons showed

reduced ATP-evoked Ca²⁺ responses together with increased KCl- and DHPG-evoked responses.

Abnormalities in intracellular stores are equally prominent. Fibroblasts from fragile X syndrome,

tuberous sclerosis, and sporadic ASD have shown depressed IP3R-mediated Ca²⁺ release, both at

the level of global responses and local elementary events. In ASD-relevant astrocyte and neuronal

models, mitochondria and endoplasmic reticulum (ER) are also repeatedly implicated: TSC

astrocytes show reduced store-operated Ca²⁺ entry, reduced basal mitochondrial Ca²⁺, reduced Ca²⁺



influx, altered ER-related responses, and diminished responsiveness to stimulation, while

ASD-derived dopaminergic neurons have shown ER Ca²⁺ accumulation together with reduced

mitochondrial Ca²⁺. Taken together, these findings suggest that ASD calcium phenotypes are

heterogeneous in direction but recurrent in domain: spontaneous transients, stimulus-evoked

entry, ER release, SOCE, mitochondrial buffering, and network-level coordination can all be

altered.


29.2 Fragmentation of current explanations

These findings are usually interpreted within separate explanatory compartments. Increased

spontaneous transients are often read as channel or excitability defects; reduced ATP- or

IP3-mediated responses as receptor or ER-store pathology; altered mitochondrial Ca²⁺ as a

bioenergetic lesion; and calcium-channel genetics as evidence for primary calciumopathy. Each of

those interpretations can be valid in its own domain, especially in syndromic cases such as

Timothy syndrome or CACNA1D gain-of-function. However, what is rarely asked is whether

these apparently separate observations are being sampled within a tissue whose ionic baseline has

already shifted.

The preceding chloride section proposed exactly such a shift: repeated osmotic or sodium-volume

regulatory stress may produce osmolyte debt, NKCC1-biased recovery, delayed neuronal chloride

reset, weakened inhibitory leverage, astrocyte volume loading, reduced extracellular-space

margin, and greater Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase workload. If that framework is correct, then at least some

calcium findings need not be read as primary defects of calcium machinery. The key change may

instead lie in membrane operating conditions that alter how often calcium-permissive states occur.

Calcium abnormalities may therefore arise from normal calcium machinery functioning within an

altered ionic neighbourhood rather than from intrinsic calcium-channel pathology.


29.3 Calcium duty-cycle shift in a chloride-weighted ionic

neighbourhood

The most useful reframing is not “more calcium” or “less calcium”, but a shift in calcium duty

cycle: the fraction of routine network activity spent in calcium-permissive membrane states. In

the chloride framework, the key output of higher intracellular chloride is not necessarily frank

hyperexcitation. It is weaker inhibitory leverage: E_GABA moves closer to resting membrane

potential, GABA_A conductances become less hyperpolarising and less stabilising, and routine

synaptic activity is more likely to leave the membrane near the activation range of voltage-gated

Ca²⁺ channels. The decisive variable is therefore not simply the amplitude of individual calcium

events, but the probability and frequency with which ordinary synaptic fluctuations create entry

opportunities. Frequent small depolarisations during routine network use more often leave the

membrane within VGCC activation range, increasing the likelihood of brief channel opening

without requiring larger individual events.

That formulation helps reconcile why ASD calcium findings often point in opposite directions. A

system exposed to a higher calcium duty cycle may initially show increased spontaneous

transients, greater depolarisation-evoked responses, or more local calcium activity. With time,



however, the same system may also show reduced coordinated calcium waves, blunted

receptor-evoked store release, altered resting membrane potential, or dampened responsiveness

because ER, mitochondria, and extrusion systems have been carrying a higher cumulative burden.

Amplitude-based readouts can therefore diverge even when the upstream change is the same. The

mixed ASD literature is consistent with this interpretation: some models show increased

spontaneous or depolarisation-linked calcium activity, whereas others show decreased

spontaneous transients, impaired connectivity, or reduced ATP-evoked responses.


29.4 Depolarisation-driven Ca²⁺ entry under altered GABA polarity

This bridge is the most direct. Depolarising GABA is well known to raise intracellular Ca²⁺

through voltage-gated calcium channels, and synaptic GABA_A activation can induce Ca²⁺ rises

in immature hippocampal neurons. Similar findings in developing neocortex show that

endogenous GABA_A receptor activation can depolarise cells sufficiently to activate VGCCs and

drive calcium-dependent signalling. Importantly, this does not require a binary switch from

inhibitory to fully excitatory GABA. A modest reduction in shunting and hyperpolarising reserve

is enough to increase the number of calcium-permissive windows during routine synaptic activity.

That is closely aligned with the causal arc laid out in the chloride section, which explicitly

predicts more frequent subthreshold depolarisations, more frequent openings of VGCCs, and

easier relief of NMDA-receptor Mg²⁺ block once chloride weighting persists. Within such a state,

calcium channels need not be intrinsically abnormal. They may be responding appropriately to a

membrane that now spends more time in a depolarisation-prone regime. This offers a

physiological route to the increased spontaneous transients seen in some ASD models and to the

increased KCl- or DHPG-evoked responses reported in differentiated idiopathic ASD neurons. At

the same time, a persistent depolarisation bias can also produce the opposite readout in other

models by favouring abnormal inactivation of VGCCs, as demonstrated in 22q11.2-deletion

neurons.


29.5 Sodium gradients, NCX strain, and Ca²⁺ clearance

A chloride-weighted state also changes calcium handling from the clearance side. NKCC1 does

not import chloride alone; it brings coupled Na⁺ entry and thereby increases the burden on the

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase. Repeated NKCC1-biased recovery would therefore be expected to narrow the

effective Na⁺ gradient available for other transport tasks. That matters because the Na⁺/Ca²⁺

exchanger (NCX) depends on the inward Na⁺ gradient to extrude Ca²⁺ efficiently in forward

mode. Because NCX is one of the major rapid Ca²⁺ clearance mechanisms in neurons, even

modest weakening of that gradient should make fast calcium removal more state-dependent.

In primary neurons, removal of extracellular Na⁺ slows the fast decay of depolarisation-evoked

calcium transients about threefold, consistent with a major contribution of forward-mode NCX to

rapid calcium clearance. Conversely, in neocortical astrocytes, activity-related Na⁺ elevations can

drive NCX into reverse mode and generate Ca²⁺ influx. Mechanical stimulation can do the same

in astrocytes, where reverse-mode NCX contributes substantially to Ca²⁺ and Na⁺ homeostasis.

The implication is not that NKCC1 automatically reverses NCX everywhere or all the time. The



narrower claim is that chronic Na⁺ loading makes NCX-dependent Ca²⁺ extrusion more contingent

on local state, and that reverse-mode recruitment becomes more likely during bursts of activity,

during local Na⁺ accumulation in restricted compartments, or during intense astrocytic transporter

activity.

The result is a more demand-sensitive clearance regime and a higher energetic bill. When

forward-mode NCX is less effective, more of the buffering burden must be carried by

intracellular sequestration and slower extrusion routes. Whatever the immediate route of removal,

final restoration still depends heavily on ATP-dependent pumps: PMCA at the plasma membrane

and SERCA at the ER. At the same time, Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase work is already increased by

NKCC1-associated Na⁺ entry. Calcium handling therefore contributes directly to elevated ATP

turnover rather than standing apart from it, providing a natural bridge to the later phosphate and

energetic-reserve discussion. This remains a plausible but still under-tested link from chloride

physiology to the mixed calcium phenotypes reported in ASD.


29.6 ER and mitochondrial buffering responses

Once cytosolic Ca²⁺ entry opportunities become more frequent and membrane extrusion becomes

more state-dependent, ER and mitochondria must absorb more of the buffering workload. This

changes how ER and mitochondrial findings should be read. Depressed IP3R-mediated release,

altered SOCE, ER Ca²⁺ accumulation, or reduced mitochondrial Ca²⁺ do not necessarily imply that

those organelles were the initiating lesion. They may instead reflect compartments that have been

recruited more heavily as compensatory sinks for recurrent cytosolic calcium entry. In that

setting, altered ER filling, altered release sensitivity, and altered coupling to mitochondria are

plausible secondary consequences of repeated buffering demand rather than obligatory primaries.

The published ASD data fit this reinterpretation surprisingly well. Monogenic fragile X and TSC

fibroblasts, as well as fibroblasts from sporadic ASD, show depressed IP3R-mediated release.

TSC astrocytes show reduced SOCE, reduced basal mitochondrial Ca²⁺, reduced Ca²⁺ influx,

altered ER-related responses, and poor responsiveness to ATP, glutamate, and DHPG.

ASD-derived dopaminergic neurons show ER Ca²⁺ accumulation together with low mitochondrial

Ca²⁺. Rather than treating these as isolated and incompatible findings, they can be read as

different compartments reporting the same workload redistribution: a higher cytosolic calcium

duty cycle, less uniform extrusion reserve, and heavier buffering transfer to ER and mitochondria.

As mitochondrial uptake demand rises, mitochondrial redox strain would also be expected to

increase and may therefore feed into the ROS-linked amplification loop described earlier in the

manuscript.


29.7 Astrocyte calcium signalling and osmotic stress

Astrocytes provide the most direct physiological bridge between osmotic regulation and calcium

signalling. The chloride section already placed them in the first-burden position because they are

the major CNS volume buffers and are more permissive than neurons to chloride-based volume

defence. In parallel, the swelling literature shows that hypo-osmotic stress itself elicits astrocytic

Ca²⁺ signals, particularly in subpial and perivascular endfeet, and that these responses are strongly



dependent on IP3R2, attenuated by disruption of AQP4 anchoring, and reduced by TRPV4

blockade. In retinal glia, IP3R2-mediated calcium signalling is required for osmotic volume

regulation. Astrocytic calcium is therefore not merely a downstream bystander; it is part of the

machinery by which swollen glia sense and regulate their volume state.

This makes astrocyte calcium especially relevant to the proposed chloride-weighted

neighbourhood. Repeated swelling would be expected to recruit TRPV4-linked

mechanotransduction and IP3R2-dependent astrocytic Ca²⁺ signalling as part of ongoing volume

regulation, which can in turn reshape transporter localisation, cytoskeletal anchoring, and local

release of purines or gliotransmitters. That possibility was already anticipated in the chloride

section’s mechanotransductive persistence argument. It is also notable that experimental

impairment of astrocytic IP3R2-mediated Ca²⁺ signalling is sufficient to produce ASD-like

behaviours in mice, indicating that glial calcium pathways can influence autism-relevant circuit

output rather than merely responding to it.


29.8 Extracellular-space compression and network Ca²⁺ dynamics

The chloride framework also predicts a change in calcium dynamics at the network scale.

Astrocytic chloride and water loading compress the extracellular space, increase diffusion

resistance, and reduce extracellular volume fraction. Under those conditions, the same amount of

K⁺ efflux, glutamate release, or ATP release is distributed into less space, so local concentrations

rise more sharply. Diffusion paths between neighbouring membranes shorten, yet dissipation of

the resulting transients becomes slower because diffusion resistance is higher. Independent

physiological studies support these consequences at the systems level. Hypo-osmotic conditions

reduce extracellular volume fraction and enhance epileptiform activity in immature hippocampus,

while osmolarity-induced shrinkage of extracellular space can itself promote nonsynaptic

bursting. More recently, cell swelling triggered by reduced osmolarity was shown to increase

neuronal excitability rapidly through NMDA-receptor activation.

The calcium consequence is that K⁺, glutamate, and ATP microdomains are amplified and persist

longer. That favours depolarisation, prolongs glutamatergic and purinergic spillover, facilitates

NMDA-receptor opening, and increases the probability of burst-like calcium entry. In this setting,

calcium abnormalities would not be confined to single cells or single channels. They would

emerge as network-level changes in synchrony, burst probability, and compartmental coupling

between neurons and astrocytes. This is especially relevant for ASD because it offers a route from

a glia-first, chloride-weighted state to recurrent observations of altered excitability, calcium

waves, and state-dependent decompensation without requiring a primary lesion in every

calcium-handling component.


29.9 Genetic modifiers of calcium signalling within an altered ionic

environment

This framework does not dismiss calcium-related genetics. It changes their role. Some

ASD-associated variants clearly act directly on calcium machinery: CACNA1C mutations in

Timothy syndrome and CACNA1D de novo mutations in ASD produce gain-of-function



calcium-channel phenotypes, and patient-derived models of Timothy syndrome show abnormal

calcium signalling and activity-dependent gene expression. ATP2B2 is associated with ASD and

encodes PMCA2, a plasma-membrane calcium pump that extrudes Ca²⁺ from the cytosol. OXTR

variants associated with ASD can reduce agonist-induced IP3 formation and intracellular Ca²⁺

mobilisation. RYR2 has also emerged in ASD CNV and variant studies, making altered

ER-release sensitivity a plausible genetic modifier of calcium signalling.

In a chloride-weighted interpretation, these genes are best understood as gain modifiers within an

altered ionic baseline rather than as obligatory initiators of pathology. CACNA1C or CACNA1D

variants would make a given depolarisation more calcium-consequential. ATP2B2 variants would

reduce extrusion reserve once calcium duty cycle rises. OXTR variants would alter one of the

major IP3-linked routes by which receptor activation recruits intracellular stores. RYR2 variants

would change the sensitivity of CICR-like amplification within ER-mitochondrial buffering

networks. Even 22q11.2-deletion models point in this direction: the calcium phenotype in those

neurons was attributed not to a primary channel mutation but to a shift in resting membrane

potential that secondarily altered channel availability. The broader implication is that

calcium-pathway genes may be expected to appear repeatedly in ASD if ionic physiology is

upstream, because they determine how strongly an altered ionic neighbourhood is translated into

calcium phenotypes.


29.10 Integrated physiological synthesis

The integrated interpretation can now be stated compactly. Repeated osmotic instability drives

repeated regulatory volume decrease with loss of KCl and compatible osmolytes. If restitution is

incomplete, osmolyte debt accumulates and the least costly recovered baseline may no longer be

the original one. Under those conditions, chloride becomes a fast defended osmolyte, especially

in astrocytes, and recovery can drift toward NKCC1-biased chloride reloading with slower

neuronal chloride reset. The immediate functional consequence is not necessarily uniformly

excitatory GABA, but weaker inhibitory leverage: routine network activity spends more time near

calcium-permissive membrane states, more often approaching the activation range of VGCCs and

more readily relieving NMDA-receptor Mg²⁺ block. Calcium channels in this environment need

not be intrinsically defective. They may be responding appropriately to a membrane that now

presents them with more frequent depolarising opportunities.

Once this duty-cycle shift occurs, calcium dysregulation can follow along several convergent

paths. First, depolarisation-driven Ca²⁺ entry events become more frequent. Second,

NKCC1-associated Na⁺ loading narrows the Na⁺ gradient and makes NCX-dependent calcium

clearance more state-dependent, especially during bursts, local Na⁺ accumulation, or intense

astrocytic transporter work, while also tightening the energetic margin available to

ATP-dependent PMCA and SERCA and adding to Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase demand. Third, ER and

mitochondria assume more buffering responsibility, so changes in IP3R-mediated release, SOCE,

ER filling, and mitochondrial Ca²⁺ no longer need to be interpreted as isolated primary lesions.

Fourth, astrocytic swelling itself recruits TRPV4-, AQP4-, and IP3R2-linked calcium signalling

as part of volume sensing and regulation. Fifth, extracellular-space compression amplifies K⁺,

glutamate, and ATP microdomains, increasing the probability of burst-like and



network-synchronised calcium entry. The result would not be one universal calcium abnormality.

It would be a family of state-dependent calcium phenotypes: increased spontaneous transients in

some systems, blunted receptor-evoked store release in others, altered mitochondrial buffering,

altered astrocytic calcium activity, and greater network-level synchrony or instability under load.

This synthesis remains physiologically plausible rather than fully demonstrated. The strongest

links are already well supported individually: depolarising GABA can drive calcium entry; Na⁺

gradients strongly shape NCX behaviour; hypo-osmotic stress elicits astrocytic calcium

signalling; and extracellular-space shrinkage increases excitability and burst propensity. What

remains under-tested is the full developmental chain linking repeated osmotic stress to a durable

chloride-weighted baseline and then to the spectrum of calcium phenotypes seen in ASD. Even

so, this framework offers a coherent way to connect findings that are currently treated as separate:

calcium abnormalities in ASD may often reflect the downstream behaviour of largely normal

calcium machinery operating under altered membrane conditions within a chloride-weighted

ionic neighbourhood.

Conceptual diagram proposal

A simple conceptual figure could be drawn as a left-to-right cascade:​

Repeated osmotic instability / hypo-osmolality​

→ repeated RVD with KCl and compatible-osmolyte loss​

→ osmolyte debt and constrained recovery​

→ NKCC1-biased recovery state + delayed KCC2 maturation​

→ chloride-weighted intracellular physiology​

→ E_GABA shifts toward resting potential and inhibitory leverage weakens​

→ increased time spent in calcium-permissive membrane states​

→ increased frequency of small Ca²⁺ entry events​

→ more state-dependent Ca²⁺ clearance (NCX strain) + higher ATP-dependent PMCA/SERCA

and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase workload​

→ redistribution of buffering work to ER and mitochondria​

→ altered neuronal, astrocytic, and network-level Ca²⁺ signalling.

A parallel side branch from “osmotic instability” to “astrocyte swelling / endfoot deformation”

could feed into “TRPV4–AQP4–IP3R2-dependent astrocyte Ca²⁺ signalling for volume

regulation”, which then rejoins the main pathway at the level of buffering adaptations,

extracellular-space compression, and amplified network signalling.


30 Phosphate Reserve and ATP Turnover Under

Chloride-Weighted Ionic Stress

A chloride-weighted intracellular state converts an ionic baseline shift into a sustained energetic

burden. NKCC1-biased chloride loading is accompanied by Na⁺ entry. Each episode of chloride

defence therefore increases the downstream work required of the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase to restore the

Na⁺ gradient on which further transport depends. At the same time, the weaker inhibitory leverage



and higher Ca²⁺ duty cycle outlined in the preceding section would be expected to recruit Ca²⁺

handling more frequently, increasing work at ATP-dependent plasma-membrane and ER Ca²⁺

pumps (PMCA and SERCA) while also increasing reliance on mitochondrial buffering. The

consequence is not necessarily frank energy failure, but persistently elevated ATP turnover to

preserve Na⁺, K⁺, and Ca²⁺ gradients within a chloride-weighted ionic neighbourhood.

That higher ATP turnover places phosphate cycling under the same pressure. ATP hydrolysis

continuously generates ADP and inorganic phosphate (Pi), which must be reincorporated through

oxidative phosphorylation and other intracellular phosphotransfer systems if ATP regeneration is

to keep pace with demand. Yet phosphate supply is not fully independent of ionic state. Major Pi

transporters in brain cells, including the PiT family, are Na⁺-coupled, so the same weakening of

the Na⁺ gradient that increases pump demand can also make phosphate import less favourable.

The result need not be absolute phosphate deprivation. The narrower physiological prediction is

that supplementation of intracellular phosphate from the extracellular compartment becomes

more state-dependent precisely when ATP turnover is highest.

Within this framework, phosphate reserve is best understood as part of energetic resilience rather

than as a primary osmotic variable. A chloride-weighted state narrows ATP margin by increasing

the cost of restoring ion gradients while simultaneously making ATP regeneration more

dependent on preserved Na⁺-linked phosphate entry and mitochondrial throughput. This extends

the calcium section mechanistically: altered Ca²⁺ signalling is not simply adjacent to chloride

weighting, but one route by which chloride weighting is translated into sustained ATP and

phosphate demand. Once ATP margin is narrowed, the system becomes more vulnerable to the

next step in the cascade, namely astrocytic ion-water loading and the extracellular-space

compression that follows.


31 Extracellular-Space Compression as a Gain Mechanism

A sustained rise in ATP turnover is not only a metabolic issue. In a glia-first chloride-weighted

state, the same Na⁺- and Cl⁻-linked loading that raises pump work also draws water. Astrocytic

volume regulation therefore becomes increasingly dependent on a metabolically supported

ion-water cycle. Because astrocytes are the principal CNS volume buffers and are more

permissive than neurons to chloride-based volume defence, this burden is likely to be expressed

first as glial swelling. Given the normally small extracellular-space (ECS) volume fraction, even

modest astrocytic expansion can disproportionately compress the extracellular compartment.

Once ECS volume fraction falls, the extracellular milieu becomes a higher-gain signalling

environment. The space available for dilution shrinks, tortuosity rises, and locally released solutes

clear less efficiently. As a result, K⁺, glutamate, ATP, protons, and related metabolites occupy a

smaller volume for longer, so local concentration transients become larger even without any

increase in total release. ECS compression therefore amplifies signalling in a literal physical

sense: it strengthens extracellular microdomains by reducing the tissue’s buffering and diffusional

margin.



This amplification feeds directly back into the earlier chloride-calcium sequence. Higher local K⁺

and glutamate would be expected to favour depolarisation, facilitate NMDA-receptor activation,

and increase the probability that routine activity produces additional Ca²⁺ entry; extracellular ATP

and protons can further strengthen purinergic and metabolic-stress signalling. Attempts to clear

these more concentrated transients would themselves increase the downstream ATP cost of

astrocytic transport, reinforcing the energetic burden described above. The consequence is not

merely passive tissue crowding, but a plausible gain stage through which intracellular ionic stress

is converted into amplified extracellular signalling and broader network-level physiology.


32 Cytoskeletal and Membrane-Scaffold Persistence After

Ionic Stress

Repeated exposure to this compressed, high-gain extracellular environment would be expected to

engage structural response systems as well as ionic ones. Cytoskeletal dynamics are regulated by

ATP availability, Ca²⁺-dependent signalling, purinergic input, membrane tension, and

mechanotransductive pathways recruited during swelling. Actin, microtubules, and associated

scaffold proteins therefore sit at a convergence point: they receive simultaneous information

about energetic load, ionic load, and mechanical strain. Under sustained chloride-weighted stress,

that combination would be expected to shift cytoskeletal turnover, cortical tension, endfoot

geometry, and vesicular trafficking even without overt structural injury.

Those structural adjustments matter because transporter function is partly a localisation problem.

In astrocytes, endfoot organisation and scaffold integrity help maintain the local arrangement of

AQP4, Kir4.1, connexins, and associated clearance machinery; in neurons, cytoskeletal anchoring

influences receptor clustering, vesicle trafficking, and the surface stability of transporters such as

KCC2. If swelling- and Ca²⁺-linked remodelling alters that architecture, membrane proteins may

remain redistributed after the initiating ionic disturbance has subsided. This provides a plausible

persistence layer within the Sodium-Struggle framework: the initiating disturbance is ionic, but

part of what persists may be a scaffold-dependent reorganisation that changes how later ionic

stress is handled.

The claim here is intentionally narrow. The framework does not require a single dominant

cytoskeletal lesion, and the specific molecular anchors would need empirical definition. The

narrower synthesis is that repeated ionic, energetic, and mechanical stress can leave behind

membrane-scaffold adjustments that slow full reset, alter the thresholds or geometry of later

volume responses, and stabilise a chloride-weighted operating point. Cytoskeletal remodelling is

therefore not a separate parallel story, but a structural continuation of the same sodium-volume

defence burden.


33 Recovery Constraints: Why Ionic Reset May Be Incomplete

Recovery from such a state is therefore a multi-compartment reset rather than a single-variable

reversal. Na⁺ gradients must be rebuilt by the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase, intracellular Cl⁻ must be

re-equilibrated, Ca²⁺ loads must be cleared, ATP and Pi must be regenerated, and astrocytic



volume must recede sufficiently for the ECS to reopen. Because these variables are coupled,

improvement in one domain does not guarantee recovery in the others. A tissue may regain

near-baseline membrane potentials while still carrying narrowed energetic reserve, slowed

chloride reset, altered Ca²⁺ buffering, or residual ECS compression.

Several recovery constraints follow directly from the preceding cascade. If the Na⁺ gradient

remains partially weakened, Pi import and NCX-dependent Ca²⁺ clearance remain less favourable

while pump demand stays elevated. If astrocytes remain even modestly swollen, extracellular K⁺,

glutamate, ATP, and proton transients will continue to be concentrated within a restricted space,

prolonging local purinergic, depolarising, and metabolic-stress signals after the initiating

disturbance has passed. If cytoskeletal and scaffold arrangements have shifted, transporter

relocalisation, endfoot geometry, membrane-protein stability, and later volume-response

thresholds may normalise only slowly. Recovery would then be expected to proceed unevenly,

with some variables resetting rapidly and others retaining a physiological memory of the stress

state.

The network consequence is not necessarily a permanently hyperexcitable system, but a different

operating regime. Relative to the original baseline, such a system would be expected to show

weaker inhibitory precision, tighter extracellular coupling, higher metabolic cost per unit activity,

and less reserve against high-frequency use or renewed osmotic challenge. Routine function may

therefore appear near normal at low demand while decompensation, dyscoordination, or

burst-prone behaviour emerges earlier under load. Within the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis,

incomplete ionic reset thus provides a continuous physiological route from transient

developmental sodium-volume instability to a more durable, internally stable, but altered network

state, without invoking permanent structural damage or a single irreversible lesion.


34 Astrocytic osmotic imprinting: volume regulation, osmolyte

debt, and extracellular-space constraints

34.1 Astrocytes as designated volume responders in early sodium

instability

In the sodium-struggle framework, early development is characterised not only by the possibility

of low-effective-sodium pressure, but by the organism’s repeated deployment of

anti-hyponatraemia measures: integrated endocrine and renal actions that defend extracellular

tonicity and preserve circulating sodium signalling without requiring sustained hyponatraemia.

The central claim of this section is that even when anti-hyponatraemia measures succeed at the

whole-body level, the brain’s local volume economy still experiences episodic osmotic strain.

Within the brain, astrocytes are positioned and equipped to carry the majority of that strain, and

this normal physiological role provides a plausible route from early sodium instability to enduring

sodium–oxygen vulnerability at the neurovascular unit.

Neurons cannot tolerate meaningful swelling. Their membrane excitability depends on narrow

ionic gradients and tightly constrained extracellular space, and their geometry leaves little room



for reversible volume excursions without disrupting synaptic signalling. Astrocytes, by contrast,

are structurally and functionally configured to swell. They express high levels of aquaporin-4

(AQP4), a water channel that increases membrane water permeability; their cytoplasm is

comparatively compliant; and their processes occupy strategic interfaces at synapses and along

blood vessels. These features make water influx during osmotic perturbation not only likely, but

operationally unavoidable. Astrocytes are therefore the brain’s designated volume responders: the

cell population expected to transiently accept water when extracellular tonicity falls, thereby

limiting swelling stress on neurons and preserving the ionic microenvironment in which neuronal

firing remains stable.

This framing is critical because it treats astrocytic swelling as normal physiology rather than

evidence of cellular failure. The hypothesis advanced here does not require primary neuronal

injury, and it does not require chronic hyponatraemia. It requires only repeated recruitment of a

protective, glia-centred volume programme during sensitive developmental windows.


34.2 Osmolyte-based volume regulation under hypo-osmotic stress

To understand why astrocytes can translate early anti-hyponatraemia measures into long-lasting

vulnerability, it is necessary to be explicit about the physical logic of osmotic stress. Osmolality is

the concentration of dissolved particles in a fluid; tonicity is the portion of that osmotic signal that

is “effective” across cell membranes, meaning it drives water movement because the solutes

cannot rapidly equilibrate. When extracellular tonicity falls, water enters cells down its chemical

potential gradient. In brain tissue, where the extracellular space is small and diffusion distances

are short, even modest hypo-osmotic shifts can produce rapid cellular water influx.

Astrocytes respond to swelling with regulatory volume decrease, a conserved process in which

the cell lowers its own intracellular osmolarity so that water can exit and volume can return

toward baseline. Mechanistically, this is achieved by exporting solutes and accompanying water.

A key point for neurodevelopmental logic is that astrocytes do not primarily solve swelling by

exporting inorganic ions in bulk. Large fluxes of K⁺, Na⁺, or Cl⁻ would directly distort

extracellular excitability, inhibitory tone, and synaptic transmission. In developing networks,

where activity-dependent refinement depends on the fidelity of local ionic and transmitter

microenvironments, this would be a costly solution.

Instead, astrocytes preferentially export compatible organic osmolytes, small organic molecules

whose intracellular concentration can vary over a wide range without disrupting protein structure

or enzyme function. Prominent examples include taurine, myo-inositol, and betaine. These solutes

can be released through swelling-activated pathways, including volume-regulated anion channels

(VRAC), which are membrane channels that open when the cell swells and allow osmolytes and

anions to exit. In established osmotic physiology, this strategy is protective on the timescale that

matters most during an acute hypo-osmotic event: it lowers intracellular osmolarity quickly,

relieves swelling pressure, and helps preserve the extracellular ionic conditions required for

neuronal signalling.



The hypothesis-level step comes next. If early life repeatedly recruits this protective astrocytic

response, the very mechanism that preserves stability during each episode can leave behind a

slow-to-repair biochemical deficit that changes the operating conditions of the neurovascular unit.


34.3 Osmolyte debt as a developmental state variable

Organic osmolytes are not instantly replenished. Their re-accumulation requires

transporter-mediated uptake and metabolic support, processes that are intrinsically slower than

channel-mediated efflux. In early development, this asymmetry matters: repeated osmotic

challenges can occur on timescales shorter than full osmolyte recovery. Under those conditions,

each subsequent swelling episode begins with lower baseline osmolyte reserves than the one

before.

This section uses the term osmolyte debt to denote a persistent deficit in intracellular compatible

osmolytes produced by repeated regulatory volume decrease events that outpace re-accumulation.

Osmolyte debt is not damage and does not imply that astrocytes are failing at baseline. It is best

conceptualised as a developmental state variable, a form of physiological “memory” of prior

osmotic stress that persists even when serum sodium later normalises and even when overt

hyponatraemia is absent. The debt is a bookkeeping consequence of asymmetric kinetics: fast loss

during swelling, slow regain during recovery.

Why does such a debt matter for sodium–oxygen vulnerability? Osmolyte content contributes to

an astrocyte’s buffering margin, the reserve capacity that allows it to accommodate acute osmotic,

ionic, and metabolic loads while maintaining local microdomain control. When osmolyte reserves

are reduced, a given hypo-osmotic perturbation produces larger volume excursions and requires

more frequent or more intense compensatory responses. Those compensations are not free.

Maintaining ion gradients, restoring transporter function, and re-accumulating osmolytes all

demand ATP, and in brain tissue ATP supply is tightly coupled to oxygen delivery. Thus,

osmolyte debt provides a mechanistic bridge from early-life osmotic defence to later-life

sensitivity under conditions of limited oxygen availability or transient micro-hypoperfusion, even

if baseline conditions appear normal.

This inference is conservative in one respect: it does not require chronic swelling at rest. It

predicts a reduced tolerance for repeated or clustered physiological stressors, because the system

is operating with less osmotic reserve.


34.4 AQP4 up-regulation, end-foot depolarisation, and why localisation

dominates quantity

A second, complementary route from early osmotic stress to enduring vulnerability involves

AQP4. AQP4 is the dominant water channel in astrocytes, highly enriched in perivascular



end-feet, the specialised astrocytic membranes that wrap cerebral microvessels. Under osmotic

stress, AQP4 expression can increase as part of tonicity-responsive adaptation. The established

physiology here is straightforward: increasing water permeability can accelerate equilibration and

facilitate coordinated water movement during volume challenges.

The key hypothesis-relevant nuance is that localisation matters more than quantity. In the mature

neurovascular unit, AQP4 is not merely abundant; it is polarised. “Polarity” in this context means

that AQP4 is concentrated at specific membrane domains, especially perivascular end-feet, rather

than being evenly distributed across the astrocyte surface. This spatial organisation confines large

water fluxes to a controlled vascular interface and supports coordinated coupling between water

movement and ion handling.

Repeated swelling and recovery cycles, particularly during development when astrocyte

morphology and perivascular specialisation are still being established, plausibly promote a partial

loss of that tight localisation. The term AQP4 depolarisation is used here to mean a

redistribution of AQP4 away from sharply confined end-foot domains toward a broader astrocytic

membrane distribution. This does not require a decrease in AQP4. It can occur alongside AQP4

up-regulation, producing a state in which the astrocyte is highly water-permeable but less

spatially constrained in where water crosses.

Why does this matter for sodium–oxygen coupling? Astrocytic end-feet are not only water

interfaces; they are major sites of potassium (K⁺) handling and vascular signalling. Kir4.1, an

inwardly rectifying potassium channel expressed by astrocytes, contributes to K⁺ uptake and

spatial redistribution, sometimes described as “K⁺ siphoning,” meaning the directed movement of

excess extracellular K⁺ away from active synaptic zones and toward regions where it can be

dissipated, including perivascular domains. Although the precise molecular coupling between

AQP4 localisation and Kir4.1 function remains an active area of research, the physiological logic

is clear: water flux geometry and K⁺ flux geometry are interdependent because ionic movements

alter osmotic gradients, and osmotic equilibration alters extracellular space and diffusion.

AQP4 depolarisation therefore predicts a loss of microdomain confinement for water movement,

with secondary consequences for K⁺ clearance dynamics and for the astrocyte’s ability to deliver

sharply localised vascular signals during neural activity.


34.5 Astrocyte-mediated constraints on neurovascular precision

The phrase astrocyte-mediated constraints on neurovascular precision is used here in a strictly

operational sense. It does not imply pathology. It denotes a trade-off in which astrocytes,

repeatedly recruited for volume defence, operate in a mode that prioritises protection of cellular

and tissue volume over fine-grained spatial and temporal control.

Concretely, reduced neurovascular precision refers to diminished spatial and temporal fidelity of

water and ion handling at the neurovascular unit, manifesting as less precise matching of local



blood flow to local neural activity, delayed K⁺ clearance after neuronal firing, and broader

astrocytic Ca²⁺ signalling domains.

Each component follows from standard physiology. If osmolyte debt reduces reserve, astrocytes

must engage volume-regulatory responses more readily, widening the spatial footprint of

osmolyte and water flux. If AQP4 depolarisation broadens the membrane territory through which

water can move, water shifts become less confined to perivascular end-feet and more distributed

across astrocytic processes. If K⁺ clearance depends on local extracellular space geometry and on

efficient astrocytic uptake and redistribution, then swelling-related changes in extracellular

volume fraction and membrane flux geometry can extend the time required for K⁺ to return to

baseline after bursts of activity. If astrocytic Ca²⁺ signals participate in coordinating local vascular

responses, broader Ca²⁺ domains can translate into less focal vascular modulation.

Astrocytic swelling reduces extracellular volume fraction, narrowing the extracellular space and

thereby shortening the effective diffusion distances over which ions and transmitters must travel

before altering local membrane environments.

None of these effects requires that astrocytes are broken. The claim is that they are over-recruited

into a defensive operating regime, and that this regime has predictable costs in precision.


35 Interstitial, Perivascular, and Glymphatic Fluid Handling as

Downstream Readouts of Sodium–Volume Defence

The astrocytic and interstitial mechanisms described in Section 19 operate at the scale of cells,

microdomains, and local tissue geometry. Those mechanisms do not terminate at the parenchymal

boundary. Changes in astrocytic volume regulation, extracellular-space constraints, and

perivascular membrane specialisation necessarily propagate to larger-scale fluid-routing pathways

that govern the movement of interstitial fluid, cerebrospinal fluid, and solutes through and out of

the brain.

In the Sodium-Struggle framework, altered perivascular and glymphatic findings reported in

some autism spectrum disorder cohorts are therefore not treated as primary causes of

neurodevelopmental divergence, but as macroscopic readouts of earlier sodium–volume defence

imprinting. When astrocytes repeatedly absorb osmotic strain during development, osmolyte debt

and altered membrane flux geometry reduce the precision with which water and ions are

exchanged at perivascular interfaces. These changes are predicted to alter the efficiency, timing,

and spatial confinement of interstitial–CSF exchange, even in the absence of overt injury or

chronic swelling at rest.

Perivascular spaces (PVS) provide a structural corridor through which interstitial fluid

communicates with cerebrospinal fluid. Enlargement of these spaces, as observed in some infant

and child imaging studies, is compatible with a state in which fluid movement is less efficiently

cleared and more prone to transient stasis. Within this framework, PVS dilation does not imply

excessive clearance capacity, but rather a mismatch between interstitial fluid entry, perivascular



transport, and downstream drainage under conditions of reduced astrocytic reserve and altered

flux localisation.

Similarly, reports of excess extra-axial cerebrospinal fluid during early development can be

interpreted as a system-level consequence of altered pressure and clearance gradients following

repeated sodium–volume defence. If venous capacitance, perivascular exchange geometry, or

meningeal outflow pathways are secondarily constrained, cerebrospinal fluid may preferentially

accumulate in subarachnoid compartments during sensitive windows, leaving a persistent

anatomical footprint even after systemic sodium measures normalise.

At the level of astrocytic specialisation, the distinction between aquaporin-4 quantity and

localisation is particularly relevant. As described in Section 19, partial depolarisation of AQP4

away from sharply confined end-foot domains broadens the membrane territory through which

water can move. While such redistribution may support acute volume equilibration, it is predicted

to degrade the spatial confinement required for efficient, directional perivascular flow, thereby

reducing glymphatic efficiency as measured by proxy indices without requiring a loss of AQP4

expression per se.

Taken together, these considerations place glymphatic indices, perivascular space morphology,

and extra-axial CSF volume within the Sodium-Struggle hypothesis as downstream,

state-dependent markers of altered fluid handling. They reflect the cumulative effects of early

sodium–volume defence on astrocytic reserve, interstitial geometry, and vascular coupling, rather

than constituting independent pathogenic drivers. As such, these findings are expected to vary

across individuals and developmental stages, and to track physiological state and stress exposure

more closely than static diagnostic categories.


36 Centralisation of Perfusion: Preferential Blood

Redistribution Under Sodium–Volume Defence

37 Splanchnic Reservoir Imprinting: Preferential Abdominal

Volume Recruitment and GI Consequences

37.3.1 Definition

This subsection describes what is referred to here as the Splanchnic Reservoir Hypothesis, in

which gastrointestinal motility, perfusion, and secretion are dynamically and reversibly altered in

response to effective sodium–volume signalling. In this framework, the gastrointestinal tract is

treated as a sodium- and volume-sensitive system whose behaviour reflects cellular sodium

handling, endocrine state, and autonomic tone, rather than dietary sodium intake per se. The GI

system functions as a major vascular and fluid reservoir, a large epithelial sodium-transport

surface, and a densely innervated interface linking endocrine, autonomic, and vascular control

systems. Enteric signalling refers to the integrated output of enteric neural circuits, autonomic



inputs (vagal and sympathetic), and endocrine mediators that together regulate motility, secretion,

absorption, and visceral sensation.


37.3.2 Volume sensing defined

Volume sensing is used here in a narrow, mechanistic sense: the integration of (i) visceral stretch

signals arising from the gut wall, (ii) splanchnic blood pooling and redistribution within the

abdominal vascular compartment, and (iii) effective circulating volume signals conveyed through

baroreflex pathways, RAAS-related cues, and stress-hormone dynamics. This is not a

metaphorical construct. It refers to a concrete physiological process by which mechanical and

haemodynamic information is integrated and translated into autonomic output that directly alters

gastrointestinal perfusion and function.


37.3.3 Physiological evidence for volume-sensitive GI

behaviour

Clinical and experimental observations support the view that gastrointestinal function is highly

sensitive to changes in both luminal and vascular volume, independent of structural pathology.

Osmotically active agents that increase luminal water content, such as magnesium salts and

vitamin C, are well known to loosen stool and accelerate transit, illustrating that luminal volume

itself is a primary determinant of motility. These examples are cited as proof-of-principle that

increased intraluminal fluid promotes propulsion, regardless of whether water entry is osmotic or

endogenously regulated. Importantly, these effects arise through alterations in epithelial transport,

smooth-muscle activity, and neural reflexes, rather than through mechanical obstruction or tissue

injury.

Hormonal context further modulates this volume sensitivity. Vasopressin (AVP), aldosterone, and

cortisol influence epithelial sodium and water transport, vascular tone, and autonomic balance,

thereby coupling effective circulating volume to gastrointestinal behaviour. Experimental and

clinical studies demonstrate that AVP signalling alters gut perfusion and motility, and that

stress-related endocrine states can either suppress or accelerate transit depending on baseline

autonomic tone. Together, these mechanisms show that the GI system responds not simply to

intake, but to effective volume signalling shaped by transport capacity, receptor sensitivity, and

endocrine state.


37.3.4 Splanchnic sympathetic activation and volume withdrawal from

the GI tract

A central feature of the Splanchnic Reservoir Hypothesis is the role of sympathetic splanchnic

nerve activation. High splanchnic sympathetic tone does not add fluid to the gastrointestinal tract.

Instead, increased sympathetic splanchnic efferent signalling actively constricts mesenteric

resistance vessels and reduces splanchnic venous capacitance, shifting blood from stored

(“unstressed”) to pressure-generating (“stressed”) volume and mobilising blood from the

abdominal reservoir into the central circulation. This withdrawal of vascular volume from the gut



wall is accompanied by suppression of epithelial secretion and promotion of sodium-coupled

water reabsorption. In functional terms, the gastrointestinal tract acts as a prioritised donor of

blood and fluid volume, relinquishing splanchnic resources to preserve perfusion of the heart and

brain during pressure-defence states such as stress, illness, dehydration, or low effective

circulating volume. As this reservoir is drained, luminal volume collapses, stool desiccates, and

motility is gated.

This donor role reflects the large compliance of the splanchnic vascular bed and its capacity for

rapid volume redistribution. Repeated engagement of this brain-sparing pattern renders gut

perfusion and function highly state-dependent and unstable, rather than fixed or intrinsically

pathological.


37.3.5 Corroborative contexts and natural experiments

Established human physiology provides several “natural experiments” in which the splanchnic

compartment is measurably treated as a recruitable reservoir under autonomic–endocrine volume

defence. A commonly under-emphasised mechanism is capacitance control: baroreflex-driven

sympathetic output targets not only arteriolar resistance but also venous compliance, actively

mobilising the venous reservoir to support central perfusion. Orthostasis provides a particularly

clear demonstration: upright posture promotes abdominal pooling, eliciting compensatory

sympathetic recruitment, and orthostatic-intolerance phenotypes are notable for a high burden of

state-dependent GI symptoms (nausea, bloating, constipation, diarrhoea) consistent with

redistribution-linked gating rather than primary gut pathology.

Postprandial states provide an additional integrator, as meal ingestion increases splanchnic

demand at the same time that posture and baroreflex defence may require reservoir withdrawal,

forcing a physiological conflict between the hyperemic demands of digestion and the

vasoconstrictive demands of central perfusion. Heat stress and strenuous exercise further illustrate

this principle: both recruit systemic pressure-defence patterns that reduce splanchnic perfusion

and are commonly accompanied by GI symptoms, demonstrating that reversible gastrointestinal

consequences can follow from systemic prioritisation in otherwise healthy physiology.


37.3.6 Sodium-dependent epithelial mechanisms

These macroscopic shifts in blood volume and perfusion are mirrored by microscopic shifts in

epithelial ion transport, linking systemic volume defence to local control of luminal water

content. A comprehensive review of chronic constipation emphasises that disorders of gut

motility and fluid–electrolyte transport—under neural, endocrine, and ion-channel control—are

inherently connected to constipation, reinforcing that intestinal transit and stool consistency are

tightly coupled to electrolyte-dependent fluid handling rather than to structural pathology. Sodium

plays a central role in this process: sodium and water enter the intestinal lumen passively,

increasing luminal fluid content and secretion (Gonzalez-Martinez et al., 2014). At the epithelial

level, sodium handling directly governs water movement. The epithelial sodium channel (ENaC),

expressed on the superficial epithelial cells of the distal colon and rectum, mediates luminal Na⁺

absorption, which is coupled to basolateral Na⁺ extrusion via the Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase. Alterations in



this sodium-transport axis—whether driven by endocrine state, autonomic tone, or effective

circulating volume—translate directly into changes in luminal water content, stool consistency,

and transit speed.


37.3.7 Hemodynamic Selection: A Physiological Driver of Dysbiosis

Within the Splanchnic Reservoir Hypothesis, alterations in the gut microbiome are interpreted not

as primary drivers but as downstream ecological consequences of repeated, state-dependent

changes in perfusion, secretion, and transit. Recruitment of the splanchnic reservoir withdraws

vascular volume from the gut wall, suppresses epithelial secretion, slows propulsion, and lowers

distal luminal water content, thereby reshaping the intestinal micro-environment on short time

scales. These conditions impose selection by stasis and desiccation, favouring organisms adapted

to low-flow, slow-transit, and low-water niches while disadvantaging taxa that depend on stable

perfusion, regular mixing, and consistent substrate delivery.

Importantly, such selection does not imply pathogenic overgrowth or absence of specific taxa.

Organisms such as Bacteroides are widely regarded as normal commensals and are not

consistently absent in autism-spectrum cohorts; rather, they are often reported at normal or

elevated relative abundance. In this framework, apparent prominence of such taxa reflects

differential attrition of more flow-, hydration-, or cross-feeding-dependent commensals, rather

than primary expansion or causal dysbiosis.

Repeated low-flow states can, in principle, increase barrier vulnerability by stressing

energy-dependent tight-junction maintenance, without implying chronic ischemic injury or

primary inflammatory disease. In this framework, commonly reported dysbiosis patterns—such

as expansion of stress-tolerant or opportunistic organisms and reduction of fragile

commensals—are interpreted as readouts of an altered hemodynamic and osmotic terrain, rather

than as initiating causes.

Specific examples that align with this ecological logic include methane-associated communities

(for example Methanobrevibacter smithii), which are repeatedly linked to slowed transit and

constipation and may stabilise under reservoir-drained conditions. Selected spore-forming

Clostridia taxa, frequently reported in ASD dysbiosis studies, are ecologically suited to

low-water, low-mixing environments and may likewise be favoured. In contrast, commonly cited

commensals such as Bifidobacterium and Prevotella, which rely on hydrated mucus layers,

regular mixing, and consistent substrate delivery, may be disproportionately disadvantaged during

prolonged or repeated reservoir activation.

Microbial metabolic functions are likewise expected to track these state shifts. Microbial sterol

metabolism is plausibly sensitive to reservoir-mediated changes in transit, bile acids, and

secretion, although taxa-level directionality remains to be established. This is relevant because

host cellular cholesterol availability contributes to membrane-level buffering against osmotic

stress; secondary alterations in microbial sterol handling could therefore amplify cellular

vulnerability to volume shifts without serving as a primary driver.



Autonomic state may further intersect with micronutrient handling. Sustained vagal

under-buffering could plausibly blunt the efficiency of intrinsic-factor–dependent vitamin B12

handling by disrupting coordinated gastric secretory signalling and motility, although this remains

to be tested directly.

Taken together, this perspective identifies a previously under-appreciated physiological driver of

the luminal ecosystem, helping to explain why microbiome findings in autism are heterogeneous,

state-dependent, and variable over time. Dysbiosis, when present, is thus treated as an expressive

marker of splanchnic volume defence, not its origin.


37.3.8 Mechanistic linkage back to primary imprinting

After primary sodium-instability imprinting, enteric manifestations can arise as tertiary outputs,

because gut function sits at the intersection of multiple imprinted systems.


37.3.8.1 Autonomic gating of motility and secretion

A primary imprint toward sympathetic dominance and reduced vagal buffering alters motility

patterns in a state-dependent manner. During stress recruitment, sympathetic outflow slows

gastric emptying, reduces propulsive motility, increases sphincter tone, and shifts secretion and

absorption toward conservation. The same individual may therefore alternate between

constipation-predominant and diarrhoea-predominant patterns across different physiological

states, without requiring primary gastrointestinal disease. Transitions out of a defended state can

also produce “reopening” effects (restoration of perfusion, secretion, and propulsion), which may

be experienced clinically as urgency or looser stools, especially when autonomic tone and

endocrine state change rapidly.

Consistent with this framing, meta-analytic work in autism reports lower parasympathetic-linked

HRV/RSA on average, supporting reduced vagal buffering in some cohorts while remaining

heterogeneous across individuals.


37.3.8.2 Splanchnic haemodynamics and effective circulating volume

defence

Vascular imprinting that prioritises pressure defence can produce exaggerated splanchnic

vasoconstriction or unstable redistribution during heat exposure, dehydration, illness, postural

change, or gait-related hydrostatic loading. Because the splanchnic bed serves as a large,

compliant volume reservoir, these shifts directly alter gut wall perfusion and visceral sensation,

feeding back into autonomic output and perceived threat states.


37.3.8.3 Endocrine coupling to sodium and water handling

Primary endocrine imprinting that alters aldosterone and cortisol dynamics modifies epithelial

sodium transport and fluid handling across the gut, particularly during physiological challenge.

This produces fluctuations in luminal water content and stool consistency that track hydration



status, intercurrent illness, and stress-hormone oscillation, rather than reflecting fixed structural

pathology.


37.3.9 What this does not claim

This subsection does not propose a gut-first mechanism for autism, does not treat microbiome

variation as a primary causal driver, and does not imply that gastrointestinal symptoms are

universal or persistent. Enteric changes are treated as state-dependent expressions of an imprinted

control architecture, not as independent disease entities.


37.3.10 Required anchor statement

Enteric manifestations are best understood as expressive outputs of autonomic and endocrine

imprinting, rather than as independent drivers of neurodevelopmental change.


37.4 Secondary and Tertiary Consequences of Repeated Splanchnic

Volume Defence

When the pressure-defence configuration described in Section 26.3 is repeatedly recruited, a

governing constraint becomes salient: acute defence of arterial pressure and central perfusion is

functionally incompatible with the microvascular vasodilation and permeability shifts that

effective inflammatory responses typically require. In defended states, the same haemodynamic

priorities that preserve distribution under threat also tend to restrict regional hyperaemia, limit

endothelial leakage, and favour adrenergic and glucocorticoid signalling environments that are

not permissive for rapid, energetically costly inflammatory amplification. Secondary and tertiary

consequences, in this framing, arise less from a new primary mechanism and more from repeated

cycling between two physiological programs whose optimal operating conditions are mutually

misaligned. These downstream effects are expected to be state-dependent, thresholded, and

subset-limited, with developmental timing shaping whether the pattern becomes recurrent or

remains clinically silent.

Immune timing modulation provides the clearest illustration of this incompatibility. During

defended states, inflammatory signalling can be plausibly blunted in a biphasic manner: first by

constrained tissue access for immune effectors under vasoconstricted, low-permeability

conditions, and second by autonomic and endocrine context that tends to suppress or re-route

cytokine programmes toward containment rather than expansion. This does not imply immune

deficiency, nor a fixed inflammatory phenotype. Rather, it predicts a temporal decoupling

between exposure or tissue perturbation and the visible inflammatory response, with relative

quiescence during defence followed by rebound during recovery when perfusion, permeability,

and cellular trafficking re-open. In subsets with frequent recruitment, repeated delay and rebound

cycles could plausibly distort symptom timing, exaggerate post-event flares, or shift the phase

relationship between inflammatory tone and circadian transitions, without asserting that

inflammation is primary or that these patterns are universal.

A second exemplar is gut epithelial vulnerability as a tissue-level cost of repeated defence,

framed as functional and potentially reversible. As a hypothesis-level extension, villus tips may



be uniquely susceptible to transient hypoxia during even moderate splanchnic hypoperfusion

because counter-current oxygen exchange within villi can favour oxygen shunting away from the

tip. In this view, episodic brush-border enzyme dysfunction, altered transporter performance, or

transient malabsorptive features could arise without fixed structural disease, particularly when

defended states coincide with feeding or high luminal workload. A further hypothesis-level

contributor is a recovery-phase metabolic sink: restoration of epithelial Na⁺/K⁺ gradients and

barrier energetics following defended periods is ATP-intensive and may transiently compete with

other demands during reperfusion and repair. In subsets, that recovery cost could interact with

oxygen- and ATP-dependent handling pathways across the gut–liver axis, including intermittent

constraints on conjugation capacity such as sulfation, and with mechanical factors that transiently

impede mesenteric lymph and chyle transport when venous pressures shift or abdominal bracing

is sustained. These extensions are offered to deepen tissue-level plausibility for timing-linked

symptoms, not as diagnostic claims.

The same timing logic can plausibly extend beyond immune and epithelial domains into other

metabolic and endocrine coordination problems, renal sodium and water handling, interoceptive

signalling, and behavioural-support systems that rely on accurate visceral afference and stable

energy availability. During high-sympathetic states, visceral afferent throughput may be gated,

with muted hunger, thirst, pain, or urge signals during defence and sensory rebound during

recovery, potentially compounding the perceived discontinuity between physiological stressors

and symptoms. Within this framing, reduced thirst signalling and delayed perception of bladder

fullness are expected corollaries of the same defended autonomic configuration, rather than

independent behavioural phenomena. In subsets, sustained sympathetic bias may favour fluid

retention and sphincter engagement during defence, followed by abrupt urgency or voiding once

inhibition is lifted, creating patterns of low spontaneous drinking, withholding, or accidents that

track state transitions rather than intent. Taken together, the secondary and tertiary consequences

described in this section are best interpreted as contingent expressions of repeated state switching

under developmental constraint, not as gut-first causation, not as a universal profile, and not as a

redefinition of autism.


38 Motor-Assisted Perfusion Stabilization

38.1 Toe Walking as a Perfusion-Stabilizing Adaptation (Illustrative,

Non-Diagnostic)

Toe walking is commonly interpreted as a sensory preference, musculoskeletal anomaly, or habit.

An alternative, physiology-based interpretation consistent with the framework developed in

Section 24 is that toe walking may, in some contexts, function as a mechanical counter-maneuver

that augments venous return and stabilizes cerebral perfusion during upright posture, particularly

when autonomic or volume buffering is fragile.

In upright humans, the gastrocnemius–soleus complex plays a major role in supporting venous

return by compressing deep venous structures in the lower limb and promoting upward blood

flow against gravity. This skeletal muscle pump is especially important during quiet standing or



brief pauses in movement, when venous pooling can occur rapidly in dependent vascular beds. In

early childhood, vascular tone, venous valvular function, and myogenic responses are still

calibrating, increasing venous compliance and reducing the effectiveness of passive resistance to

pooling.

Toe walking engages the skeletal muscle pump continuously. By maintaining plantarflexion, the

child sustains isometric calf contraction, converting the lower limb into a more pressurized

venous column and limiting venous capacitance. This sustained muscle tension can support

central venous return not only during stepping, but also during the frequent stop-and-start pauses

characteristic of early exploration, when the risk of orthostatic pooling is greatest.

The walking window is characterized by a sudden increase in hydrostatic load (ρ·g·h) before

volume–pressure reflexes, vascular tone, and mechanical support systems have fully matured. If

effective circulating volume is limited or autonomic buffering is already strained, toe walking

may provide a direct, low-level mechanical means of supporting preload and cardiac output,

without requiring conscious strategy or cortical planning.

This framing does not imply that toe walking is universal, pathological, or causal. Rather, it is

presented as a context-dependent, potentially compensatory behavior that may arise when upright

posture places disproportionate demands on perfusion control. As muscle pump efficiency

improves, vascular tone matures, and circulating volume expands, such behaviors would be

expected to diminish or resolve in many cases.

This interpretation is hypothesis-generating and testable. It aligns with established orthostatic

physiology and with clinical observations that active calf engagement improves upright tolerance

in older individuals, while remaining agnostic about diagnosis, necessity, or sufficiency.


38.2 Hand Flapping as a Distal Blood-Pumping Mechanism During Arousal

(Illustrative, Non-Diagnostic)

Hand flapping is frequently interpreted as a behavioral or sensory phenomenon. Within the

Sodium-Struggle framework, an alternative, physiology-based interpretation is that repetitive

upper-limb movements may, in some contexts, function as a distal blood-pumping mechanism

that supports venous return and central perfusion during states of heightened arousal, particularly

when buffering capacity is limited.

The upper extremities constitute highly compliant vascular compartments with dense superficial

and deep venous networks. During sympathetic activation, peripheral vasoconstriction increases
