venous capacitance and predisposes distal regions to pooling, especially when effective

circulating volume is constrained. Rhythmic contraction of forearm, wrist, and hand musculature

compresses these venous structures and promotes centripetal blood flow via valve-directed

pathways, transiently augmenting venous return to the thorax.

Hand flapping engages this distal muscle pump at high frequency and low energetic cost. Unlike

gross postural adjustments or locomotor activity, such movements can be expressed immediately



during standing or sitting without altering base of support or requiring complex motor planning.

This makes them mechanically well-suited to brief arousal-related demand spikes, including those

associated with excitement or engagement, which may transiently increase cerebral metabolic

demand while simultaneously challenging preload and perfusion stability.

Importantly, arousal in this context is not pathological and need not be aversive. Even positively

valenced states can increase sympathetic tone and redistribute blood volume, narrowing perfusion

margins in individuals with fragile volume or autonomic buffering. Under such conditions,

repetitive distal movements capable of enhancing venous return may emerge automatically as part

of the system’s repertoire of motor-assisted perfusion stabilization, without implying intention,

learning, or conscious strategy.

This framing does not suggest that hand flapping is necessary, sufficient, or diagnostic. Rather, it

is presented as a context-dependent mechanical output that is capable of supporting central

circulation under specific physiological constraints. As vascular tone matures, circulating volume

expands, and autonomic coordination improves, reliance on such distal blood-pumping

movements would be expected to diminish in many individuals.


38.3 Extreme Lateral Gaze and Peri-Cranial Sensory Anchoring as

Subcortical State-Stabilization (Illustrative, Non-Diagnostic)

In addition to gross motor patterns such as toe walking and hand flapping, many autistic children

exhibit subtler but highly stereotyped behaviors involving extreme lateral gaze, often combined

with periocular finger rubbing, holding objects beside the head while looking sideways at them,

or preferring social interaction while maintaining a pronounced sideways gaze. These behaviors

are frequently interpreted in social or behavioral terms. Within the Sodium-Struggle framework,

however, they may be understood as additional expressions of subcortical regulatory engagement

that provide relief associated with improved state stabilization under conditions of physiological

constraint.

Unlike casual sideways glances, extreme lateral gaze requires the eyes to be actively held against

elastic recoil of orbital tissues. Maintaining this position recruits gaze-holding circuitry within a

set of brainstem integrator nuclei, including the paramedian pontine reticular formation (PPRF),

medial vestibular nuclei, and nucleus prepositus hypoglossi. These nuclei are responsible for

sustaining eye position rather than initiating eye movement, and critically, they do not function in

isolation. These nuclei sit at the interface of oculomotor control, vestibular input, autonomic tone,

and arousal regulation, forming part of a broader brainstem network that stabilizes internal state

during changes in posture, engagement, or arousal.

Engagement of these nuclei is amplified when gaze is held far laterally, as stronger and more

sustained neural activity is required to maintain eye position. In physiological systems operating

near their buffering limits, increased signal strength within these integrator circuits may improve

coherence across vestibulo-oculomotor and autonomic domains, reducing internal instability. In

this context, the relevance of extreme gaze lies not in visual exploration, but in its capacity to



strongly recruit fast, developmentally early stabilizing circuits that bypass metabolically costly

cortical control.

Periocular finger rubbing and holding objects beside the head introduce an additional stabilizing

component through trigeminal afferent input, particularly via the ophthalmic division. Sustained,

predictable tactile and proprioceptive input from the peri-cranial region engages brainstem

sensory nuclei that project to the parabrachial nucleus and reticular formation, further integrating

somatosensory, autonomic, and arousal signals. When objects are held beside the head and

viewed with extreme lateral gaze, they function as stable visual anchors, allowing prolonged

activation of gaze-holding circuitry while simultaneously providing continuous trigeminal and

proprioceptive input. These combined inputs converge on the same subcortical regulatory hubs,

reinforcing state stabilization without requiring gross movement or postural change.

Some children also generate low-complexity, self-produced auditory input during these behaviors,

such as the faint sound of finger rubbing. Auditory signals are integrated centrally via the inferior

colliculus, which projects to both the parabrachial nucleus and reticular formation, contributing

modestly to arousal regulation by increasing sensory predictability. While this auditory

component is likely secondary, it may further reduce internal variability during arousal-related

demand spikes.

A similar physiological logic applies to the observation that some autistic children prefer to

engage socially while maintaining sideways gaze toward others. Extreme lateral viewing reduces

reliance on high-demand foveal and frontal cortical processing while preserving peripheral visual

awareness and sustained engagement of vestibulo-oculomotor stabilizing circuitry. This

configuration allows continued interaction while limiting cortical load, again favoring subcortical

regulatory control when buffering margins are narrow.

These behaviors should not be interpreted as intentional strategies, learned habits, or attempts to

trigger endocrine release. Rather, they are best understood as automatic, context-dependent

motor–sensory patterns that engage fast integrative circuitry spanning the brainstem and

hypothalamus. Although nuclei such as the paraventricular nucleus (PVN) and supraoptic nucleus

(SON) are central to autonomic coordination and vasopressin-mediated volume defence, their

relevance here is contextual rather than causal. These behaviors occur within a physiological

milieu already shaped by sodium–volume and autonomic regulation, without implying that the

behaviors themselves initiate vasopressin release.

Within the Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis, extreme lateral gaze combined with peri-cranial sensory

anchoring represents a downstream expression of regulatory physiology operating near its limits,

in which subcortical state-stabilization produces relief by improving synchrony between arousal,

autonomic tone, and perfusion capacity. As buffering capacity matures or physiological

constraints ease, reliance on such patterns would be expected to diminish, consistent with their

context-dependent and non-diagnostic nature.



39 Vagal Down-Regulation for Pressure and Perfusion

Preservation

(Most plastic downstream consequence; functional set-point recalibration)

Under the Sodium-Struggle (Hyponatraemia / Sodium-Switch) framework, autonomic regulation

is treated as a downstream, highly adjustable layer of defence: a functional recalibration that

protects pressure and perfusion when effective sodium is limited. This level of the phenotype is

therefore positioned as the most plastic downstream consequence in the Section 12

roadmap—less “sticky” than receptor imprinting, and far less permanent than adrenal zonation or

nephron architecture. Its enduring signature is not a fixed resting abnormality, but a

context-dependent pattern of prioritization that can remain latent at baseline and become

conspicuous only when the system is asked to stabilize perfusion under load.

Given the receptor signature summarized elsewhere (see Table 4-4: AVPR1A/1B ↑, OXTR ↓,

AQP4 ↑, with context-dependent AVPR2/AQP2) and the EA-CSF hypothesis logic (Box 4-B), the

Sodium-Struggle model treats endocrine and receptor-level economies as capable of consolidating

into autonomic set-point recalibration. These upstream patterns are therefore read not only as

molecular markers, but as developmental constraints that can shape how autonomic thresholds are

learned for defending pressure and perfusion.


Autonomic prioritization under sodium–perfusion threat

During low-effective-sodium states, the autonomic system is compelled to preserve arterial

pressure and maintain perfusion to vulnerable territories. In this setting, parasympathetic (vagal)

influence is reduced conditionally—not because regulation has failed, but because the

moment-to-moment cost of strong vagal buffering can be incompatible with pressure

preservation. Stabilization is temporarily carried more heavily by sympathetic recruitment,

vasopressinergic signalling, and cortisol-mediated support. The defining claim is prioritization

rather than breakdown: the organism reallocates autonomic influence toward immediate

circulatory stability, accepting short-term reductions in vagal modulation as part of an adaptive

sodium–perfusion defence strategy with developmental trade-offs.


What becomes imprinted: calibrations and thresholds, not a single

resting outcome

The imprint proposed here is not a single autonomic “output,” but a set of calibrated targets that

shape how the system responds when perfusion is threatened. The principal targets include: vagal

set-point calibration; baroreflex gain and sensitivity; vestibulo–autonomic integration; thresholds

for sympathetic recruitment; and the coordination between vagal regulation and

AVP/RAAS/cortisol signalling. These calibrations are explicitly time-window dependent,

heterogeneous across individuals, and partially plastic. In other words, the imprint is expressed

most clearly as altered response logic—how rapidly, how strongly, and under what conditions

different stabilizing pathways are recruited—rather than as a uniform baseline signature.



Because vagal calibration is a hub-level modulator, state-dependent recalibration can plausibly

shape trait-level readouts across multiple downstream domains, without being deterministic.

Possible expressions include cardiac variability profiles (HRV reactivity and recovery), GI

regulation, immune balance, and the timing of stress recovery and emotion regulation. In some

individuals, the same autonomic instability may also amplify signatures in speech and motor

coordination (e.g., dyspraxia or childhood apraxia of speech) as downstream expressions that

become more legible under load rather than as universal outcomes.


Vagal fragility is not fixed low tone

A central boundary in this model is the distinction between persistent low vagal tone (which is

not claimed) and fragile vagal regulation under load (which is claimed). Adult individuals with

ASD do not necessarily have low baseline vagal tone. Many may show near-typical baseline HRV

and normal resting parasympathetic markers, and this does not falsify autonomic imprinting.

What persists is context-dependent fragility, not constant suppression.

Accordingly, the imprint is expected to appear as exaggerated drops in vagal indices during

common physiological challenges—illness, dehydration, postural change, motion, or sensory

stress—despite unremarkable resting measures. Vagal regulation may partially recover, overshoot,

or oscillate between withdrawal and vagotonia across time and context, reflecting an adaptive and

adjustable strategy rather than a fixed deficit. The autonomic phenotype is thus better

conceptualized as state-dependent reactivity and recovery dynamics than as a static resting trait.


Sympathetic bias as residual architecture

Repeated sodium-defence episodes are proposed to leave a residual architecture in which

sympathetic recruitment, vasopressin signalling, and cortisol mediation become the most readily

available stabilizing options. Even if vagal tone later appears to normalize at rest, these pathways

may remain dominant, faster to recruit, and harder to down-regulate. This residual bias offers a

physiological account—without requiring permanent vagal failure—of hyper-arousal, sensory

amplification, and difficulty returning to baseline after stress. The trade-off is intelligible in the

model’s terms: a system repeatedly tasked with defending pressure under sodium–perfusion

constraint learns to prioritize rapid stabilizing strategies, and later expresses that prioritization as

a tendency toward strong and persistent mobilization when challenged.


Hypothesis Box: Cortical over-connectivity cascade

(vestibulo–autonomic -> perfusion -> ligand landscape ->

microstructure)

The following cascade is offered explicitly as a hypothesis-level mechanism within the

Sodium-Struggle framework, intended to be separable from the core autonomic argument above.

●​ Vestibulo–autonomic compensation for low vagal tone produces transient cortical perfusion

instability.

●​ A ligand scarcity background (from low fetal/early-infant AVP context) drives local AVPR1A

up-regulation.



●​ Later brief AVP surges acting on this hypersensitized substrate provoke excessive

heparan-sulfate proteoglycan (HSPG) synthesis at synaptic and vascular interfaces.

●​ HSPG microdomains modulate synaptogenesis/pruning and can reinforce pockets of

hyper-connected cortex.

This is presented as a bounded hypothesis within the Sodium-Struggle framework rather than an

established mechanism; it is included here to motivate targeted measurements linking

vestibulo–autonomic load, transient perfusion dynamics, and AVP-linked ligand landscapes

during development.


Lifespan expression as context-dependent instability

Because this imprint is partially plastic and context-sensitive, its expression across development

is expected to be variable rather than uniform. Early on, it can present as vagal fragility under

posture, motion, and novelty, with difficulty settling after stress and episodes described clinically

as pallor, zoning-out, or near-syncope-like events. As demands broaden, childhood expression

may emphasize hyper-arousal in challenging contexts, labile or atypical HRV profiles, strong

sensory–emotional coupling, and pronounced “good days / bad days” variability. By adolescence

and adulthood, trajectories diverge: some individuals appear physiologically typical at baseline

yet show the characteristic “fine until suddenly not fine” pattern—instability under stress despite

normal labs, with fatigue and burnout-like states emerging when cumulative load exceeds the

system’s state-dependent capacity to stabilize.


Alignment with ASD autonomic findings without collapsing

heterogeneity

This section is designed to align with the mixed autonomic literature in ASD without reducing it

to a single defect model. Many (not all) studies report reduced HRV and reduced parasympathetic

markers alongside increased sympathetic indices, consistent with a system that relies readily on

sympathetic stabilization under challenge. At the same time, heterogeneity and context

dependence are expected, including toddler findings in which baseline HRV may be atypically

high—an observation that fits a developmental account of shifting set-points rather than a

universal, fixed suppression. Clinical observations frequently described in ASD—motion

sensitivity, odd gait, syncope-like episodes, and meltdown patterns—are interpreted here as

expressions of autonomic distinctiveness under specific loads, not as evidence for a single

permanent failure state. The model explicitly rejects “low vagal tone = autism.”


Testable predictions and boundary conditions

If autonomic imprinting under sodium–perfusion constraint is correct, a primary prediction is that

resting autonomic measures can appear normal while challenge responses are exaggerated.

Differences should emerge most reliably during dynamic perturbations—vestibular challenge,

postural change, illness, dehydration, and sensory or emotional demand—rather than during

baseline testing. Regressive cases are predicted to show greater instability during the walking

window, when upright locomotion amplifies vestibulo–autonomic requirements and exposes



fragile regulation. Accordingly, HRV and perfusion measures should be assessed dynamically,

emphasizing reactivity, recovery, and state transitions rather than single resting snapshots.

●​ Larger copeptin swings in the relevant subgroup and challenge conditions.

●​ Transient fronto-temporal hypoperfusion during early gait/postural challenges measurable by

fNIRS (or similar optical perfusion measures).

●​ Increased frequency of “odd gait” features—wider step width, slower speed, longer

stance—used as clinical proxies for vestibulo–autonomic dysregulation.

Finally, this section establishes explicit boundary conditions: the pattern is not claimed to be

universal, not claimed to be permanent, and not offered as an intervention prescription. The

central claim is adaptive recalibration with developmental trade-offs—an autonomic strategy for

defending perfusion under sodium constraint that can remain subtle at rest, become prominent

under load, and vary across individuals and developmental time-windows.


40 Mechanical Engagement: The Walking Window as a

Conditional Probe of Imprinted Physiology

40.0 Purpose and scope

This section is a conditional engagement module. It describes a common developmental context

in which an already-imprinted sodium–volume–perfusion physiology may be stressed or revealed,

without implying that regression is universal, that walking is causal, or that upright locomotion is

required for later phenotype expression. The locomotor transition window is framed here as

crawling to standing to walking: a period in which whole-body mechanics change quickly and the

infant must repeatedly solve upright perfusion control while integrating vestibular, postural, and

exploratory demands.

While most children enter a locomotor transition window, the hypothesis does not claim that

regression is universal, and many children later diagnosed with ASD show early baseline features

without a discrete loss of skills. Accordingly, the walking window is treated as a probe of

pre-existing constraints rather than a trigger. It is neither required nor an upstream explanation for

sodium struggle or diagnosis.

The goal of the section is narrow. It specifies why the mechanics of upright locomotion can

engage autonomic regulation, venous return, and perfusion margins in ways that make an earlier

calibration legible, especially when state transitions become frequent. The section emphasizes

physiological engagement of existing control loops (fast autonomic reflexes and slower volume

defenses) rather than introduction of novel mechanisms.


40.1 Why this transition is mechanically unique



Crawling, standing, and walking are often discussed as motor milestones, yet the transition also

represents a shift in whole-body physics and cardiovascular boundary conditions. The infant

moves from a relatively horizontal, low hydrostatic-gradient posture to a vertical column in which

head, heart, abdomen, and lower body are separated along gravity. This change is not limited to

an increased cranial hydrostatic load; it also increases dependence on venous return management

and on rapid compensation for preload variability during frequent posture and motion transitions.

Established physiology supports three coupled mechanical facts relevant to upright posture:

Gravity produces dependent venous pooling and reduces central blood volume.​

In upright posture, blood volume redistributes toward dependent venous beds, lowering effective

venous return and transiently reducing cardiac preload. This is not inherently pathological; it is a

basic mechanical consequence of standing.

Venous return is supported by active mechanical assists that are variably available during

early gait.​

In mature locomotion, the skeletal muscle pump and respiratory pump contribute to venous

return and help stabilize preload during upright activity. Early walking, however, is characterized

by short bouts, variable co-contraction, frequent pauses, and frequent near-falls and recoveries. A

conservative developmental extension is that mechanical venous return assistance is intermittent

and inconsistent during this phase, even when overall health is typical.

Dynamic orthostasis differs from quiet standing.​

Quiet standing is a steady-state problem; early gait is predominantly a dynamic orthostatic

problem. The relevant stressor is not merely “being upright,” but being upright while repeatedly

transitioning through sit-to-stand, squat-to-stand, stand-to-fall, turn-to-reach, and stop–start

cycles, each of which can impose abrupt swings in venous return and arterial pressure.

Key mechanical changes during the locomotor transition include:

●​ A larger head-to-heart distance during upright posture, increasing the vertical pressure

gradient that must be defended to preserve cerebral perfusion. This is the most intuitive

gravitational component, but it is only one part of the load.​

●​ Greater dependence on venous return stabilization because upright posture increases

gravitational pooling into dependent venous compartments. The consequence is increased

variability in preload during transitions, which must be buffered by autonomic and

mechanical mechanisms.​

●​ Explicit engagement of the splanchnic venous reservoir as a gravity-sensitive

capacitance compartment. The splanchnic circulation functions as a large, compliant

venous reservoir whose filling state is sensitive to posture and sympathetic tone. During

repeated postural transitions, the splanchnic compartment can contribute to beat-to-beat

and breath-to-breath variability in venous return, making preload control a continuously



active problem rather than a one-time adjustment.​

●​ Immature or inconsistent skeletal muscle pump support during early walking. Early gait

provides less reliable rhythmic calf and leg-muscle pumping than later walking or

running, and frequent pauses and instability can intermittently remove mechanical

assistance to venous return at exactly the moments when gravity-dependent pooling is

highest.​

●​ Faster swings in venous return with rapid state changes. Sit-to-stand and squat-to-stand

transitions can produce abrupt reductions in preload before compensatory

vasoconstriction and heart-rate adjustments fully engage.​

●​ Higher requirement for beat-to-beat stability under movement. Exploration involves short

bursts of acceleration, deceleration, head turns, and reorientation rather than steady-state

stance.​

●​ Increased vestibular drive during motion. Head motion and orientation changes become

more complex and more frequent, adding continuous sensory input into brainstem

autonomic reflex control.​


The walking window is therefore distinct not because it introduces a novel physiology, but

because it compresses pressure management and venous return management into a

high-frequency set of real-time control problems. The probe value of the window arises from

repeated sampling of these control demands under daily-life conditions, not from any claim that

upright posture is intrinsically injurious.


40.2 The autonomic challenge: vagal buffering, baroreflex gain,

vestibulo-autonomic coupling

Upright locomotion requires a coordinated autonomic strategy operating across arterial and

venous domains. Parasympathetic (vagal) activity contributes buffering and braking of heart rate

and supports flexible recovery, while sympathetic efferents provide rapid pressor support through

changes in cardiac inotropy and chronotropy and through vasoconstriction. In the upright context,

sympathetic regulation is also relevant to capacitance control (venous tone, including splanchnic

venous tone), because venous return is a primary determinant of preload and therefore of cardiac

output during transitions.

During early gait, the baroreflex must operate effectively across frequent perturbations because

the system is repeatedly asked to stabilize pressure and flow during transitions that occur in

seconds rather than minutes. This is best framed as a demand on coordination and bandwidth of

existing reflex loops, not as a novel mechanism.



Developmental control-system constraints (conservative framing).​

In early life, cardiovascular reflex coordination and gain are still maturing. This does not imply

deficit; it implies that reflex parameters (gain, latency, integration across efferent outputs) are still

being tuned across development. A plausible extension is that frequent perturbations during the

locomotor transition can therefore be more consequential for moment-to-moment perfusion

stability than the same perturbations later in childhood, because the controller is still in a

calibration-rich phase.

Cerebral buffering interacts with systemic control.​

Cerebral autoregulation and neurovascular coupling act as local buffering mechanisms that

support cerebral blood flow across systemic blood pressure fluctuations. In early life,

autoregulatory operation may sit closer to lower pressure limits in some contexts, meaning that

small transient reductions in arterial pressure or cardiac output can more readily translate into

measurable changes in cerebral oxygen delivery during rapid posture changes. This statement is

conditional and mechanistic; it describes a narrower margin under some physiological states, not

a universal vulnerability.

Vestibulo–autonomic coupling as concrete substrate.​

The vestibular system is not merely a balance sensor here. Vestibular afferents projecting to

vestibular nuclei interact with autonomic brainstem circuitry, including nuclei that integrate

visceral and baroreceptor information and shape vagal and sympathetic output. The vagus nerve

(cranial X) and vestibular pathways (cranial VIII afferents to brainstem nuclei) therefore converge

functionally on shared autonomic brainstem integration points, making vestibulo–autonomic

coupling a physiological substrate rather than a metaphor. In practice, vestibular-driven

adjustments can contribute to rapid blood pressure stabilization during motion, while vagal

modulation shapes dynamic range, timing, and recovery.

Bridge to sodium–volume defense logic (slow-loop integration; bounded).​

This section describes engagement of existing fast loops, not a new mechanism. If earlier

imprinting has already down-tuned vagal buffering, narrowed perfusion margins, or shifted

baseline volume regulation, then the locomotor transition increases how often the system must

recruit compensatory stabilization. Repeated fast-loop challenges (seconds–minutes) can

plausibly increase reliance on slower renal and endocrine volume defenses (hours–days) that

defend effective circulating volume and osmolality. This is conceptually consistent with the

sodium–volume defense logic developed elsewhere in the manuscript; it is not a re-derivation of

RAAS or AVP pathways.

Crucially, this subsection remains conditional: it proposes that if inhibitory vagal input is reduced

or if volume/perfusion margins are narrow, baroreflex and vestibulo-sympathetic reflexes may

carry a larger share of pressure control during frequent motion. In that setting, transient

reductions in cerebral perfusion are more likely to occur during movement transitions than at rest.

See Section 16 for the foundational vagal and autonomic regulation framework that this

subsection is invoking rather than re-deriving.



40.3 Hydrostatic load and lever-arm effects as a conditional amplifier

A simple physical relationship helps define why upright posture can act as an amplifier of

pre-existing constraints. The hydrostatic pressure gradient across a vertical column scales with

ρ·g·h, where ρ is fluid density, g is gravitational acceleration, and h is vertical height. As infants

move toward upright stance and early walking, the effective h between heart level and cranial

circulation increases relative to crawling, and pressure management must compensate for that

gravitational head (see schematic comparison of hydrostatic column geometry across postures).

This subsection is intentionally bounded. Stature and body length are proposed only as

conditional amplifiers of the orthostatic control problem, not as determinants. Larger h can

increase the magnitude of transient pressure dips during rapid postural transitions, particularly

when buffering is fragile and compensatory reflexes must carry more of the stabilizing load.

Hydrostatic load also interacts with venous return mechanics. Upright posture increases

dependent venous filling pressures and promotes pooling into gravity-sensitive capacitance beds,

including the lower extremities and the splanchnic venous reservoir, effectively increasing

vascular compliance of the dependent circulation relative to the crawling state. Because the

splanchnic compartment is both large and actively regulated by sympathetic tone, its

posture-sensitive filling state can contribute to preload variability during repeated transitions. In

this sense, “hydrostatic amplification” should be read as a coupled arterial–venous problem:

defending cranial perfusion requires both adequate arterial pressure and stable venous return.

Illustrative clinical analogues (existence proofs only; not diagnostic or causal).​

In older children and adolescents, conditions described as orthostatic intolerance, initial

orthostatic hypotension, and POTS demonstrate that upright posture and rapid standing transitions

can be non-trivial for perfusion control in some physiologies, often through low preload and

compensatory autonomic strategies. Separately, hypermobility and connective-tissue compliance

phenotypes illustrate that mechanical properties of the vascular–venous system can modulate

orthostatic tolerance via effects on pooling and venous return. These references serve only as

analogical support for the general principle that upright mechanics can meaningfully load

autonomic and volume regulation; they are not claims that infants have adult syndromes, nor that

these syndromes explain ASD.

This is not deterministic, not a risk-factor framing, and not a claim about necessity or sufficiency.

It is a mechanical modifier that can influence how strongly upright locomotion exposes an

already-imprinted vestibulo–autonomic and sodium–volume calibration.


40.4 Why cortex matters here

The walking window matters in this framework not because motor cortex is uniquely targeted, but

because locomotor exploration increases the demand placed on association cortex, which is

integrative, metabolically expensive, and often perfusion-marginal in watershed topologies.



Association territories must combine vestibular, proprioceptive, visual, and interoceptive inputs

while coordinating attention, prediction, and state regulation under continuous movement. If

perfusion stability is already constrained, then frequent, brief perturbations can impose repeated

micro-variation in the cortical supply–demand relationship during the very period when

integrative workload is expanding.

A conservative physiological bridge is that cerebral autoregulation and neurovascular coupling

must buffer these transient systemic perturbations in real time. When operating near lower

pressure limits in particular states, small reductions in arterial pressure or cardiac output during

rapid transitions can more readily translate into transient reductions in cerebral oxygen delivery,

even if baseline rest measures appear normal. This does not imply injury; it specifies a

mechanism by which variability in systemic control can become visible at the cortical level

during high-demand motion.

In compressed form, this framework further proposes a speculative but testable downstream

possibility: repeated transient perfusion instability during vestibularly intense exploration could

bias receptor-level and glial–synaptic adaptations (for example, AVPR1A-related signaling

changes) in vulnerable cortical territories, shaping pruning and network timing under constrained

margins. This remains a hypothesis-level extension and should be read as contingent on

demonstrable perfusion variability during the window. Section 25 describes how early

sodium–perfusion instability can be recorded as timing artefacts in association cortex; the

walking window is one early developmental context in which that structurally constrained cortex

is asked to operate under rapidly fluctuating autonomic, venous-return, and vestibular demands.

Box 40-A. Toe Walking as a Perfusion-Stabilizing Adaptation During

the Walking Window (Illustrative, Non-Diagnostic)

Toe walking is commonly interpreted as a sensory preference, musculoskeletal anomaly, or habit.

An alternative, physiology-based interpretation consistent with the framework developed in

Section 24 is that toe walking may, in some contexts, function as a mechanical counter-maneuver

that augments venous return and stabilizes cerebral perfusion during upright posture, particularly

when autonomic or volume buffering is fragile.

In upright humans, the gastrocnemius–soleus complex plays a major role in supporting venous

return by compressing deep venous structures in the lower limb and promoting upward blood

flow against gravity. This skeletal muscle pump is especially important during quiet standing or

brief pauses in movement, when venous pooling can occur rapidly in dependent vascular beds. In

early childhood, vascular tone, venous valvular function, and myogenic responses are still

calibrating, increasing venous compliance and reducing the effectiveness of passive resistance to

pooling.

Toe walking engages the skeletal muscle pump continuously. By maintaining plantarflexion, the

child sustains isometric calf contraction, converting the lower limb into a more pressurized

venous column and limiting venous capacitance. This sustained muscle tension can support

central venous return not only during stepping, but also during the frequent stop-and-start pauses

characteristic of early exploration, when the risk of orthostatic pooling is greatest.

The walking window is characterized by a sudden increase in hydrostatic load (ρ·g·h) before

volume–pressure reflexes, vascular tone, and mechanical support systems have fully matured. If

effective circulating volume is limited or autonomic buffering is already strained, toe walking

may provide a direct, low-level mechanical means of supporting preload and cardiac output,

without requiring conscious strategy or cortical planning.

This framing does not imply that toe walking is universal, pathological, or causal. Rather, it is

presented as a context-dependent, potentially compensatory behavior that may arise when upright

posture places disproportionate demands on perfusion control. As muscle pump efficiency

improves, vascular tone matures, and circulating volume expands, such behaviors would be

expected to diminish or resolve in many cases.

This interpretation is hypothesis-generating and testable. It aligns with established orthostatic

physiology and with clinical observations that active calf engagement improves upright tolerance

in older individuals, while remaining agnostic about diagnosis, necessity, or sufficiency.



41 Cortical Architecture as a Timing-Sensitive Developmental

Record of Sodium–Perfusion Instability

41.1 Orientation: Why Cortex Is Not a Primary Regulator

Throughout this manuscript, renal, endocrine, autonomic, and astrocytic systems are treated as the

primary physiological “players” that actively regulate sodium, volume, and perfusion across life.

Cortical architecture is not placed in that category. It does not function as an ongoing

physiological control loop and does not initiate the sodium-struggle cascade. Instead, cortical

structure constitutes a developmental record of how upstream sodium–perfusion constraints were

applied during corticogenesis. In this framework, the cortex is best read as a record of timing,

synchrony, and oxygen-perfusion gating, rather than as a parallel regulator.

It is important to distinguish the form of cortical hypoperfusion discussed in this section from the

compensatory redistributive hypoperfusion described in the most recent preceding sections. In the

splanchnic, cutaneous, and autonomic domains, reduced perfusion reflects active physiological

trade-offs, in which blood flow is redistributed to defend core pressure, volume, or

survival-critical functions under stress. Cortical hypoperfusion in this framework arises through a

different mechanism. It does not result from active diversion of blood away from cortex, but from

downstream limitations of delivery. When upstream sodium–volume and perfusion constraints

narrow pressure and timing margins, blood flow may fail to reach distal cortical territories

uniformly or synchronously. In this sense, cortical hypoperfusion reflects downstream exposure to

propagation limits within the vascular tree, rather than a redistributive process.


41.2 Oxygen as a Developmental Timing Signal

Corticogenesis proceeds under a physiologically low-oxygen milieu in which neural stem and

progenitor populations remain proliferative. Angiogenic ingrowth raises local oxygen availability

and, in doing so, provides a timing signal that coordinates the transition from proliferation to

differentiation. The relevant developmental variable is the moment and spatial uniformity of the

low-to-higher oxygen transition; oxygen is treated here as a gating cue, not as a proxy for tissue

disruption. When the oxygen rise occurs earlier, later, or less synchronously across adjacent

neurogenic fields, progenitor cohorts do not exit the cell cycle in a temporally aligned manner.

These shifts alter the pace of radial unit expansion, the schedule of laminar assembly, and the

alignment between neuronal production and the metabolic support that accompanies vascular

maturation. Thus, oxygen availability functions as a gate that timestamps corticogenesis, and

upstream sodium–perfusion instability is expressed developmentally through its effects on the

stability of that gate.


41.3 Why Association Cortex Is Especially Vulnerable

Not all cortical territories are equally timing-sensitive. Evolutionarily expanded association

regions are characterised by prolonged neurogenic timelines, extended periods of radial unit

amplification, and high metabolic demand during late gestational construction. These same

regions are commonly positioned in perfusion-marginal zones at the distal ends of arterial trees,

where small variations in perfusion translate into larger variations in oxygen availability at the

tissue level. In such fields, the oxygen-gated shift from proliferation to differentiation must

remain coherent across broad spatial domains for normal areal and laminar proportions to emerge.

Accordingly, when upstream systems constrain sodium and perfusion with oscillatory or spatially

uneven consequences, association cortex functions as a timing-sensitive recorder: it is



preferentially where minor asynchronies in vascular and metabolic support are written into stable

architecture.


41.4 Developmental Timing Artifacts, Not Defects

The downstream consequences of oxygen-timing asynchrony are best described in terms of

developmental scheduling rather than in terms of post hoc failure. An uneven oxygen rise across

neighbouring fields can cause some radial units to exit proliferation while adjacent units remain

proliferative, yielding local discontinuities in areal expansion and laminar progression. Similarly,

small differences in the temporal alignment between neuronal differentiation, glial maturation,

and vascular support can produce patch-like laminar patterns and subtle irregularities in

minicolumn spacing. These architectural features are timing artefacts rather than defects: they

reflect stable outcomes of mismatched developmental clocks, not evidence of a destructive event

or a failed program. Within this causal hierarchy, the cortex does not explain why

sodium–perfusion instability occurred; it records the timing and spatial coherence of gated

developmental transitions during the calibration of upstream control systems.


41.5 Table 41 — Developmental Timing Map of Cortical Features

For clarity, the developmental timing logic described above is summarised schematically in Table

25. This table should be read as a developmental timing map—a reader-control device for the

logic in this section—rather than as new evidence or as a catalogue of cortical differences.

41.6 Cortex as Artefact That Influences Later Physiology

Although the cortex does not regulate physiology directly, its structure can bias how existing

regulatory systems are engaged later in life. By shaping prediction error, sensory integration

bandwidth, and the salience assigned to interoceptive and exteroceptive signals, cortical

architecture can increase or decrease the frequency with which defensive autonomic and

endocrine programs are recruited, even when the underlying regulatory constraints remain

unchanged. Architectural context shapes perception and integration of internal and external

signals, influences prediction and salience assignment, and constrains behavioural engagement

patterns. Through these interfaces, the cortex can modulate the pattern and context with which



renal, endocrine, autonomic, and glial "player" systems are recruited, without becoming an

upstream driver of sodium, volume, perfusion, or hormonal set-points.


41.7 Bridge to Tertiary Expression

This section has described how upstream sodium–perfusion constraints are written into cortical

structure during corticogenesis as a timing- and synchrony-sensitive record. The following

sections address how that structurally constrained cortex participates in later, state-dependent

systemic expression, while preserving the upstream causal hierarchy established in the direct

downstream regulatory domains.

42 Secondary Systemic Consequences of Early

Sodium-Instability Imprinting

The preceding sections described a primary developmental imprinting layer produced by early

sodium-volume instability, including renal programming, adrenal routing shifts, autonomic bias, vascular



and microperfusion fragility, and hypothalamic or glial stress signalling. The chloride section then added an

intracellular persistence layer: repeated osmotic and volume-regulatory stress may stabilise a

chloride-weighted intracellular operating point, especially in astrocytes, through NKCC1-biased ion

handling, astrocyte volume loading, reduced extracellular-space buffering margin, weaker inhibitory

leverage through chloride effects on E_GABA, increased Ca²⁺ signalling duty cycle, increased

Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase workload, and reinforcement through ATP, ROS, BH4, NO, and AVP-linked loops. The

present section addresses the still later consequences that become plausible once those earlier layers are

already in place.

These consequences are downstream-of-downstream phenomena. They are conditional, heterogeneous, and

strongly state-dependent, rather than universal readouts or fixed chronic disease states. They are not

proposed as primary causes of autism. Their expression should depend on later physiological load,

including infection, heat, dehydration, pain, sleep disruption, sustained stress, repeated illness, or clustered

sensory and metabolic demand. Within this later tier, the chloride-weighted intracellular state may also

make sympathetic and AVP-dominant stabilisation easier to re-enter during dehydration, illness, sensory

load, or sleep disruption, thereby reinforcing autonomic lability as an amplifier rather than a primary cause.


42.1 Metabolic imprinting: insulin resistance and constrained metabolic

flexibility

Metabolic imprinting is used here in a restricted physiological sense: a persistent bias toward stress-linked

insulin resistance and constrained metabolic flexibility, meaning reduced efficiency in shifting between

glucose and lipid utilisation across changing conditions. The primary imprinting layer already biases the

organism toward preservation of effective circulating volume rather than metabolic efficiency. The

chloride-weighted intracellular operating point does not replace that logic. It tightens it from within.

The mechanistic bridge is straightforward. NKCC1-linked chloride defence brings Na⁺ into the cell with

Cl⁻, Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase workload rises in order to preserve low intracellular Na⁺, and energetic headroom

narrows. At rest, that compression may be modest. Under challenge, however, a larger fraction of ATP

turnover must be devoted to restoring gradients and preserving cell volume before other functions are

serviced. The result is less reserve for insulin-mediated glucose disposal, mitochondrial substrate switching,

and rapid post-stress recovery.

Several routes then converge. A cortisol- and catecholamine-supported defence posture increases hepatic

glucose output during perceived volume threat, while sympathetic bias and reduced vagal buffering can

alter pancreatic, hepatic, and skeletal-muscle perfusion during challenge. Because insulin-dependent

glucose clearance depends in part on adequate microvascular recruitment in muscle, a vasoconstrictive

defence state can reduce glucose disposal even without primary pancreatic failure. Earlier vascular and

microperfusion fragility further reduce the dynamic range through which tissues can increase substrate

delivery when heat, dehydration, illness, exertion, pain, or sleep loss raise demand. The chloride-weighted

intracellular state adds a further energetic tax at precisely these moments. Glucose may therefore be

mobilised aggressively for defence, yet tissue-level use becomes less efficient exactly when

ion-homeostatic cost is highest.

This interpretation preserves hierarchy. Metabolic effects remain tertiary outputs of earlier endocrine,

autonomic, and vascular imprinting, tightened further by the intracellular energetic cost of

chloride-weighted ion handling. The model does not recast autism as a primary metabolic disorder, does not

imply that insulin resistance is universal, and does not require a fixed chronic disease state. It proposes only



that, in relevant subsets, stress-linked insulin resistance and constrained metabolic flexibility may emerge

more readily because sodium-volume defence has already been developmentally prioritised and then made

more expensive by chloride-weighted intracellular persistence.


42.2 ATP-mediated tissue state and glial buffering regime

The chloride-weighted intracellular operating point also has a tissue-state consequence. The relevant logic

is sequential: chloride-weighted state, increased ion-transport cost, recurrent ATP release, glial buffering

regime, altered extracellular-space geometry. In that sequence, ATP is not treated as the initiating lesion. It

is a downstream broadcast of ion-energetic strain, while astrocytes become the principal tissue

compartment through which that strain is absorbed and managed. The result is a buffering-first regime in

which more of the tissue’s moment-to-moment budget is directed toward maintenance of extracellular

stability and less remains available for rapid refinement, wide buffering margin, and precise recovery under

load.


42.2.1 ATP as broadcast of ion-energetic strain

In this framework, extracellular ATP is best understood as a broadcast of ion-energetic strain. Repeated

NKCC1-linked chloride defence increases coupled Na⁺ entry and therefore increases Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase

demand. Where swelling, chloride recovery, or ionic reset recur, ATP expenditure rises accordingly, and

part of that burden may be signalled extracellularly through recurrent low-grade ATP release, together with

downstream adenosine generation. This does not require cell injury or overt inflammation. It can emerge

from repeated homeostatic work.

The functional implication is that ATP and adenosine signalling may repeatedly inform surrounding cells

that preservation of ionic viability is expensive. Under those conditions, local tissue may become more

conservative in what it attempts to refine, more dependent on reserve, and more likely to prioritise stable

operation over rapid optimisation. Experience-dependent development remains possible, but it becomes

more conditional on whether the local buffering budget is already heavily committed. Circuits that require

tight timing, rapid phase adjustment, or repeated high-frequency support are therefore likely to show

greater variability when a substantial fraction of ATP turnover is being spent simply to preserve gradients

and volume. In developmental contexts, this buffering-first allocation may also narrow maturation

bandwidth, so that refinement becomes more contingent on whether sufficient ionic and metabolic reserve

is available when tuning signals occur.


42.2.2 Glial buffering regime and ECS geometry

ATP signalling is strongly glia-weighted in both sensing and response. In a chloride-weighted tissue,

recurrent ATP release is likely to recruit astrocytic buffering functions, including K⁺ uptake, glutamate

clearance, water handling, osmotic management, and maintenance of extracellular composition. Because

astrocytes are already the most permissive CNS compartment for chloride-based volume defence, this

redistribution of workload falls most heavily on the same cells that are already prone to volume loading.

The result is astrocyte volume loading and, with repeated recruitment, astrocyte volume-loading–associated

ECS compression. Because extracellular space constitutes only a modest fraction of brain volume, even

modest astrocyte volume loading can materially alter extracellular-space geometry. The consequence is a

reduced extracellular-space buffering margin. In a smaller ECS, the same amount of released extracellular

K⁺, glutamate, ATP, and related signals produces a larger concentration change. Diffusion resistance rises,

uptake machinery begins closer to capacity, and activity-dependent transients increase more quickly and



decay more slowly. The tissue may therefore appear reasonably controlled at rest yet become less forgiving

during sustained or repeated use.

This is the sense in which a glial buffering regime alters tissue state. It is not simply that glia are more

active. It is that a larger share of tissue budget is being routed into extracellular containment, with less spare

bandwidth for rapid tuning, broad dilution, or precise reset. A speculative but testable extension is that

altered astrocyte end-foot geometry and AQP4-dependent water handling could further slow re-expansion

of extracellular buffering margin after demand. Because astrocytes form coupled networks capable of

distributing buffering workload and signalling across regions, local ion-energetic strain may propagate as a

broader tissue-state shift without requiring cell injury or overt inflammation.


42.2.3 Astrocyte-neuron support coupling strain

A chloride-weighted glial buffering regime may also strain astrocyte-neuron support coupling. Astrocytes

do more than clear K⁺ and glutamate. They participate in local energy distribution, glutamine cycling, redox

support, trophic signalling, synaptic maintenance, and preservation of the extracellular conditions on which

neuronal precision depends. When a larger share of astrocytic ATP turnover, membrane transport, and

volume-regulatory effort is repeatedly committed to buffering, less reserve remains for these support

functions.

The predicted effect is not wholesale astrocyte failure. It is reduced flexibility of support. Neurons that rely

on rapid astrocytic assistance for transmitter recycling, metabolic resupply, antioxidant support, or

restoration of local extracellular conditions may therefore recover more slowly after clustered use. Under

developmental conditions, the same reserve redistribution may make activity-dependent refinement more

contingent on whether the surrounding astrocyte network is operating within reserve or already in a

high-buffering state.


42.2.4 Neurovascular coupling imprecision

Local blood-flow matching is especially sensitive to this kind of reserve redistribution. Neurovascular

coupling depends on astrocyte end-feet, Ca²⁺-dependent gliovascular signalling, purinergic signalling,

endothelial responsiveness, and a sufficiently balanced NO milieu. A chloride-weighted intracellular

operating point can plausibly reduce the precision of this matching through several convergent routes.

Astrocyte volume loading may alter end-foot geometry, increased Ca²⁺ signalling duty cycle may make

gliovascular signalling noisier, recurrent ATP release may change local purinergic tone, and ROS to BH4 to

NO shifts may reduce vasodilatory reliability.

Placed on top of earlier vascular and microperfusion fragility, the expected readout is not fixed

hypoperfusion but greater imprecision in matching local flow to local demand. Small timing errors in

delivery can matter even when average perfusion is not grossly abnormal. Under quiet conditions the

mismatch may be modest. Under rapid, repeated, or multisystem demand, however, reduced precision of

local blood-flow matching could compound chloride, ATP, and ECS constraints and prolong recovery after

activity.


42.2.5 State-dependent unmasking and slow recovery

These tissue-state effects should be strongly state-dependent. Many may remain modest or clinically silent

at rest, then become more legible when demand clusters, for example during illness, heat, dehydration, poor

sleep, pain, sustained attention, repeated sensory input, or combined physiological stressors. In those



settings the tissue may enter a high-buffering state in which more ATP is committed to ion transport,

astrocyte volume loading increases, astrocyte volume-loading–associated ECS compression becomes

functionally important, and extracellular transients become larger for a given amount of activity.

Recovery from such states is expected to be slower than clearance of any single transmitter pulse. The

relevant variables are distributed across ion gradients, astrocyte volume, extracellular-space buffering

margin, ATP and adenosine signalling, perfusion matching, and metabolic reserve. Reset therefore requires

more than synaptic quiet. It requires repayment of ion-energetic debt and re-expansion of buffering margin.

This offers a plausible bridge to delayed recovery after clustered demand, while remaining speculative but

testable rather than established as a universal trait.


42.3 Network-level consequences of chloride-weighted physiology

Against that ATP-mediated, glial buffering background, several network-level consequences of

chloride-weighted physiology become more plausible.

PV interneuron vulnerability and gamma timing. Increased intracellular Cl⁻ shifts E_GABA in the positive

direction and weakens inhibitory leverage. At the same time, astrocyte volume-loading–associated ECS

compression allows extracellular K⁺ to accumulate more quickly during activity and reduces the margin for

rapid extracellular dilution. Fast-spiking parvalbumin interneurons operate with high firing rates, narrow

timing requirements, and substantial energetic demand. They therefore sit close to the buffering limits that

are most tightened in a chloride-weighted tissue. The predicted consequence is not simple loss of inhibition.

It is reduced precision of inhibitory recruitment, greater phase jitter, and less stable gamma-range timing

under load.

Oligodendrocyte maturation and myelin timing. Oligodendrocyte lineage cells retain NKCC1 expression,

and their maturation is sensitive to Ca²⁺-dependent developmental signalling. A chloride-weighted

environment, by increasing Ca²⁺ signalling duty cycle and reducing local metabolic and buffering reserve,

may therefore influence the timing or pattern of oligodendrocyte maturation and myelin deposition. Any

resulting effect is expected to concern timing precision rather than uniform demyelination, and may be

especially relevant along fast inhibitory pathways where conduction synchrony materially shapes network

timing.

Seizure vulnerability. The most direct network-level extension concerns reserve against hyperexcitable

transitions. A more positive E_GABA, weaker inhibitory leverage, faster extracellular K⁺ accumulation,

glutamatergic spillover when buffering margin narrows, and slower return to baseline all reduce the

threshold at which ordinary activity can tip into instability. This is established physiology in broad terms,

but the present model places it downstream of sodium-volume imprinting and chloride-weighted

persistence. It does not imply that seizures are universal, autism-specific, or continuously present. It

predicts only that seizure reserve may be lower in the relevant subsets, especially during fever, sleep

disruption, dehydration, or other load states.


42.4 Sleep and recovery of extracellular buffering margin

Sleep may be one of the main physiological windows during which a chloride-weighted tissue can reverse

part of the day’s ion-energetic debt. Reduced sensory throughput and altered neuromodulatory tone may

permit restoration of ion gradients, partial re-expansion of extracellular-space buffering margin, and

replenishment of metabolic reserve. If astrocyte volume loading and altered end-foot water handling



contribute to daytime ECS compression, sleep may also be the period in which astrocyte volume normalises

most effectively and interstitial-perivascular exchange becomes more permissive again.

This framing is speculative but testable. It does not treat sleep disturbance as a primary cause of the

underlying imprint. Rather, it proposes that sleep functions as a recovery window for chloride-weighted

physiology. On that view, sleep disruption or fragmentation would have disproportionate downstream

effects because it shortens the interval available for resetting ion gradients, extracellular-space buffering

margin, and energetic reserve. The prediction is not generic fatigue alone, but slower recovery from

clustered demand, heightened next-day sensory or autonomic lability, and greater vulnerability to

re-entering a high-buffering state.


42.5 Immune and microglial priming

Immune and microglial priming are best framed here as altered activation threshold and altered response

duration, not as chronic baseline inflammation. Priming denotes increased readiness to activate more

readily, or to resolve more slowly, when challenged. It does not require persistent inflammatory tone. A

chloride-weighted intracellular operating point, especially when expressed through recurrent ATP

signalling, extracellular ionic variability, and astrocyte volume-loading–associated ECS compression, may

create a background in which challenge cues carry more salience.

The chloride-linked bridge is specific. Microglia are highly sensitive to extracellular ATP and to changes in

local ionic context. In a tissue with a reduced extracellular-space buffering margin, the same ATP pulse, K⁺

release, or glutamatergic spillover produces a larger local concentration shift than it would in a more

expansive ECS. Repetition of this signal environment may lower the threshold at which subsequent

infection, heat, dehydration, pain, or sleep loss is interpreted as biologically meaningful. Earlier vascular

and autonomic imprinting may add to this by making local delivery, waste clearance, and cortisol timing

less stable during challenge.

The expected result is not universal chronic neuroinflammation. It is a system that may appear quiescent at

baseline yet activate more readily, or recover more slowly, once stressed. In that sense, chloride weighting

does not create a standing inflammatory disease state. It may prime responsiveness by repeatedly pairing

purinergic signalling with extracellular ionic variability and astrocyte volume-loading–associated ECS

compression. This remains a plausible bridge rather than a settled conclusion, but it is physiologically

specific and testable.


Interpretive boundary

The chloride-weighted intracellular operating point does not replace the upstream explanations already

advanced for reduced nephron endowment, placental brain-sparing haemodynamics, adrenal zonation drift,

or splanchnic reservoir recruitment. Those remain part of the primary sodium-volume imprinting layer.

Chloride weighting enters later, as the intracellular persistence-and-amplification layer that can prolong,

localise, or amplify downstream consequences once the earlier sodium-volume architecture has already

been set.

This framework also generates several physiological expectations. Under conditions that reduce energetic

headroom, activity should produce larger extracellular ionic and transmitter transients, greater variability in

timing-dependent network measures, and slower recovery of extracellular buffering variables than of fast

synaptic signalling metrics alone.



Closing synthesis

The hierarchy therefore remains explicit. Early sodium-volume instability may first imprint renal, adrenal,

autonomic, vascular, and hypothalamic-glial regulation. Repeated osmotic and volume-regulatory stress

may then stabilise a chloride-weighted intracellular operating point that preserves volume safety at the cost

of higher ion-transport burden, astrocyte volume loading, reduced extracellular-space buffering margin,

weaker inhibitory leverage, and greater susceptibility to ATP, redox, and AVP-linked reinforcement. Only

after those two layers are in place do the later outputs considered here become more plausible: constrained

metabolic flexibility, ATP-mediated glial buffering regimes, imprecise neurovascular matching, variable

network timing, sleep-dependent recovery failure, and immune or microglial priming. In this formulation,

chloride weighting is neither the initiating insult nor a universal disease state. It is the intracellular

persistence-and-amplification layer that helps explain why later-life phenotypes can diverge, fluctuate, and

intensify under load while preserving sodium-volume primacy.


43 Opposite Sodium Contexts, Opposite Midline Outcomes [H]

Midline phenotypes should not be collapsed into a single sodium story. Holoprosencephaly

belongs to primary forebrain midline failure, classically through impaired Sonic Hedgehog

signalling. The hypernatraemia sometimes seen in HPE is secondary—typically reflecting

hypothalamic or pituitary injury with adipsic or central-diabetes-insipidus physiology—rather

than a causal mirror image of the dilutional states discussed in this manuscript. Its craniofacial

direction is therefore toward hypotelorism because the midline itself fails.

The ASD-facing craniofacial proposal advanced here is narrower and mechanistically different.

The manuscript does not propose partial HPE. It proposes that transient dilutional or

near-hyponatraemic states, especially when stress increases fetal glucocorticoid access during

timing-sensitive windows, could indirectly bias frontonasal and cranial-neural-crest growth. The

relevant route, if real, would be through altered growth kinetics and local lipid/SHH conditions

rather than through failure of hemispheric separation.

Accordingly, opposite sodium contexts should predict opposite midline outcomes. Primary HPE

with secondary hypernatraemic physiology maps to hypotelorism; the hypothesised dilutional

cortisol-biased pathway maps, at most, to the subtle hypertelorism reported in some ASD

subgroups. The point is not equivalence but contrast: ASD-relevant widening, if present, is not

attenuated holoprosencephaly. This remains a bounded hypothesis, not a claim that

glucocorticoids alone determine craniofacial separation.


44 Endocrine-Mineral Interactions

Bracket tags used below denote evidentiary status: [S] = supported/background physiology; [H]

= hypothesis-level extension; [S/H] = mixed section.


44.1.1 Rationale and Framing

This section explains how phosphate is handled during sodium-volume defence physiology.

Section 11.9 has already established the upstream transport event: phosphate flux can provoke

proximal phosphaturia and segment-specific sodium reallocation. The purpose here is not to



repeat that transport mechanism, but to show how the resulting renal signal is interpreted once the

organism is already defending effective sodium availability or extracellular volume. Under

Sodium-Struggle conditions, phosphate handling therefore becomes part of the same

compensatory architecture that governs renal sodium conservation, effective arterial filling, AVP

recruitment, cortisol support, and vascular tone.

The organising concept is a proximal-distal nephron dissociation loop. Phosphate disposal is

initiated proximally, whereas sodium conservation may be recruited distally; the two tasks are

therefore not solved within a single nephron segment. This spatial dissociation creates regulatory

strain: phosphate can continue to be cleared even while distal transport and haemodynamic

systems are being mobilised to defend sodium and perfusion. The sequence followed in this

section is therefore: phosphate flux -> proximal phosphaturia -> PTH (acute bridge) -> delayed

FGF-23 recruitment -> distal NCC-mediated sodium retention -> effective arterial

volume/perfusion -> RAAS branch states -> AVP/cortisol compensation -> oscillatory

sodium-volume regulation.


44.1.2 Proximal-Distal Nephron Dissociation Loop

Increased phosphate flux is first handled by the proximal tubule. The rapid adaptive phosphaturic

response is dominated by reduced apical NaPi-IIa abundance and activity, with NaPi-IIc

contributing more modestly, and PTH serving as the acute endocrine bridge that stabilises

phosphate disposal. This places the transport logic from Section 7.10 into endocrine context:

phosphate is being cleared at the same time that the kidney must decide how much sodium can be

spared under sodium-volume defence conditions.

With repeated or sustained phosphate exposure, FGF-23 is recruited on a slower timescale and

functions as an endocrine integrator rather than an acute first responder. By lowering calcitriol

and reinforcing the phosphaturic bias, FGF-23 helps maintain proximal phosphate disposal;

through canonical signalling in alpha-Klotho-expressing distal tubular targets, it can also favour

NCC-mediated NaCl retention in the distal convoluted tubule. The result is a true nephron

dissociation loop: phosphate disposal proceeds proximally while sodium conservation is

progressively shifted distally.

Vitamin D signalling is treated here only as a modifier of phosphate entry into this loop, chiefly

through effects on intestinal phosphate absorption, rather than as the primary driver of

downstream endocrine configuration. Secondary intrarenal modifiers - including renal dopamine

phosphaturia, tubuloglomerular and connecting-tubule feedback, ANP, and pressure natriuresis can alter the gain of the loop, but they do not replace its core sequence of proximal phosphaturia,

PTH bridging, delayed FGF-23 recruitment, and distal NCC-mediated sodium retention.


44.2 RAAS Branch States and Endocrine Feedback

Once the nephron dissociation loop is established, the next determinant is effective arterial

volume and perfusion. RAAS is therefore treated here as a branch point rather than as a pathway

uniformly suppressed by phosphate or FGF-23. One branch is a classical RAAS-dominant state:

persistent underfilling, low effective sodium availability, or impaired perfusion sustains renin and

aldosterone, with AVP and cortisol recruited as needed. The other branch is a lower-renin state in

which repeated phosphate exposure, delayed FGF-23 recruitment, distal NCC-mediated sodium

retention, and preserved tone reduce the drive for high systemic renin output. Hybrid states are



expected between these poles, especially when chronic phosphate exposure coexists with low

calcitriol, altered thyroid tone, or tissue-specific angiotensin signalling.

This branch structure helps place AVP and cortisol in the correct position of the cascade. When

pressure defence is only partial or when water retention outpaces osmotic correction, AVP can

conserve water rapidly and cortisol can support vascular responsiveness while limiting

nitric-oxide-dependent vasodilation. These responses preserve perfusion more readily than they

restore osmotic balance, so sodium, volume, and tone may continue to oscillate even when overt

hypotension is avoided. FGF-23 is thus better viewed as part of an FGF-23/NCC-supported

branch architecture than as a universal renin-suppressive switch.

Thyroid tone, growth state, and phosphate load modulate the gain of these branch conditions

rather than redefine the core sequence. Low T4 can lower renin-aldosterone drive and sustain

antidiuresis, thereby shifting compensation toward AVP and cortisol. Growth, recurrent dietary

phosphate exposure, or highly absorbable inorganic phosphate sources can enlarge the phosphate

signal entering the loop and increase reliance on delayed FGF-23 integration.
