Figure 44-1 Proximal–distal nephron dissociation loop (schematic)

Figure 44-1. Proximal–distal nephron dissociation loop linking phosphate handling to

sodium-volume defence. Phosphate flux induces proximal phosphaturia while distal

NCC-mediated sodium retention preserves effective arterial volume. Because renal

transport responses, endocrine signalling, and vascular responses operate on different

time scales, the system tends toward oscillatory sodium-volume regulation (“osmotic

pendulum”).


44.3 Vascular / Klotho Modifiers [S/H]

Klotho status requires compartmental distinction. Canonical FGF-23 signalling to distal NCC

depends on transmembrane alpha-Klotho in renal distal tubular cells; this is not equivalent to a

single global low-Klotho state. By contrast, circulating soluble Klotho and vascular Klotho

environments are better treated as modifiers of endothelial resilience, oxidative stress, and

nitric-oxide signalling.

Direct phosphate loading, oxidative stress, and functionally low soluble-Klotho vascular

environments may bias the circulation toward reduced NO bioavailability and higher tone. These

vascular effects can reinforce a defended-pressure state and alter the haemodynamic reading of

the nephron dissociation loop, but they are context-dependent and should not be simplified into a

single obligatory FGF-23 vascular action. Chronic phosphate exposure may coexist with lower

soluble Klotho or with vascular terrains that behave as relatively Klotho-poor, yet the renal

distal-nephron requirement for alpha-Klotho remains intact within the canonical NCC arm.

Within this framework, vascular and skeletal corollaries should be interpreted cautiously.

Reduced bone mineral density reported in some autism cohorts is compatible with altered mineral

handling during repeated phosphaturic and calcitriol-lowering states, but it is not specific

evidence for a phosphate-driven endocrine mechanism. Likewise, any relation between Klotho

measures and autism populations remains preliminary and is better treated as exploratory context

than as established biomarker evidence.


44.4 Environmental and Population Modifiers [S/H]

Environmental exposures that impair cellular volume recovery can amplify, rather than create, the

present loop. Toxicants that disrupt actin polymerisation or barrier function may prolong

osmolyte loss and increase reliance on endocrine compensation. Highly absorbable phosphate

additives can enlarge the upstream phosphate signal entering the nephron dissociation loop, while



endocrine-disrupting co-exposures may alter GR/MR balance or vascular reactivity. Their effects

are therefore best viewed as gain modifiers of an already unstable sodium-volume defence

system.

Population and developmental context may shift where the RAAS branch point sits. Backgrounds

shaped by chronic heat or salt stress, sex differences in early RAAS tone, renal maturity, growth

rate, diet, and fluid habits could all influence whether repeated phosphate exposure is absorbed

into a classical RAAS-dominant response or into a lower-renin or hybrid state once distal sodium

retention and vascular tone partly defend effective arterial volume. Within the present framework,

osmotic mismatch is only one additional physiological lens and does not displace major social,

diagnostic, and developmental explanations for group differences.

Nutritional contexts that lower vascular resistance may also modify branch behaviour. For

example, strongly vasodilatory dietary conditions, including some high-PUFA patterns, are

cardiometabolically favourable in most settings but could increase reliance on AVP/cortisol

support in individuals already prone to low effective sodium tone or unstable extracellular

volume. This remains a context-dependent hypothesis rather than a primary explanation.


44.5 Oscillatory Sodium-Phosphate Regulation [S/H]

The core oscillation logic follows directly from the loop. Repeated phosphate flux drives

proximal phosphaturia; PTH bridges the early phase; delayed FGF-23 recruitment stabilises

phosphate disposal while favouring alpha-Klotho-dependent distal NCC sodium retention. The

resulting change in effective arterial volume and perfusion determines whether the system

occupies a classical RAAS-dominant state, a lower-renin FGF-23/NCC-supported state, or a

hybrid between the two. When pressure defence outpaces osmotic correction, AVP-mediated

water retention and cortisol-mediated vascular support are repeatedly recruited, making

sodium-volume regulation oscillatory rather than smoothly self-limiting.

Oscillation is expected because the relevant mechanisms operate on mismatched secretory

mechanisms and time scales. Proximal transport responses are rapid, and hormones such as PTH

and AVP are pre-synthesised and stored in secretory vesicles, allowing release within minutes

through exocytosis. By contrast, sustained FGF-23 recruitment requires de novo transcription in

osteocytes followed by endocrine secretion, so it integrates repeated phosphate exposure over

many hours to days. RAAS signalling can adjust over minutes to hours, whereas vascular

oxidative or low-NO states evolve more slowly. These mismatched cellular mechanisms make

oscillatory sodium-volume regulation physiologically likely rather than exceptional.

The resulting physiology is an osmotic pendulum rather than a linear pathway. Phosphate

clearance, sodium retention, water conservation, and vascular tone do not reset at the same speed,

so the system may oscillate between partial correction and renewed compensatory recruitment.

This formulation keeps phosphate handling inside sodium-volume defence physiology instead of

treating it as a parallel mineral pathway.


44.6 Synthesis [S/H]

The unifying view proposed here is not that phosphate alone causes neurodevelopmental change,

but that phosphate handling becomes part of a broader Sodium-Struggle architecture whenever

sodium conservation, water balance, and effective arterial filling are repeatedly unstable.

Different combinations of timing, intensity, branch state, renal maturity, and compensatory



success could therefore generate distinct endocrine and receptor-set-point trajectories while still

belonging to the same systems-physiology family.

Operationally, the model predicts phase-dependent signatures rather than a single biomarker.

Candidate readouts include urinary phosphate and PTH during early phosphaturic phases; delayed

FGF-23 with lower calcitriol during repeated phosphate exposure; variable renin and aldosterone

according to branch state; copeptin or AVP and cortisol during pressure-support phases; and

reduced nitric-oxide proxies or increased oxidative markers in higher-tone vascular states. These

markers would be expected to vary across time and context rather than converge on a single static

endocrine profile.

On that basis, the endocrine-mineral section remains a systems-physiology bridge within the

manuscript: phosphate flux is translated into proximal phosphaturia, endocrine interpretation,

branch-specific sodium conservation, and compensatory vascular support. The proximal-distal

nephron dissociation loop is therefore the central organising mechanism by which phosphate

handling enters Sodium-Struggle physiology and contributes to oscillatory sodium-volume

regulation.


45 The Intergenerational Sodium Struggle: Transmission of

Structural Constraints and Receptor Imprinting

46 The Male Skew in Sodium-Struggle Outcomes

46.1 Sex-Biased Resolution of Sodium Threat

A consistent feature of autism spectrum disorder is its male predominance. Within the

Sodium-Struggle framework, this skew does not arise from sex-specific exposures, but from

sex-specific resolution strategies to the same anti-hyponatraemia challenges. In brief, males are

more vulnerable because anti-hyponatraemia compensation in males shifts more rapidly toward

pressure-based, cortisol-dominant, and sympathetic solutions, whereas females retain buffering,

aldosterone-mediated, and volume-stabilizing strategies for longer developmental windows.

At the level of vasopressin signalling, sodium-struggle provokes pulsatile or sustained arginine

vasopressin (AVP) release to defend effective volume. AVP action can be channelled through

renal V2 receptors, favouring water handling and buffering, or through vascular V1a receptors,

favouring vasoconstriction and pressure defence. Under stress, males show a greater bias toward

V1a-mediated responses, while females preserve V2-mediated buffering. The consequence is

higher vascular tone and reduced microvascular perfusion in males, exposing the developing

brain to repeated pressure-based defence rather than volume-based stabilization, and increasing

susceptibility to perfusion instability during sensitive developmental periods.

A similar divergence appears within the renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system (RAAS).

Anti-hyponatraemia activates RAAS in both sexes, but this activation can be resolved through

aldosterone-dominant sodium recovery or angiotensin II–dominant vasoconstriction. Males shift

more readily toward angiotensin II dominance, while females preserve aldosterone



responsiveness. Angiotensin-driven defence maintains blood pressure at the cost of vascular

constriction, including placental and cerebral beds, whereas aldosterone supports epithelial

sodium recovery with less ischemic burden. Thus, males more often defend sodium via

constriction rather than recovery.


46.2 Low Renin as a Gatekeeper of Maladaptive Sodium Defence

When aldosterone signalling is weak, inconsistent, or developmentally suppressed, cortisol

increasingly substitutes as a mineralocorticoid signal. This cortisol substitution is earlier and more

pronounced in males, reflecting higher glucocorticoid receptor (GR) tone and a faster shift toward

cortisol-dominant sodium defence. In females, estrogen and more effective 11β-HSD2 gating

protect mineralocorticoid receptor (MR) specificity. Cortisol-dominant defence preserves pressure

but suppresses growth, alters neuronal maturation timing, and interferes with inhibitory

interneuron development, trading developmental refinement for short-term survival.

Anti-hyponatraemia compensation also recruits the sympathetic nervous system. Males exhibit

stronger sympathetic surges and slower down-regulation, whereas females retain greater

parasympathetic buffering. Chronic sympathetic tone reduces tolerance to cerebral blood-flow

variability, alters neurovascular coupling, and biases stress-reactive circuitry, imprinting a

“pressure-first” physiological logic during brain wiring.


46.3 Downstream Developmental Consequences of Pressure-Dominant

Defence

At the level of ionic regulation, sex differences extend beyond neuronal KCC2.

Anti-hyponatraemia activates the WNK–SPAK/OSR1 signalling axis, which regulates chloride

transporters broadly, including NKCC1, KCC2, and renal NKCC2. Male physiology shows

greater stress-responsive plasticity in this axis, producing larger swings in transporter expression

and intracellular chloride handling. As a result, intracellular chloride remains elevated for longer,

delaying the GABAergic excitatory-to-inhibitory switch even before full KCC2 maturation. In

parallel, males appear more prone to transporter reprogramming (including NKCC2 instability in

the kidney), whereas females preferentially buffer stress hormonally rather than rewiring transport

machinery.

Hyponatraemia adaptation further requires osmolyte loss (e.g., taurine, myo-inositol, betaine) to

prevent cellular swelling. Males lose osmolytes more rapidly and recover them more slowly,

while estrogen protects osmolyte pools in females. Because taurine and inositol are

neuroprotective and required for inhibitory interneuron maturation, osmolyte debt allows

neuronal survival but compromises developmental refinement.



Barrier integrity also differs by sex. The combined effects of sodium-struggle, cortisol, and

angiotensin II increase blood–brain barrier permeability. Males exhibit greater stress-induced

barrier leak, whereas estrogen stabilizes tight junctions in females. The resulting ionic noise,

inflammatory susceptibility, and altered CSF–interstitial gradients further destabilize circuit

formation in males.

Finally, anti-hyponatraemia states subtly suppress thyroid signalling and reduce Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase

efficiency. Male brains are more sensitive to early thyroid insufficiency, while females benefit

from redundancy via estrogen–thyroid crosstalk, slowing ion and energy recovery in males during

critical windows.

Taken together, when sodium is threatened early in life, females predominantly defend through

buffering, aldosterone, volume stability, and delayed stress signalling, including additional

endocrine reserve such as higher prepubescent DHEA-S that cushions cortisol dominance when

zona fasciculata output monopolizes adrenal precursors. Males, in contrast, more often defend

through pressure, cortisol, sympathetic tone, and transporter rewiring. The same exposure

therefore produces adaptation in females but a higher developmental cost in males, providing a

mechanistic basis for the male predominance observed in autism spectrum disorder.


47 Downstream Convergence: From Physiology to Phenotype

47.0 Purpose and Scope

Autism spectrum disorder (ASD) is defined clinically by persistent differences in social

communication and interaction, alongside restricted, repetitive patterns of behaviour, interests, or

activities, with onset in early development. The central question addressed in this section is

therefore a causal one: what physiological conditions give rise to this specific

neurodevelopmental phenotype?

This section establishes the mechanistic infrastructure explaining why the recognised autism

behavioural phenotype emerges in its characteristic clinical form under the Sodium-Struggle

hypothesis. Rather than treating behaviour as a domain separate from physiology, we trace the

downstream propagation of early sodium–volume and perfusion defence imprints described in

preceding sections. The goal is to demonstrate that the phenotype is not a collection of arbitrary

psychological deficits, but a coherent set of dignity-preserving, load-management outcomes

arising from a developing system operating under specific haemodynamic and delivery

constraints.



47.1 First Principles: Defended Variables and the Meaning of “Success”

47.1.1 Defended variables under sodium–volume challenge

Under the assumptions of the Sodium-Struggle framework, early low effective sodium and/or low

effective circulating volume recruits anti-hyponatraemia and anti-hypovolaemia defence. This

defence re-weights the AVP–RAAS–cortisol economy and can leave durable shifts in thresholds

and gains, including receptor drift, altered signalling ratios, and bias in vascular tone and reserve.

In this framing, the relevant operating condition is not a global breakdown state but a narrowing

of stability margins around defended variables, specifically:

●​ arterial pressure stability​

●​ venous return (preload) stability​

●​ oxygen delivery stability, with particular relevance to distal territories where delivery

margin is most limited​

These constraints persist developmentally and shape how the organism manages load across

contexts.


47.1.2 Redefining success: distance from the failure boundary

A central control-theoretic reframing is that “success” is defined as maintaining distance from a

failure boundary. In this context, syncope (fainting) represents the failure boundary—a state in

which combined arterial pressure, venous return, and delivery constraints exceed controllable

margin.

As detailed in earlier autonomic sections, when pressure preservation becomes paramount,

parasympathetic buffering can be conditionally inhibited, and stabilisation is carried by

sympathetic recruitment together with vasopressinergic signalling and cortisol support. The

implication is that many observed behavioural shifts are best understood as earlier control actions

that reduce variance or peak load, thereby lowering the probability of crossing the failure

boundary.

In this light, the rarity of overt fainting in ASD populations is not contradictory to physiological

fragility. It is consistent with successful upstream compensation and behavioural load-shedding.


47.2 The Core Cost Problem: High-Penalty Haemodynamic Manoeuvres

To preserve defended variables under narrowed margins, the system has access to stabilisation

manoeuvres that are effective but carry substantial energetic and visceral cost. These manoeuvres

form a class, including:

●​ splanchnic venous recruitment​



●​ intense sympathetic vasoconstriction​

●​ AVP/cortisol carry​

●​ baroreflex operation at high gain​

●​ venous return instability across transitions​

A critical scale clarification is that the splanchnic vascular bed, under sympathetic control, can

sequester or mobilise approximately 20–25% of circulating blood volume, along with a

substantial fraction of total body fluid via capacitance vessels and capillary fluid shifts. This

makes it a high-leverage stabilisation resource, but also a prominent source of visceral cost,

variability, and discomfort when recruited.

Because these manoeuvres are costly, behavioural load-shedding becomes the lowest-cost

stabilisation option for reducing the frequency and intensity with which the high-penalty

manoeuvre class must be invoked.


47.2.1 Upright posture and motion as a load stack

As described in Section 24, upright posture mechanically increases pooling into gravity-sensitive

capacitance beds, explicitly including the splanchnic reservoir. When upright posture is combined

with motion and social engagement, a cumulative load stack emerges:

●​ hydrostatic pooling increases venous return noise​

●​ compensatory vasomotor actions raise sympathetic and endocrine cost​

●​ gut perfusion trade-offs increase the probability of visceral consequences​

Within this framework, a preference for sitting or stillness is not interpreted as passivity. It is a

variance-reduction strategy that limits hydrostatic amplification, reduces preload variability, and

lowers the likelihood that high-cost haemodynamic manoeuvres will be required.


47.3 The Cortical Bottleneck: Watershed Anatomy and Delivery

Margins

The structural vulnerability relevant to ASD is spatially specific. Key association regions

involved in social cognition, including the superior temporal sulcus (STS), temporoparietal

junction (TPJ), and prefrontal cortex (PFC), are metabolically demanding and anatomically

positioned in distal watershed territories, particularly along the middle cerebral artery–posterior

cerebral artery (MCA–PCA) borderzones.



In such distal terrain, oxygen delivery is preferentially susceptible to systemic perfusion

limitation when margins narrow due to reductions in effective pressure head. This constraint is

best framed as delivery limitation, not intentional diversion. By analogy to distal irrigation,

tissues furthest from the supply source operate with reduced reserve, making small systemic

perturbations disproportionately expressed as local delivery shortfall or increased variability.


47.3.1 Why social processing is uniquely expensive

Social interaction imposes a distinctive metabolic burden. It requires high-bandwidth multimodal

integration, rapid state transitions, and continuous prediction updating. These demands

disproportionately recruit STS, TPJ, and PFC networks, precisely in regions where delivery

margin is mechanically constrained.

Under narrowed systemic margin, social processing therefore becomes the fastest route to a

supply–demand boundary. Where the term “vigilance” is used, it is defined physiologically as an

increase in arousal or salience gain that amplifies metabolic cost under cue volatility in

perfusion-marginal tissue.


47.4 Local AVPR1A (V1a) Imprinting: A Gain Constraint, Not a Trait

Consistent with Section 17.4, endocrine signalling in this framework is broadcast, while

tissue-level receptor imprinting determines local interpretation and response.

Such local gain constraints can bias vascular tone, constrain dynamic dilation and reserve, and

increase sensitivity to transient instability in specific beds without implying global temperament

or personality features. As summarised in Table 27-2, AVPR1A up-weighting aligns with

vasoconstrictive bias and phenotype clusters characterised by rigidity and perseveration, while

OXTR down-weighting aligns with diminished eye contact. This alignment situates both

social-perceptual differences and restricted/repetitive behaviours within a single

endocrine–vascular economy.


47.5 Behaviour as Load-Shedding: Mechanistic Derivation of the

Phenotype

The causal chain defended here is:

Early sodium–volume and perfusion defence → autonomic prioritisation and vascular-tone

bias → dynamic delivery constraints in watershed association cortex → high cost of

demand-volatile social processing → behavioural load-shedding → recognisable ASD

phenotype.

Behaviour is treated as part of the stabilisation stack, not as a psychological overlay.


47.5.1 Social-perceptual behaviours



Reduced eye contact and gaze aversion emerge as input down-weighting under delivery

constraint. Eye contact is a high-salience, high-bandwidth input that rapidly increases

STS/TPJ/PFC integration demand. In a system with constrained dynamic dilation and local gain

bias, reducing sustained eye contact lowers peak integrative demand and decreases the probability

of approaching a local supply–demand boundary.

Mid-interaction drop-out reflects the same control logic. As predicted by Box 25, association

networks operating near constrained margins show state-dependent variability and “fine until

suddenly not fine” transitions. Engagement is maintained while the supply–demand gap remains

within margin; a small additional load can precipitate a threshold crossing into a lower-integration

state. This transition does not require affective panic. It is a control-system boundary event.


47.5.2 Posture, movement, and rhythmic repetition

Preference for sitting quietly reflects variance reduction. Upright posture increases hydrostatic

load and splanchnic pooling, raising preload variability and the likelihood of invoking high-cost

stabilisation manoeuvres. Sitting reduces hydrostatic amplification and multisensory integration

demands, biasing the system toward a lower-variance regime.

The contribution of the skeletal muscle pump to venous return is established physiology. On that

basis, a plausible and testable extension is that low-variance rhythmic movement can be

structurally favoured under constraint because it provides predictable motor output and may offer

auxiliary venous return support, reducing preload variability and haemodynamic cost. In this

view, stimming is auxiliary haemodynamic labour, not merely a soothing behaviour.


47.5.3 Collapse without panic

“Stress” is defined operationally as any condition that recruits the sodium-defence stack,

including posture transitions, motion, sensory density, illness, heat, or dehydration. Under such

load, the system may transition from a high-integration/high-variance mode to a

low-integration/low-variance mode. This collapse of engagement reflects context-conditioned

prioritisation and threshold crossing in a constrained control system, not affective escalation.


47.5.4 GI–social coupling: the splanchnic switch

The tight coupling of gastrointestinal symptoms and social contexts is explained mechanically via

the splanchnic switch. Social processing demand threatens cerebral delivery, increasing the

probability of recruiting sympathetic, vasopressinergic, and cortisol-supported stabilisation. This

reshapes splanchnic tone and gut perfusion, producing visceral discomfort as a peripheral readout

of stabilisation cost.

The common report of stomach aches before school is consistent with context-conditioned

autonomic preparation. Contexts with a reliable history of high load stacks can trigger early

recruitment of the defence state, such that visceral symptoms appear prior to entry into the

context itself.



47.6 Explicit DSM Convergence: Why the Domains Co-Occur

Within this framework, the two DSM-defined domains of ASD are predictably co-expressed

under shared constraint, because they represent complementary components of a single control

solution.

Social communication and interaction differences reflect a state-dependent cortical cost ceiling

under delivery constraint. Restricted, repetitive behaviours reflect the lowest-variance behavioural

regime available to reduce demand volatility and haemodynamic cost. Because social interaction

is the most demand-volatile channel, and repetition is the most stabilising channel, their

co-occurrence follows directly from the underlying economics.


47.7 Clustering, Persistence, and Generalisation

These behaviours cluster because they reduce overlapping costs in the same stabilisation

economy: orthostatic variance, integrative demand volatility, and autonomic recruitment intensity.

Their persistence reflects durable threshold and gain shifts, producing context-dependent fragility

rather than a fixed resting abnormality. Their generalisation reflects sensitivity to load class, not

stimulus category. Any context that recruits the sodium-defence stack can bias behaviour toward

the same stabilising patterns.

Ultimately, the child is not avoiding the person; the child is avoiding the haemodynamic and

visceral cost required to sustain the interaction.


47.8 Conclusion

Early sodium–volume and perfusion defence can imprint a pressure-prioritised

autonomic–vascular economy in which stabilisation under load relies more readily on

sympathetic recruitment and vasopressin/cortisol support, narrowing dynamic delivery margins in

watershed association cortex. In this setting, high-bandwidth social processing disproportionately

taxes STS/TPJ/PFC integration, necessitating load-shedding via gaze down-weighting,

engagement down-shifts, postural variance reduction, and low-variance repetitive regimes. These

behaviours reduce haemodynamic and visceral cost and function as successful prevention steps

that allow rare overt failure to coexist with challenge-revealed fragility, yielding the recognisable

ASD phenotype as a coherent downstream outcome of early physiological constraint.


48 Discussion

Note: to be added to the discussion:


Speculative fever-triggered cellular reset: heat-shock, cytoskeletal

remodeling, and pyrin regulation

A further speculative possibility is that fever contributes to transient improvement not only

through endocrine and neuromodulatory state shifts, but also through a short-lived cellular repair

program. Febrile-range temperatures are sufficient to activate the heat-shock response, with



HSF1-dependent transcription and induction of HSP70/HSP72 documented both in human

leukocytes exposed to fever-like whole-body hyperthermia and in cultured cells maintained at

38.5–39.5°C. This suggests that fever can engage a bona fide chaperone response at physiologic

temperatures rather than acting only as a nonspecific metabolic stressor.

These chaperone systems are relevant because they do more than buffer damaged proteins. Hsp70

machinery can extract polypeptides from aggregates and support refolding, Hsp27 can remodel

actin-linked stress-fiber and focal-adhesion dynamics, and Hsp90 is required for microtubule

stability and vesicular organization. Inflammasome signaling is also chaperone-sensitive: HSP70

restrains NLRP3 activation, whereas HSP90 helps maintain the stability of NLRP3-dependent

inflammatory machinery. Thus, heat-shock responses can in principle reorganize both structural

and inflammatory signaling complexes during febrile stress.

Cytoskeletal organization is particularly relevant in this context because actin filaments undergo

continuous turnover and are vulnerable to oxidative and metabolic damage. Under conditions of

cellular stress, misfolded or oxidized cytoskeletal proteins can accumulate and interfere with

normal actin filament dynamics, producing a form of cytoskeletal proteostasis stress. Heat-shock

proteins and associated quality-control systems help repair or remove such damaged components

while promoting cytoskeletal remodeling. Fever therefore has the potential to transiently improve

cytoskeletal organization through chaperone-mediated repair and increased actin turnover.

Pyrin provides a specific link between cytoskeletal control and innate immune regulation.

Established work shows that active RhoA signals through PKN1/2 to phosphorylate pyrin,

enabling 14-3-3 binding and maintaining pyrin in an inhibited state; disruption of this pathway

releases that restraint. Pyrin regulation is also shaped by microtubule dynamics downstream of

dephosphorylation, helping explain the sensitivity of pyrin-driven disorders such as familial

Mediterranean fever to colchicine and related cytoskeletal perturbation. In that sense, pyrin sits at

a junction where cytoskeletal organization, stress signaling, and inflammasome readiness

converge.

Taken together, these observations allow a cautious conceptual model in which fever transiently

activates cellular repair processes capable of reorganizing cytoskeletal and inflammasome

regulation. In simplified form, the sequence might be summarized as:

baseline state​

cytoskeletal proteostasis stress or damaged actin components​

↓​

imperfect regulation of cytoskeleton-linked danger-sensing systems such as pyrin

febrile inflammatory state​

↓​

HSF1 activation and heat-shock response​

↓​

induction of HSP70, HSP90, and HSP27​

↓​



protein refolding, actin turnover, and cytoskeletal remodeling​

↓​

temporary stabilization of inflammasome and stress-signaling complexes

recovery phase​

↓​

short-term improvement in cellular signaling stability.

Within this framework, the potential benefit would not arise from pyrin activation itself but from

fever-induced HSF1/HSP activity transiently improving proteostasis, reorganizing cytoskeletal

architecture, and stabilizing stress-sensitive regulatory complexes that influence inflammasome

thresholds and related signaling pathways. A brief period of improved intracellular organization

during fever or early recovery could in principle yield more stable neuronal signaling and thereby

support improved network function.

This proposal remains a hypothesis-level inference rather than a demonstrated autism mechanism.

However, it offers a plausible cellular-level contributor to the fever-improvement phenomenon

that could operate alongside endocrine, autonomic, and cytokine-mediated explanations.


48.1 PV interneuron maturation, pruning, and selective vulnerability to

sodium-struggle states

A growing body of work has independently implicated three phenomena in autism spectrum

conditions: (i) delayed or reduced parvalbumin (PV) interneuron maturation, (ii) impaired

activity-dependent synaptic pruning, and (iii) early cortical overgrowth with persistence of

immature connectivity. Although each has been well described, they have rarely been integrated

into a single developmental mechanism.

PV-positive GABAergic interneurons play a central role in regulating critical-period timing and

circuit refinement. Work by Hensch and colleagues established that maturation of PV

interneurons gates the opening and closure of critical periods, providing the precise inhibitory

timing required for stable circuit consolidation. In parallel, extensive pruning literature, including

microglia-mediated synaptic elimination, demonstrates that pruning depends on temporally

patterned neuronal activity rather than absolute firing rate. Synchronous, well-timed activity

stabilises selected synapses, whereas poorly coordinated or noisy firing fails to generate robust

pruning cues.

Within this framework, PV interneurons do not directly execute pruning. Instead, they function as

upstream regulators of the activity patterns that permit pruning to occur. When PV maturation is

delayed or functionally blunted, pyramidal neurons tend to fire excessively or asynchronously,

producing ambiguous calcium signals and reduced engagement of pruning pathways. The

resulting phenotype is not neuronal over-proliferation, but prolonged retention of immature

synapses and apparent network overgrowth.



Importantly, PV interneurons are among the neuronal components most sensitive to

sodium-struggle states and osmotic instability. Their maturation occurs late in development and

depends on successful completion of several volume- and ion-dependent processes, including

cellular shrinkage, stable calcium buffering, and robust upregulation of KCC2

(potassium–chloride cotransporter 2). Conditions that impair effective sodium availability

promote intracellular water influx and disrupt regulatory volume decrease, opposing the

shrinkage-dependent transition required for PV expression and fast-spiking inhibitory function.

As a result, PV interneurons may survive but fail to reach full functional maturity, leading to

reduced PV expression and impaired inhibitory timing.

This selective vulnerability provides a mechanistic bridge between early osmotic stress and later

circuit-level outcomes. Rather than increasing neuronal growth directly, sodium-struggle states

delay the maturation of PV-mediated inhibitory timing, weakening the activity-dependent signals

required to initiate synaptic pruning. In this way, delayed PV maturation offers a coherent

explanation for preserved neuron numbers, excess synapses, early cortical overgrowth, and

long-term circuit rigidity observed in autism spectrum conditions.


48.2 Convergent osmotic pathways across diverse autism risk exposures

A wide range of exposures and conditions have been associated with increased autism risk,

including maternal stress, gestational diabetes, maternal infection, premature birth, SIADH-like

hormonal profiles, elevated maternal or neonatal vasopressin, early electrolyte instability, and

sodium-independent excitatory exposures such as MSG or ammonia. These factors appear

unrelated on the surface; they belong to different medical specialties and are rarely discussed

together. Yet they share a deeper physiological theme: each can produce either true

hyponatraemia or a biochemical state that mimics hyponatraemia, creating the same downstream

osmotic and endocrine consequences.

In several of these pathways—maternal stress, high-AVP states, prematurity, SIADH-like

profiles—the mechanism involves excess vasopressin and impaired free-water clearance. In

gestational diabetes, extremely high maternal glucose creates an “osmotic illusion,” in which fetal

cells experience a net inward movement of water despite normal sodium values. Other pathways

operate through infection-related excitatory osmotic stress, ammonia accumulation, or

sodium-independent glutamatergic mechanisms (e.g., MSG) that cause intracellular swelling

through different routes. When viewed together, these seemingly disparate risk factors converge

on a single physiological vulnerability: impaired osmotic adaptability during critical windows of

early neurodevelopment.

This reframing helps explain why many environmental and perinatal contributors to autism have

been difficult to integrate into traditional synaptic or genetic theories. The exposures do not share

a chemical structure, a genome-wide signature, or a behavioural pattern; they share a

physiological consequence—a shift in the osmotic environment that prevents neurons and glia

from performing the volume transitions required for normal development.

48.3 Osmotic timing errors as a unifying developmental framework

Across multiple strands of autism research—neuroimaging, post-mortem morphology,

developmental trajectory studies, glial physiology, and hormonal signalling—a consistent pattern

emerges: early proliferation is excessive, differentiation is delayed, synaptic density becomes

abnormally high, pruning is impaired or mistimed, and cortical circuits retain immature

characteristics well beyond the expected developmental window. These features have been widely

described but rarely attributed to a single physiological driver. The present framework identifies

one such candidate: impaired osmotic adaptability during early development, specifically the

inability of neurons and glia to perform the volume transitions required for timely differentiation

and circuit stabilisation, as reflected in delayed inhibitory maturation, impaired pruning, and

persistent circuit immaturity.

Neural stem cells must undergo a brief, regulated period of shrinkage before they can exit

proliferation and enter differentiation. This shrinkage is both a mechanical and biochemical

signal, activating ion channels, cytoskeletal elements, and transcriptional programs essential for

maturation. Subtle, chronic intracellular swelling—whether due to true hyponatraemia or other

sodium-independent osmotic stresses—disrupts this transition. Neural precursors continue to

proliferate but fail to mature, producing excessive numbers of unstable, immature synapses and

generating the “pruning backlog” observed in autistic cortex. This developmental timing error

naturally predicts the early brain overgrowth followed by plateau, delayed synapse stabilisation,

persistent immaturity in circuit physiology, and atypical inhibitory control that define autism’s

early trajectory.

Glial cells exhibit similar vulnerabilities. Astrocytes require intact regulatory volume decrease

(RVD) to perform glutamate clearance, potassium buffering, metabolic support, and aquaporin-4

reorganisation. When RVD is impaired, glia remain in a reactive, immature state, amplifying

excitatory/inhibitory imbalance and reducing the precision of synaptic pruning. These glial effects

reinforce the neuronal delays described above, extending the developmental window in which

circuits remain unstable and energetically inefficient.

A notable feature of this mechanism is that it does not require overt or clinically recognised

hyponatraemia. Many early-life conditions can produce osmotic instability — including hormonal

states (maternal stress and AVP excess, SIADH-like profiles), sodium-independent excitatory

exposures (MSG, ammonia, infections), early cortisol dominance, and electrolyte shifts

associated with illness or inflammation. These conditions do not appear in medical records, often

resolve spontaneously, and leave no direct biomarker in later life. Their impact lies in timing:

even brief windows of impaired shrinkage can shift the trajectory of neurogenesis, differentiation,

pruning, and autonomic imprinting.



This helps explain why autism research, despite extensive study, has struggled to identify a

singular cause. Osmotic mechanisms sit at the intersection of nephrology, developmental

neurobiology, endocrinology, glial physiology, and mechanotransduction—fields that typically

operate in isolation. The developmental importance of cell-volume transitions is only beginning

to be recognised, with most foundational work emerging within the past decade (2016–2022).

Meanwhile, autism research remains dominated by genetic and synaptic theories, even though

most idiopathic cases lack a genetic driver and cannot be explained by synaptic mechanisms

alone. Moreover, the rapid, population-level rise in autism prevalence cannot be accounted for by

genetic change, which occurs far too slowly to produce an epidemic-scale increase. These

dominant frameworks are not easily reconciled with the geographic clustering seen in autism

prevalence, which aligns more closely with environmental, nutritional, and hydrological patterns

than with genetic distributions.

Within this context, the osmotic timing-window hypothesis offers an integrative model: early-life

exposures that impair cellular shrinkage—whether through true hyponatraemia or through

sodium-independent osmotic phenocopies—can converge on a shared developmental outcome.

This outcome matches the known sequence of autistic neurodevelopment more closely than any

single prior model: altered neurogenesis, delayed but excessive synaptogenesis, atypical pruning,

persistent circuit immaturity, and early overgrowth followed by plateau.

This framework does not exclude genetic contributions; rather, it provides a biological rationale

for why diverse genetic and environmental factors can appear to lead to a similar developmental

phenotype. Any factor that reduces osmotic adaptability or impairs the

shrinkage-to-differentiation transition may amplify or unmask a shared downstream pathway. In

this sense, osmotic vulnerabilities may serve as an organising principle capable of unifying the

heterogeneous findings across autism research.


Box 48-1 One mechanism, multiple read‑outs (receptor‑network view)

AVP/OXT axis: AVPR1A/1B sensitization; AVPR2/AQP2 down‑shift; OXTR down‑shift under

cortisol bias → high‑AVP vs low‑AVP phenotypes across contexts. [H]

MR/GR axis: Sodium‑to‑cortisol switch with endocrine lock‑in → GR tilt, reduced AVP

sensitivity, altered OXTR; persists even after sodium normalizes. [H]

RAAS cross‑talk: ATP6AP2‑mediated Ang II amplification and trafficking effects couple osmotic

stress to RAAS imprinting. [H]

Cell‑identity hysteresis: collecting‑duct vasopressin escape; Notch/Hes1/Foxi1‑dependent fate

drift with incomplete reversibility; endothelial MR tilt toward low‑NO tone. [H]

Timing: pre‑keratinization (NFAT5 bias, osmolyte debt) versus postnatal analogues determine

which networks express the imprint. [H]

Reviewer takeaway. One upstream error signal—insufficient sodium—can drive multi‑receptor

and cell‑state imprinting (AVPR/OXTR, MR/GR, RAAS; epithelial/endothelial identity).

“High‑AVP” and “low‑AVP” autisms are different read‑outs of the same osmotic mechanism, not

different causes. [H]



49 Shared Developmental Origins of Autism, Cardiometabolic

Disease, Chronic Kidney Disease, and Schizophrenia

The working premise of this section is that autism spectrum disorder (ASD) and the adult metabolic triad of

cardiovascular disease (CVD), insulin resistance/type 2 diabetes (IR/T2D), and chronic kidney disease

(CKD) may represent different temporal and organ-specific expressions of a shared Sodium-Struggle

physiology: a body trained by early or chronic threats to effective sodium and perfusion to defend against

Anti-Hyponatraemia using high-gain, stress-heavy endocrine, autonomic, and vascular strategies. Which

phenotype emerges—ASD, adult metabolic disease, both, or neither—depends on genetic susceptibility,

developmental timing, the specific Decapath (route by which sodium threat is sensed), and cumulative

environmental inputs. A crucial addition is a “hardware” constraint that can set the gain on the entire

system: congenital oligonephronia (low nephron endowment). The classical Brenner Hypothesis frames

low birth weight and fetal growth restriction as developmental arrests of nephrogenesis that lower nephron

number, biasing the organism toward salt-sensitive hypertension and CKD later in life. Here we propose

that ASD can be the neurodevelopmental manifestation of the same structural deficit when it is expressed

during a period in which cortical perfusion and neurovascular coupling are still being calibrated. In this

sense, hypertension/CVD and—potentially—other “neuro-renal” phenotypes with heavy vascular and

autonomic load, including schizophrenia, may represent later or alternate expressions along a shared

developmental trajectory rather than unrelated comorbidities.

At its core, the Sodium-Struggle Hypothesis proposes that repeated episodes of true or threatened

hyponatraemia, low effective circulating volume, or oxygen-limited Na⁺ transport (e.g. Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase

compromise under hypoxia) drive a long-term re-weighting of regulatory systems. The

renin–angiotensin–aldosterone system (RAAS), vasopressin (AVP), the hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal

(HPA) axis, sympathetic and vagal branches, endothelial and microvascular beds, aquaporins and

osmolytes, and the FGF-23/Klotho–phosphate axis are all recruited to keep sodium and perfusion “safe.”

Over time this defensive ensemble can settle into a new default: low-renin but high-gain RAAS behaviour,

cortisol-heavy compensation, sympathetic predominance with fragile vagal braking, endothelial stiffening

with perfusion fragility, and osmotic instability governed by sensitive AVP/AQP2/AQP4 circuits. Adrenal

zonation is hypothesised to drift from a mineralocorticoid-dominant zona glomerulosa (ZG,

CYP11B2-rich) toward a glucocorticoid-dominant, zona fasciculata-like (ZF, CYP11B1-rich) architecture,

biasing the system toward glucocorticoid (GR) rather than mineralocorticoid (MR) control; this “ZG→ZF

Drift” remains speculative but provides a useful organising metaphor.

Reduced nephron endowment operationalises this framework as a structural gain knob. If nephrogenesis is

curtailed by fetal hypoperfusion/hypoxia (a brain-sparing “fetal triage” state), renal reserve is permanently

reduced. Maintaining life-sustaining glomerular filtration rate (GFR) with fewer nephrons obliges

single-nephron hyperfiltration, implemented by Angiotensin II-mediated constriction of the efferent

arteriole (“efferent clamping”), functionally increasing AT1 signalling gain to preserve intraglomerular

pressure. This “hardware” constraint forces a physiology in which High-RAAS/High-Sympathetic tone is

not merely maladaptive signalling but a required survival strategy to defend filtration and circulating

volume in the face of reduced filtration surface area. The compensatory state is protective in the short

term—preserving kidney function and preventing catastrophic sodium loss—but it is costly: the same

vasomotor set-point that “squeezes” the kidney raises resistance in other beds, increasing vulnerability to

cortical micro-hypoperfusion (a plausible contributor to ASD expression in childhood) and driving

cumulative vascular and glomerular injury (“burnout”) that appears later as hypertension, CVD, and CKD.

This trade-off is the developmental logic linking early Anti-Hyponatraemia defence to divergent

phenotypes across the lifespan.



Early-life epidemiology around both ASD and the metabolic triad is compatible with this view. ASD risk is

elevated by maternal infection and inflammation, preeclampsia and other hypertensive disorders,

gestational diabetes and metabolic syndrome, placental insufficiency and hypoxia, maternal stress and

glucocorticoid exposure, and preterm birth and low birthweight. Many of these same exposures increase the

offspring’s risk of later hypertension, CKD, IR/T2D, and CVD, and are well recognised within the

“developmental origins of health and disease” literature as programming nephron number, vascular

structure, and insulin sensitivity. In both literatures, recurrent motifs include maternal and placental

vascular pathology, altered RAAS signalling, volume and sodium instability, and neonatal hypoxia or

dehydration, suggesting shared stress domains even when specific outcome phenotypes differ. Within this

shared domain, reduced nephron endowment provides a concrete mechanistic link—especially for the

replicated preterm/low-birth-weight signal—because premature transition to ex-utero life and fetal growth

restriction can arrest nephrogenesis and lock in low renal reserve, thereby lowering the threshold for

High-RAAS/High-Sympathetic Anti-Hyponatraemia strategies.

Within ASD, a surprisingly coherent set of biomarkers and physiological signatures point toward

vulnerability of sodium and water handling, even though overt hyponatraemia is not a defining feature.

Cerebrospinal fluid AVP concentrations are significantly lower in neonates and children who later develop

ASD compared with controls, and lower CSF AVP predicts both ASD diagnosis and greater social

symptom burden. This low-central-AVP state coexists with clinical case reports of extreme polydipsia,

SIADH-like dilutional hyponatraemia, and even diabetes insipidus in autistic individuals, especially in

those with intellectual disability or psychotropic exposure, indicating that when the water–sodium system is

perturbed, it can behave in a labile, sometimes dangerous way. Autonomic markers show reduced

high-frequency heart-rate variability and vagal activity with relative sympathetic predominance in many

autistic cohorts, indicative of a system that mobilises rapidly but relaxes poorly. Cerebral perfusion studies

using SPECT and arterial spin labelling repeatedly demonstrate regional and sometimes global

hypoperfusion—particularly in prefrontal, temporal, and fusiform regions—often correlated with symptom

severity, pointing to a brain whose microvasculature is both inflamed and perfusion-fragile. Endothelial and

oxidative markers, including urinary F2-isoprostanes, suggest an oxidative, endothelial-activating milieu

even in young autistic individuals. Metabolically, autistic children and adolescents show elevated

HOMA-IR and increased risk of type 2 diabetes and dyslipidaemia relative to BMI-matched peers,

consistent with insulin resistance that cannot be fully attributed to obesity. Mitochondrial biomarkers,

including raised lactate and abnormal acyl-carnitines, support the notion of “mitochondrial allostatic load.”

Bone mineral density is on average lower and fracture risk higher in autistic children and adults, hinting

(without proving) at chronic disturbances in mineral metabolism that might intersect with phosphate and

FGF-23/Klotho signalling. Direct measurements of FGF-23, Klotho, and RAAS components in ASD

cohorts are almost non-existent; yet the aggregate picture—low central AVP, water-handling fragility,

autonomic dysregulation, cortisol-stress sensitivity, oxidative endothelial injury, perfusion abnormalities,

and low BMD—aligns with a phenotype in which effective sodium and tonicity are recurrent stress points.

The nephron-endowment bridge adds a structural reason these stress points may become chronic: low renal

reserve compresses the physiological margin for water and sodium perturbations, increasing the need for



high-gain vasomotor and endocrine responses that can secondarily destabilise cortical perfusion during

development.

By contrast, the CVD–IR–CKD triad has a well-developed biomarker literature that foregrounds RAAS

activity, AVP/copeptin, sodium handling, and FGF-23/Klotho as central actors. Elevated renin, angiotensin

II, and aldosterone—or high tissue RAAS activity despite low measured renin—drive efferent arteriolar

constriction, glomerular hypertension, vascular stiffness, and left-ventricular hypertrophy. Low-renin,

salt-sensitive hypertension phenotypes, particularly prevalent in some Black populations, illustrate the

“quiet baseline, high-gain response” configuration that the sodium-struggle model emphasises. Copeptin, a

surrogate for AVP, is persistently elevated in CKD and heart failure and predicts CKD progression, incident

CVD, and mortality; hyponatraemia in these settings is a powerful adverse prognostic marker. CKD is

characterised by rising FGF-23 and falling Klotho, which are linked to arterial calcification, endothelial

dysfunction, and left-ventricular hypertrophy even after adjustment for traditional risk factors. Low-grade

systemic inflammation, oxidative stress, reduced HRV with sympathetic dominance, insulin resistance and

dyslipidaemia, and microvascular rarefaction in muscle, heart, and kidney complete a pattern that is

recognisably similar to the stress-loaded ASD physiology, albeit appearing later in life and with a different

primary phenotype. Brenner’s developmental logic makes the time shift legible: the same nephron-sparse

architecture that requires hyperfiltration in childhood may first present clinically as neurovascular fragility

(ASD-linked hypoperfusion signatures) and only later as glomerulosclerosis, salt-sensitive hypertension,

and overt CKD.

In this expanded cluster view, schizophrenia warrants consideration as another neuro-renal phenotype that

may, in a subset, share upstream developmental constraints with ASD and the CVD–IR–CKD triad. A

“smoking gun” proof-of-principle is 22q11.2 deletion syndrome (22q11.2DS): a recurrent copy-number

lesion that simultaneously loads risk onto neurodevelopment and renal endowment. Autism-spectrum

diagnoses or clinically significant autistic traits are frequently reported in 22q11.2DS (commonly cited in

the ~20–50% range across ascertainment strategies), and schizophrenia-spectrum illness occurs at markedly

elevated rates (~20–25% by adulthood). In parallel, 22q11.2DS is a recognized genetic driver of congenital

anomalies of the kidney and urinary tract (CAKUT), including renal agenesis and renal hypoplasia among

reported findings.

For renal physiology, agenesis (absent kidney) and hypoplasia (small kidney) are not ancillary

malformations but severe forms of congenital oligonephronia—large decrements in nephron mass that

compress renal reserve from birth. This hardware deficit validates the Sodium-Struggle premise that

nephron-sparse architecture can force High-RAAS/High-Sympathetic survival physiology: reduced renal

mass lowers the threshold at which Decapath insults recruit Anti-Hyponatraemia compensation and

obligate Ang II/AT1 “efferent clamping” to defend GFR and circulating volume, even when plasma renin is

feedback-suppressed. The same vasomotor set-point that preserves filtration pressure can, via systemic

spillover, bias the cerebral microcirculation toward hypoperfusion during development, while its chronic

use drives the later Brenner-style trajectory of pressure–natriuresis strain (salt-sensitive hypertension, CKD

progression, vascular burnout, and insulin resistance). In this sense, 22q11.2DS functions as a “golden

triangle” lesion—ASD, schizophrenia, and CAKUT arising from a single developmental

hit—demonstrating that the genetic architecture of neurodevelopmental vulnerability is directly coupled to

the genetic architecture of nephron endowment, validating the Neuro-Renal cluster model.



Developmental Timing and Gene-Class Leverage: Why a Shared

Sodium-Perfusion Vulnerability Can Read Out as ASD or

Schizophrenia

Within the Sodium-Struggle cluster, autism spectrum disorder (ASD) and schizophrenia can be treated as

different developmental read-outs of a shared vulnerability in sodium defence and perfusion stability, rather

than as categorically unrelated disorders. The key divergence is the interaction between developmental

timing and gene-class leverage. ASD-associated genetic liability is enriched for regulators with

disproportionate leverage during prenatal and early postnatal construction, when neurogenesis, migration,

synaptogenesis, interneuron placement, and neurovascular coupling are being established.

Schizophrenia-associated liability, by contrast, is enriched for regulators whose strongest phenotypic

leverage occurs during adolescent and early adult refinement, when activity-dependent plasticity,

myelination, inhibitory control under load, and microglial synaptic selection dominate. Under this framing,

the same mild, developmentally imprinted sodium-struggle state can yield early-life circuit miscalibration

(ASD-leaning) or later-life refinement failure (schizophrenia-leaning), depending on when the most

consequential genetic “gain knobs” are active.

ASD-leaning genotypes tend to load onto construction-phase gene classes that can couple directly to

sodium-perfusion instability, because they govern tissue growth, vascular and glial scaffolding, and the

energetic cost of excitability at the moment the system is being built. These include chromatin and

transcriptional regulators (for example, CHD8), growth-signalling regulators (for example, PTEN), and

early excitability calibration genes (for example, SCN2A). Such classes plausibly interact upstream with

sodium-struggle constraints by altering both demand, via neuronal and synaptic overproduction or altered

maturation schedules, and supply, via microvascular patterning and neurovascular coupling trajectories that

must be calibrated in a perfusion-fragile, stress-hormone- and autonomic-biased milieu. In this context,

early pruning can be delayed or inefficient without requiring an opposite mechanism later. Selective

microglial pruning is metabolically expensive, and under intermittent micro-hypoperfusion, osmotic

lability, and high-gain stress signalling, the high energy budget required for microglial motility and

phagocytosis may be intermittently unavailable. The system can therefore default to a preservation mode,

stabilising local connectivity and economising long-range integration rather than expending energy on

fine-grained refinement. This is a timing effect: the same upstream physiology exerts its strongest

phenotypic leverage when it coincides with maximal architectural plasticity and maximal dependence on

stable energy delivery and perfusion for orderly circuit assembly.

Schizophrenia-leaning genotypes, by contrast, tend to load onto refinement-phase gene classes that are less

likely to initiate sodium struggle but can strongly modulate how an already present, usually mild

sodium-struggle state is expressed when the brain is asked to re-optimise under adolescent or early adult

load. These include genes involved in activity-dependent plasticity and excitatory synapse tuning (for

example, CACNA1C and GRIN2A/GRIN2B), immune-modulated pruning (for example, C4A), stress-axis

persistence and glucocorticoid sensitivity (for example, FKBP5), and dopaminergic gating of salience and

learning (for example, DRD2). Here adolescence functions as a stress test rather than an origin. Rapid

somatic growth, particularly vertical elongation of the vascular column during height spurts, sharply

increases the hydrostatic pressure gradient required to perfuse the brain against gravity, amplifying the

burden placed on an already imprinted, high-gain baroreflex and sodium-perfusion control system.​

**** [Insert schematic: Vertical elongation of the vascular column during adolescence increases

hydrostatic load and baroreflex demand]​

Rising metabolic demand, sleep and circadian disruption, and increased psychosocial and inflammatory

exposures further magnify small oscillations in perfusion, tonicity, and endocrine–autonomic set-points that



were already present from earlier programming. Under these conditions, refinement can become excessive

or stochastic because the mechanisms that translate patterned activity into synaptic retention or elimination

are themselves the loci of genetic leverage, operating on inputs rendered unstable by sodium-perfusion

fragility, stress-heavy compensation, and fluctuating immune gating under high-gain autonomic control.

The result is not “the opposite of ASD,” but a later-phase failure mode characterised by imprecise selection,

inappropriate elimination, or unstable strengthening of circuits during the developmental window when

refinement dominates.

These two pruning narratives are therefore compatible rather than contradictory. Early-life

sodium-perfusion constraint combined with construction-phase genetic leverage can bias toward

under-selective, inefficient early pruning and locally over-stabilised connectivity, while the same upstream

constraint combined with refinement-phase genetic leverage can bias toward over-selective or stochastic

later pruning and degraded signal-to-noise in distributed networks. Importantly, this does not imply that

sodium struggle explains schizophrenia universally. Rather, it frames schizophrenia, in a subset of cases, as

a neuro-renal phenotype embedded within the broader Sodium-Struggle cluster, in which pre-existing, often

subclinical cardiorenal constraints and high-gain stress physiology lower the threshold at which adolescent

refinement mechanisms convert ordinary developmental demands into clinically salient circuit-level

instability. This subset framing preserves heterogeneity while providing a mechanistic route by which

shared early-life sodium-perfusion vulnerability can diverge into ASD-leaning versus

schizophrenia-leaning outcomes via timing and gene-class leverage, rather than distinct disease etiologies.

A further environmental strand tightening the sodium-struggle narrative comes from the water-hardness

literature. Multiple ecological and observational studies, synthesised in recent systematic reviews, report

that higher drinking-water hardness—reflecting higher concentrations of calcium and magnesium and, by

implication, higher total dissolved solids (TDS)—is associated with lower CVD mortality, whereas very

soft or low-mineral water is often linked to higher CVD risk, greater arterial stiffness, and older “arterial

age.” A recent study in Slovak communities, for example, found that residents consuming soft, low-mineral

water had significantly higher aortic pulse-wave velocity and arterial age than those drinking harder water,

despite similar lifestyles. Experimental work in children suggests that very low-mineral water can raise

homocysteine, worsen lipid profile, and increase oxidative stress, likely through disordered calcium and

vitamin D metabolism. Not all cohort studies agree, and confounding by diet and socioeconomic factors is

substantial; nonetheless, the weight of evidence supports the idea that chronically low-TDS water is at least

a modest cardiovascular stressor. From a sodium-struggle perspective, long-term consumption of

low-mineral water is an environmental analogue of mild dilutional pressure: it reduces the mineral

contribution to dietary osmolality and may foster subtle diuresis and electrolyte loss, repeatedly asking

kidney, RAAS, AVP, and endothelium to defend effective sodium and tonicity. Over years, this could

promote exactly the kind of high-gain, stiff-artery architecture seen in the triad, especially in individuals

whose early life has already imprinted sodium-struggle compensation. The nephron-endowment bridge

sharpens the prediction: chronically low-TDS exposures should be most destabilising in those with

nephron-sparse “hardware,” because the buffering capacity for repeated dilutional challenges is structurally

constrained.
