Rather than implying that ASD and the triad share a discrete set of “the same genes,” this pattern suggests

that both phenotypes draw disproportionately on gene networks that regulate excitability, mitochondrial

robustness, endothelial and microvascular architecture, osmotic and sodium transport, and redox buffering.

In the sodium-struggle framing, these networks define organ vulnerability to recurrent sodium/volume and

hypoxic threats: in some individuals, brain circuits are the limiting factor; in others, kidney and vasculature

are. Nephron endowment adds a complementary non-genetic substrate on which these networks operate: a



nephron-sparse organism must run higher endocrine/autonomic “gain” to remain sodium-safe, raising the

probability that neurovascular development will be shaped under chronic vasomotor constraint.

A key conceptual move in this manuscript is to treat “sodium struggle” as a general, organism-level

response that can occur in anyone, not as a synonym for ASD. The outcome of such a struggle depends on

four interacting axes. First, genetic makeup sets thresholds in the systems just described: how easily RAAS

and AVP are activated, how resilient mitochondria and endothelium are to oxidative load, how plastic

adrenal zonation and aquaporin expression may be. Second, timing matters: the same

hyponatraemia-adjacent insult in a fetus, a neonate, a toddler, or a middle-aged adult will strike organ

systems at very different developmental stages. Fetal and early-infant brain, placenta, and kidney are still

wiring their vascular and endocrine networks; mid-life organs are already constrained by prior remodelling.

Third, “which Decapath” is engaged—hypovolaemic loss, dilutional excess of free water,

RAAS-suppressive contexts (phosphate/FGF-23, RO/low-TDS water, vasodilatory lipids), inflammatory

cytokine storms, or endocrine drug exposures—will determine which sensors and effectors are most heavily

recruited. A low-sodium diet with high AVP is not physiologically equivalent to a high-phosphate,

RAAS-suppressed state with low AVP but high FGF-23; the imprints on adrenal, endothelium, and

aquaporins will differ. Finally, cumulative environment—heat exposure, dehydration, infections, diet,

low-mineral water, medication combinations (e.g. SSRIs plus thiazides), psychosocial stress—adds decades

of “reminder” signals that either reinforce or partially reverse the original imprint. Across all four axes,

nephron endowment can be treated as a measurable “hardware limiter” that shifts the threshold at which

any Decapath becomes high-gain Anti-Hyponatraemia physiology.

Within this multidimensional space, one can understand why some individuals manifest primarily with

ASD traits, others with the adult CVD–IR–CKD triad, some with both, and some with neither. In one child,

a combination of genetically fragile synaptic and vascular genes, early placental hypoxia, neonatal sodium

instability, low central AVP, and constrained renal reserve may push the imprint toward brain networks

governing social cognition and arousal, producing ASD with autonomic and metabolic peculiarities but

relatively preserved kidney function early in life. In another, the same early vascular and renal stresses,

superimposed on nephron-sparse “hardware” and later life high-salt, low-TDS water consumption, lead to

adult low-renin hypertension, progressive CKD, IR, and CVD with little overt neurodevelopmental

difference. Some autistic teenagers already show IR, orthostatic intolerance, and headaches during heat or

dehydration—the mixed phenotype anticipated when brain and kidney/vasculature are co-vulnerable.

Others compensate robustly: genetic buffers, milder early insults, greater nephron reserve, or more

mineral-replete environments may allow sodium-struggle systems to operate without ever manifesting as

clinical ASD or triad disease.

Viewed in this light, the epidemiologic coincidence between ASD’s “low-effective-sodium” signatures and

the association of CVD with low-TDS drinking water becomes less trivial. ASD cohorts show evidence,

direct or indirect, of low central AVP and abnormal AVP responsivity, electrolyte instability under

behavioural or iatrogenic polydipsia, osmotic fragility, autonomic dysregulation with sympathetic bias,

cortisol-biased stress compensation, perfusion fragility and endothelial differences on imaging, and low

bone mineral density suggesting perturbations in mineral and possibly FGF-23/Klotho biology. The

CVD–IR–CKD triad, meanwhile, is enriched in phenotypes that, at a systems level, look like the long-term

consequences of defending against hyponatraemia and low tonicity: low-renin, salt-sensitive hypertension;

intermittent hyponatraemia and high copeptin; high FGF-23 with low Klotho; endothelial stiffness and

microvascular rarefaction; oxidative and mitochondrial strain; and marked vulnerability to even small

oscillations in sodium and volume. The suggestive observation that very low-mineral water consumption is

associated with higher arterial stiffness and adverse lipid and homocysteine profiles in children and adults

adds a concrete environmental substrate that can repeatedly push sodium-struggle systems in a dilutional

direction.



Taken together, these convergences do not prove that sodium-struggle imprinting is the common root of

ASD and the metabolic triad. Established physiology clearly supports overlapping pathways in

inflammation, oxidative stress, mitochondrial strain, HPA and autonomic dysregulation, endothelial

dysfunction, and insulin resistance; these alone can account for much of the observed biomarker overlap

without invoking sodium explicitly. The additional steps proposed here—that early hyponatraemia-adjacent

episodes, Decapath-specific RAAS/AVP recruitment, and possible adrenal zonation shifts leave a

long-lived imprint on sodium-defence architecture; that this architecture is visible in ASD as

low-effective-sodium vulnerability and in the triad as full-blown cardiorenal-metabolic disease; and that

low-TDS environmental exposures help maintain the struggle into adulthood—remain plausible but

incompletely tested. Key components, such as ZG→ZF Drift, aquaporin-encoded “osmotic memory,” and

characteristic low-baseline/high-gain RAAS profiles in autistic adults, are speculative. The

nephron-endowment bridge is less speculative in structure (nephron number is a fixed developmental trait)

but still requires direct testing in ASD cohorts; its value here is as a unifying hardware principle that makes

the RAAS/sympathetic “high-gain” set-point developmentally intelligible and mechanistically linkable to

the Brenner pathway.

The unifying value of the sodium-struggle perspective, however, lies in the way it reshapes testable

questions. It predicts, for example, that autistic adults with insulin resistance at normal BMI and adults with

low-renin, salt-sensitive hypertension and CKD share not only generic stress markers but specific

signatures of sodium-defence geometry: low resting renin with exaggerated RAAS and AVP spikes under

osmotic or orthostatic challenge; cortisol-heavy stress responses; labile serum sodium and copeptin;

endothelial stiffness disproportionate to age and BMI; and, in a subset, elevated FGF-23 with relative

Klotho deficiency even before overt CKD. It suggests that extreme ends of the water-hardness spectrum

(very low-TDS intake, heavily softened or desalinated water without mineral reconstitution) may be

particularly injurious in individuals whose early life already imprinted a sodium-struggle architecture—and

particularly in those whose hardware (low nephron endowment) compresses the buffering capacity for

repeated dilutional or salt-wasting challenges. And it implies that examining adrenal zonation, aquaporin

expression, and RAAS/AVP recruitment curves across ASD, triad, and control populations—while

stratifying by prematurity/low birthweight history and proxies of renal reserve—may reveal whether the

shared biomarker tapestry we currently see is simply the generic face of chronic stress, or the footprint of a

deeper, sodium-centred organising principle that links neurodevelopmental variation to late-life

cardiometabolic disease.


50 Conclusion: The Sodium Struggle as a Physiological

Framework

This manuscript began with a simple but demanding proposal: that a substantial share of autism

may be understood not as the direct consequence of a single lesion, gene, or environmental insult,

but as a developmental imprint left by repeated recruitment of sodium–volume–perfusion defence

physiology during sensitive windows of fetal and early postnatal life. In this framework, the

initiating signal is usually not clinical hyponatraemia narrowly defined, but movement toward

low-effective-sodium pressure—a condition in which extracellular tonicity, effective arterial

volume, and tissue perfusion become unstable or are transduced as unstable by the sensing

systems that normally preserve them. Because these variables are among the least negotiable in

biology, the organism responds with powerful short-horizon stabilisation. The central suggestion

of this manuscript is that when these survival responses are repeatedly engaged while regulatory

systems are still being calibrated, the lasting consequence may be distributed developmental

imprinting.



What gives this hypothesis its explanatory potential is that it connects levels of physiology that

are often studied separately. At the systems level, sodium–volume threat recruits a coordinated

defensive architecture that includes AVP, RAAS, autonomic vasomotor control,

glucocorticoid-mediated circulatory support, and renal sodium and water conservation. At the

cellular level, the same state favours a more volume-preserving ionic operating point, with

increased intracellular Na⁺ and Cl⁻, greater NKCC1 weighting, delayed chloride extrusion, and

subtle but persistent cellular swelling. At the energetic level, restoration of those gradients

increases Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase work and ATP demand, potentially amplifying purinergic signalling and

oxidative stress. At the redox and vascular level, oxidative stress may rise, BH4 availability may

fall, nitric-oxide signalling may weaken, and endothelial relaxation reserve may narrow, biasing

vascular tone toward constriction precisely when tissues are already vulnerable to perfusion

instability. The framework is therefore not a single-molecule hypothesis. It is a control-systems

hypothesis about what happens when survival physiology is repeatedly asked to defend sodium,

volume, and perfusion during development.

The hypothalamic controller layer helps unify this picture. Within the model developed here,

circulatory or osmotic threat can recruit magnocellular vasopressin neurons in the supraoptic and

paraventricular nuclei, with consequences that extend beyond endocrine water balance alone.

Systemic AVP release supports free-water handling and circulatory defence, while local dendritic

AVP signalling within hypothalamic tissue provides a plausible route for recruiting nearby

parvocellular AVP-expressing autonomic and CRH-linked populations. In that configuration,

hypothalamic output may become relatively more vasopressin-weighted, sympathetic, and

cortisol-supported, while oxytocin-mediated buffering may be relatively reduced under survival

constraint. This is not framed here as a pathological decision or a programme with foresight. It is

a plausible controller response when perfusion and extracellular stability are threatened. The

developmental question is what repeated occupation of that controller state may do to thresholds,

gain, receptor expression, and recovery kinetics across the wider sodium–volume survival axis.

From this perspective, the enduring consequence proposed by this manuscript is not persistent

electrolyte abnormality but distributed regulatory imprinting. Repeated defence recruitment

during developmental calibration windows could plausibly recalibrate vascular smooth-muscle

tone, endothelial nitric-oxide responsiveness, renal sodium handling, baroreflex and autonomic

gain, hypothalamic AVP–oxytocin balance, astrocytic osmotic buffering, extracellular-space

regulation, and cellular ion-transport programmes. In the developing brain, these imprints may

prolong chloride-weighted and mildly swollen cellular states, delay shrinkage-to-differentiation

transitions, alter inhibitory maturation, and increase the energetic cost of maintaining ionic

precision.

At the cellular level, development depends on a precise choreography of swelling and shrinking.

A healthy cell must expand at the right moments and then contract again as it exits proliferation,

commits to a fate, and integrates into functional networks. When sodium–volume defence leaves

cells lingering in a slightly swollen, chloride-weighted configuration, this choreography becomes

distorted. The shrinkage-to-differentiation transition can be delayed, and cells begin to operate

within a shifted ionic neighbourhood, in which calcium signalling, metabolic demand, and

excitability respond to a different electrochemical environment. Development does not require

catastrophic failure to change direction. It can be redirected by persistent shifts in timing,

threshold, and energetic reserve.

Such shifts may remain partly compensated during early infancy and only become more visible

during periods of increased physiological demand. The walking window illustrates this logic

particularly well. When infants begin upright locomotion, orthostatic circulation, baroreflex



demand, cerebellar coordination, cortical motor activity, and astrocytic buffering requirements all

increase simultaneously. Physiological imprints established earlier may then become more

apparent as the system attempts to meet these new demands.

A crucial point, which cannot be emphasised enough, is what this framework does not claim. It

does not claim that autism is caused simply by low sodium, still less by insufficient salt intake in

ordinary dietary terms. It does not require that infants or pregnant mothers typically pass through

overt clinical hyponatraemia. It does not propose a single endocrine signature that should appear

uniformly across all cases, nor does it deny the importance of genetics, immune signalling,

synaptic development, mitochondrial function, obstetric events, or environmental exposures.

Rather, the hypothesis is offered as a physiological scaffold within which these factors may

converge. Genetic variation may influence resilience, buffering capacity, and recovery kinetics.

Immune, metabolic, toxicologic, renal, or hydrochemical exposures may act as different routes

into the same defensive state. The claim is therefore neither reductive nor totalising. It is that

repeated defence of sodium–volume–perfusion stability may represent a missing axis along which

many established autism-associated findings can be reorganised.

One reason such explanations matter is that they restore a measure of intellectual agency. For

many families and clinicians, autism still appears as a constellation of observations without a

clear physiological story. Genetic findings identify risk but rarely illuminate the

moment-to-moment biology through which developmental trajectories diverge. A mechanistic

framework does not promise immediate control over outcomes, nor does it imply that autism can

be prevented through any simple intervention. What it can offer is orientation: a way to

understand how ionic stability, vascular regulation, endocrine defence, and developmental timing

may intersect within the same physiological landscape. In that sense, the value of this hypothesis

lies not in assigning blame or prescribing action but in making previously disconnected

observations intelligible and giving parents, researchers, and autistic individuals a clearer map of

the terrain.

Because the framework centres on the defence of sodium, volume, and perfusion, it also draws

attention to aspects of modern environments that intersect with those systems. Water mineral

composition, dietary phosphate load, oxidative stress burden, and other environmental conditions

that influence vascular tone or ion handling may therefore deserve closer physiological scrutiny.

Recognising these factors allows us to distinguish between osmotic allies and osmotic saboteurs

within the environments that shape early development. These observations should not be

interpreted as prescriptive advice or immediate intervention strategies. Their importance lies in

identifying domains where careful research may reveal how everyday environmental conditions

interact with developmental physiology.

The explanatory scope of the model is inherently testable. It predicts phase-dependent endocrine

geometries rather than a single uniform pattern; specific combinations of copeptin, renin,

aldosterone, cortisol, and vascular tone under defined obstetric and neonatal contexts; evidence of

chloride-weighted cellular states and altered NKCC1/KCC2 balance; signatures of increased ATP

demand, purinergic signalling, and osmolyte depletion; redox states consistent with ROS

amplification and reduced nitric-oxide support; and vascular or perfusion abnormalities

concentrated in late-maturing cortical territories. Some of these signals may appear not as

persistent abnormalities at rest but as altered responses to challenge—orthostatic transitions,

illness, osmotic stress, metabolic load, or developmental transitions such as the walking window.

If the sodium–volume defence framework proves useful in autism research, its implications may

extend further—not as a sweeping claim of common causation but as an invitation to explore



related questions in other fields where ionic regulation, vascular tone, autonomic balance, and

metabolic stress intersect. Disorders such as schizophrenia, chronic fatigue syndromes, and

cardiovascular disease may offer opportunities to examine whether similar forms of survival

physiology operate under different conditions and timescales. Whether those connections

ultimately prove strong or weak remains an empirical question, but the framework provides a

disciplined way to investigate them.

Scientific progress rarely begins with a single new fact. More often it occurs when familiar

observations are rearranged around a more useful organising principle. The proposal advanced

here is that sodium–volume defence may represent such a principle for a portion of autism

biology. Whether that proposal ultimately stands or falls will depend not on rhetoric but on

evidence. The task now is not to declare the story finished, but to follow its implications with

seriousness, scepticism, and curiosity wherever the physiology leads.
